1

Recognize the syndrome

Differential · Sepsis · FUO

Home — Basic ID Approach

Concise inpatient approach: history, source identification, empiric therapy, source control, stewardship.

  • History: travel, exposures (ticks, animals), recent healthcare/antibiotics, devices.
  • Exam: targeted to suspected source; document entry points, devices, neurologic status.
  • Diagnostics: obtain blood cultures (2 sets), urine, wound or respiratory cultures before antibiotics when possible.
  • Empiric therapy: cover likely pathogens given site, severity, and local antibiogram; reassess at 48–72 hrs.
  • Source control: drain abscess, remove infected devices, debride necrotic tissue — essential.

Clinical pearls

  • Sepsis: give broad-spectrum antibiotics within 1 hour of recognition; fluids and source control.
  • Neutropenic fever: immediate anti-pseudomonal IV therapy.
  • Always check renal function before dosing; many agents require adjustment.

Sepsis & Septic Shock

Surviving Sepsis Campaign 2026 (Evans, Prescott, Antonelli et al. Crit Care Med / Intensive Care Med 2026). 129 recommendations; 46 new statements. Endorsed by 24 organizations including IDSA.

TermDefinitionClinical surrogate
SepsisLife-threatening organ dysfunction from dysregulated host response to infectionSOFA increase ≥2 from baseline
Septic ShockSepsis + circulatory, cellular, and metabolic abnormalitiesVasopressors to maintain MAP ≥65 mmHg AND lactate >2 mmol/L despite adequate resuscitation

Screening tool update (SSC 2026 — Strong recommendation): Use NEWS, NEWS2, MEWS, or SIRS over qSOFA as a single screening tool. qSOFA has insufficient sensitivity for screening and is no longer recommended as a primary screening tool. Sepsis is a clinical diagnosis and should not be ruled in or out using a single biomarker.

SSC 2026 introduces a stratified antibiotic timing framework — replacing the "1 hour for all" approach with diagnosis-certainty-based timing.

Clinical scenarioRecommendationStrength
Septic shock (definite/probable)Administer antimicrobials immediately, ideally within 1 hour of recognitionStrong
Probable or definite sepsis without shockAdminister antimicrobials immediately, ideally within 1 hour of recognitionStrong
Possible sepsis without shockTime-limited rapid investigation; if concern for infection persists, antibiotics within 3 hours from when sepsis first suspectedConditional
Low likelihood of infection, no shockSuggest deferring antimicrobials while monitoring closelyConditional

Prehospital antibiotics (NEW): For adults with definite/probable septic shock and anticipated time to in-hospital evaluation >60 minutes, suggest administering antimicrobials in the ambulance or flight (Conditional). Requires structured prehospital sepsis screening protocol in place.

Procalcitonin (NEW): Suggest using clinical evaluation alone — rather than PCT + clinical evaluation — to decide whether to start antimicrobial therapy (Conditional). PCT does not improve antibiotic initiation decisions in sepsis.

Fluid Resuscitation

  • Sepsis and septic shock are medical emergencies — treatment begins immediately (Good Practice Statement)
  • 30 mL/kg IV crystalloid in the first 3 hours for sepsis-induced hypoperfusion or septic shock (Conditional — individual patient context applies)
  • Balanced crystalloids (lactated Ringer's) preferred over normal saline — weak evidence of reduced mortality and AKI
  • Albumin — no clear benefit over balanced crystalloids; more expensive; not routine
  • Reassess after each bolus using dynamic measures (pulse pressure variation, passive leg raise, point-of-care echo) — avoid excess fluid

Vasopressors & MAP Targets

RecommendationTargetStrength
Initial MAP target (general population)65 mmHg (±5 mmHg in practice)Strong
Age ≥65 years (NEW)60–65 mmHg range — lower target acceptable in elderlyConditional
Peripheral vasopressors (NEW)Start peripherally to restore MAP — do not delay while obtaining central accessConditional

Vasopressor Selection

AgentDoseRole
Norepinephrine0.01–3 mcg/kg/minFirst-line vasopressor
Vasopressin0.03–0.04 units/min (fixed)Add as second agent to spare norepinephrine
Hydrocortisone200 mg/day IV (continuous or divided)If NE ≥0.25 mcg/kg/min at 4h — reduces vasopressor duration

ICU admission (NEW — SSC 2026): For patients with sepsis or septic shock requiring ICU, suggest admission within 6 hours of recognition (Conditional).

Suspected sourceEmpiric regimenNotes
Unknown / community-acquiredVancomycin + Piperacillin-tazobactam 4.5g IV q6h—
Unknown / healthcare-associatedVancomycin + Cefepime 2g IV q8h (or Meropenem if ESBL/MDR risk)MDR risk: prior MDR colonization, prolonged hospitalization, prior broad-spectrum antibiotics
Pneumonia (CAP)Ceftriaxone 2g IV q24h + Azithromycin 500mg (or Levofloxacin 750mg)Severe CAP: add vancomycin if MRSA risk
Intra-abdominalPip-Tazo 4.5g IV q6h or Ceftriaxone 2g + Metronidazole 500mgSource control within 6 hours mandatory
UrosepsisCeftriaxone 2g IV q24hNarrow based on susceptibilities; 7 days after adequate source control
CNS / meningitisCeftriaxone 2g IV q12h + Vancomycin + Dexamethasone 0.15mg/kg q6hAdd ampicillin if age >50 (Listeria); dex before/with first antibiotic dose
Neutropenic feverCefepime 2g IV q8h or Pip-Tazo 4.5g IV q6hAdd vancomycin only for specific indications (hemodynamic instability, MRSA risk, skin/soft tissue source)
Anaerobic coverage (SSC 2026 — NEW): Suggest against routine anaerobic coverage in sepsis without specific risk factors (Conditional). Add anaerobic coverage only when risk factors present: intra-abdominal/deep gynecologic source, necrotizing SSTI, head/neck infection, CNS abscess/empyema.

Empiric antifungal (SSC 2026): Suggest against empiric antifungal therapy in most sepsis (Conditional). Consider case-by-case in patients with immunosuppression, prolonged antibiotics, prolonged hospitalization, or intra-abdominal source.
  • Source control urgently: Evaluate for anatomical source requiring emergent control (Good Practice Statement); ideally within 6 hours of diagnosis if source control needed (Conditional)
  • De-escalate at 48–72h based on culture results — narrow spectrum, discontinue unnecessary antibiotics
  • Duration: 7 days adequate for most sepsis without complicated source; PCT-guided discontinuation (PRORATA trial) reduces exposure without increasing mortality
  • MDR pathogens: Broaden empiric coverage when specific MDR risk factors present (prior MDR colonization, recent broad-spectrum antibiotics, prolonged hospitalization in high-prevalence unit). Narrow when risk is low (Conditional both ways)

Fever of Unknown Origin

Definition

  • Temp >38.3°C on multiple occasions, duration >3 weeks, no diagnosis after 3 days inpatient evaluation or 3 outpatient visits

Causes by Category

CategoryFrequencyExamples
Infectious35%Endocarditis, abscesses, TB, EBV/CMV, brucellosis, Q fever
Malignancy20%Lymphoma (most common), leukemia, renal cell carcinoma
Inflammatory / autoimmune20%AOSD (ferritin >10,000), GCA, SLE, IBD, sarcoidosis
Undiagnosed10–15%—

Diagnostic Approach

  • Tier 1: CBC, CMP, LFTs, ESR, CRP, ferritin, LDH, blood cultures ×3, HIV, ANA, RF, SPEP
  • Tier 2: EBV/CMV serology, hepatitis B/C, Brucella, Bartonella, Q fever, Lyme, ANCAs
  • Imaging: CT chest/abdomen/pelvis (first-line); PET/CT for occult infection, malignancy, vasculitis
  • Drug fever: Onset 7–10d after new drug; relative bradycardia; eosinophilia; defervescence within 72–96h of stopping

Avoid empiric antibiotics in classic FUO — they mask cultures and complicate workup. Exceptions: neutropenic FUO, deteriorating status, high TB suspicion.

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Fever resolves spontaneously → may be viral illness that self-resolved during workup; do not continue broad workup if clinical improvement
  • Empiric antibiotics causing fever to improve → may be confirming occult infection, or drug fever itself; stop antibiotics and observe
  • Autoimmune markers (ANA, ANCA, ferritin) elevated → adult-onset Still disease, vasculitis, lymphoma; rheumatology/hematology consultation
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Rapid clinical deterioration during workup → FDG-PET/CT to identify occult malignancy/vasculitis; bone marrow biopsy if lymphoma suspected
  • Ferritin >10,000 → hemophagocytic lymphohistiocytosis (HLH); hematology urgently; high mortality without treatment
  • New organ dysfunction during workup → expedite diagnosis; broad empiric coverage while cultures/biopsies pending
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Diagnosis established → targeted treatment; fever resolution confirms correct diagnosis
  • If no diagnosis at 3–6 months and patient stable → watchful waiting; 25–50% of FUO resolve without diagnosis
  • Empiric steroids: only after infection excluded; may be appropriate for adult Still disease or vasculitis

Organism-based drug selection. For site-specific regimens see Step 4 — Manage Syndrome.

📖 Drug Reference Guide: 💊 Vancomycin, 💊 Ceftriaxone, 💊 Meropenem, 💊 Azithromycin, 💊 Ciprofloxacin

Penicillin Allergy Evaluation

~90% of reported penicillin allergies are NOT true IgE-mediated allergies. Evaluation enables optimal antibiotic selection.

TypeCharacteristicsExamplesRisk Level
Type I (IgE-mediated)Immediate (<1-6 hours), urticaria, angioedema, anaphylaxis, bronchospasm"Throat swelling", "couldn't breathe", "hives all over", "needed epinephrine"HIGH — avoid unless skin testing negative
Severe non-IgESJS/TEN, DRESS, serum sickness, hemolytic anemia, interstitial nephritisBlistering rash, fever + rash + organ involvement, onset days-weeksHIGH — avoid penicillins AND cephalosporins
Benign/Low-riskDelayed, mild rash, GI upset, "unknown", childhood reaction not remembered"Rash as a kid", "stomach upset", "not sure what happened"LOW — likely can use cephalosporins, possibly penicillins

Historical teaching of 10% cross-reactivity is WRONG.

Actual cross-reactivity rates based on R1 side chain similarity:

ScenarioCross-ReactivityClinical Implication
Overall cephalosporin cross-reactivity with penicillin allergy~1-2%Low risk. Most patients with "penicillin allergy" can receive cephalosporins.
Aminopenicillins → 1st/2nd gen cephalosporins (similar R1 side chain)~10-15%Amoxicillin/ampicillin share side chain with cefadroxil, cephalexin, cefaclor. Avoid these specific agents.
Any penicillin → 3rd/4th gen cephalosporins<1%Ceftriaxone, cefepime, ceftazidime have distinct R1 side chains. SAFE in most penicillin-allergic patients.

Bottom Line: Ceftriaxone and cefepime can be safely given to most patients with penicillin allergy (except anaphylaxis or severe reactions). 1st/2nd gen cephalosporins have higher cross-reactivity with aminopenicillins specifically.

Step 1: Characterize the Reaction

  • What happened? (specific symptoms)
  • How soon after taking the drug? (minutes vs. days)
  • Did they need treatment? (epinephrine, hospitalization)
  • How long ago? (>10 years, IgE wanes)
  • Have they tolerated penicillins or cephalosporins since?

Step 2: Risk Stratify

Risk CategoryHistoryRecommendation
Low RiskUnknown reaction, GI upset, non-urticarial rash, reaction >10 years ago, tolerated cephalosporins sinceGive cephalosporins (any generation) without testing. Consider penicillin challenge if needed.
Moderate RiskUrticaria or pruritic rash within 6 hours, isolated angioedemaGive 3rd/4th gen cephalosporins (ceftriaxone, cefepime). Consider penicillin skin testing if penicillin strongly preferred.
High RiskAnaphylaxis, bronchospasm, hypotension, required epinephrineAvoid penicillins and consider avoiding cephalosporins. Penicillin skin testing before use. Use alternative class.
ContraindicatedSJS/TEN, DRESS, hemolytic anemia, interstitial nephritisNEVER rechallenge with penicillins or cephalosporins. Absolute contraindication.

Step 3: Alternatives When Avoiding Beta-Lactams

  • Gram-positive coverage: Vancomycin, daptomycin, linezolid
  • Gram-negative coverage: Fluoroquinolones, aminoglycosides, aztreonam (NO cross-reactivity with penicillins)
  • Anaerobic coverage: Metronidazole, clindamycin

Penicillin Skin Testing

  • Indication: High-risk allergy history when penicillin is strongly preferred (e.g., syphilis in pregnancy, enterococcal endocarditis)
  • NPV: >95% — negative test means <1% chance of IgE-mediated reaction
  • Process: Prick test followed by intradermal testing with penicillin G, penicilloyl-polylysine (Pre-Pen), and minor determinant mixture
  • If negative: Can give penicillins safely (consider supervised first dose)

Desensitization

  • Indication: TRUE IgE-mediated allergy AND no alternative antibiotic acceptable
  • Process: Incremental doses given over 4-12 hours in monitored setting (ICU or allergy clinic)
  • Duration: Desensitization is temporary — must maintain continuous drug exposure. If stopped >24-48h, must repeat desensitization.
  • Contraindicated: SJS/TEN, DRESS, other severe non-IgE reactions
3

Interpret cultures & pathogens

Blood cultures · Antibiogram · MDR organisms

Blood Culture Interpretation

Distinguishing true pathogens from contaminants is critical for appropriate antibiotic use.

OrganismClinical SignificanceKey Actions
Staphylococcus aureusALWAYS significant — even 1 positive bottleRepeat cultures, TEE (within 5-7 days), evaluate for metastatic infection, ID consult recommended. Minimum 14 days IV therapy.
Streptococcus pneumoniaeAlways significantSource: usually pneumonia, meningitis, or spontaneous. Look for immunocompromise if recurrent.
Streptococcus pyogenes (GAS)Always significantSource: skin/soft tissue, pharyngitis, necrotizing fasciitis. Aggressive infection — evaluate for deep source.
Streptococcus agalactiae (GBS)Always significantCommon in diabetics, elderly, pregnancy. Look for skin, UTI, or endovascular source.
Enterobacteriaceae (E. coli, Klebsiella, etc.)Always significantSource: UTI, biliary, intra-abdominal, line infection. Repeat cultures to document clearance.
Pseudomonas aeruginosaAlways significantOften healthcare-associated. Look for pneumonia, UTI, line infection, neutropenia.
Candida speciesALWAYS significant — even 1 positive bottleRemove all lines, repeat cultures until negative, ophtho exam, echocardiogram if risk factors. ID consult. Minimum 14 days from first negative culture.
Neisseria meningitidisAlways significantMeningitis, meningococcemia. Droplet precautions. Prophylaxis for close contacts.
Listeria monocytogenesAlways significantMeningitis, bacteremia in immunocompromised, elderly, pregnancy. Requires ampicillin (not cephalosporins).
OrganismContaminant ProbabilityWhen to Consider True Pathogen
Coagulase-negative Staphylococci (S. epidermidis, etc.)~70-80% are contaminantsTrue pathogen if: ≥2 bottles positive with same species, prosthetic device/hardware, central line, immunocompromised. Single positive bottle usually contaminant.
Corynebacterium (Diphtheroids)Usually contaminantTrue pathogen if: prosthetic valve, hardware, immunocompromised, multiple positive bottles
Cutibacterium acnes (formerly Propionibacterium)Usually contaminantTrue pathogen if: shoulder hardware, CNS shunt, prosthetic joint. Takes 5-14 days to grow.
Bacillus species (not B. anthracis)Usually contaminantTrue pathogen if: IV drug use, central line, immunocompromised, multiple positive bottles
Viridans group StreptococciSometimes contaminantTrue pathogen if: endocarditis risk factors, multiple positive bottles, neutropenia. Single bottle in healthy patient may be contaminant.
MicrococcusAlmost always contaminantRarely pathogenic. Repeat cultures if clinically concerned.

Key Principle: For likely contaminants, repeat blood cultures before starting antibiotics (unless critically ill). Two positive bottles with same organism = more likely true pathogen.

Why 2 Sets (4 Bottles) Are Recommended

  • Single positive bottle more likely to represent contamination
  • Sensitivity for detecting bacteremia increases with volume cultured
  • Two positive bottles with same organism = high specificity for true bacteremia

Decision Framework

ScenarioInterpretationAction
1/4 bottles positive with S. aureus or CandidaTRUE PATHOGENFull workup and treatment. These are never contaminants.
1/4 bottles positive with coag-neg StaphLikely contaminant (~80%)Repeat cultures. If repeat negative and low-risk patient, observe. If prosthetic device, treat as pathogen.
2/4 bottles positive with coag-neg Staph (same species)More likely pathogen (~50-70%)Evaluate for line infection, hardware. Consider treatment, especially if clinical signs of infection.
1/4 bottles positive with Gram-negative rodUsually pathogenRepeat cultures, evaluate source, treat. GNRs are rarely contaminants.
Polymicrobial bacteremiaConsider GI sourceEvaluate for intra-abdominal abscess, perforation, biliary infection. May need imaging.

Time to Positivity

Clinical Pearl: Faster time to positivity generally correlates with higher bacterial burden and more severe infection. Cultures positive in <12 hours suggest high-grade bacteremia.

Culture-Negative Endocarditis Organisms (HACEK and Beyond)

  • HACEK organisms: Haemophilus, Aggregatibacter, Cardiobacterium, Eikenella, Kingella — slow-growing, cause culture-negative endocarditis
  • Coxiella burnetii (Q fever): Requires serology
  • Bartonella: Serology and PCR; associated with cat exposure, homelessness
  • Tropheryma whipplei: Whipple disease; PCR for diagnosis
  • Brucella: Prolonged incubation needed; notify lab if suspected

When to Suspect Anaerobic Bacteremia

  • Intra-abdominal source (especially with polymicrobial cultures)
  • Female genital tract infections
  • Aspiration pneumonia with abscess
  • Decubitus ulcers or diabetic foot infections
  • Common anaerobes: Bacteroides fragilis, Clostridium species, Fusobacterium

Persistent Bacteremia — Red Flag

If cultures remain positive >48-72 hours despite appropriate antibiotics:

  • Endocarditis (get TEE)
  • Undrained abscess (get imaging)
  • Infected hardware/device (may need removal)
  • Wrong antibiotic (check susceptibilities)
  • Septic thrombophlebitis (consider anticoagulation)

Antibiogram Interpretation & Use

Local resistance patterns guide empiric therapy. The antibiogram is your hospital's susceptibility report card.

Definition

An antibiogram is a summary of antimicrobial susceptibility test results for organisms isolated at a specific institution over a defined period (usually 1 year). It shows the percentage of isolates susceptible to each antibiotic tested.

Why It Matters

  • Resistance patterns vary significantly by hospital, unit, and region
  • National guidelines provide general recommendations; local antibiogram provides YOUR hospital's data
  • Empiric therapy should target >80-90% susceptibility for serious infections

Limitations

  • Reflects past patterns (typically 1 year old data)
  • Aggregates all isolates — doesn't distinguish ICU vs. floor, inpatient vs. outpatient
  • May not reflect individual patient's prior cultures or risk factors
  • Only includes tested isolates (selection bias)

Example Antibiogram (Simplified)

OrganismAmpPip-TazoCeftriaxoneCefepimeCiproGentTMP-SMX
E. coli (n=500)55%92%88%94%72%89%75%
Klebsiella pneumoniae (n=200)0%88%85%91%90%92%85%
Pseudomonas aeruginosa (n=150)-85%-87%78%88%-
MSSA (n=300)-100%100%100%-100%98%
MRSA (n=100)0%0%0%0%--95%

Numbers represent % of isolates susceptible. "-" = not tested or not applicable.

Interpreting the Numbers

  • >90%: Excellent choice for empiric therapy
  • 80-90%: Acceptable for non-severe infections; consider alternatives for severe infections
  • <80%: Generally avoid for empiric therapy; may use if culture-directed

Scenario 1: Uncomplicated UTI

Your antibiogram shows E. coli susceptibility: TMP-SMX 75%, Ciprofloxacin 72%, Nitrofurantoin 95%

  • Best empiric choice: Nitrofurantoin (95% susceptibility)
  • Avoid TMP-SMX and ciprofloxacin empirically (<80%)
  • This is why IDSA now recommends nitrofurantoin first-line for uncomplicated cystitis

Scenario 2: Hospital-Acquired Pneumonia

High-risk patient, your antibiogram shows: P. aeruginosa susceptibility to pip-tazo 85%, cefepime 87%, meropenem 95%

  • For severe infection, want >90% coverage
  • Best empiric choice: Meropenem (or combination therapy with pip-tazo + aminoglycoside for double coverage)

Scenario 3: Skin/Soft Tissue Infection

Your antibiogram shows MRSA prevalence 35% of all S. aureus isolates

  • If MRSA prevalence >10-20%, consider empiric MRSA coverage (vancomycin, doxycycline, TMP-SMX) for purulent SSTIs
  • If MRSA prevalence <10%, empiric MSSA coverage (cephalexin, dicloxacillin) may be reasonable

General Principles

  • Match severity to susceptibility threshold: Severe infections need >90% coverage; mild infections may accept lower
  • Consider individual risk factors: Recent antibiotics, prior cultures, healthcare exposure may shift probability toward resistance
  • De-escalate when cultures return: Antibiogram guides empiric therapy; definitive therapy is culture-directed

ESBL (Extended-Spectrum Beta-Lactamase) Producers

  • Organisms: E. coli, Klebsiella, Proteus
  • Resistance: All penicillins and cephalosporins (including 3rd/4th gen), aztreonam
  • Treatment: Carbapenems (meropenem, ertapenem); pip-tazo may work for UTI with low inoculum
  • Clue on antibiogram: Susceptible to cefepime in vitro but cephalosporin failure clinically. Some labs report "ESBL screen positive."

AmpC Producers

  • Organisms: "SPICE" — Serratia, Providencia, Indole-positive Proteus, Citrobacter, Enterobacter
  • Risk: May appear susceptible to 3rd-gen cephalosporins but develop resistance during therapy
  • Treatment: Cefepime (stable to AmpC), carbapenems, fluoroquinolones if susceptible

CRE (Carbapenem-Resistant Enterobacteriaceae)

  • Mechanisms: KPC (most common in US), NDM, OXA-48
  • Treatment: Ceftazidime-avibactam (KPC), meropenem-vaborbactam, combination therapy; ID consult recommended

MRSA

  • Check your antibiogram for MRSA prevalence (% of S. aureus that is MRSA)
  • Also check susceptibility to clindamycin (D-test for inducible resistance), TMP-SMX, doxycycline

VRE (Vancomycin-Resistant Enterococcus)

  • Usually E. faecium (E. faecalis resistance less common)
  • Treatment: Linezolid, daptomycin (not for pulmonary infections)

Why Stratification Matters

A single hospital antibiogram aggregates very different patient populations. Resistance patterns differ significantly between:

  • ICU vs. general medical floors
  • Inpatient vs. outpatient (ED, clinic)
  • Different specimen types (blood vs. urine vs. respiratory)
  • Community-onset vs. hospital-onset infections

Example: E. coli Cipro Susceptibility

PopulationCipro Susceptibility
Overall hospital72%
Outpatient urine (ED)85%
Inpatient urine (floor)68%
ICU urine55%
Blood cultures60%

Using the stratified data, you might use cipro empirically for an ED UTI (85%) but not for an ICU patient (55%).

Ask Your Pharmacy/Micro Lab

  • Many hospitals now produce unit-specific or specimen-specific antibiograms
  • If not available, ask — this data may exist but not be widely distributed

Pathogen Overview — Selected Genera (expanded)

  • Gram-negative rod; common cause of UTI, intra-abdominal infection, bacteremia.
  • ESBL prevalence variable — check local antibiogram; resistant isolates often require carbapenem.
  • Virulence: adhesins, endotoxin; therapy guided by susceptibility.

🦠 Pathogen Index →

  • Encapsulated gram-negative rod; causes pneumonia, UTI, liver abscess.
  • Carbapenemase producers (KPC, NDM) are significant — infection control & ID consultation recommended.

🦠 Pathogen Index →

  • Non-fermenter; common in HAP/VAP, wound infections, device-related infections.
  • Resistance via efflux pumps, porin loss, beta-lactamases; biofilms complicate device infections.
  • Therapy: higher dosing, extended infusions of beta-lactams often beneficial; tailor to MICs.

🦠 Pathogen Index →

  • Important in cystic fibrosis and immunocompromised hosts; often intrinsically resistant.
  • Combination therapy frequently required; consult susceptibility/ID.

🦠 Pathogen Index →

  • Anaerobic gram-negative rod common in intra-abdominal infections; beta-lactamase production common.
  • Prefer agents with anaerobic coverage: metronidazole, beta-lactam/beta-lactamase inhibitors, carbapenems.

🦠 Pathogen Index →

Recognition

  • Filamentous, gram-positive anaerobe; commensal of oral cavity, GI tract, female genital tract; not transmitted person-to-person
  • Cervicofacial (most common, 50%): Jaw swelling, indurated mass, sinus tracts draining sulfur granules; often follows dental procedure/trauma
  • Pulmonary: Chronic pneumonia, empyema, chest wall involvement; crosses tissue planes (lobar boundaries ignored)
  • Abdominal/pelvic: Abdominal mass, sinus tracts; associated with IUD use (Actinomyces on Pap smear)
  • Classic: chronic indolent course, woody-hard induration, disregard for anatomical barriers, sinus tracts, sulfur granules

Workup

  • Culture from sinus tract drainage, pus, or biopsy: Alert lab — anaerobic culture, hold ≥2 weeks; fastidious grower
  • Sulfur granules (on Gram stain or gross pathology): Basophilic granules with peripheral palisading gram-positive filaments — pathognomonic
  • CT/MRI: Identify extent; crosses anatomical planes (lobar, fascial) — characteristic feature
  • Biopsy: Required for diagnosis in most cases; tissue Gram stain + culture
  • Colonization of IUD: Actinomyces on Pap smear is common; treat only if symptomatic pelvic actinomycosis

Empiric Therapy

RegimenDoseDuration
IV induction (moderate-severe)Penicillin G 10–20 MU/day IV divided q4–6h4–6 weeks IV, then oral step-down
Oral maintenanceAmoxicillin 500mg PO TID OR Penicillin V 500mg PO QID6–12 months total (necessary to prevent relapse)
Mild/cervicofacialAmoxicillin 500mg PO TID3–6 months
PCN allergyDoxycycline 100mg PO BID6–12 months

Source Control

  • Surgical drainage for abscess + excision of sinus tracts — essential for cure in extensive disease
  • Dental source: dental extraction, periodontal treatment
  • IUD removal for pelvic actinomycosis
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not responding to amoxicillin → confirm diagnosis (sulfur granules on histology?); consider inadequate duration; surgical drainage adequate?
  • Actinomyces on Pap smear with IUD but asymptomatic → observe or remove IUD; do not treat antibiotics for colonization alone
  • Crosses lung lobe boundaries on CT → Actinomyces crosses fascial planes; if diagnosis suspected do not dismiss based on "atypical" distribution
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Empyema necessitans (chest wall breakthrough) → surgical debridement + drainage + prolonged antibiotics
  • CNS involvement (rare — brain abscess) → neurosurgery + IV penicillin × 4–6 weeks then oral
  • Extensive pelvic actinomycosis with bowel involvement → surgical consultation; prolonged antibiotics
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • IV to oral: after initial clinical response (typically 4–6 weeks IV), step down to oral amoxicillin
  • Total duration must be long (6–12 months) — premature discontinuation leads to relapse in thick fibrotic tissue
  • Sinus tract closure confirms adequate source control; imaging resolution lags clinical improvement significantly

🦠 Pathogen Index →

  • Acid-fast bacillus; RIPE therapy (rifampin, isoniazid, pyrazinamide, ethambutol) initial phase.
  • Diagnosis and management require public health and specialist involvement due to public health implications and prolonged therapy.

Recognition

  • Filamentous, weakly acid-fast, gram-positive aerobic actinomycete; environmental (soil, decomposing matter)
  • Risk: Immunocompromised (solid organ transplant, high-dose steroids, AIDS, hematologic malignancy); pulmonary alveolar proteinosis
  • Primary pulmonary: Subacute pneumonia, cavitary lesions, nodules — often mistaken for TB or fungal
  • Dissemination in 30–50%: Brain abscess (most common disseminated site), skin/soft tissue, bone
  • Always consider dissemination: if pulmonary Nocardia, perform MRI brain + skin exam

Workup

  • Culture (respiratory specimens, blood, tissue): Slow-growing (up to 2 weeks); alert lab to hold cultures; Gram stain shows branching filamentous gram-positive rods
  • Modified AFB (Kinyoun or Ziehl-Neelsen): Weakly acid-fast (distinguishes from Actinomyces which is not acid-fast)
  • Species identification and susceptibility testing: Critical — different Nocardia species have different drug susceptibility patterns
  • MRI brain: Ring-enhancing abscesses — look for CNS dissemination in all pulmonary cases
  • Galactomannan, β-D-glucan: Negative (helps exclude Aspergillus/Candida from differential)

Empiric Therapy

SyndromeRegimenDuration
Pulmonary (non-CNS, immunocompetent)TMP-SMX 5–10 mg/kg/day divided BID-TID PO6–12 months
Pulmonary (immunocompromised)TMP-SMX + Imipenem 500mg IV q6h + Amikacin 7.5mg/kg IV q12h (induction × 4–6 weeks)Total 12 months
CNS involvementTMP-SMX + Imipenem ± Amikacin (induction)Minimum 12 months total
Cutaneous/lymphocutaneous (immunocompetent)TMP-SMX DS 1 tab BID PO3–6 months

Susceptibility-guided therapy is essential — N. farcinica is often resistant to TMP-SMX; N. brasiliensis common in cutaneous; always speciate and test.

Source Control

  • Brain abscess: neurosurgical drainage or aspiration (diagnostic + therapeutic) for large or accessible lesions
  • Empyema: chest tube drainage
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Pulmonary nodules + immunocompromised + weakly acid-fast on bronch → Nocardia; do not mistake for Aspergillus (galactomannan negative) or TB (AFB strongly positive)
  • TMP-SMX failure → check species susceptibility; N. farcinica often TMP-SMX resistant; use imipenem + amikacin
  • CNS lesions developing on treatment → inadequate CNS penetration; imipenem + TMP-SMX combination for CNS
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • CNS dissemination → neurosurgery for large/accessible abscesses; triple combination therapy
  • Pulmonary Nocardia + progressive respiratory failure → ICU; IV combination therapy
  • Solid organ transplant patient → reduce immunosuppression if possible; ID consultation
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Induction IV therapy (4–6 weeks) then oral TMP-SMX transition when stable
  • Duration is long (6–12 months) — emphasize adherence; transplant patients often 12 months minimum
  • Response monitoring: serial imaging at 2–3 months; brain lesions may take months to resolve

Recognition

  • Antibiotic-associated diarrhea and colitis; watery to bloody diarrhea, cramping, fever, leukocytosis
  • Risk factors: Recent antibiotics (especially clindamycin, fluoroquinolones, cephalosporins, carbapenems), age >65, hospitalization, PPI use, IBD, immunocompromise
  • Severity classification (IDSA/SHEA 2021):
    • Non-severe: WBC <15K + Cr <1.5mg/dL
    • Severe: WBC ≥15K OR Cr ≥1.5mg/dL
    • Fulminant: Hypotension, shock, ileus, megacolon
  • Recurrence: 25% after first episode; increases with each recurrence

Workup

  • GDH + toxin EIA (reflex): GDH screens (sensitive), toxin EIA confirms; positive GDH + negative toxin EIA → NAAT to resolve
  • NAAT/PCR (stool): Most sensitive; can be positive with colonization — clinical correlation essential (3+ loose stools/day)
  • Toxigenic culture: Gold standard; rarely needed clinically
  • CT abdomen/pelvis: Pancolitis, bowel wall thickening, pericolonic stranding, ascites — assess severity; perforation?
  • Do NOT test formed stool; do NOT test "test of cure" — PCR remains positive after treatment

Empiric Therapy

SeverityRegimenDuration
Non-severe (first episode)Vancomycin 125mg PO QID OR Fidaxomicin 200mg PO BID10 days
Severe (first episode)Vancomycin 125mg PO QID10 days
Fulminant (ileus/megacolon)Vancomycin 500mg PO/NG QID + Metronidazole 500mg IV q8hUntil clinical response; surgery consultation
First recurrenceFidaxomicin 200mg PO BID (preferred — lower recurrence rate)10 days
Second or more recurrenceVancomycin taper/pulse OR Fidaxomicin; followed by Bezlotoxumab (mAb for prevention)As per protocol
Multiple recurrencesFecal Microbiota Transplant (FMT) — 80–90% cure rateID/GI consultation

IV metronidazole alone is no longer recommended for CDI — inferior outcomes compared to oral vancomycin.

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 3–5 days → CT abdomen; assess for fulminant/toxic megacolon; surgery consultation urgently
  • WBC rising or creatinine worsening despite treatment → reassess severity; fulminant CDI? superinfection?
  • Recurrence after completing treatment → new CDI episode; not treatment failure; fidaxomicin preferred for first recurrence
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Fulminant CDI with peritonitis or perforation → emergent colectomy (subtotal colectomy preferred); mortality >50%
  • Toxic megacolon (colon dilation >6cm) → NPO, NG decompression, surgery consultation; loop ileostomy + colonic lavage alternative to colectomy
  • Hypotension/shock → ICU; vasopressors; add IV metronidazole to vancomycin enema/NG
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Clinical improvement + >3 normal stools/day or no diarrhea → complete 10-day course; no test-of-cure
  • Contact precautions: maintain until diarrhea resolved + 48h; soap and water handwashing (alcohol gel does NOT kill spores)
  • Recurrence prevention: avoid unnecessary antibiotics; probiotics have limited evidence but low risk; bezlotoxumab (Zinplava) reduces recurrence in high-risk

🦠 Pathogen Index →

  • Species: Primarily E. faecium (vanA, vanB genes); E. faecalis less common
  • Risk factors: Prior vancomycin, prolonged hospitalization, ICU, immunosuppression, GI colonization
  • Treatment:
    • Linezolid 600mg IV/PO q12h (preferred for many infections, oral bioavailability 100%)
    • Daptomycin 6-8 mg/kg IV daily (higher doses for bacteremia 8-12 mg/kg; NOT for pneumonia)
    • Tigecycline (for intra-abdominal/skin only; poor serum levels)
  • Duration: UTI 5-7 days; bacteremia 7-14 days; endocarditis 6 weeks (ID consult)
  • Pearl: VRE bacteremia—remove lines if present; search for intra-abdominal/biliary source

🦠 Pathogen Index →

  • Organisms: E. coli, Klebsiella pneumoniae, Proteus mirabilis most common
  • Resistance pattern: Resistant to 3rd-gen cephalosporins (ceftriaxone, ceftazidime), aztreonam; often co-resistant to fluoroquinolones, TMP-SMX
  • Risk factors: Prior 3rd-gen cephalosporin or fluoroquinolone use, healthcare exposure, travel to endemic areas (India, Middle East)
  • Treatment:
    • Severe infections/bacteremia: Meropenem 1g IV q8h or Ertapenem 1g IV daily (carbapenems are treatment of choice)
    • UTI (uncomplicated): May use nitrofurantoin, fosfomycin if susceptible; TMP-SMX if susceptible
    • Pip-tazo: May be used if MIC ≤16 and non-critically ill (controversial—MERINO trial suggests carbapenem superiority for bacteremia)
  • Pearl: Always confirm susceptibilities; report may show cephalosporin "susceptible" but clinical failure can occur

🦠 Pathogen Index →

  • Organisms: K. pneumoniae (KPC most common in US), E. coli, Enterobacter
  • Mechanisms: KPC (Klebsiella pneumoniae carbapenemase), NDM, OXA-48, VIM, IMP
  • Risk factors: Prior carbapenems, prolonged hospitalization, ICU, mechanical ventilation, international travel (NDM—South Asia)
  • Treatment (ID consult strongly recommended):
    • Ceftazidime-avibactam: 2.5g IV q8h—covers KPC and OXA-48 (NOT NDM)
    • Meropenem-vaborbactam: 4g IV q8h—covers KPC
    • Imipenem-relebactam: Alternative for KPC
    • Cefiderocol: Siderophore cephalosporin—covers NDM, difficult GNRs
    • Combination therapy: Consider for severe infections (e.g., high-dose meropenem + polymyxin or aminoglycoside)
  • Mortality: 40-50% for CRE bacteremia—highlights severity
  • Infection control: Contact precautions, aggressive screening, single rooms

🦠 Pathogen Index →

  • Characteristics: Gram-negative coccobacillus, environmental persistence, rapid resistance development
  • Clinical syndromes: VAP (most common), CLABSI, wound infections, meningitis (post-neurosurgical)
  • Risk factors: ICU, mechanical ventilation, prior broad-spectrum antibiotics, wounds/burns, deployed military
  • Treatment:
    • MDR Acinetobacter: Ampicillin-sulbactam (high dose 9g IV q8h—sulbactam has intrinsic activity)
    • XDR/pan-resistant: Polymyxin B or Colistin (often in combination)
    • Sulbactam-durlobactam (Xacduro): Newly approved for hospital-acquired pneumonia
    • Tigecycline: May be used for non-bacteremic infections (achieves poor serum levels)
    • Combination therapy: Often used for severe infections (carbapenem + polymyxin if any susceptibility)
  • Pearl: Colonization vs. infection distinction important—may not need treatment if just colonizing respiratory tract

🦠 Pathogen Index →

  • Characteristics: Intrinsically resistant to carbapenems (L1 and L2 beta-lactamases)
  • Clinical syndromes: Pneumonia (especially VAP), CLABSI, wound infections, often in immunocompromised/CF patients
  • Risk factors: Prior carbapenems, prolonged hospitalization, ICU, malignancy, steroids, CF
  • Treatment:
    • First-line: TMP-SMX 15-20 mg/kg/day (TMP component) IV divided q6-8h
    • Alternatives: Levofloxacin, Minocycline, Ticarcillin-clavulanate, Ceftazidime-avibactam (check susceptibility)
    • Severe infections: Consider combination therapy (TMP-SMX + fluoroquinolone or minocycline)
  • Duration: 7-14 days depending on source
  • Pearl: Do NOT use carbapenems—intrinsic resistance; monotherapy may select further resistance

🦠 Pathogen Index →

  • Characteristics: Gram-positive rod, facultative intracellular, grows at refrigerator temps
  • Clinical syndromes: Meningitis, bacteremia, rhombencephalitis (brainstem), gastroenteritis, maternal-fetal infection
  • Risk factors: Age >50, pregnancy, immunocompromise (steroids, transplant, HIV), cirrhosis, diabetes, iron overload
  • Source: Contaminated food—deli meats, soft cheeses, unpasteurized dairy, cantaloupe
  • Treatment:
    • First-line: Ampicillin 2g IV q4h (± Gentamicin for synergy in severe/meningitis)
    • Penicillin allergy: TMP-SMX (Bactrim) 10-20 mg/kg/day TMP component
    • NOT susceptible to: Cephalosporins (important—why ampicillin added to meningitis empiric regimen in elderly)
  • Duration: Bacteremia 2 weeks; meningitis 3+ weeks; rhombencephalitis/brain abscess 6+ weeks
  • Pearl: CSF may show mononuclear predominance and only mildly elevated protein—can mimic viral/TB
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Ceftriaxone empiric meningitis not improving → Listeria not covered by cephalosporins; add ampicillin if age >50, pregnant, immunocompromised
  • CSF culture negative but clinical meningitis + listeria risk factors → empiric ampicillin; blood culture may be positive
  • Rhombencephalitis (brainstem signs + fever) → Listeria unique presentation; ampicillin + gentamicin
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Altered consciousness, focal deficits, brainstem signs → MRI brain; neurology consultation
  • Respiratory failure from brainstem involvement → ICU; mechanical ventilation
  • Listeria bacteremia in pregnancy → immediate treatment; high risk of preterm delivery and fetal loss
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Clinical improvement + CSF sterilizing → complete 21 days for meningitis (bacteremia alone: 14 days)
  • Gentamicin (if added for synergy) can typically be stopped after 2 weeks
  • Pregnancy: treat throughout pregnancy + intrapartum with ampicillin; neonatal evaluation mandatory

🦠 Pathogen Index →

  • Characteristics: Gram-negative rod (stains poorly), obligate intracellular, environmental (water systems)
  • Clinical syndromes: Atypical pneumonia, Pontiac fever (self-limited febrile illness)
  • Risk factors: Smoking, COPD, immunosuppression, age >50, recent travel, hospital water exposure
  • Clinical clues: High fever, relative bradycardia, hyponatremia, diarrhea, confusion, elevated LFTs, low phosphorus
  • Diagnosis:
    • Urine antigen: Serogroup 1 only (most common), 80% sensitive, 99% specific, remains positive for weeks
    • Respiratory culture: Requires special media (BCYE agar)—specify Legionella if suspected
    • PCR: Increasingly available
  • Treatment:
    • Preferred: Levofloxacin 750mg IV/PO daily OR Azithromycin 500mg IV/PO daily
    • Severe/immunocompromised: Consider combination (fluoroquinolone + azithromycin) initially
  • Duration: 5-10 days (mild); 14-21 days (severe/immunocompromised)
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not responding to azithromycin → switch to fluoroquinolone (levofloxacin preferred for severe); fluoroquinolones have better intracellular penetration
  • Urine Legionella antigen negative → only detects serogroup 1 (80–90% of cases); culture or PCR from BAL for other serogroups
  • Hyponatremia + confusion + GI symptoms + pneumonia → Legionella until proven otherwise; send urine antigen immediately
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Respiratory failure (ARDS from Legionella) → ICU; high-flow O2 or mechanical ventilation; fluoroquinolone IV
  • Multi-organ failure (hepatitis, AKI, encephalopathy) → Legionella disseminated disease; ID consultation
  • Cluster of cases → public health notification; source identification (cooling towers, water systems)
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Clinically improving, tolerating PO, afebrile × 48h → oral step-down (levofloxacin 750mg daily or azithromycin)
  • Total duration: 5–7 days non-severe; 10–14 days severe/ICU/immunocompromised
  • Urine antigen remains positive for weeks — do not use to guide duration

🦠 Pathogen Index →

  • Epidemiology: Fungal infection in immunocompromised—HIV (CD4 <200), transplant, high-dose steroids, hematologic malignancy
  • Clinical: Subacute dyspnea (days to weeks), dry cough, fever, hypoxemia with exertion; desaturation with ambulation
  • Imaging: Bilateral interstitial/ground-glass infiltrates, often perihilar; can be normal early or atypical (nodules, cysts, pneumothorax)
  • Diagnosis:
    • Induced sputum: 50-90% sensitive if available
    • BAL: >95% sensitive—gold standard
    • Beta-D-glucan: Elevated (>80 pg/mL), good negative predictive value
    • LDH: Typically elevated (non-specific)
  • Treatment:
    • First-line: TMP-SMX 15-20 mg/kg/day (TMP component) IV divided q6-8h × 21 days
    • Adjunctive steroids (if PaO2 <70 or A-a gradient >35): Prednisone 40mg PO BID × 5 days, then 40mg daily × 5, then 20mg daily × 11
    • Alternatives: Pentamidine IV, Atovaquone (mild), Clindamycin + Primaquine (moderate)
  • Prophylaxis indications: HIV with CD4 <200, HSCT, high-dose steroids (≥20mg prednisone × 4+ weeks), certain chemo/biologics
  • Populations: Transplant (SOT, HSCT), HIV (CD4 <50), high-dose steroids/biologics
  • Clinical syndromes: Colitis (diarrhea, bleeding), Pneumonitis, Retinitis, Hepatitis, Encephalitis
  • Diagnosis:
    • CMV PCR (blood): Quantitative viral load—for monitoring, rising VL suggests active disease
    • Tissue biopsy: Owl's eye inclusions, immunohistochemistry—gold standard for end-organ disease
  • Treatment:
    • Ganciclovir: 5 mg/kg IV q12h (induction 2-3 weeks), then 5 mg/kg IV daily (maintenance)
    • Valganciclovir: 900mg PO BID (induction), then 900mg PO daily—oral option if absorbing
    • Foscarnet: For ganciclovir-resistant CMV or if neutropenic; nephrotoxic, electrolyte wasting
  • Monitoring: Weekly CMV PCR during treatment; toxicities (neutropenia, thrombocytopenia, renal for foscarnet)
  • HSV Reactivation:
    • Mucocutaneous (oral, genital)—can be severe, hemorrhagic, disseminated
    • Esophagitis: Odynophagia, discrete ulcers on EGD
    • Hepatitis: Rare, severe, high mortality
    • Encephalitis: Even in immunocompetent—temporal lobe
  • HSV Treatment:
    • Mucocutaneous: Acyclovir 400mg PO TID or Valacyclovir 1g BID × 7-14 days; IV if severe
    • Disseminated/Visceral: Acyclovir 10 mg/kg IV q8h × 14-21 days
    • Encephalitis: Acyclovir 10 mg/kg IV q8h × 14-21 days
  • VZV Reactivation (Shingles):
    • Dermatomal vesicles on erythematous base; can disseminate in immunocompromised
    • Complications: PHN, HZO (ophthalmic), Ramsay Hunt, meningoencephalitis, VZV vasculopathy
  • VZV Treatment:
    • Localized (immunocompetent): Valacyclovir 1g PO TID × 7 days
    • Immunocompromised or disseminated: Acyclovir 10 mg/kg IV q8h until no new lesions × 48h, then oral to complete 10-14 days
  • Pearl: Adjust acyclovir for renal function; ensure adequate hydration (crystalluria risk)
  • Epidemiology: Environmental NTM (water, soil); disease in HIV (CD4 <50), structural lung disease, bronchiectasis, CF
  • Clinical Syndromes:
    • Disseminated (HIV): Fever, night sweats, weight loss, diarrhea, hepatosplenomegaly, cytopenias
    • Pulmonary (non-HIV): Chronic cough, nodular infiltrates, bronchiectasis (Lady Windermere syndrome—RML/lingula)
  • Diagnosis:
    • Disseminated: Blood culture (isolator/lysis centrifugation), bone marrow biopsy
    • Pulmonary: ATS/IDSA criteria—symptoms + imaging + microbiology (2+ sputum cultures OR 1 BAL OR tissue)
  • Treatment (always multidrug):
    • Core regimen: Azithromycin 250-500mg daily (or Clarithromycin) + Ethambutol 15 mg/kg daily + Rifampin (or Rifabutin for HIV)
    • Severe/disseminated: Add Amikacin or Streptomycin initially
  • Duration: 12 months after culture-negative (pulmonary); lifelong suppression may be needed in HIV
  • Prophylaxis (HIV with CD4 <50): Azithromycin 1200mg weekly (less common now with effective ART)
4

Manage the syndrome

By anatomic site
Jump to anatomic site
🫀 Endovascular 🧠 CNS 🫁 Respiratory 🟤 GI / Hepatobiliary 🔬 GU & STI 🦴 MSK & Spinal 🩹 Skin & SSTI 🏥 Perioperative ✈️ Travel & Vectors
🫀 Endovascular & Bloodstream
● Time-critical

🚨 Mandatory ID Consultation: S. aureus bacteremia is the single most common driver of ID consultation. ID involvement reduces 90-day mortality by 35–50%. Consult ID on every SAB.

Initial Workup

  • Blood cultures q24–48h until 2 consecutive negatives
  • TTE within 24–72h; TEE if TTE non-diagnostic, IVDU, prosthetic valve, or persistent bacteremia
  • Source identification: examine all IV lines, wounds; CT if no obvious source; spine MRI if back pain or prolonged bacteremia
  • Metastatic foci: vertebral osteo, septic joints, epidural abscess, endophthalmitis (fundoscopic exam — see Endophthalmitis)

Empiric Therapy

SettingRegimenNotes
No MRSA riskOxacillin 2g IV q4h or Cefazolin 2g IV q8hDe-escalate from vanc when MSSA confirmed
Healthcare-associated / MRSA riskVancomycin 15–20 mg/kg IV q8–12h (AUC-guided)Switch to oxacillin/cefazolin if MSSA
Vancomycin-intolerantDaptomycin 6–10 mg/kg IV q24hNOT for pneumonia

Duration

CategoryCriteria (ALL must be met)Duration
UncomplicatedNo endocarditis, no devices, no metastatic foci, no IVDU, cultures clear ≤72h14 days IV from first negative culture
ComplicatedFails any above criterion4–6 weeks IV minimum

⚡ Pearls

  • De-escalation: Switch vancomycin → oxacillin/cefazolin immediately when MSSA confirmed — vancomycin inferior for MSSA
  • Persistent bacteremia >72h: Look harder — TEE, spine MRI, joint aspiration
  • Rifampin: NOT routine for SAB; reserved for biofilm (PJI, CIED) once bacteremia cleared
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Bacteremia not cleared by 72h on appropriate β-lactam → TEE if not done, look for metastatic foci (vertebral MRI, joint aspiration)
  • MSSA identified but still on vancomycin → switch to nafcillin/cefazolin immediately (vancomycin inferior for MSSA)
  • No source identified after 5 days → CT chest/abdomen/pelvis, spine MRI, ophthalmology exam
  • High vancomycin troughs with rising creatinine → check AUC-guided dosing or switch to daptomycin
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Persistent bacteremia >5–7 days despite source control and appropriate antibiotics
  • New embolic phenomena (stroke, septic pulmonary emboli, limb ischemia)
  • Echocardiographic evidence of large vegetation, perivalvular abscess, or hemodynamic compromise → cardiothoracic surgery
  • Spinal epidural abscess or vertebral osteomyelitis with neurologic deficits
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • MSSA confirmed → switch vancomycin to cefazolin or nafcillin/oxacillin (same day)
  • Two consecutive negative blood cultures + clinically improved + no metastatic foci → confirm duration (14 days uncomplicated, 4–6 wks complicated)
  • Stable, afebrile, cultures negative × 48h → consider OPAT evaluation for IV completion

Modified Duke Criteria (2023 ESC Update)

Definite IE: 2 major, OR 1 major + 3 minor, OR 5 minor criteria

Major Criteria:

  • Blood cultures: Typical organisms (S. aureus, viridans strep, S. gallolyticus, HACEK, Enterococcus) from 2 separate cultures; OR persistently positive cultures 12h apart; OR ≥3 positive cultures over >1h
  • Imaging: Vegetation, abscess, pseudoaneurysm, dehiscence, or new valve regurgitation on echo; OR abnormal activity on 18F-FDG PET/CT (prosthetic) or cardiac CT showing abscess/pseudoaneurysm

Minor Criteria:

  • Predisposition (IVDU, prosthetic valve, prior IE, structural heart disease)
  • Fever ≥38°C
  • Vascular phenomena (emboli, mycotic aneurysm, Janeway lesions, conjunctival hemorrhage)
  • Immunologic phenomena (Osler nodes, Roth spots, glomerulonephritis, positive RF)
  • Microbiologic evidence not meeting major criteria

Empiric Therapy (Before Culture Results)

ScenarioRegimenNotes
Native valve, community-acquiredVancomycin + CeftriaxoneCovers MSSA, MRSA, strep, enterococcus
Native valve, IVDUVancomycin + CeftriaxoneHigh MRSA prevalence; add gentamicin if critically ill
Prosthetic valve (early <1yr)Vancomycin + Gentamicin + Rifampin + CefepimeCover staph, GNR, enterococcus
Prosthetic valve (late >1yr)Vancomycin + CeftriaxoneSimilar to native valve

Duration by Organism & Valve

OrganismNative ValveProsthetic Valve
Streptococci (MIC ≤0.12)4 weeks (2 wks if + gent)6 weeks
Enterococcus4-6 weeks (+ gent synergy)6 weeks minimum
Staphylococcus (MSSA)6 weeks≥6 weeks + rifampin + gent × 2wks
Staphylococcus (MRSA)6 weeks vanc≥6 weeks + rifampin + gent × 2wks
HACEK4 weeks6 weeks

Surgical Indications

  • Emergent (within 24h): Refractory pulmonary edema, cardiogenic shock from severe AI/MR
  • Urgent (within days): Uncontrolled infection despite abx, abscess, enlarging vegetation, persistent bacteremia >7d, fungal IE
  • Elective: Large vegetation >10mm with embolic event, severe valve dysfunction once stabilized
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Blood cultures persistently negative → consider HACEK, Bartonella, Q fever, Brucella serology; send to reference lab
  • Duke criteria not met after workup → rethink: marantic endocarditis, NBTE, cardiac mass, other bacteremia source
  • Not responding to penicillin/ampicillin → check MIC, ensure no high-level aminoglycoside resistance (enterococcal IE)
  • Streptococcal IE not clearing → reconsider abscess, device seeding, or tolerant organism
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Heart failure (new or worsening) from valve dysfunction → emergent surgical evaluation
  • Perivalvular abscess, fistula, or destructive lesion on TEE → surgery likely within days
  • Persistent bacteremia >7 days or large vegetation (>10mm) with embolic risk
  • Embolic stroke — surgical timing complex; neurosurgery + cardiothoracic multidisciplinary discussion
  • Candida or other fungal endocarditis → surgery almost always required
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Susceptibility data available → narrow to most targeted agent (e.g., penicillin G for susceptible Streptococcus)
  • Enterococcal IE (MSSE) → amoxicillin monotherapy or switch from combination if high-level resistance present
  • Stable after 2 weeks IV for uncomplicated native valve streptococcal IE → consider switch to oral (POET trial evidence)

🚨 Medical Emergency: Mortality 40–60%. Treat ALL positive blood cultures — never a contaminant. Remove central line whenever possible.

Species & Susceptibility

SpeciesFrequencyKey Point
C. albicans~35%Fluconazole susceptible; echinocandin first-line for BSI
C. glabrata~25%Reduced fluconazole susceptibility; echinocandin preferred
C. parapsilosis~15%Reduced echinocandin susceptibility; fluconazole if susceptible
C. aurisEmergingPan-resistant possible; echinocandin + contact ID + infection control immediately

Treatment

ScenarioRegimenDuration
All candidemia (initial)Caspofungin 70mg load → 50mg IV q24h or Micafungin 100mg IV q24h14 days from first negative culture
Step-down (stable, susceptible)Fluconazole 400mg PO/IV q24h after 5–7 days echinocandinComplete 14 days total

Mandatory Workup

  • Ophthalmology within 48–72h — endophthalmitis in ~10%
  • Echocardiogram — Candida endocarditis rare but devastating
  • Serial blood cultures q24–48h until 2 consecutive negatives
  • Remove/replace CVC as soon as possible
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Candidemia persisting >5 days on echinocandin → check for echinocandin resistance (FKS mutation); C. parapsilosis has reduced susceptibility — consider fluconazole if susceptible
  • No obvious source → consider endovascular source (endocarditis, thrombophlebitis), hepatosplenic candidiasis
  • C. auris isolated → contact infection control immediately; enhanced resistance testing required
  • Worsening despite antifungal → CVC still in place? Ophthalmology done? Deep-seated focus?
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Hemodynamic instability or septic shock → ICU-level management
  • Candida endocarditis identified → cardiothoracic surgical evaluation
  • Endophthalmitis with vitritis → intravitreal antifungal + vitreoretinal surgery (urgent)
  • Hepatosplenic candidiasis in neutropenic patient → ID consultation, prolonged therapy
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Clinically stable, CVC removed, susceptibility confirms fluconazole-sensitive species → step down to fluconazole 400mg daily after 5–7 days echinocandin
  • Two consecutive negative blood cultures + afebrile + no deep foci → confirm 14-day duration from first negative culture
  • Neutrophil recovery in neutropenic patient → reassess for step-down

Overview

  • Infected arterial aneurysm — misnomer ("mycotic" = fungal-appearing grossly, not fungal etiology); almost always bacterial
  • Pathogenesis: Hematogenous seeding of arterial wall (from bacteremia or endocarditis) → vessel wall infection → weakening and aneurysmal dilation → risk of rupture
  • Locations: Aorta (most common — especially thoracoabdominal), intracranial (5–10% of intracranial aneurysms), femoral, mesenteric, visceral arteries

Microbiology

  • S. aureus — most common (IVDU, CLABSI, endocarditis source)
  • Salmonella spp. — classical cause of aortic mycotic aneurysm in elderly (atherosclerotic aorta), especially in Asia
  • Streptococcus, Enterococcus, gram-negative rods
  • Fungi (Candida, Aspergillus) — rare; post-cardiac surgery, immunocompromised

Clinical Features

  • Fever + localizing pain (back/abdominal pain for aortic; headache for intracranial) + pulsatile mass
  • Intracranial mycotic aneurysm (ICMA): Headache, CN palsy, subarachnoid hemorrhage; often presents with rupture; associated with infective endocarditis in 2–4%
  • Leukocytosis, elevated CRP/ESR, positive blood cultures

Diagnosis

  • CT angiography: Initial imaging — lobulated saccular aneurysm, often eccentric, periaortic inflammation, gas in wall (pathognomonic)
  • MR angiography / conventional angiography: Intracranial — CTA or digital subtraction angiography; repeat if initial negative and endocarditis present
  • PET/CT: Detects aortic infection/inflammation; useful in diagnostically uncertain cases
  • Blood cultures: Positive in ~50–75%

Treatment

ComponentDetails
IV antibioticsPathogen-directed; empiric: Vancomycin + Ceftriaxone; prolonged course (6 weeks minimum for S. aureus)
Surgical / endovascular repairRequired for most aortic mycotic aneurysms — endovascular stenting (EVAR) preferred in suitable anatomy; open repair if ruptured or endovascular not feasible
Intracranial mycotic aneurysmSmall, unruptured — trial of IV antibiotics + serial imaging; surgical clipping or endovascular coiling if enlarging, ruptured, or not responding
Lifelong suppressive antibioticsOften recommended after endovascular repair (prosthetic material); oral fluoroquinolone or TMP-SMX
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving on antibiotics → re-image (CT angio): enlarging? new leakage? endovascular leak around stent?
  • Cultures negative → send for Salmonella, Brucella, fungi; consider PET/CT if still no source
  • Fever returning after apparent response → anastomotic infection? secondary graft infection?
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Rupture or impending rupture (rapidly enlarging on serial imaging, pain, hemodynamic instability) → emergent vascular surgery
  • Intracranial mycotic aneurysm enlarging or new neurologic deficit → neurosurgery/interventional neuroradiology
  • Ongoing bacteremia despite antibiotics → surgery to excise infected vessel segment
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • After endovascular/surgical repair: continue IV antibiotics for 6 weeks minimum (S. aureus, GNR)
  • Oral suppressive antibiotics often continued long-term after endovascular repair (prosthetic material in infected field)
  • Intracranial mycotic aneurysm shrinking on serial imaging with antibiotics → continue to monitor; defer surgery if stable
○ Condition-specific

Definition (NHSN/CDC)

  • Central line in place >2 days AND on day of or day before BSI
  • Blood culture positive for recognized pathogen OR common commensal from ≥2 separate cultures
  • No other apparent source

Common Pathogens

  • Coagulase-negative Staphylococci (most common)
  • S. aureus (MSSA & MRSA)
  • Enterococcus
  • Candida species
  • Gram-negative rods (Klebsiella, E. coli, Pseudomonas, Enterobacter)

Management by Organism

OrganismLine RemovalDuration
S. aureusREMOVE (always)4-6 weeks if complicated; 2 weeks if uncomplicated, TEE negative
CandidaREMOVE (always)14 days from first negative culture
PseudomonasREMOVE7-14 days
EnterococcusRemove recommended7-14 days
Coag-neg StaphMay salvage with abx lock therapy5-7 days if line removed; 10-14 days if retained
GNR (non-Pseudomonas)Attempt salvage possible7-14 days

Complicated CLABSI (Requires Longer Therapy)

  • Persistent bacteremia >72h despite appropriate therapy and line removal
  • Endocarditis (get TTE, consider TEE especially for S. aureus)
  • Septic thrombophlebitis
  • Osteomyelitis or metastatic infection
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • BSI not clearing after CVC removal → look for suppurative thrombophlebitis (venous ultrasound), endocarditis, or metastatic foci
  • S. aureus CLABSI → mandatory workup as SAB: TTE/TEE, fundoscopy, prolonged treatment
  • Candida CLABSI → treat as candidemia: ophthalmology within 48h, echo, 14 days from first negative culture
  • Organism does not match line site → rethink: GI source? wound? sinusitis (NGT)?
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Hemodynamic instability → sepsis management; ICU if criteria met
  • Suppurative thrombophlebitis identified → vascular surgery; anticoagulation + prolonged antibiotics
  • Cannot remove CVC (tunneled, implanted port) → ID consult for antibiotic lock therapy protocol; outcomes inferior
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • CoNS CLABSI with CVC removed, clinically improving → 5–7 days antibiotics
  • GNR or Enterococcus with line removed, improving → 7–14 days culture-directed
  • S. aureus: treat as SAB (14 days minimum uncomplicated); do not shorten

Classification

  • Pocket infection: Localized to generator site — erythema, warmth, swelling, purulent drainage, skin erosion/exposure of device
  • Lead infection/CIED-associated endocarditis: Vegetation on lead or valve (diagnosed by TEE)

Microbiology

OrganismFrequencyClinical Note
Coagulase-negative staphylococci (CoNS)Most common (40-50%)Often indolent presentation
S. aureus (MSSA/MRSA)30-40%More virulent, higher complication rate, faster progression
Gram-negative rods5-10%Less common; may suggest pocket contamination
EnterococcusRare—

🚨 MANAGEMENT PRINCIPLE: Complete Device Removal Required

CRITICAL: Complete device removal (generator AND all leads) is REQUIRED for all CIED infections (pocket or lead). Antibiotics alone are insufficient and associated with high failure rates (>80% relapse). Partial removal or antibiotics without extraction is NOT acceptable management.

Management Steps

  1. Blood cultures × 2 (before antibiotics)
  2. TEE (evaluate for lead vegetation, valve involvement)
  3. Start empiric antibiotics: Vancomycin 15-20mg/kg IV q8-12h + Gentamicin 1mg/kg IV q8h (until cultures available)
  4. Plan for complete device extraction — Coordinate with EP cardiology or CT surgery
    • Temporary pacing if pacemaker-dependent
    • Extraction (percutaneous lead extraction preferred if available; open extraction if complex)
    • Re-implantation on contralateral side after adequate treatment (typically 2-4 weeks for pocket, 4-6 weeks for lead IE)
  5. Tailor antibiotics based on cultures (typically 2 weeks post-extraction for pocket, 4-6 weeks for lead IE)

Antibiotic Duration (Post-Extraction)

ScenarioDuration
Pocket infection only, no bacteremia10-14 days post-extraction
Pocket infection with bacteremia2 weeks post-extraction (from first negative blood culture)
Lead infection/CIED-IE without valve involvement4 weeks post-extraction
CIED-IE with valve vegetation or osteomyelitis6 weeks post-extraction (treat as native valve endocarditis)

⚡ HIGH-YIELD PEARL: S. aureus Bacteremia + CIED

Any patient with S. aureus bacteremia and a CIED should be presumed to have device infection until proven otherwise.
• Obtain TEE (look for lead vegetation)
• Plan for device extraction even if TEE negative (sensitivity ~25-70% for lead vegetations)
• Do NOT just treat with antibiotics — device must come out

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • BSI clearing but local wound infection persisting → consider pocket infection; device extraction may be needed even with negative cultures
  • Generator pocket infection with no BSI → still requires extraction in most cases; antibiotics alone have >80% failure rate
  • Organism unexpected (GNR, Candida) → immunosuppression? recent procedure? hematogenous seeding?
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Vegetation on leads on TOE → device extraction + prolonged IV antibiotics
  • Septic pulmonary emboli from lead vegetation → extraction + extended treatment
  • Hemodynamic instability or lead endocarditis → ICU + urgent extraction planning
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • After complete extraction + generator pocket debridement: S. aureus → 4–6 weeks IV; CoNS → 2–4 weeks
  • Re-implantation: new site after bacteremia cleared (typically 72h after extraction for CoNS, 14 days for S. aureus)
  • Oral step-down for selected cases at 2 weeks with ID guidance (POET/OVIVA data)

Classification

TypeSourceCommon Pathogens
Endogenous (hematogenous)Bacteremia or fungemia seeding the eye via choroidal vasculatureS. aureus, Streptococci, Candida spp., Klebsiella (hvKP), Aspergillus
Exogenous — post-surgicalIntraocular surgery (cataract most common)CoNS (#1), S. aureus, Streptococci, GNR
Exogenous — post-traumaticPenetrating eye injuryBacillus cereus (soil/vegetable matter — aggressive, hours to blindness), S. aureus, Streptococci
Exogenous — endophthalmitis after intravitreal injectionAnti-VEGF injections, increasingly commonCoNS, oral streptococci (talking during procedure)

Clinical Features

  • Acute vision loss, eye pain, photophobia, floaters, red eye
  • Hypopyon: Layering of white cells in anterior chamber — hallmark finding
  • Endogenous: May be bilateral; often insidious onset; vision loss may precede pain; high index of suspicion in bacteremic/fungemic patients
  • Bacillus cereus: Rapid fulminant destruction within 12–24h; ring abscess; history of penetrating trauma with soil/organic material

🚨 Mandatory ophthalmology consultation in:
• All S. aureus bacteremia — fundoscopic exam within 48–72h
• All candidemia — dilated fundoscopic exam within 48–72h (10% prevalence)
• Hypervirulent Klebsiella liver abscess (hvKP) — endophthalmitis in up to 10%
• Any bacteremic patient with visual complaints — do not attribute to ICU medications

Diagnosis

  • Clinical + ophthalmology exam: Slit lamp and dilated fundoscopy — vitreous cells, hypopyon, chorioretinal infiltrates
  • Vitreous/aqueous tap (vitreoretinal surgery): Culture and Gram stain — highest diagnostic yield; performed by ophthalmology
  • B-scan ultrasound: If media opacity prevents fundoscopy; shows vitreous opacities, retinal detachment
  • Blood cultures: Identify source in endogenous; may guide antibiotic duration

Treatment

TypeIntravitreal AntibioticsSystemicSurgery
Bacterial endophthalmitis (exogenous)Vancomycin 1mg/0.1mL + Ceftazidime 2.25mg/0.1mL intravitreal (injected by ophthalmology)Adjunctive — IV antibiotics based on culturesPars plana vitrectomy (PPV) if poor visual acuity at presentation (light perception only)
Endogenous bacterialIntravitreal antibiotics + IV systemic (source-directed)IV antibiotics for bacteremia source — 4–6 weeks for S. aureusPPV for severe cases
Candida endophthalmitis (chorioretinitis without vitritis)Usually NOT required initiallyFluconazole 400–800mg IV/PO daily (excellent ocular penetration)Intravitreal antifungal + PPV if vitritis or macula threatened
Candida endophthalmitis (+ vitritis)Intravitreal voriconazole 100mcg/0.1mL or amphotericin B 5–10mcg/0.1mLIV voriconazole or echinocandin (poor ocular penetration — switch to fluconazole/voriconazole for ocular disease)PPV strongly recommended
Bacillus cereusIntravitreal vancomycin + clindamycinIV vancomycin + clindamycin; carbapenem if GNR co-suspectedEmergency PPV — every hour counts

⚡ Key Pearls

  • Echinocandins have poor ocular penetration — do not rely on caspofungin/micafungin for Candida endophthalmitis; use fluconazole or voriconazole
  • Duration for Candida: Minimum 4–6 weeks from resolution of ocular findings (not just clearance of candidemia)
  • Candida chorioretinitis without vitritis: Systemic fluconazole alone may be sufficient — ophthalmology follow-up every 1–2 weeks essential
  • SAB endophthalmitis: Even if ophthalmology exam is normal, extend treatment to 4 weeks minimum if any retinal lesion seen
  • Bilateral involvement: Strongly suggests endogenous/hematogenous source — intensify source search
IDSA Candidiasis Guidelines: Ophthalmologic exam recommended for all patients with candidemia within the first week. Dilated exam by an ophthalmologist experienced in intraocular infections — not just bedside penlight exam.
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Vision not improving or worsening at 48–72h after intravitreal antibiotics → repeat vitreous tap; consider resistant organism, fungal co-infection
  • Endogenous endophthalmitis: source of bacteremia/fungemia not yet identified → re-examine: dental source? occult abscess? unrecognized IVDU? hypervirulent Klebsiella liver abscess?
  • Candida: not responding to fluconazole → check species (C. glabrata/krusei resistant); check ocular penetration of current regimen
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Light perception or worse visual acuity → pars plana vitrectomy (PPV) strongly indicated; urgent vitreoretinal surgery
  • Bacillus cereus suspected (post-traumatic, soil contamination) → emergency PPV within hours; every hour matters
  • Bilateral involvement → intensify systemic therapy; likely hematogenous dissemination requiring source control
  • Retinal detachment on B-scan → PPV
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Candida chorioretinitis without vitritis, susceptible species → step down to oral fluconazole 400–800mg (excellent ocular penetration)
  • After treatment, minimum 4–6 weeks from resolution of ocular findings (not just clearance of candidemia)
  • Confirmed susceptible bacterial endophthalmitis responding → complete systemic course (duration depends on organism and source)

Overview

  • Infection of prosthetic vascular graft — rare (1–6%) but high morbidity/mortality; limb loss and death common without prompt treatment
  • Location: Aortic grafts most serious; infrainguinal grafts most common (higher contamination risk from groin incision)
  • Timing: Early (<4 months from implant) — S. aureus, GNR, Candida (operative contamination); Late (>4 months) — CoNS, S. aureus (bacteremic seeding)

Clinical Features

  • Fever, wound erythema/drainage over graft, tender pulsatile mass, sepsis
  • Aortograft-enteric fistula (AEF): Life-threatening complication of aortic graft — GI bleed (herald bleed then massive hemorrhage), sepsis; CT shows gas around graft, bowel adherent to graft
  • Peripheral graft: Groin wound breakdown, sinus tract, false aneurysm formation

Microbiology

  • CoNS (S. epidermidis) — most common overall; indolent biofilm infection
  • S. aureus — aggressive; often presents early; high risk of AEF
  • Gram-negative rods (E. coli, Pseudomonas, Klebsiella) — early infection, perineal/groin location

Diagnosis

  • CT with contrast: Gas around graft (>3 months post-op = pathognomonic), perigraft fluid, soft tissue thickening, pseudoaneurysm
  • PET/CT (18F-FDG): Sensitive for low-grade/chronic infection (CoNS); localizes extent
  • Blood cultures: Positive in ~50%
  • Intraoperative cultures: Highest yield; perigraft fluid and graft material cultures

Treatment

ComponentDetails
AntibioticsEmpiric: Vancomycin + Pip-Tazo (cover MRSA + GNR + anaerobes); culture-directed; long-term suppression often required
Graft excision (definitive)Remove infected prosthetic material whenever possible; in-situ rifampin-soaked graft or autologous vein reconstruction preferred over extra-anatomic bypass for aortic infections
Salvage (selected cases)Debridement + graft preservation with omental flap — only for early, limited infection with susceptible organisms (CoNS); high recurrence rate
AEFEmergency surgery — graft excision + bowel repair + arterial reconstruction; very high mortality even with surgery
Lifelong suppressionOften necessary when graft cannot be fully removed; oral rifampin-based regimens
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Elevated WBC + graft site pain but negative CT → MRI or PET/CT for occult graft infection; early infection may be CT-occult
  • Blood cultures growing CoNS → may represent contaminant; repeat cultures; true graft infection if consistently positive or clinical context
  • Aortic graft infection presenting as GI bleed → aortoenteric fistula until proven otherwise; CT angiography urgently
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Aortoenteric fistula (massive GI bleed + aortic graft) → vascular surgery emergency; endovascular bridging then open repair
  • Septic shock + vascular graft → ICU resuscitation + urgent vascular surgery; mortality >30% even with optimal management
  • Perigraft gas or abscess → vascular surgery for graft excision + extra-anatomic bypass or in-situ reconstruction
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • After graft excision + reconstruction: S. aureus → 6 weeks IV; GNR → 4–6 weeks; indefinite oral suppression for in-situ reconstruction
  • MSSA confirmed → switch to nafcillin/cefazolin; β-lactam superior
  • Graft retention (aortic) with negative cultures after antibiotic lavage → indefinite oral suppression; not curative but may be only option
🧠 CNS
● Time-critical

Empiric Therapy by Age/Risk

PopulationLikely PathogensEmpiric Regimen
Adults 18-50S. pneumoniae, N. meningitidisCeftriaxone 2g IV q12h + Vancomycin 15-20mg/kg IV q8-12h
Adults >50 or immunocompromised+ Listeria monocytogenesAbove + Ampicillin 2g IV q4h
Neurosurgery/head trauma/CSF shuntS. aureus, GNR, PseudomonasVancomycin + Cefepime 2g IV q8h (or Meropenem)

Dexamethasone

  • Indication: Give BEFORE or WITH first antibiotic dose if bacterial meningitis suspected
  • Dose: Dexamethasone 0.15 mg/kg IV q6h × 4 days
  • Benefit: Reduces mortality and hearing loss in pneumococcal meningitis; less clear for other organisms
  • Discontinue if: CSF gram stain/culture shows non-pneumococcal organism (some experts continue regardless)

CSF Interpretation

ParameterBacterialViralTB/Fungal
WBC>1000 (often >5000)10-50050-500
Differential>80% PMNLymphocyte predominantLymphocyte predominant
Glucose<40 mg/dL (or CSF:serum <0.4)NormalLow
Protein>200 mg/dL50-100100-500
Opening pressureElevated (>20 cmH2O)Normal/mild ↑Often elevated

Pearl: Early bacterial meningitis or partially treated may show lymphocyte predominance. Repeat LP in 12-24h if uncertain.

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • CSF not improving at 24–48h (worsening pleocytosis, rising protein) → consider viral (HSV, enterovirus), TB, fungal, or carcinomatous meningitis
  • CSF cultures negative → send HSV PCR, enterovirus PCR, cryptococcal antigen, MTb PCR, Lyme antibody (CSF), cytology
  • No clinical improvement at 48h → review steroid use, ensure adequate dosing, consider CT for abscess/empyema
  • Recurrent bacterial meningitis → look for anatomic defect (CSF leak, mastoiditis, sinusitis, LP scar)
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Declining GCS or new focal deficits → urgent CT + neurosurgery/neurology consultation
  • Herniation signs (Cushing triad, posturing, blown pupil) → emergent ICP management, neurosurgery
  • Cerebral venous sinus thrombosis identified → anticoagulation + neurology
  • Waterhouse-Friderichsen syndrome (meningococcal) → ICU, vasopressors, DIC management
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Meningococcal disease (N. meningitidis) confirmed → narrow to penicillin G; discontinue vancomycin
  • Pneumococcal confirmed susceptible to penicillin (MIC ≤0.1) → penicillin G monotherapy; stop vancomycin
  • Dexamethasone: discontinue at 4 days or if non-pneumococcal etiology confirmed (limited benefit beyond pneumococcal)
  • Afebrile × 48h, improving clinically, CSF improving → confirm 10–14 day course for pneumococcal, 7 days meningococcal

Overview

  • Purulent collection between the dura and arachnoid — neurosurgical emergency; rapid spread in the subdural space → seizures, herniation
  • Source: Sinusitis (frontal most common, 60–70%) → direct extension; otitis media/mastoiditis; meningitis; post-neurosurgery/trauma
  • Demographics: Young adults (sinusitis source); often previously healthy

Clinical Features

  • Fever, severe headache, altered mental status — may rapidly progress to seizures, focal deficits, coma
  • Meningismus: present in ~80%
  • Key: Often misdiagnosed as meningitis initially; distinction critical — LP contraindicated in subdural empyema (herniation risk + LP may be normal)
  • Seizures in 30–50% — often the presenting event

Diagnosis

  • MRI with gadolinium: Investigation of choice — crescent-shaped extraaxial collection with restricted diffusion (DWI positive = pus); better than CT for posterior fossa and early disease
    • CT with contrast: Hypodense crescent collection with peripheral enhancement; may miss small collections
  • Do NOT perform LP — high risk of herniation; LP rarely helpful (may show normal CSF)
  • Blood cultures: Positive in ~35%

Microbiology

  • Streptococci (viridans, S. anginosus group, S. milleri) — most common from sinusitis source
  • Staphylococcus aureus — post-traumatic, post-surgical
  • Gram-negative rods — otogenic source; immunocompromised
  • Anaerobes — commonly part of polymicrobial mix from sinusitis

Treatment

  • Emergency neurosurgical drainage: Craniotomy (preferred for large collections) or burr hole drainage; do not delay for additional imaging
  • Empiric antibiotics: Ceftriaxone 2g IV q12h + Metronidazole 500mg IV q8h + Vancomycin 15–20 mg/kg IV q8–12h (MRSA coverage)
  • Duration: 4–6 weeks IV antibiotics; longer if associated osteomyelitis
  • Seizure prophylaxis: Levetiracetam — continue for minimum 3 months; longer if seizures occurred
  • Source control: Treat underlying sinusitis/otitis — ENT involvement for sinus drainage
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving after drainage + antibiotics → repeat MRI: is collection fully drained? residual empyema? new loculation?
  • Cultures negative → consider Streptococcus anginosus group (may be fastidious), anaerobes (longer incubation), TB
  • New seizures or focal deficit during treatment → repeat neuroimaging, consider cortical vein thrombosis
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Deteriorating neurologic status → emergent re-exploration neurosurgery
  • Associated osteomyelitis of adjacent bone → prolonged antibiotics + possible craniectomy
  • Cerebral venous sinus thrombosis → anticoagulation + neurology consultation
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Cultures allow narrowing → target most directed agent (often penicillin/cephalosporin + metronidazole for polymicrobial)
  • Clinical improvement + sterile post-drainage cultures → complete 4–6 weeks total; longer if osteomyelitis present
  • Seizure-free → antiepileptic taper discussion at 3–6 months with neurology

Etiology & Source

  • Contiguous spread (40-50%): Sinusitis, otitis, dental infection → frontal/temporal lobe abscess
  • Hematogenous (25%): Endocarditis, lung abscess, pulmonary AVMs → often multiple abscesses
  • Trauma/neurosurgery: S. aureus predominant
  • Cryptogenic (25%): No source identified

Microbiology (Often Polymicrobial 30-60%)

SourceCommon Pathogens
Contiguous spreadStreptococci (viridans, S. anginosus group), Anaerobes (Bacteroides, Fusobacterium, Prevotella)
Trauma/NeurosurgeryS. aureus (MSSA/MRSA), Coagulase-negative staph
HematogenousS. aureus, Streptococci, Gram-negatives
ImmunocompromisedNocardia, Toxoplasma, Aspergillus, Cryptococcus, Listeria

Empiric Treatment

ScenarioRegimenDuration
Standard (contiguous/cryptogenic)Ceftriaxone 2g IV q12h + Metronidazole 500mg IV q8h6-8 weeks IV
+ MRSA risk (trauma, post-op)Add Vancomycin 15-20mg/kg IV q8-12h6-8 weeks IV
ImmunocompromisedConsider Nocardia coverage (TMP-SMX) or fungal (voriconazole, amphotericin)Per organism

Source Control & Surgical Indications

  • Surgical drainage indicated if: Abscess >2.5 cm, mass effect/neurologic deterioration, or no improvement after 2 weeks of antibiotics
  • Aspiration vs excision: Stereotactic aspiration preferred (less invasive); excision if multiloculated or failed aspiration
  • Serial imaging: MRI to monitor response (expect slow resolution over weeks-months)

🚨 CRITICAL SAFETY POINTS

  • LP is CONTRAINDICATED in brain abscess (risk of herniation)
  • Diagnosis: MRI with contrast (ring-enhancing lesion with restricted diffusion on DWI)
  • CT acceptable if MRI unavailable, but MRI is superior for detection and characterization
  • Steroids: Controversial; only for significant mass effect causing herniation risk (may impair antibiotic penetration)
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 72h → ensure adequate neurosurgical drainage; repeat MRI to assess size
  • Cultures negative → send for Nocardia, MTb, Toxoplasma IgG/IgM (if immunocompromised), Aspergillus, Cryptococcus
  • Immunocompromised patient with ring-enhancing lesion → broaden differential: Toxoplasma (empirically treat), PML, CNS lymphoma (biopsy if no Toxo response)
  • New or enlarging lesion on treatment → consider antibiotic penetration (some agents poor CNS penetration), resistance
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Rapid expansion of abscess, impending herniation → emergent neurosurgery
  • Rupture into ventricular system (pyocephalus) → neurosurgery emergency; high mortality
