  • Deteriorating neurologic exam despite drainage → ICU, ICP monitoring
  • Multiple abscesses + immunocompromised → ID + neurology + neurosurgery multidisciplinary
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Source identified and targeted → narrow antibiotics based on culture (e.g., pencillin G + metronidazole for oral streptococci + anaerobes)
  • Lesion <2.5cm, clinically stable → antibiotics alone may be sufficient with serial imaging
  • After 6–8 weeks IV, stable or resolved on imaging → consider oral step-down for selected organisms (e.g., metronidazole PO for anaerobes)

Definitions & Epidemiology

  • VP shunt infection rate: 5–15% (highest in children <6 months); most occur within 6 months of placement
  • EVD (external ventricular drain) infection rate: 0–22%; risk increases with duration >5–7 days
  • Ventriculoperitoneal (VP) shunt most common; also VA (ventriculoatrial — causes bacteremia/endocarditis) and LP shunts

Microbiology

PathogenFrequencyNotes
Coagulase-negative staphylococci (CoNS)40–50%S. epidermidis — indolent course; biofilm former
S. aureus (MSSA/MRSA)20–30%More aggressive; distal shunt infection common
Gram-negative rods15–20%More common in EVD; neonates; post-neurosurgery
Propionibacterium (Cutibacterium) acnes5–10%Indolent; often delayed presentation; gram-positive rod on Gram stain

Clinical Presentation

  • VP shunt: Fever, headache, nausea, altered mental status; shunt malfunction (ICP signs); wound erythema/tenderness along shunt tract; peritoneal pseudocyst (distal)
  • VA shunt: As above + bacteremia features; shunt nephritis (immune complex GN) with chronic infection
  • EVD: New fever + meningismus + CSF pleocytosis in setting of EVD in place
  • Key distinction: Low-grade fever alone in shunted patient warrants tap — CSF may look surprisingly benign with CoNS

Diagnosis

  • Shunt tap (neurosurgery-performed): CSF WBC, glucose, protein, Gram stain, culture — most reliable; reservoir tap preferred
  • Blood cultures: Especially for VA shunts; may be positive in VP shunts with bacteremia
  • Abdominal CT/ultrasound: Evaluate for distal complications (pseudocyst, peritonitis)
  • CSF interpretation: Even mild pleocytosis (>10 WBC) in a shunted patient with fever warrants treatment — do not dismiss low cell counts

Treatment

StepAction
1. Remove shunt/EVDHardware removal is standard of care for VP/VA shunt infections (antibiotics alone have >80% failure rate); EVD replacement or removal depending on clinical need
2. External ventricular drainPlace EVD for CSF diversion while treating; allows CSF monitoring and intrathecal antibiotics if needed
3. Empiric IV antibioticsVancomycin 15–20 mg/kg IV q8–12h + Cefepime 2g IV q8h (or meropenem for Pseudomonas risk)
4. Intrathecal/intraventricular antibioticsConsider for refractory ventriculitis or poor CSF penetration: Vancomycin 10–20mg/day or Gentamicin 4–8mg/day intrathecally (via EVD)
5. DurationCoNS: 10–14 days; S. aureus / GNR: 14–21 days; continue until CSF cultures negative
6. Shunt re-implantationAfter CSF sterilization (typically 10–14 days); confirm 2 negative CSF cultures before re-implanting
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • CSF not sterilizing after 48–72h on appropriate antibiotics → ensure hardware removed; check EVD for biofilm; consider intrathecal antibiotics
  • CoNS isolated — is this a contaminant? → repeat CSF; clinical correlation essential; treat if clinical signs + consistent CSF profile
  • Abdominal symptoms + VP shunt → consider distal shunt complications (pseudocyst, peritonitis) — CT abdomen
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • ICP rising despite EVD → neurosurgery for shunt revision or EVD management
  • Gram-negative ventriculitis → add intrathecal gentamicin; prolonged IV course; high mortality risk
  • Systemic sepsis from VA shunt → treat as bacteremia; consider removing shunt surgically
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Two consecutive sterile CSF cultures → proceed with shunt re-implantation planning
  • CoNS confirmed: 10–14 days from CSF sterilization → re-implant
  • GNR or S. aureus: 14–21 days from sterilization before re-implanting
○ Condition-specific

Recognition

  • Viral meningitis: Fever + headache + meningismus + photophobia; altered consciousness uncommon (distinguishes from encephalitis)
  • Viral encephalitis: Fever + altered mental status, behavioral change, seizures, focal deficits; meningism variable
  • Common causes: Enteroviruses (most common viral meningitis), HSV-1 (most common sporadic encephalitis), VZV, EBV, CMV (immunocompromised), arboviruses (WNV, EEE, SLE, Powassan)
  • Seasonal: Enteroviruses — summer/fall; arboviruses — mosquito season; HSV — year-round

Workup

  • LP (mandatory): Opening pressure, cell count (lymphocytic pleocytosis), protein (mildly elevated), glucose (normal distinguishes viral from bacterial), Gram stain, culture
  • CSF viral PCR panel: HSV-1/2 (most important — treatable), VZV, CMV, EBV, enteroviruses; send early before acyclovir
  • Arbovirus serology (CSF + serum): WNV IgM (CNS), EEE, SLE; send if endemic/seasonal
  • MRI brain: Temporal lobe signal abnormality = HSV encephalitis until proven otherwise
  • EEG: Temporal lobe changes, FIRDA pattern in HSV encephalitis

Empiric Therapy

PathogenTreatment
HSV encephalitisAcyclovir 10mg/kg IV q8h × 14–21 days; START EMPIRICALLY — do not wait for PCR
VZV encephalitisAcyclovir 10–15mg/kg IV q8h × 10–14 days
CMV (immunocompromised)Ganciclovir 5mg/kg IV q12h ± Foscarnet
Enteroviral meningitisSupportive — self-limited; Pleconaril (investigational) for severe cases
WNV / arboviralSupportive ICU care; no specific antivirals
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • No temporal lobe changes on MRI + negative HSV PCR → enteroviral, arboviral; consider bacterial meningitis if glucose low
  • Immunocompromised patient with atypical encephalitis → extend CSF panel: Toxoplasma, Cryptococcus CrAg, CMV, JC virus (PML)
  • Autoimmune encephalitis (anti-NMDAR, LGI1, CASPR2) mimics viral encephalitis — send autoimmune encephalitis panel in CSF if no pathogen identified
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Herniation signs or focal deficits → MRI before LP (do not delay acyclovir); CT if MRI unavailable
  • Status epilepticus → ICU, antiepileptics (levetiracetam, valproate), anesthesia if refractory
  • Respiratory failure (EEE, acute flaccid paralysis from WNV, severe encephalitis) → mechanical ventilation
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • HSV PCR negative at 72h (taken early) → repeat LP/PCR at 1 week; negative PCR on day 1 does not exclude HSV
  • Clinical improvement + negative HSV PCR + alternative diagnosis confirmed → stop acyclovir
  • Enteroviral meningitis: self-limited; discharge when comfortable with PO, no meningismus

Recognition

  • Subacute onset (days–weeks): headache, fever, nausea; meningismus often absent or mild; altered mental status
  • Elevated ICP: Headache + visual changes + papilledema — most life-threatening complication; kills more than the fungus
  • HIV (CD4 <100), transplant, hematologic malignancy, high-dose corticosteroids
  • Pulmonary cryptococcosis (non-CNS): may be asymptomatic or cough/dyspnea — lower risk for dissemination in immunocompetent

Workup

  • LP (mandatory): Opening pressure (often >250 mmH2O); CSF CrAg (highly sensitive/specific); India ink (60–80% sensitivity); CSF culture; cell count (usually lymphocytic, modest elevation)
  • Serum CrAg: >95% sensitivity for cryptococcal meningitis in HIV; also useful for monitoring
  • CD4 count, HIV viral load
  • CT head before LP (rule out mass lesion, but do not delay LP if no focal findings or papilledema)
  • Fungal blood cultures (positive in ~70%)

Empiric Therapy

PhaseRegimenDuration
InductionLiposomal AmB 3–4 mg/kg IV q24h + Flucytosine 25mg/kg PO QID2 weeks
ConsolidationFluconazole 400mg PO daily8 weeks
Maintenance (HIV)Fluconazole 200mg PO dailyUntil CD4 >100 × 6 months on ART
Non-HIV / transplant inductionSame; consolidation/maintenance per transplant guidelines × 12 monthsLonger if immunocompromised

Source Control

  • ICP management is critical: Repeat LP daily (target OP <200 mmH2O) until ICP controlled; remove 20–30 mL CSF per LP
  • Lumbar drain or VP shunt if LP cannot control ICP
  • Acetazolamide and corticosteroids NOT recommended for elevated ICP in Cryptococcus
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not clinically improving at 2 weeks → repeat LP (ongoing elevated ICP?); CSF culture still positive → consider AmB + 5-FC combination extension; check flucytosine levels (target 40–60 mcg/mL)
  • Fluconazole-resistant Cryptococcus → MIC testing; voriconazole or posaconazole salvage with ID consultation
  • IRIS post-ART initiation with cryptococcal meningitis → hold or defer ART; corticosteroids for severe IRIS (controversial)
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • ICP >350 mmH2O with herniation signs → emergent large volume LP; neurosurgery for lumbar drain or shunt
  • Coma or altered consciousness → ICU; aggressive ICP management; consider empiric treatment of elevated ICP
  • Respiratory failure (disseminated) → ICU; ARDS management
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Induction complete, clinically improved, CSF culture negative at 2 weeks → transition to fluconazole consolidation
  • ART initiation in HIV: defer 5 weeks after starting antifungal treatment (reduces IRIS mortality — COAT trial)
  • Maintenance fluconazole can be stopped when CD4 >100 × 6 months on ART; serum CrAg negative
  • Risk factors: HIV/AIDS (CD4 <100), solid organ transplant, hematologic malignancy, prolonged steroids
  • Presentation: Subacute onset (days–weeks), headache, fever, meningismus (may be subtle), altered mental status, cranial nerve palsies; elevated ICP is hallmark
  • Diagnosis: Serum + CSF CrAg (sensitivity >99%), India ink, fungal culture; CSF typically lymphocytic pleocytosis, low glucose, elevated protein

Treatment (IDSA 2010)

PhaseRegimenDuration
InductionLiposomal amphotericin B 3–4 mg/kg IV daily + flucytosine (5-FC) 25 mg/kg PO QID≥2 weeks (HIV); ≥4 weeks (transplant/immunocompromised)
ConsolidationFluconazole 400 mg PO daily8 weeks (HIV); 8 weeks (transplant)
Maintenance/suppressionFluconazole 200 mg PO dailyHIV: at least 1 year + CD4 >100 on ART × ≥3 months; transplant: 6–12 months

ICP Management (Critical)

  • Measure opening pressure at every LP
  • If OP >25 cmH₂O → therapeutic LP to reduce to <20 cmH₂O or 50% initial pressure
  • Daily LPs until ICP controlled; consider lumbar drain if persistent elevation
  • VP shunt for refractory elevated ICP
  • No role for acetazolamide or steroids for routine ICP management in cryptococcal meningitis (steroids increase mortality — AmBisome trial)

Recognition

  • Fever, headache, meningismus — often subacute/indolent (days to weeks); altered consciousness, cranial nerve palsies (CN VI, CN III), focal deficits
  • Highest risk: HIV, young children, immigrants from endemic countries, household contact with active TB, malnutrition, alcohol use disorder
  • Complications: Hydrocephalus (communicating → obstructive), vasculitis (stroke), cerebral infarction, tuberculoma
  • Base-of-skull meningitis: cranial nerve involvement common (CN VI, CN VII, CN III)
  • Mortality 25–30% even with treatment; severe neurologic sequelae in survivors

Workup

  • LP: CSF glucose <45 mg/dL (or CSF:serum glucose <0.5) — low glucose; lymphocytosis (100–500); high protein (>100mg/dL)
  • CSF AFB smear: Sensitivity 20–40%; send ≥3 samples (large volume, centrifuged)
  • CSF MTb culture: Gold standard but takes weeks; also blood cultures
  • CSF MTb PCR (Xpert MTB/RIF): ~80% sensitive, >95% specific; also detects rifampin resistance
  • CT/MRI head: Hydrocephalus, basal meningeal enhancement, tuberculomas, infarcts; repeat if clinical deterioration
  • HIV testing; CD4 count; chest CT for pulmonary TB

Empiric Therapy

ComponentRegimenDuration
InductionRIPE: Rifampin 10mg/kg + Isoniazid 5mg/kg + Pyrazinamide 25mg/kg + Ethambutol 15mg/kg — all PO daily2 months
ContinuationRifampin + Isoniazid daily7–10 months (total 9–12 months for TB meningitis)
Adjunctive dexamethasone0.4 mg/kg/day IV × 2 weeks → taper over 6 weeks total6 weeks total (reduces mortality)
Pyridoxine (B6)25–50mg PO daily with isoniazidThroughout treatment

Dexamethasone reduces mortality in TB meningitis (NEJM 2004 RCT) — start with antibiotics, regardless of HIV status.

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • CSF PCR negative but clinical/epidemiologic presentation consistent → treat empirically; PCR sensitivity ~80%; do not withhold treatment
  • Not improving at 2–4 weeks → drug-resistant TB? Xpert MTb/RIF detects rifampin resistance; consult ID; IRIS?
  • Worsening after initial improvement → IRIS (especially if HIV + ART initiation); hydrocephalus developing; new vascular event (TB vasculitis)
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Hydrocephalus (communicating or obstructive) → neurosurgery for VP shunt or external ventricular drain; may be emergent
  • Cerebral herniation → emergent neurosurgery; ICP management; osmotic therapy
  • Respiratory failure → ICU; mechanical ventilation; continue RIPE
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Airborne isolation until AFB smear negative × 3 (or clinical improvement + <5% smear positivity)
  • Transition plan: confirm drug susceptibility at 6–8 weeks; MDR-TB requires specialist management
  • ART in HIV/TB meningitis: defer 4–8 weeks after starting TB treatment to reduce IRIS risk (especially if CD4 <50)

Who Receives Prophylaxis

Close contacts of confirmed/probable Neisseria meningitidis — chemoprophylaxis within 24h of identification:

  • Household contacts (shared sleeping/eating space in 7 days before illness)
  • Childcare/daycare contacts
  • Persons with direct contact with oral secretions (kissing, mouth-to-mouth resuscitation, intubation without PPE)
  • NOT indicated for: casual contacts, medical staff with standard PPE, same classroom without direct secretion contact

Prophylaxis Regimens

AgentDoseNotes
Ciprofloxacin (preferred)500 mg PO ×1First-line in most US guidelines; not in pregnancy (use rifampin or ceftriaxone)
Rifampin600 mg PO q12h × 4 doses (adults); 5 mg/kg q12h ×4 doses (<1 month); 10 mg/kg q12h ×4 doses (>1 month)Not in pregnancy; drug interactions; orange secretions
Ceftriaxone250 mg IM ×1 (adults); 125 mg IM ×1 (<15 years)Preferred in pregnancy; also eliminates nasopharyngeal carriage

Public health reporting: Notify local/state health department within 24h. Targeted meningococcal vaccination (MenACWY or MenB) may be recommended for outbreak control.

  • Head of bed elevated 30°; avoid hypotension (target MAP ≥80 mmHg)
  • Avoid hypercapnia — target PaCO₂ 35–40 mmHg; brief hyperventilation (PaCO₂ 30–35) can be used as bridge in herniation
  • ICP monitoring: Reserved for comatose patients, herniation signs, progressive deterioration
  • Osmotherapy: Mannitol 0.25–1 g/kg IV over 20 min OR 3% NaCl for elevated ICP with herniation; maintain serum osmolarity <320 (mannitol) or Na 145–155 (hypertonic saline)
  • Dexamethasone 0.15 mg/kg IV q6h × 4 days — give BEFORE or WITH first antibiotic dose; primarily benefits S. pneumoniae meningitis; reduces hearing loss and neurologic complications
  • Seizure prophylaxis: Not routinely recommended; treat active seizures with lorazepam then levetiracetam/phenytoin; monitor with EEG if altered mentation
  • Neurosurgery consultation: Hydrocephalus (LP or EVD); abscess or empyema requiring drainage; cerebral herniation

Recognition

  • Culex mosquito; peak July–October; Eastern/Central US (WNV fever); Mississippi Valley, Plains states (neuroinvasive)
  • West Nile fever (80%): Fever, headache, myalgia, rash; self-limited 3–6 days
  • Neuroinvasive disease (1%): Meningitis (most common), encephalitis (confusion, tremors, myoclonus), or acute flaccid myelitis/paralysis (polio-like — worst prognosis)
  • High risk for neuroinvasive: age >60, immunocompromised, DM, hypertension, renal disease
  • Acute flaccid paralysis: asymmetric limb weakness, areflexia; MRI shows anterior horn cell signal → permanent deficit in majority

Workup

  • WNV IgM (CSF): Highly specific for neuroinvasive WNV; detectable by day 3–8 of neuroinvasive illness
  • WNV IgM (serum): Cross-reactive with other flaviviruses; confirm with PRNT at CDC/state lab if needed
  • WNV PCR (serum/CSF): Less sensitive than serology in neuroinvasive disease; viremia brief
  • MRI brain/spine: Anterior horn cell T2 signal in myelitis/AFP; basal ganglia, thalamus, brainstem in encephalitis
  • CSF: Lymphocytic pleocytosis, elevated protein
  • EMG/NCS: Lower motor neuron pattern in AFP

Empiric Therapy

No specific antiviral treatment. Supportive care only.

SyndromeManagement
WNV feverSupportive: hydration, acetaminophen; no hospitalization usually required
WNV meningitisSupportive: analgesia, antiemetics, hydration; hospitalize if severe
WNV encephalitisICU-level supportive care; seizure management; ICP monitoring if severe cerebral edema
Acute flaccid paralysisVentilatory support if respiratory muscles involved; physical/occupational therapy; IVIG (not proven but used)
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Serology negative but clinical neuroinvasive syndrome in endemic/seasonal context → repeat IgM 7–10 days later; early presentation may be seronegative
  • AFP pattern on EMG → distinguish from GBS (ascending, sensory involvement, albumino-cytologic dissociation) vs WNV AFP (asymmetric, pure motor, anterior horn)
  • Worsening despite supportive care → reassess for bacterial superinfection, SIADH (hyponatremia), seizure activity
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Respiratory failure from AFP or severe encephalitis → mechanical ventilation; ICU
  • Status epilepticus → standard antiepileptic management; ICU
  • Severe cerebral edema → ICP monitoring; osmotic therapy; neurology/neurosurgery
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • WNV fever: self-limited; discharge when clinically improved + tolerating PO
  • Neuroinvasive: extended rehabilitation; AFP recovery poor (most have permanent deficits); neurology follow-up
  • No antiviral to guide — duration based on clinical trajectory

Recognition

  • Alphavirus; Culiseta melanura mosquito (freshwater swamp birds → humans via bridge vectors); Atlantic/Gulf coast, Midwest US; late summer/early fall
  • Rapid fulminant encephalitis: high fever, severe headache → rapid deterioration to coma; seizures in majority
  • Highest mortality of US arboviruses: 30–70% case fatality; 30–35% of survivors with severe neurologic sequelae
  • Rare but severe — typically <10 cases/year in US; spiked 2019 (38 cases)
  • Risk: any age; outdoor exposure in endemic areas; no vaccine available

Workup

  • EEE IgM/IgG serology (serum + CSF): Diagnostic; IgM detectable within first week
  • EEE PCR (CSF/serum): Best early in illness during viremia
  • MRI brain: Basal ganglia, thalamus, brainstem signal changes; diffuse or focal cortical involvement
  • CSF: Leukocytosis (can be neutrophilic early, then lymphocytic), elevated protein
  • EEG: Diffuse slowing, periodic complexes
  • Report to state health department — notifiable disease

Empiric Therapy

No specific antiviral treatment. ICU-level supportive care.

InterventionNotes
Seizure controlLevetiracetam, valproate, benzodiazepines; high seizure burden
ICP managementHead of bed 30°, osmotic therapy (mannitol/hypertonic saline), avoid hyperthermia
Mechanical ventilationMost cases require intubation from rapid deterioration
IVIG / corticosteroidsUsed in some centers without proven benefit; individualize
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Rapid deterioration despite ICU supportive care → reassess ICP management; consider repeat MRI for expanding lesions
  • Serology negative early → repeat at 7–14 days; early illness may be seronegative
  • Consider HSV encephalitis simultaneously until excluded by PCR — acyclovir empirically while awaiting results
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Coma or herniation → neurosurgery consultation; ICP monitoring; consider barbiturate coma if refractory ICP
  • Respiratory failure → early intubation; most severe EEE cases require mechanical ventilation
  • Multiorgan failure (rare) → ICU full supportive care
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Survival: most deaths within first week; survivors require prolonged ICU care + extensive rehabilitation
  • Neurologic deficits: 30–35% of survivors have severe long-term disability (seizures, cognitive impairment, motor deficits)
  • Arboviral disease reporting: notify state health department within 24h

Recognition

  • Flavivirus; Culex mosquito; urban/suburban eastern and central US; summer/fall peaks
  • Milder than EEE; mortality 2–30% (higher in elderly)
  • Spectrum: asymptomatic/mild febrile illness (majority) → aseptic meningitis → encephalitis
  • Elderly patients: more severe neurological involvement; hyponatremia (SIADH) common
  • Tremor, ataxia characteristic (vs EEE which causes rapid deep coma)

Workup

  • SLE IgM serology (serum + CSF): Diagnostic; cross-reacts with WNV and other flaviviruses — confirm with PRNT
  • CSF: Lymphocytic pleocytosis, elevated protein, normal glucose
  • MRI brain: Substantia nigra T2 signal (characteristic when present); diffuse changes
  • CBC: Leukocytosis; hyponatremia from SIADH

Empiric Therapy

No specific antiviral. Supportive care.

  • Seizure management: levetiracetam
  • SIADH: fluid restriction ± hypertonic saline for severe hyponatremia
  • ICU for encephalitis with altered mental status
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Hyponatremia + encephalitis → always check for SIADH; correct slowly to avoid osmotic demyelination
  • Flavivirus serology cross-reactivity → cannot distinguish SLE from WNV by IgM alone; PRNT required; management is same (supportive)
  • Not improving → reassess for bacterial meningitis co-infection; bacterial superinfection
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Respiratory failure or severe encephalitis → ICU; mechanical ventilation
  • Refractory seizures → ICU; continuous EEG monitoring
  • Severe hyponatremia (<120 mEq/L) → ICU; hypertonic saline with neurology/nephrology
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Most cases self-limited with supportive care; elderly at highest risk for persistent deficits
  • SIADH resolution → hyponatremia corrects with fluid restriction
  • Neurologic recovery: weeks to months; follow-up with neurology for persistent deficits

Recognition

  • Flavivirus; Culex mosquito; Asia-Pacific; traveler to endemic areas (South/Southeast Asia, Far East, Pacific Islands)
  • Most infections asymptomatic; encephalitis in 1 in 250 infections; mortality 20–30%, severe sequelae in 30–50% survivors
  • Acute encephalitis with fever, altered mental status, seizures; Parkinsonian features (mask-like face, cogwheel rigidity, tremor) characteristic
  • Thalamic lesions on MRI relatively specific
  • Vaccine available (Ixiaro) — recommended for travelers to endemic areas >1 month or with rural/outdoor exposure

Workup

  • JEV IgM (serum + CSF): Diagnostic; detectable within 4 days of illness
  • PCR: Low sensitivity in clinical illness
  • MRI brain: Bilateral thalamic lesions (T2/FLAIR) — characteristic; also basal ganglia, brainstem, cortex
  • CSF: Lymphocytic pleocytosis
  • Report to CDC Arboviral Diseases Branch

Empiric Therapy

No specific antiviral. ICU supportive care.

  • Seizure management: high seizure burden; aggressive antiepileptic treatment
  • ICP management if cerebral edema
  • Corticosteroids: not proven beneficial; generally not recommended
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Travel to Asia + encephalitis + thalamic lesions → JEV until proven otherwise; serology diagnostic
  • Cross-reactive flavivirus serology → confirm with PRNT if WNV or Dengue also possible
  • Parkinsonian features may persist → long-term neurology follow-up
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Respiratory failure → mechanical ventilation; ICU
  • Status epilepticus → ICU; continuous EEG monitoring
  • Cerebral edema → ICP management; neurosurgery if herniation risk
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • No antiviral to guide duration
  • Neurological rehabilitation for survivors with persistent deficits
  • Vaccination counseling for future travel to endemic areas

Recognition

  • Lyssavirus; bite from infected mammal (dog most common globally; bat, raccoon, skunk, fox in US); rare — ~2 cases/year in US
  • Incubation: 1–3 months (range days to years); virus travels along peripheral nerves centrally
  • Prodrome: Fever, headache, pain/paresthesia at bite site (pathognomonic)
  • Encephalitic form (furious, 80%): Hydrophobia, aerophobia, autonomic instability, agitation, hallucinations → coma
  • Paralytic form (dumb, 20%): Progressive ascending paralysis mimicking GBS
  • Virtually 100% fatal once neurologic symptoms develop; <20 documented survivors worldwide

Workup

  • Nuchal skin biopsy (nape of neck): Direct fluorescent antibody (DFA) for rabies antigen in nerve fibers around hair follicles — most practical ante-mortem test
  • CSF: PCR for rabies RNA; may be normal
  • Saliva: RT-PCR for rabies virus
  • Serum/CSF neutralizing antibodies: In unvaccinated patient = diagnostic
  • Brain biopsy post-mortem: Negri bodies (pathognomonic)
  • Contact public health immediately — potential exposure investigation

Empiric Therapy

No proven treatment once symptoms develop. Milwaukee Protocol (induced coma + antivirals) has occasional survivors but not reproducibly effective.

  • ICU palliative/comfort care in most cases
  • Milwaukee Protocol (investigational): ketamine-induced coma + amantadine + ribavirin; contact CDC for guidance
  • Minimize stimulation (hydrophobia, aerophobia triggered by external stimuli)

Post-Exposure Prophylaxis (PEP) — if not yet symptomatic

StatusRegimen
UnvaccinatedHRIG 20 IU/kg (infiltrate at wound, remainder IM) + Rabies vaccine days 0, 3, 7, 14
Previously vaccinatedRabies vaccine days 0 and 3 only (no HRIG)
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Any animal bite + exposure risk + prodromal paresthesias at bite site → PEP immediately; do not wait for symptoms
  • Bat exposure even without visible bite → PEP (bat bites may be imperceptible)
  • Ascending paralysis → exclude GBS (CSF albumino-cytologic dissociation, nerve conduction studies); rabies paralytic form if CSF normal
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Once neurologic symptoms present → ICU for supportive care; contact CDC Poxvirus and Rabies Branch (404-639-1050)
  • Suspect human-to-human transmission risk (organ donation from undiagnosed case) → notify public health urgently
  • PEP failure (symptoms despite prophylaxis) → extremely rare; contact CDC
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • PEP is highly effective when started promptly — >99% effective if given before symptom onset
  • Once symptomatic: terminal disease; goals-of-care discussion early; palliative care involvement
  • Wound care: thorough washing with soap/water for 15 min reduces viral load significantly

Recognition

  • HSV-1: Most common cause of sporadic viral encephalitis in adults; year-round, any age
  • Fever + headache + altered mental status + behavioral change; focal deficits (aphasia, hemiparesis); seizures (often temporal lobe)
  • Classic: temporal lobe involvement — olfactory hallucinations, memory disturbance, personality change
  • CSF pleocytosis often present but can be normal early; RBCs may suggest hemorrhagic necrosis
  • Start acyclovir empirically — do NOT wait for PCR result; untreated mortality 70%; survivors have severe neurologic sequelae

Workup

  • MRI brain with gadolinium (first): T2/FLAIR hyperintensity in temporal lobe, insula, cingulate gyrus — highly suggestive; normal MRI does not exclude early HSV
  • LP: CSF PCR for HSV-1/2 (sensitivity 96%, specificity 99%); may be false-negative in first 72h — repeat if initial negative + high suspicion
  • EEG: Temporal lobe periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges (PLEDs) — supportive, not diagnostic
  • CBC, CMP, coagulation studies
  • Send CSF also for VZV PCR, CMV PCR, enterovirus, Cryptococcus CrAg, autoimmune encephalitis panel if no pathogen found

Empiric Therapy

RegimenDoseDuration
Acyclovir IV (start IMMEDIATELY)10 mg/kg IV q8h (adjust for renal function)14–21 days; 21 days for confirmed HSV encephalitis
After IV course: step-downValacyclovir 1g PO TID × 90 days (reduces long-term neurologic complications — HELO trial)3 months oral suppression

Acyclovir nephrotoxicity: Ensure adequate hydration (IV fluids); monitor creatinine; dose-adjust for eGFR <50.

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • HSV PCR negative at 72h → repeat LP/PCR at 1 week; early sampling (day 1) false-negative in 5–10%; do not stop acyclovir based on single negative early PCR
  • No temporal lobe changes on MRI → consider anti-NMDAR encephalitis and other autoimmune etiologies; send autoimmune encephalitis CSF panel
  • Not improving at 1 week → confirm adequate acyclovir dosing (check levels); consider acyclovir resistance (foscarnet) in immunocompromised
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Status epilepticus → ICU; antiepileptics (levetiracetam, valproate); anesthesia if refractory
  • Herniation/severe cerebral edema → neurosurgery; ICP monitoring; osmotic therapy
  • Respiratory failure from severe encephalitis → mechanical ventilation; ICU
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Clinical improvement + HSV PCR positive confirming diagnosis → complete 21 days IV
  • Tolerating PO, stable neurologically → oral valacyclovir 1g TID × 90 days after IV course
  • Seizure prophylaxis: continue antiepileptics for minimum 1 year post-encephalitis; neurology follow-up

Overview

  • CNS infection with larval stage (cysticercus) of Taenia solium (pork tapeworm) — most common parasitic cause of epilepsy worldwide
  • Transmission: Fecal-oral ingestion of T. solium eggs (from infected human feces — not pork); autoinfection possible
  • Geography: Latin America, sub-Saharan Africa, South/Southeast Asia; increasing in US due to immigration
  • Key concept: Person eats eggs (from contaminated feces) → larvae migrate to CNS; eating undercooked pork causes intestinal tapeworm (taeniasis) not NCC

Clinical Presentations

FormFeaturesPrognosis
Parenchymal (most common)Seizures (new-onset epilepsy in adult from endemic area = NCC until proven otherwise); headache; may have focal deficitsGood with treatment
Subarachnoid / racemoseLarge grape-like cysts in basal cisterns; hydrocephalus; meningitis; CN palsies; chronic meningitisSerious; high morbidity
VentricularObstructive hydrocephalus; cyst in 4th ventricle (Bruns' syndrome — positional headache)Requires surgical intervention
SpinalMyelopathy, radiculopathy — rareVariable

Diagnosis

  • MRI brain: Most sensitive — cysts at various stages (vesicular, colloidal, granular, calcified); perilesional edema; scolex (dot within cyst = "hole with dot" sign — pathognomonic)
  • CT: Better for calcified lesions; shows ring-enhancing or calcified lesions
  • Serology (EITB): Enzyme-linked immunotransfer blot — specific (>99%); sensitivity lower with single calcified lesion; CSF serology less useful
  • Del Brutto diagnostic criteria: Absolute (scolex on imaging), major, minor, epidemiologic criteria used for diagnosis

Treatment

ScenarioManagement
Single viable cyst (parenchymal)Albendazole 400mg PO BID × 8–30 days + Dexamethasone (to reduce inflammatory response when cyst dies); antiepileptic for seizures
Multiple viable cystsAlbendazole + Praziquantel combination (superior to either alone); prolonged course; dexamethasone
Calcified lesions onlyNo antiparasitic treatment; seizure management only
Subarachnoid / racemoseProlonged albendazole (months); high-dose dexamethasone; methotrexate for steroid-sparing; neurosurgery for hydrocephalus
VentricularEndoscopic cyst removal (preferred) + albendazole

Steroids are mandatory when starting antiparasitic therapy — dying cysts trigger intense inflammatory response that can worsen symptoms acutely. Do not start albendazole without steroid cover.

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Seizure in immigrant from Latin America/SE Asia/Sub-Saharan Africa + ring-enhancing lesion → NCC until proven otherwise; MRI with gadolinium essential
  • Not responding to albendazole → confirm viable cysts (enhancing = viable); calcified cysts do not respond to antiparasitics
  • Worsening edema/symptoms after starting antiparasitic → HAIE (host anti-inflammatory encephalitis); steroids mandatory before or with antiparasitic therapy
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Mass effect with herniation risk → neurosurgery consultation; steroids + osmotic therapy first
  • Intraventricular cysts (obstructive hydrocephalus) → neurosurgery for VP shunt or endoscopic removal; antiparasitics risky without CSF diversion
  • Status epilepticus → ICU; antiepileptics; defer antiparasitic treatment until stable
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Enhancing lesions resolve on MRI → treatment course complete; calcified cysts are dead parasites and do not require continued therapy
  • Seizure-free on antiepileptics × 2 years → neurology for AED taper discussion
  • Single cyst, good response → albendazole 7–14 days; multiple viable cysts → 28–30 days
🫁 Respiratory
● Time-critical

🚨 Airway emergency in severe cases. Do not perform laryngoscopy or examine oropharynx in agitated adult or any child with suspected epiglottitis without airway equipment immediately available.

Presentation

  • "Four D's": Dysphagia, Dysphonia (muffled/hot potato voice), Drooling, Distress
  • Severe sore throat (often disproportionate to pharyngeal exam), odynophagia, high fever
  • Tripod/sniffing position: Patient leaning forward with neck extended — characteristic; do not force supine
  • Stridor is a late and ominous finding in adults
  • Adults increasingly affected (Hib vaccine reduced childhood disease)

Diagnosis

  • Lateral neck X-ray: "Thumbprint sign" (enlarged epiglottis) — useful but not sensitive; do not delay airway for imaging if unstable
  • Flexible fiberoptic laryngoscopy: Definitive diagnosis in cooperative, stable patients; performed by ENT or anesthesia
  • CT neck: Alternative in stable patients; shows epiglottis swelling and can identify abscess
  • Blood/throat cultures: Positive in ~25%; H. influenzae, S. aureus, Streptococci, GAS

Microbiology

  • Adults: H. influenzae (non-typeable), S. aureus (MRSA), S. pyogenes, S. pneumoniae, polymicrobial
  • Children (unvaccinated): H. influenzae type b; Hib vaccination has made this rare in vaccinated populations

Management

StepAction
AirwayKeep patient calm and sitting upright; prepare for intubation (awake fiberoptic preferred) or surgical airway; ENT + anesthesia at bedside
AntibioticsCeftriaxone 2g IV q24h or Ampicillin-sulbactam 3g IV q6h; add vancomycin if MRSA suspected
SteroidsDexamethasone 0.15 mg/kg IV — may reduce airway edema; no RCT evidence but widely used
Duration7–10 days total; transition to oral (amoxicillin-clavulanate) when tolerating PO
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Adult with sore throat + dysphagia disproportionate to visible pharyngeal findings → lateral neck X-ray or flexible nasopharyngoscopy; do NOT dismiss as pharyngitis
  • Not improving at 24–48h on antibiotics → CT neck to exclude peritonsillar or deep neck space abscess, parapharyngeal extension
  • Stridor developing → airway more compromised than initially assessed; reassess urgently
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Stridor, drooling, inability to manage secretions, tripod positioning → airway management immediately; anesthesia + ENT at bedside
  • Rapidly deteriorating → do NOT attempt direct laryngoscopy without controlled airway team present; fiberoptic intubation preferred
  • Peritonsillar or deep neck abscess identified → ENT consultation for drainage
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Airway secure + improving on IV antibiotics → oral step-down when tolerating swallowing and afebrile × 48h
  • Total duration: 7–10 days; amoxicillin-clavulanate PO to complete
  • H. influenzae type b confirmed → close contacts: rifampin prophylaxis
○ Condition-specific

Recognition

  • Fever, cough (productive or dry), dyspnea, pleuritis; new infiltrate on CXR
  • Severe CAP criteria: 1 major (septic shock, mechanical ventilation) OR 3+ minor (RR ≥30, PaO2/FiO2 <250, multilobar, confusion, BUN ≥20, WBC <4K, PLT <100K, temp <36°C, hypotension requiring fluids)
  • Common organisms: S. pneumoniae, atypicals (Legionella, Mycoplasma, Chlamydophila), H. influenzae, S. aureus (post-influenza), GNR (healthcare-associated)
  • ATS 2025 update: IDSA did not endorse 2 recommendations (empiric antibiotics for viral CAP in outpatients/non-severe inpatients) — use clinical judgment

Workup

  • CXR (or CT if uncertain): Confirm infiltrate; lobar vs interstitial vs multilobar
  • Blood cultures × 2: Before antibiotics if hospitalized; positive in ~6% — guides therapy
  • Sputum Gram stain + culture: Send for all hospitalized; before antibiotics if possible
  • Urine Legionella antigen: All severe CAP, ICU admissions, travel exposure
  • Urine Pneumococcal antigen: Severe CAP
  • MRSA nasal swab: If MRSA risk (prior MRSA, recent hospitalization, severe necrotizing pneumonia)
  • Respiratory viral panel (COVID, flu, RSV) — guides stewardship
  • Procalcitonin: guides duration; not recommended for initiation decisions

Empiric Therapy

SettingRegimenNotes
Non-severe inpatient (no ICU)β-lactam (Ceftriaxone 1–2g IV q24h or Ampicillin-sulbactam) + Azithromycin 500mg IV/PO dailyOR Respiratory fluoroquinolone monotherapy (Levofloxacin 750mg or Moxifloxacin 400mg)
Severe CAP (ICU)β-lactam + Azithromycin OR β-lactam + FluoroquinoloneAdd Vancomycin or Linezolid if MRSA risk; add anti-Pseudomonal coverage if P. aeruginosa risk
Atypical CAP suspectedFluoroquinolone monotherapy OR β-lactam + AzithromycinLegionella urine antigen guides; doxycycline alternative to azithromycin
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • No response at 48–72h → review culture data; consider atypical (Legionella urine antigen sent?), fungal (endemic exposure — Histo, Cocci), PCP (immunocompromised?), non-infectious (PE, malignancy, COP)
  • MRSA nasal swab negative → 95% NPV; de-escalate MRSA coverage
  • Positive respiratory viral panel in non-severe CAP → carefully weigh antibiotics per clinical trajectory (see ATS 2025 controversy)
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Respiratory failure (new or worsening hypoxemia) → ICU, reassess for ARDS, consider intubation criteria
  • Cavitary lesion developing → think S. aureus (MRSA), Klebsiella, anaerobes/aspiration, fungal, TB
  • Empyema developing → chest tube drainage, thoracic surgery consultation
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Afebrile × 48–72h, improving oxygenation, tolerating PO → oral step-down; no benefit to completing IV course
  • Total duration: 5 days for most non-severe CAP; up to 7 days severe; ATS 2025 suggests 3–5 days for non-severe if stable + inflammatory markers normalizing
  • Procalcitonin-guided duration: stop when <0.25 or declined ≥80% from peak
SettingRegimenDuration
Outpatient (no comorbidities)Amoxicillin 1 g PO TID or doxycycline 100 mg BID or azithromycin (if low pneumococcal resistance)5 days
Outpatient (comorbidities: DM, CKD, CHF, immunocompromise)Amoxicillin-clavulanate 875/125 mg BID + macrolide or respiratory fluoroquinolone (levofloxacin 750 mg daily or moxifloxacin 400 mg daily)5 days
Inpatient non-ICUβ-lactam (ceftriaxone 2 g IV q24h or ampicillin-sulbactam 3 g IV q6h) + macrolide (azithromycin 500 mg IV/PO daily) OR respiratory fluoroquinolone monotherapy5 days (if good response)
ICU (no Pseudomonas risk)β-lactam + azithromycin or β-lactam + respiratory FQ5–7 days
ICU + Pseudomonas riskAnti-pseudomonal β-lactam (pip-tazo, cefepime, or meropenem) + cipro or levofloxacin or + aminoglycoside + azithromycin7 days
+ MRSA risk (cavitary lesion, prior MRSA, IV drug use, influenza with PNA)Add vancomycin or linezolid7–14 days depending on organism

Procalcitonin: Supports antibiotic initiation if >0.25 mcg/L; serial values (q48h) can guide de-escalation. PCT alone should not withhold antibiotics in clinically convincing CAP.

Steroids: Not routinely recommended for CAP. Consider in severe CAP with refractory septic shock or if adrenal insufficiency suspected.

Recognition

  • HAP: Pneumonia ≥48h after hospital admission, not incubating at admission
  • VAP: Pneumonia ≥48h after endotracheal intubation
  • Risk organisms: S. aureus (MRSA), Pseudomonas, Klebsiella, Acinetobacter, Enterobacter, Stenotrophomonas
  • Early onset (<5 days) vs late onset (≥5 days): late onset has higher MDR risk
  • Clinical: new or worsening infiltrate + 2 of: fever, leukocytosis/penia, purulent secretions, worsening oxygenation
  • CPIS score (Clinical Pulmonary Infection Score) ≥6 supports diagnosis

Workup

  • Tracheal aspirate (VAP) or sputum (HAP): Gram stain + quantitative culture; semi-quantitative acceptable; obtain before antibiotics
  • BAL or protected specimen brush: More specific; threshold 10³–10⁴ CFU/mL
  • Blood cultures × 2
  • MRSA nasal swab: High NPV (≥95%) — negative swab allows withholding MRSA coverage
  • CXR/CT chest
  • Assess MDR risk: prior IV antibiotics ≤90 days, hospitalization ≥5 days, septic shock, ARDS, renal replacement therapy

Empiric Therapy

Coverage neededRegimen
MRSA coverage indicated (prior MRSA, MRSA nares+, ICU >20% MRSA prevalence, septic shock)Vancomycin 15–20 mg/kg IV q8–12h (AUC-guided) OR Linezolid 600mg IV/PO q12h
Anti-Pseudomonal β-lactam (all HAP/VAP)Piperacillin-tazobactam 4.5g IV q6h OR Cefepime 2g IV q8h OR Meropenem 1–2g IV q8h
MDR risk → double GNR coverageAdd aminoglycoside (amikacin 15–20 mg/kg q24h) OR ciprofloxacin
Acinetobacter suspectedMeropenem + Ampicillin-sulbactam (sulbactam activity)

Source Control

  • VAP: optimize secretion drainage, early mobilization, HOB ≥30°, ventilator bundle maintenance
  • Consider extubation readiness — continued ventilation increases VAP risk
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • MRSA nasal swab negative + no other MRSA risk factors → withhold vancomycin/linezolid (de-escalation)
  • Culture grows unusual organism (Aspergillus, Nocardia, fungi) → immunocompromised? Prolonged steroid use?
  • Not improving at 48–72h → reassess cultures, consider resistant organism, non-infectious diagnosis
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Septic shock from VAP → ICU-level management, vasopressors, source control optimization
  • ARDS developing → lung-protective ventilation (6 mL/kg IBW, PEEP optimization)
  • Empyema complicating HAP → chest tube drainage, thoracic surgery consultation
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • MRSA coverage: if MRSA nasal swab negative + cultures growing MSSA → discontinue vancomycin
  • Culture-directed therapy: narrow to most targeted agent based on BAL/tracheal aspirate cultures
  • Duration: 7 days for most HAP/VAP (including Pseudomonas and Acinetobacter if responding); longer only if bacteremia or slow response

Definitions

  • HAP: Pneumonia developing ≥48h after hospital admission, not incubating at admission
  • VAP: Pneumonia developing >48–72h after endotracheal intubation

Risk Stratification for MDR Coverage

IDSA/ATS 2016: Empiric MRSA and anti-pseudomonal coverage for HAP if:

  • Prior IV antibiotic use in last 90 days
  • MRSA prevalence >20% in hospital/unit OR unknown prevalence
  • Septic shock at time of diagnosis
  • Structural lung disease (bronchiectasis, cystic fibrosis)
  • Known prior MDR colonization/infection

Empiric Regimens

SettingRegimen
HAP (no MDR risk, not severe)Piperacillin-tazobactam 4.5 g IV q6h OR cefepime 2 g IV q8h OR levofloxacin 750 mg IV q24h
HAP/VAP (MDR risk or ICU)Anti-pseudomonal β-lactam (pip-tazo, cefepime, or meropenem) + vancomycin or linezolid (MRSA coverage)
VAP + Pseudomonas risk (chronic lung disease, immunosuppression, prior Pseudomonas)Two anti-pseudomonal agents from different classes (β-lactam + FQ or aminoglycoside); de-escalate once cultures available

Duration & De-escalation

  • 7 days for most HAP/VAP with good clinical response — shorter courses non-inferior
  • De-escalate based on day 2–3 culture results and clinical trajectory (CPIS score, SOFA trend)
  • Use nasal MRSA PCR swab to guide vancomycin discontinuation — NPV >96% for MRSA pneumonia
  • Procalcitonin-guided de-escalation can reduce antibiotic exposure

Cross-link: 📚 IMCRG: Pneumonia

Testing Approach

Test TypeSensitivityUse When
Rapid influenza diagnostic test (RIDT antigen)50–70% (lower in adults, higher in children)POC, quick result; negative does not rule out — consider molecular test
Rapid molecular assay (e.g., ID NOW)>90%Preferred POC test if available; 15–30 min result
RT-PCR (reference standard)>95%Highest sensitivity; ICU patients, immunocompromised, outbreak investigation; 1–8h result
Respiratory multiplex panelComparable to individual RT-PCRSimultaneous detection of influenza A/B, RSV, COVID-19, other respiratory viruses

Who to Test

  • Hospitalized patients with acute respiratory illness during influenza season
  • Patients in whom influenza diagnosis will influence treatment, infection control, or public health decisions
  • Immunocompromised patients (can shed for weeks; may have atypical presentation)
  • Cluster of acute respiratory illness — suspect outbreak

Treat Without Delay (regardless of illness duration)

  • All hospitalized patients with confirmed or suspected influenza
  • High-risk outpatients (regardless of symptom duration): age ≥65, pregnancy, BMI ≥40, chronic cardiopulmonary/metabolic/renal/hepatic/neurologic disease, immunocompromise, children <2 years, residents of LTCF

Antivirals

DrugRegimenCoverage / Notes
Oseltamivir (Tamiflu)75 mg PO BID × 5 days (hospitalized or high-risk); extend to 10 days in immunocompromisedInfluenza A and B; neuraminidase inhibitor; adjust for CrCl <30 (30 mg BID); can give via NG tube (suspension or capsule dissolved)
Zanamivir (Relenza)10 mg (2 inhalations) BID × 5 daysInhaled; avoid in bronchospasm/asthma/COPD; useful if oseltamivir-resistant (H275Y mutation — H1N1)
Peramivir (Rapivab)600 mg IV × 1 doseIV option for those unable to take PO; single dose for uncomplicated influenza
Baloxavir marboxil (Xofluza)40 mg PO × 1 (≤80 kg) or 80 mg PO × 1 (>80 kg)Cap-dependent endonuclease inhibitor; active against influenza A/B; single dose; limited data in hospitalized/high-risk; emergence of resistance with single dose in some studies

Duration Considerations

  • Immunocompromised: extend oseltamivir to 10 days; serial viral loads may guide duration
  • Mechanically ventilated: IV oseltamivir solution via NGT (standard dose) or peramivir; inhaled agents impractical
  • Oseltamivir resistance (H275Y mutation in H1N1 pdm09): Use zanamivir, peramivir, or baloxavir; consult ID
  • Primary influenza pneumonia: Bilateral interstitial infiltrates; ARDS pattern; high mortality in certain populations (pregnant, obese, elderly)
  • Secondary bacterial pneumonia (most common cause of death): S. aureus (including MRSA — cavitary, necrotizing), S. pneumoniae, S. pyogenes; add antibiotics targeting MRSA (vancomycin or linezolid) + pneumococcal coverage
  • Myocarditis: New chest pain/troponin rise; ECG changes; echo for wall motion; manage as myocarditis
  • Encephalopathy/Encephalitis: Particularly in children; rare but recognized; supportive care
  • Rhabdomyolysis: Particularly influenza B; CK elevation, myalgia, AKI; aggressive IV hydration
  • ARDSnet ventilation if ARDS; prone positioning; consider early ECMO referral at centers of excellence
IndicationRegimenDuration
Post-exposure prophylaxis (PEP) — household close contact with confirmed influenza, high-risk individualOseltamivir 75 mg PO daily10 days from last exposure (start within 48h)
Institutional outbreak control (LTCF, hospital ward)Oseltamivir 75 mg PO daily for all residents/staff regardless of vaccination statusMinimum 2 weeks and until ≥1 week after last case
Pre-exposure (severely immunocompromised during high influenza activity)Oseltamivir 75 mg PO dailyDuration of influenza season; consult ID

Note: Annual influenza vaccine remains primary prevention — chemoprophylaxis does NOT substitute for vaccination. Vaccine efficacy 40–60% in typical year; still recommended even in low-efficacy seasons (reduces severity).

SeverityCriteria
Asymptomatic/Pre-symptomaticPositive SARS-CoV-2 test, no symptoms
MildSymptoms (fever, cough, sore throat, malaise) without dyspnea, hypoxia, or abnormal CXR
ModerateEvidence of lower respiratory disease by imaging or SpO₂ ≥94% on room air
SevereSpO₂ <94% on room air, RR >30, PaO₂/FiO₂ <300, lung infiltrates >50%
CriticalARDS, septic shock, multi-organ failure

Eligibility for Treatment: Mild-Moderate COVID-19 with High Risk for Progression

High-risk factors: Age ≥65, obesity, immunocompromise, DM, CKD, cardiovascular disease, COPD, pregnancy

DrugRegimenKey Considerations
Nirmatrelvir/ritonavir (Paxlovid)300/100 mg PO BID × 5 days (start within 5 days of symptom onset)Reduces hospitalization/death by ~85% in unvaccinated high-risk. Extensive drug interactions via CYP3A4 (ritonavir). Contraindicated with many drugs — check interactions carefully. Rebound phenomenon reported. Dose reduce for CrCl 30–59 (150/100 mg BID); contraindicated if CrCl <30.
Remdesivir (Veklury)200 mg IV day 1, then 100 mg IV daily × 2 days (3-day outpatient course)Reduces hospitalization by 87% in high-risk. IV only — requires infusion center. Alternative when Paxlovid not feasible.
Molnupiravir (Lagevrio)800 mg PO BID × 5 daysModest efficacy (~30% reduction); last-line. Contraindicated in pregnancy (teratogenic in animal studies). Not for <18 years.

Nirmatrelvir/Ritonavir Critical Drug Interactions

CategoryExamplesAction
ContraindicatedRifampin, carbamazepine, phenytoin, phenobarbital, St. John's wort, ergot derivatives, pimozide, amiodarone, flecainideDo NOT co-administer — significant interaction risk
Requires dose adjustment or monitoringStatins (hold simvastatin/lovastatin; reduce atorvastatin dose), tacrolimus, cyclosporine, warfarin (monitor INR), colchicine, ranolazine, rivaroxaban, apixabanHold, reduce dose, or increase monitoring
Hormonal contraceptivesCombined oral contraceptivesUse barrier method during treatment + 7 days after
DrugIndicationRegimen
RemdesivirHospitalized, requiring supplemental O₂ (not ventilated or ECMO)200 mg IV day 1, then 100 mg IV q24h × 4 days (5 days total)
DexamethasoneRequiring supplemental O₂ or mechanical ventilation (RECOVERY trial)6 mg IV/PO daily × 10 days. Do NOT use if not requiring O₂ — may worsen outcomes in early disease.
Baricitinib (JAK inhibitor)Severe/critical + elevated inflammatory markers (IL-6 pathway); preferred over tocilizumab when both available4 mg PO/NG daily × 14 days or until discharge
Tocilizumab (IL-6 inhibitor)Severe/critical COVID-19 requiring ICU-level care + rapidly escalating O₂ + CRP ≥75 mg/L8 mg/kg IV ×1 (max 800 mg); second dose at 12–24h if insufficient response

RECOVERY Trial Key Findings (landmark 2020)

  • Dexamethasone 6 mg × 10 days: NNT 8 for ventilated patients (reduced 28-day mortality 35% → 29%); NNT 25 for O₂-requiring. No benefit if no O₂ requirement.
  • Tocilizumab + dexamethasone: Additive benefit in patients with significant hypoxia and CRP elevation

Anticoagulation

  • All hospitalized COVID-19: Prophylactic anticoagulation (enoxaparin 40 mg SQ daily or heparin 5000 units SQ BID-TID) unless contraindicated
  • Non-ICU moderate COVID-19: Therapeutic anticoagulation with heparin does not improve outcomes and may increase bleeding (ATTACC/ACTIV-4a/REMAP-CAP)
  • ICU/critical COVID-19: Prophylactic preferred over therapeutic (harm signal in critically ill subgroup)
  • Transmission-based precautions: Standard + Contact + Droplet (minimum); Airborne precautions for aerosol-generating procedures (intubation, bronchoscopy, HFNC >30 L/min)
  • Isolation duration (non-immunocompromised): ≥5 days from symptom onset AND afebrile ≥24h without antipyretics AND symptoms improving
  • Immunocompromised: Extended precautions (10–20+ days); infectious disease consultation recommended; viral cultures or PCR to guide isolation duration
  • Test-to-exit strategy: Rapid antigen test negative at day 5–7 reduces transmission risk (some institutional protocols)
⚠️ Airborne Precautions Required: Negative pressure room + N95 respirator for all entering room. Surgical mask on patient during transport. Maintain until 3 negative sputum AFB smears on 3 separate days (at least 8h apart) AND clinical improvement AND effective TB therapy ×2 weeks.
  • Report to public health department immediately — mandatory reporting in all US states
  • Contact investigation (persons sharing airspace ≥8h) — public health responsibility
  • Document isolation dates; case management via local/state health department

Screening — Who to Test

  • Persons from high-burden countries (TB prevalence >20/100,000)
  • HIV-positive individuals (IGRA annually if ongoing risk)
  • Close contacts of active TB cases
  • Immunocompromised patients (transplant, biologics, TNF-inhibitors, steroids >15 mg/day for ≥1 month)
  • Incarcerated individuals, homeless, healthcare workers in high-risk settings

Diagnostic Tests

TestDetailsPreferred When
IGRA (QuantiFERON-TB Gold Plus, T-SPOT.TB)Interferon-gamma release assay; not affected by BCG vaccination; single visitPrior BCG vaccination; unlikely to return for TST reading; older children/adults
TST (Mantoux)5-TU PPD; read at 48–72h; cut-offs: ≥5 mm (HIV/immunocompromised), ≥10 mm (high-risk groups), ≥15 mm (general population)Children <5 years; serial occupational screening

Preferred LTBI Treatment Regimens (ATS/IDSA/CDC 2020)

RegimenDurationNotes
Isoniazid (INH) + rifapentine (3HP)12 weeks (once weekly directly observed)Preferred; 88–93% efficacy; not in pregnancy or age <2
Rifampin (4R)4 months dailySecond preferred; superior adherence vs 9H; contraindicated with some ART
Isoniazid + rifampin (3HR)3 months dailyAlternative; good efficacy
Isoniazid (9H)9 months dailyTraditional regimen; vitamin B6 50 mg daily to prevent neuropathy

Monitor: LFTs at baseline; repeat if symptomatic or baseline abnormality. Isoniazid hepatotoxicity risk with age >35, alcohol use, chronic liver disease.

Initial Phase (First 2 months) — RIPE × 56 doses

DrugAbbreviationDose (weight-based)Key Toxicities
RifampinR10 mg/kg/day PO (max 600 mg); or 10 mg/kg 3×/week DOTHepatotoxicity, orange secretions, drug interactions (CYP3A4 inducer), flu-like syndrome
IsoniazidI5 mg/kg/day PO (max 300 mg) daily; or 15 mg/kg 3×/week DOTHepatotoxicity, peripheral neuropathy (give B6 25–50 mg/day), drug-induced lupus
PyrazinamideP/Z15–30 mg/kg/day PO (max 2 g)Hepatotoxicity, hyperuricemia/gout, rash
EthambutolE15–20 mg/kg/day PO (max 1.6 g)Optic neuritis (check visual acuity/color vision monthly); avoid if CrCl <30 unless monitored

Continuation Phase (Months 3–6) — IR × 18 weeks

  • Isoniazid + rifampin × 18 weeks (after 2 months RIPE)
  • Extend continuation phase to 9 months total if: CNS TB, bone/joint TB, cavitary disease with positive culture at 2 months

Pulmonary TB Duration

  • Drug-susceptible pulmonary TB: 6 months total (2 RIPE + 4 RI)
  • Cavitary + culture positive at 2 months: 9 months
  • CNS/meningeal TB: 9–12 months; add dexamethasone 0.4 mg/kg/day tapered over 6–8 weeks
  • Bone/joint TB: 6–9 months
  • Pericardial TB: 6 months + prednisolone (evidence mixed — some guidelines still include)
  • Multidrug-resistant TB (MDR-TB): Resistant to at least INH + rifampin
  • Extensively drug-resistant TB (XDR-TB): MDR-TB + resistance to fluoroquinolones + at least one injectable
  • Treatment requires ID/pulmonary specialist: BPaL (bedaquiline + pretomanid + linezolid) regimen — 6 months; 89% culture conversion in ZeNix trial
  • Bedaquiline: blocks mycobacterial ATP synthase; QTc prolongation monitoring required
  • Delamanid, clofazimine, cycloserine — salvage agents
  • All MDR-TB cases must be managed with public health involvement; DOT required
🟤 GI & Hepatobiliary
● Time-critical

Clinical Presentation

📝 Charcot's Triad (50-70% of cases): Fever + RUQ pain + Jaundice
📝 Reynolds' Pentad (severe cholangitis): Above + Altered mental status + Hypotension

Microbiology

  • Gram-negatives (70-80%): E. coli, Klebsiella, Enterobacter, Citrobacter
  • Enterococcus (10-20%): Especially if elderly, prior instrumentation, healthcare-associated
  • Anaerobes (10-15%): Bacteroides, Clostridium (usually polymicrobial)
  • Polymicrobial: Common (30-50% of cases)

Empiric Treatment

SeverityRegimenNotes
Mild-ModerateCeftriaxone 2g IV q24h + Metronidazole 500mg IV q8hCovers GNR + anaerobes
OR Piperacillin-tazobactam 4.5g IV q6hSingle agent, broad coverage
OR Ampicillin-sulbactam 3g IV q6hAlternative single agent
Severe/ICUPip-tazo 4.5g IV q6h + Vancomycin 15-20mg/kg IV q8-12hAdd Enterococcus coverage
OR Meropenem 1g IV q8h + VancomycinIf ESBL risk factors
Healthcare-associatedMeropenem + VancomycinConsider Pseudomonas, ESBL, VRE

🚨 SOURCE CONTROL IS ESSENTIAL — Biliary Drainage Required

CRITICAL: Antibiotics alone will FAIL without biliary drainage. Obstruction must be relieved.

ERCP timing:
• Severe/Reynolds' pentad: URGENT within 24 hours
• Moderate: Within 24-48 hours
• Mild: Within 48-72 hours

Alternatives if ERCP unavailable/failed: Percutaneous transhepatic cholangiography (PTC), surgical drainage

Antibiotic Duration

  • After adequate biliary drainage: 4-7 days total
  • If rapid defervescence + source control: Can consider 4 days
  • If delayed drainage or persistent fever: 7 days
  • If bacteremia documented: 7 days minimum from first negative blood culture

Severity Assessment (Tokyo Guidelines)

GradeCriteria
Grade I (Mild)Meets diagnostic criteria but no organ dysfunction
Grade II (Moderate)Any 2 of: WBC >12K or <4K, Temp >39°C, Age >75, Bili >5 mg/dL, Albumin <3 g/dL
Grade III (Severe)Any organ dysfunction: Hypotension, AMS, Respiratory failure (PaO₂/FiO₂ <300), Renal (Cr >2), Hepatic (INR >1.5), Platelets <100K
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving after 24–48h of antibiotics → ensure biliary drainage achieved (repeat imaging, ERCP/PTC if not yet done)
  • Persistent fever after drainage → look for hepatic abscess, cholangitic abscess, or portal vein thrombosis (Pylephlebitis)
  • Culture grows unexpected organism (Candida, Enterococcus) → broaden; consider ascending cholangitis from biliary stent
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Charcot's triad + altered mental status + hypotension (Reynolds pentad) → Tokyo Grade III → ICU + emergent biliary drainage within hours
  • Hemodynamic instability not responding to resuscitation → emergent percutaneous drainage if ERCP not immediately available
  • Suspected perforation or necrosis → surgical consultation
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Clinically improved + draining bile + afebrile × 48h → narrow antibiotics to culture-directed therapy
  • After adequate biliary drainage, most Grade I–II: 4–7 days total antibiotics sufficient
  • Stable and tolerating PO → transition to oral antibiotics (ciprofloxacin + metronidazole) to complete course

Recognition

  • Fever + left upper quadrant or left-sided pain ± left shoulder pain (diaphragmatic irritation)
  • Splenomegaly on exam; left pleural effusion or left lower lobe infiltrate on CXR
  • Source: Hematogenous seeding (bacteremia, endocarditis — most common), contiguous spread, trauma, hemoglobinopathy (sickle cell), immunocompromise
  • Common organisms: S. aureus, Streptococci, Salmonella (SCD), GNR (GI source), fungi (immunocompromised)

Workup

  • CT abdomen with contrast: Hypodense lesion(s) in spleen; gas = anaerobic/polymicrobial; look for additional abscesses
  • Blood cultures × 2–3: Positive in 50–70% — identify organism before drainage
  • Blood cultures for fungal: If immunocompromised or prolonged hospitalization
  • TTE/TEE: Rule out endocarditis as source (mandatory workup)
  • Hemoglobin electrophoresis: If sickle cell not previously diagnosed

Empiric Therapy

OrganismEmpiric Regimen
Community-acquired (S. aureus, Streptococci most likely)Vancomycin 15–20mg/kg IV q8–12h + Ceftriaxone 2g IV q24h (covers GNR)
GI source suspectedPip-Tazo 4.5g IV q6h OR Meropenem 1g IV q8h
Fungal (immunocompromised)Micafungin 100mg IV q24h (empiric pending cultures)

Source Control

  • Percutaneous aspiration/drainage (IR-guided): Preferred initial approach; unilocular lesions respond best
  • Splenectomy: Failed drainage, multiloculated abscess, ruptured abscess, hemodynamic instability; post-splenectomy vaccines mandatory (pneumococcal, meningococcal, H. influenzae)
  • Bacteremia source control: treat endocarditis if identified
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 72–96h after drainage + antibiotics → repeat imaging; residual collection? second abscess? inadequate drainage? resistant organism?
  • Fungal splenic abscess → echinocandin empirically; B-D-glucan, fungal blood cultures
  • Salmonella splenic abscess + sickle cell → prolonged IV ceftriaxone; high relapse rate without splenectomy
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Ruptured abscess → emergent splenectomy; hemodynamic instability + peritonitis
  • Failed IR drainage + persistently positive cultures → splenectomy; cardiothoracic if left pleural extension
  • Post-splenectomy: encapsulated organism prophylaxis (penicillin V or amoxicillin) + vaccines
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Drainage successful, improving, bacteremia cleared → culture-directed IV antibiotics for 4–6 weeks minimum
  • After adequate drainage, consider oral step-down at 2–4 weeks if tolerating PO and improving
  • Post-splenectomy vaccines: pneumococcal (PCV20), MenACWY + MenB, Hib — if not previously given
○ Condition-specific

Recognition

  • Amebic colitis: Crampy abdominal pain, bloody diarrhea, tenesmus; onset 2–6 weeks after ingestion; usually without fever
  • Amebic liver abscess: RUQ pain + fever + elevated ALP; single right lobe cavity (85%); diarrhea absent in 50%
  • Epidemiology: travel/immigration from endemic areas (developing countries); MSM; institutionalized
  • Distinguish from Entamoeba dispar (non-pathogenic) — microscopically identical; requires antigen or PCR

Workup

  • Amebic colitis: Stool O&P × 3 (low sensitivity); stool Entamoeba histolytica antigen EIA (preferred); stool PCR; colonoscopy with biopsy
  • Amebic liver abscess: Serology (IgG) >90% sensitive — most useful; ultrasound (hypoechoic RUQ lesion); CT abdomen
  • Hepatic abscess: aspirate only if needed for diagnosis (usually responds to antibiotics alone) or if rupture risk
  • CBC: Leukocytosis without eosinophilia (eosinophilia NOT typical for invasive amebiasis)

Empiric Therapy

SyndromeRegimenDuration
Invasive (colitis / abscess) — tissue activeMetronidazole 750mg PO/IV TID7–10 days
Invasive — alternativeTinidazole 2g PO daily3–5 days
Luminal agent (MANDATORY after metronidazole)Paromomycin 500mg PO TID7 days
Luminal alternativeIodoquinol 650mg PO TID20 days

Luminal agent mandatory after metronidazole — eliminates intestinal cysts and prevents relapse. Metronidazole alone has 10% relapse rate without luminal clearance.

Source Control

  • Amebic liver abscess: aspiration usually NOT needed (responds to metronidazole); aspirate if no response at 3–5 days, risk of rupture (>10cm, left lobe proximity to pericardium), or diagnostic uncertainty
  • Surgical drainage rarely required
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • No response to metronidazole at 3–5 days → repeat imaging; inadequate drainage? bacterial superinfection? diagnostic error (pyogenic vs amebic abscess)?
  • Serology negative + liver abscess → bacterial abscess more likely; broaden antibiotics
  • Colitis worsening → amebiasis can cause toxic megacolon (rare); colectomy may be required
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Amebic liver abscess left lobe (>5cm, proximity to pericardium) → aspiration to prevent pericardial rupture
  • Rupture into pleural/peritoneal cavity → surgical or percutaneous drainage; continuation of antibiotics
  • Toxic megacolon from amebic colitis → ICU + surgery + antibiotics (do NOT use steroids — worsen amebiasis)
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Liver abscess: fever typically resolves 3–5 days after starting metronidazole — response confirms diagnosis
  • Radiographic resolution lags clinical improvement (months); do not use imaging to guide duration
  • Luminal eradication confirmation: repeat stool antigen 2–4 weeks after completing paromomycin

Recognition

  • Acute HBV: jaundice, RUQ discomfort, nausea, malaise, dark urine, arthralgias; preceded by prodrome
  • Fulminant hepatic failure: Encephalopathy + coagulopathy (INR >1.5) — 1% of acute HBV but high mortality without transplant
  • Chronic HBV: identified incidentally; immune active phase = ALT elevation + HBeAg+ or high viral load; inactive carrier = normal ALT + low viral load
  • Immunocompromise (chemotherapy, biologic therapy, steroids): HBV reactivation — prophylaxis mandatory before immunosuppression

Workup

  • HBsAg: Active HBV infection (acute or chronic)
  • HBcAb IgM: Acute infection (window period marker)
  • HBcAb IgG: Prior exposure (resolved or chronic)
  • HBeAg / HBeAb: Replication activity (see Serology Patterns reference card)
  • HBV DNA (quantitative): Viral load — guides treatment decisions; >2,000 IU/mL = immune active
  • CBC, CMP, INR: Assess severity; coagulopathy = fulminant risk
  • Hepatitis A, C, D co-infection; HIV co-infection; abdominal ultrasound

Empiric Therapy

ScenarioTreatment
Acute HBV (most cases)Supportive — most resolve spontaneously; no antivirals routinely for self-limited acute hepatitis
Fulminant acute HBV (encephalopathy + INR >1.5)Tenofovir disoproxil fumarate (TDF) 300mg PO daily; liver transplant evaluation
Chronic HBV (immune active — high VL, elevated ALT)TDF 300mg PO daily OR TAF 25mg PO daily (preferred if eGFR <60 or osteoporosis) OR Entecavir 0.5mg PO daily
HBV reactivation prophylaxis (immunosuppression)TDF or Entecavir starting before immunosuppression × at least 12 months after completion
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Acute HBV not resolving at 4–6 months (HBsAg persisting) → chronic infection; refer to hepatology for treatment decision
  • Rising bilirubin + rising INR → fulminant HBV; liver transplant evaluation urgently
  • Not responding to antivirals (rising viral load on therapy) → resistance testing; switch to tenofovir (most potent, lowest resistance)
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Acute liver failure (INR >1.5 + encephalopathy) → ICU, GI/hepatology, liver transplant evaluation
  • Variceal bleeding (decompensated cirrhosis with acute HBV) → GI endoscopy, ICU management
  • Severe thrombocytopenia or coagulopathy → hepatology, consider FFP/vitamin K
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Antiviral therapy for chronic HBV is lifelong in most patients — do not stop without hepatology guidance (flare risk)
  • HBeAg seroconversion (HBeAg → HBeAb) = major milestone; some can stop therapy — hepatology decision
  • Monitor: HBV DNA q3 months initially, then q6 months; ALT q3–6 months; HCC screening: ultrasound q6 months

Recognition

  • Acute HCV: usually asymptomatic or mild; 75–85% progress to chronic infection
  • Chronic HCV: identified incidentally or by screening; most have minimal symptoms until advanced fibrosis/cirrhosis
  • Complications: cirrhosis, portal hypertension, HCC, extrahepatic manifestations (cryoglobulinemia, membranoproliferative GN, porphyria cutanea tarda)
  • Inpatient relevance: newly diagnosed (screening), decompensated cirrhosis complications, HCV-related complications, pre-treatment evaluation

Workup

  • HCV Ab (screening): All adults 18–79 at least once; repeat annually in high-risk (IVDU, HIV, incarcerated)
  • HCV RNA (quantitative): Confirms active infection; baseline viral load before treatment
  • HCV genotype: Less critical in pan-genotypic DAA era but guides regimen selection
  • Liver fibrosis staging: FibroScan (elastography), FIB-4 score, or liver biopsy
  • CBC, CMP, INR: Assess for advanced disease; thrombocytopenia suggests cirrhosis
  • HIV co-infection testing; HBV serology (HBsAg — risk of HBV reactivation with DAA therapy)

Empiric Therapy

HCV is curable — >95% SVR (sustained virologic response = cure) with 8–12 week oral DAA regimens. Inpatient hospitalization is not required for treatment initiation in most cases.

RegimenUseDuration
Sofosbuvir/Velpatasvir (Epclusa)Pan-genotypic, no cirrhosis or compensated cirrhosis12 weeks
Glecaprevir/Pibrentasvir (Mavyret)Pan-genotypic, all fibrosis stages; renal failure8 weeks (treatment-naive, no cirrhosis)
Sofosbuvir/Velpatasvir/Voxilaprevir (Vosevi)Prior DAA failure or NS5A resistance12 weeks
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • SVR not achieved (detectable HCV RNA at 12 weeks post-treatment) → resistance-associated substitution testing; retreatment with Vosevi
  • HBV reactivation after starting DAAs → monitor HBsAg-positive patients; prophylaxis if HBsAg-positive
  • Worsening liver function despite SVR → HCC surveillance; evaluate for other causes of liver disease
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Acute variceal bleeding (decompensated cirrhosis) → GI endoscopy, octreotide, banding/sclerotherapy, ICU
  • Hepatic encephalopathy → lactulose, rifaximin, protein intake optimization
  • HCC identified during workup → oncology, liver transplant evaluation
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Inpatient setting: initiate HCV workup, counsel on cure, arrange outpatient DAA initiation with hepatology/ID
  • SVR12 (HCV RNA undetectable at 12 weeks post-treatment) = cure — no further HCV treatment needed
  • Post-SVR: maintain HCC surveillance (ultrasound q6 months) in cirrhosis; fibrosis may partially reverse

Diagnosis

  • Anti-HCV antibody (EIA): Initial screening; positive in chronic infection; may be negative in first 6–8 weeks of acute infection (window period) and in severely immunocompromised
  • HCV RNA (NAAT): Confirms active infection (chronic or acute); quantitative RNA is viral load; genotype for treatment planning
  • ALT: Elevated in active hepatitis; may be normal in chronic HCV
  • Acute HCV: Anti-HCV may be negative; HCV RNA positive; often identified in IVDU or needlestick exposures

Acute HCV — Inpatient Approach

  • Most acute HCV is asymptomatic; ~25% develop jaundice/symptoms
  • Spontaneous clearance: ~25–40% of acute infections (higher with symptomatic disease)
  • Treatment decision: Current guidelines support treating acute HCV — high SVR rates with DAAs; discuss with GI/hepatology
  • No acute treatment urgency: Inpatient management is supportive; outpatient ID/GI referral for DAA initiation

Chronic HCV — Key Inpatient Considerations

  • DAA therapy (pangenotypic options): Sofosbuvir/velpatasvir (Epclusa) 12 weeks; Glecaprevir/pibrentasvir (Mavyret) 8 weeks for treatment-naive, compensated cirrhosis
  • SVR12 (undetectable HCV RNA 12 weeks after treatment) = cure — achieved in >95% with modern DAAs
  • Drug interactions: Check carefully — amiodarone (serious bradycardia with sofosbuvir), rifampin (reduces DAA levels), statins
  • Decompensated cirrhosis: Avoid NS3/4A inhibitors (glecaprevir, voxilaprevir) — use sofosbuvir/velpatasvir ± ribavirin; hepatology essential
  • Needlestick exposure: No PEP available for HCV; check HCV RNA at 6 weeks post-exposure; treat if positive

Who to Test

  • Unexplained, new-onset ≥3 loose stools/24h in patients ≥2 years old
  • Do NOT test asymptomatic patients or formed stool — high false-positive rate with NAATs
  • Do NOT perform test of cure after treatment — NAAT remains positive for weeks

Preferred Testing Algorithm (IDSA/SHEA 2021)

StrategyDetails
Two-step (preferred)GDH EIA + toxin EIA; if discordant → NAAT. Positive toxin EIA = true CDI. GDH+/toxin− = colonization or early/low-toxin disease; NAAT helps differentiate.
NAAT aloneHighly sensitive; cannot distinguish colonization from active infection. Use clinical context (diarrhea burden, leukocytosis, lactate).
Toxin EIA aloneLow sensitivity (~75%); misses cases. Not recommended as standalone.

Pearl: In low-pretest-probability patients (diarrhea on laxatives, tube feeds), a positive NAAT alone may represent colonization. Treat if clinical syndrome is convincing.

SeverityCriteriaFirst-Line Therapy
Non-severeWBC <15k AND Cr <1.5 mg/dLFidaxomicin 200 mg PO BID ×10 days (preferred) OR oral vancomycin 125 mg PO QID ×10 days
SevereWBC ≥15k OR Cr ≥1.5 mg/dLFidaxomicin 200 mg PO BID ×10 days (preferred) OR oral vancomycin 125 mg PO QID ×10 days
FulminantHypotension, shock, ileus, OR megacolonOral vancomycin 500 mg QID + metronidazole 500 mg IV q8h (ileus/megacolon); rectal vancomycin enemas if ileus present

Metronidazole: No longer recommended for non-severe CDI (inferior cure rates vs vancomycin and fidaxomicin). Reserve for fulminant CDI as adjunct only.

Fidaxomicin advantage: Superior reduction in recurrence vs oral vancomycin (NNT ~12), particularly for non-027 strains. Cost-prohibitive at many institutions.

EpisodeTreatment
First recurrenceFidaxomicin 200 mg PO BID ×10d (preferred) OR vancomycin extended/tapered pulse regimen if initial treatment was standard vancomycin OR fidaxomicin
Second recurrenceFidaxomicin OR vancomycin tapered pulse regimen; add bezlotoxumab 10 mg/kg IV ×1 (monoclonal anti-toxin B; reduces recurrence by ~10–20%; use with caution if CHF/EF <35%)
≥2 recurrencesFecal microbiota transplant (FMT) — 80–90% success rate; OR SER-109 (Vowst) — spore-based microbiome therapy; bezlotoxumab adjunct

Vancomycin Tapered Pulse Regimen (1 option for recurrence)

  • 125 mg QID × 10–14 days, then 125 mg BID × 7 days, then 125 mg daily × 7 days, then 125 mg every 2–3 days × 2–8 weeks

Risk factors for recurrence: Age >65, ongoing antibiotics, PPIs, prior CDI episode, immunosuppression, ribotype 027.

  • Consult surgery early for fulminant CDI, especially with peritoneal signs, ileus, or megacolon
  • Total colectomy (with end ileostomy) if perforation, peritonitis, or failing medical therapy — mortality 35–80% even with surgery
  • Diverting loop ileostomy + antegrade colonic lavage with vancomycin — emerging alternative to colectomy in select centers
  • CT abdomen: assess for megacolon (transverse colon >6 cm), pneumatosis, free air
  • Avoid antiperistaltics (loperamide, opioids) — risk of toxic megacolon
  • Contact precautions: Gown + gloves for all patient contact; private room preferred
  • Hand hygiene: Soap and water ONLY (not alcohol gel) — C. difficile spores resistant to alcohol
  • Environmental: Sporicidal disinfectants (10% bleach or EPA-registered sporicidal agents)
  • Duration: Continue contact precautions for duration of stay; some guidelines extend 48h after diarrhea resolves
  • Discontinue offending antibiotics as soon as clinically possible — single most important modifiable risk factor
  • Avoid unnecessary PPIs in hospitalized patients — associated with 1.5–2× increased CDI risk

Cross-link: Antimicrobial Stewardship Guide

Diagnostic Criteria

  • Diagnostic paracentesis required whenever ascites patient is admitted or clinical deterioration occurs (do not delay for INR)
  • SBP: Ascitic fluid PMN ≥250 cells/mm³ regardless of culture result
  • Culture-negative neutrocytic ascites (CNNA): PMN ≥250 + negative culture — treat same as SBP (likely culture sensitivity issue)
  • Bacterascites: Positive culture + PMN <250 — repeat paracentesis in 48h; treat if symptomatic or PMN rises

Common Organisms

  • E. coli (~40%), Klebsiella pneumoniae (~20%), Streptococcus pneumoniae, Enterococcus spp.
  • Polymicrobial or anaerobes → suspect secondary peritonitis (bowel perforation) — CT abdomen; surgical consultation

Ascitic Fluid Cell Count Differential

ConditionPMN (cells/mm³)Total WBCOther
SBP≥250VariableUsually monomicrobial
Secondary peritonitisOften >1000Very highPolymicrobial, high protein, LDH >serum ULN, glucose <50
Hemorrhagic ascitesCorrect: subtract 1 PMN per 250 RBC—Exclude traumatic tap

Empiric Antibiotics

  • First-line: Cefotaxime 2 g IV q8h × 5 days (or ceftriaxone 2 g IV q24h × 5 days)
  • Hospital-acquired SBP or prior fluoroquinolone prophylaxis: Broader coverage — piperacillin-tazobactam or carbapenem (higher ESBL/FQ-resistant organism risk)
  • De-escalate or change based on culture/sensitivities
  • Repeat diagnostic paracentesis at 48h: PMN should decrease by ≥25% — if not, broaden or consider secondary peritonitis

Albumin Infusion (NEJM 1999 — Sort et al., landmark trial)

  • Indication: All SBP patients with: Cr >1 mg/dL, BUN >30 mg/dL, OR bilirubin >4 mg/dL
  • Dose: 1.5 g/kg IV on day 1 (at time of diagnosis), then 1 g/kg IV on day 3
  • Rationale: Prevents hepatorenal syndrome (HRS) — reduces in-hospital mortality from ~29% to 10%

Long-Term Antibiotic Prophylaxis

IndicationRegimen
After first episode of SBP (secondary prophylaxis)Norfloxacin 400 mg PO daily (preferred in US, but availability limited) OR ciprofloxacin 500 mg PO daily OR TMP-SMX DS 1 tab PO daily
Primary prophylaxis: Ascitic protein <1.5 g/dL + (Child-Pugh ≥9 with bili ≥3 OR Cr ≥1.2 OR Na ≤130 OR BUN ≥25)Ciprofloxacin 500 mg PO daily OR norfloxacin 400 mg daily
GI bleeding in cirrhosis (short-term)Ceftriaxone 1 g IV q24h × 7 days — reduces bacterial infections including SBP by ~40%

Concerns with long-term prophylaxis: Rising fluoroquinolone-resistant and ESBL-producing bacteria in SBP — reappraise necessity regularly; consider TIPS referral for refractory ascites.

Cross-link: 📚 IMCRG: Ascites & Paracentesis | 🔧 Procedures: Paracentesis

🔬 GU & STI
● Time-critical

🚨 Surgical emergency. Mortality 20–40%. Every hour of delay worsens outcome. Do not wait for imaging if clinical diagnosis is clear — go to OR.

Overview

  • Necrotizing fasciitis of the perineum, genitalia, and perianal region
  • Risk factors: Diabetes (most common), obesity, immunocompromised, alcohol use disorder, local trauma, anorectal/urologic surgery, Fournier's after urinary catheterization
  • Portal of entry: Anorectal (30–50%), urogenital (20–40%), cutaneous, idiopathic (10%)

Clinical Features

  • Early: Perineal pain, erythema, swelling — often disproportionate to external appearance
  • Late: Skin necrosis, crepitus (subcutaneous gas), foul-smelling discharge, systemic sepsis
  • Crepitus on exam or gas on imaging = pathognomonic
  • Pain out of proportion to skin findings — classic early warning

Diagnosis

  • Clinical diagnosis first — do not delay surgery for imaging in unstable patient
  • CT pelvis/perineum: Gas tracking along fascial planes, fluid collections; sensitivity ~88%; helps delineate extent
  • LRINEC score: Supports diagnosis (CRP, WBC, Hgb, Na, Cr, glucose) but LOW score does NOT exclude necrotizing infection

Management

StepAction
1. ResuscitationIV fluids, vasopressors if septic shock; blood cultures; hold urinary catheter if urethral involvement
2. Antibiotics (IMMEDIATELY)Vancomycin + Piperacillin-tazobactam + Metronidazole (or Meropenem). Add clindamycin for toxin inhibition (GAS)
3. Surgery (URGENT)Wide surgical debridement beyond visible involvement — OR within hours; urology + colorectal + plastics
4. Re-lookReturn to OR every 24–48h for repeat debridement until margins are clean
