5. AdjunctsHyperbaric oxygen (where available) — may reduce mortality; fecal diversion if anal sphincter involvement

Microbiology

  • Polymicrobial (nearly always): E. coli, Klebsiella, enterococci, S. aureus, Bacteroides, Clostridium, streptococci
  • Synergistic — mixed aerobic/anaerobic infection causes rapid tissue destruction via ischemia and gas production
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Post-debridement: wound margins still not clean at re-look → more aggressive debridement required; LRINEC score can be misleading
  • Wound not healing as expected → check blood glucose (DM poorly controlled), nutritional status, perfusion
  • Unusual organisms (Candida, Pseudomonas dominant) → immunosuppression? neutropenia? broaden coverage
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Extension beyond perineum → gluteal, thigh, abdominal wall involvement → wider surgical debridement + plastic surgery
  • Fecal contamination persisting → colorectal surgery, consider diverting colostomy
  • Septic shock not responding → ICU, vasopressors, consider hyperbaric oxygen referral
  • Urethral or rectal involvement → urology + colorectal involvement mandatory
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Wound margins clean, granulating, no further necrosis at re-look → reduce frequency of OR returns
  • Bacteremia cleared, afebrile, wound granulating → narrow antibiotics to culture-directed coverage
  • After source control, 2–4 weeks antibiotics typical; oral step-down when tolerating PO and wounds healing
○ Condition-specific
CategoryDefinition / Key Points
Uncomplicated cystitisPremenopausal, non-pregnant, healthy woman with lower urinary symptoms only; no structural/functional abnormalities
Complicated UTIAny UTI in: men, pregnant women, structural/functional abnormality, recent instrumentation, indwelling catheter, renal transplant, immunocompromise, hospital-acquired
Catheter-associated UTI (CAUTI)Signs/symptoms of UTI + ≥10³ CFU/mL of ≥1 organism on urine culture + urinary catheter in place ≥48h (or removed <48h prior)
Catheter-associated asymptomatic bacteriuria (CA-ASB)≥10⁵ CFU/mL in catheterized patient WITHOUT signs/symptoms — do not treat (except pre-urologic procedure)
Asymptomatic bacteriuria (ASB)≥10⁵ CFU/mL ×2 specimens women (or ×1 for catheterized) without symptoms — do not treat except pregnancy or prior to urologic procedure

IDSA explicitly recommends AGAINST treating ASB in:

  • Healthy premenopausal non-pregnant women
  • Diabetic women
  • Older community-dwelling men and women
  • Institutionalized elderly patients
  • Patients with spinal cord injury
  • Catheterized patients (CA-ASB)

TREAT ASB only in:

  • Pregnancy (risk of pyelonephritis/preterm birth) — screen at first prenatal visit
  • Prior to urologic procedures associated with mucosal bleeding (e.g., TURP)

Stewardship pearl: ASB over-treatment is one of the most common antibiotic misuse patterns in hospitalized patients. Urine cultures should only be sent when there is clinical suspicion for UTI.

Recognition

  • Fever, flank pain, CVA tenderness, nausea/vomiting ± dysuria, frequency
  • Complicated UTI (IDSA 2025): Infection that has progressed beyond the bladder (pyelonephritis, urosepsis, bacteremia)
  • High-risk features: Sepsis, obstruction, renal abscess, immunocompromise, pregnancy, anatomic abnormality, recent urologic procedure, MDR risk
  • Distinguish from uncomplicated cystitis (confined to bladder) — different treatment duration

Workup

  • Urinalysis: Pyuria (≥10 WBC/hpf), bacteriuria, nitrites (GNR only), leukocyte esterase
  • Urine culture (clean catch): ≥10³ CFU/mL (symptomatic) — mandatory before antibiotics
  • Blood cultures × 2: All inpatients with pyelonephritis/urosepsis — bacteremia in 20–30%
  • Renal ultrasound or CT: If concern for obstruction, perinephric abscess, or not responding at 48–72h
  • Creatinine, CBC: Assess severity and renal function for dosing

Empiric Therapy

IDSA 2025 4-step empiric framework: (1) Assess severity (sepsis → broader), (2) local resistance rates (>20% resistance = avoid that agent), (3) patient MDR risk factors, (4) de-escalate when cultures available

ScenarioEmpiric Regimen
No sepsis, low MDR riskCeftriaxone 1–2g IV q24h OR Pip-Tazo 3.375g IV q6h
Sepsis or MDR risk factorsCefepime 2g IV q8h OR Meropenem 1g IV q8h
ESBL risk (prior ESBL UTI, recurrent hospital exposure)Ertapenem 1g IV q24h OR Meropenem
Urosepsis with shockMeropenem 1–2g IV q8h

De-escalate to narrowest effective agent based on susceptibilities as soon as available (48–72h).

Source Control

  • Obstruction with infection: Urologic emergency — ureteral stent or percutaneous nephrostomy within hours
  • Perinephric abscess: IR-guided drainage if >3cm or not responding to antibiotics
  • Remove/replace Foley catheter in CAUTI
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 48–72h → CT abdomen/pelvis (obstruction? abscess? emphysematous pyelonephritis?); reassess cultures and susceptibilities
  • Culture grows unexpected organism (Candida, Enterococcus) → reassess whether it's a true pathogen; treat if clinically infected
  • Persistently positive blood cultures → rule out endovascular source; TTE if S. aureus or prolonged bacteremia
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Obstruction identified → emergent urology consult for decompression (stent/nephrostomy)
  • Emphysematous pyelonephritis (gas in renal parenchyma on CT) → emergent urology + ID; may require nephrectomy
  • Septic shock from urosepsis → ICU resuscitation; source control paramount
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Culture + susceptibilities available → de-escalate to narrowest effective oral agent (TMP-SMX, ciprofloxacin, or cephalosporin if susceptible)
  • Afebrile × 48h, tolerating PO, improving → IV to oral transition
  • Total duration: 7 days for uncomplicated pyelo; 10–14 days complicated; 14 days if bacteremic

Recognition

  • Catheter-associated UTI (CAUTI): symptoms (fever, rigors, altered mental status, flank pain, suprapubic pain, CVA tenderness) + urinary catheter (present or removed within 48h) + ≥10³ CFU/mL on urine culture
  • Asymptomatic bacteriuria (ASB) in catheterized patients: Do NOT treat — colonization is expected; treating increases resistance and CDI risk
  • Common organisms: E. coli, Klebsiella, Pseudomonas, Enterococcus, Candida, CoNS (usually contaminant)

Workup

  • Urine culture: Obtain through catheter or fresh specimen — do NOT culture catheter tip
  • Urinalysis: Pyuria alone does not diagnose CAUTI in catheterized patients (near-universal)
  • Blood cultures: If febrile/systemically ill
  • CT abdomen/pelvis: If no response to treatment or complication suspected (abscess, emphysematous cystitis)

Empiric Therapy

ScenarioApproach
CAUTI (symptomatic)Remove/replace catheter first, then treat based on cultures; empiric: Ceftriaxone 1g IV/PO q24h; broaden per local resistance
Candiduria (symptomatic)Fluconazole 200mg PO daily × 7–14d (if susceptible); remove/replace catheter
Candiduria (asymptomatic)Do NOT treat — almost always colonization; remove catheter if possible

Source Control

  • Remove catheter as soon as no longer clinically needed — single most effective intervention
  • Replace catheter before starting antibiotics if catheter in place >2 weeks
  • Prevention: intermittent catheterization preferred over indwelling when feasible
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Candiduria in catheterized patient without symptoms → do not treat; remove catheter
  • Symptoms persist after catheter removal + antibiotics → CT pelvis (emphysematous cystitis, abscess, foreign body, urethral pathology)
  • Culture grows multiple organisms → likely contamination; repeat culture through fresh catheter
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Sepsis from CAUTI → treat as urosepsis; emergent catheter change + IV antibiotics
  • Emphysematous cystitis (gas in bladder wall on CT) → IV antibiotics + urology consultation; may require cystoscopy
  • Fungal ball in bladder → urologic consultation; antifungal + possible irrigation
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Catheter removed, afebrile × 48h, culture-directed therapy → 7 days total for most CAUTI
  • Extended coverage: 10–14 days if delayed response or bacteremia
  • Prevention: reassess catheter necessity daily — remove when no longer indicated

Recognition

  • Sepsis arising from urinary source: fever + hemodynamic instability + urinary symptoms or imaging findings
  • Most common: Pyelonephritis with bacteremia; obstructive uropathy with upstream infection; CAUTI with bacteremia
  • High-risk: Obstruction (stones, stricture, BPH, malignancy), diabetes, immunocompromise, recent urologic instrumentation
  • Common organisms: E. coli (#1), Klebsiella, Proteus, Pseudomonas, Enterococcus

Workup

  • Urine culture + blood cultures × 2 before antibiotics
  • Urinalysis, CBC, CMP, lactate, blood cultures
  • CT abdomen/pelvis with contrast (urgent): Rule out obstruction, perinephric abscess, emphysematous pyelonephritis — these require emergent urologic intervention
  • Renal ultrasound if CT contrast contraindicated

Empiric Therapy

Antibiotics within 1 hour + urgent imaging to exclude obstruction — obstruction with infection is a urologic emergency

SeverityRegimen
No obstruction, no MDR riskCeftriaxone 2g IV q24h OR Pip-Tazo 4.5g IV q6h
Obstruction or MDR riskMeropenem 1g IV q8h
ESBL known/suspectedErtapenem 1g IV q24h OR Meropenem

Source Control

  • Obstruction = urologic emergency: Ureteral stent or percutaneous nephrostomy within hours — antibiotics alone will fail
  • Remove Foley catheter (replace); drain perinephric abscess if present
  • Septic source control is the determinant of outcome
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 24–48h despite appropriate antibiotics → obstruction not yet relieved? CT to recheck; expanding abscess?
  • Culture grows Candida → CAUTI with candidemia? Check blood cultures; treat as candidemia if positive
  • Blood cultures persistently positive → endovascular source (endocarditis, infected thrombus)? TTE
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Obstruction identified → emergent urology decompression (stent or nephrostomy); cannot wait for full resuscitation
  • Emphysematous pyelonephritis → emergent urology; may require nephrectomy; high mortality
  • Septic shock → ICU, vasopressors, source control paramount
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Obstruction decompressed + cultures available → narrow to targeted oral agent at 48–72h
  • Bacteremia cleared, tolerating PO, afebrile → oral step-down; 7–14 days total
  • Blood culture clearance in 48–72h with appropriate therapy confirms adequate source control
  • UTI is the most common identifiable source of sepsis; management follows sepsis bundle (see Sepsis section)
  • Blood cultures ×2 before antibiotics — bacteremia in 20–30% of pyelonephritis
  • Emergent imaging if obstructive uropathy suspected (obstructed infected kidney = emergency urologic drainage)
  • Obstructed + infected (pyonephrosis) → emergent nephrostomy tube or ureteral stent — source control critical to survival
  • Initial broad-spectrum coverage with pip-tazo or carbapenem ± vancomycin; narrow based on cultures
  • Duration: minimum 14 days for bacteremic urosepsis; longer if structural abnormality
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 24–48h despite appropriate antibiotics → obstruction not yet relieved? CT to recheck; expanding abscess?
  • Culture grows Candida → CAUTI with candidemia? Check blood cultures; treat as candidemia if positive
  • Blood cultures persistently positive → endovascular source (endocarditis, infected thrombus)? TTE
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Obstruction identified → emergent urology decompression (stent or nephrostomy); cannot wait
  • Emphysematous pyelonephritis (gas in renal parenchyma on CT) → emergent urology; may require nephrectomy
  • Septic shock → ICU, vasopressors, source control paramount
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Obstruction decompressed + cultures available → narrow to targeted oral agent at 48–72h
  • Bacteremia cleared, tolerating PO, afebrile → oral step-down; 7–14 days total
  • Blood culture clearance at 48–72h with appropriate therapy confirms adequate source control

Distinction

FeatureRenal Cortical Abscess (Carbuncle)Corticomedullary AbscessPerinephric Abscess
SourceHematogenous seeding (S. aureus bacteremia, IVDU)Ascending UTI; complicated pyelonephritis (obstruction, DM, anatomic abnormality)Extension from renal abscess; rarely hematogenous
LocationRenal cortex onlyCorticomedullary junction; usually unilateralGerota's fascia — between kidney and surrounding fat
PathogenS. aureus > streptococciE. coli, Klebsiella, Proteus, EnterococcusE. coli, Klebsiella, S. aureus (mixed)

Clinical Features

  • Fever, flank pain, costovertebral angle tenderness — similar to pyelonephritis but failure to improve on 72h of appropriate antibiotics for pyelonephritis should prompt imaging
  • Perinephric abscess: May have psoas sign (pain on hip extension), limp, scoliosis
  • UA: May be normal (especially with cortical abscess not communicating with collecting system)

Diagnosis

  • CT abdomen/pelvis with contrast: Imaging of choice — hypodense collection with rim enhancement; detects gas, extent, associated obstruction
  • Ultrasound: Initial screening; less sensitive than CT
  • Blood cultures: Positive in ~20–40%
  • Urine culture: May be negative with cortical abscess

Treatment

SizeApproachAntibiotics
<3 cmIV antibiotics alone; close imaging follow-upCulture-directed; empiric: Ceftriaxone + Vancomycin (cover GNR + S. aureus)
3–5 cmTrial of antibiotics; low threshold for drainageIV antibiotics + reassess at 48–72h
>5 cm or no response at 48–72hPercutaneous CT-guided drainage + IV antibioticsCulture-directed; 2–4 weeks total
Gas-forming / failed drainageSurgical drainage or nephrectomyBroad-spectrum including anaerobes
  • Relieve obstruction: Urologic intervention if ureteral obstruction identified (stent or nephrostomy)
  • Duration: Typically 2–4 weeks; guided by clinical response and follow-up imaging

Postpartum Endometritis

FeatureDetails
DefinitionInfection of uterine decidua/myometrium after delivery
Risk factorsC-section (major RF — 5–20× higher vs. vaginal), prolonged labor, prolonged ROM, multiple vaginal exams, chorioamnionitis, Group B Strep colonization
PresentationFever (>38°C) within 24–48h postpartum, uterine tenderness, foul lochia, tachycardia; may see leukocytosis
MicrobiologyPolymicrobial: Group B Strep, E. coli, Enterococcus, Bacteroides, Gardnerella, mycoplasmas

Treatment — Postpartum Endometritis

RegimenNotes
Clindamycin 900mg IV q8h + Gentamicin 5mg/kg IV q24hStandard first-line; discontinue IV 24–48h after afebrile; no oral step-down needed
Ampicillin-sulbactam 3g IV q6hAlternative single-agent option
Add Ampicillin if Enterococcus suspectedEspecially if no clinical response to clindamycin/gentamicin

Chorioamnionitis (Intraamniotic Infection)

  • Definition: Infection of amniotic fluid, membranes, placenta during pregnancy (intrapartum or postpartum)
  • Clinical diagnosis: Maternal fever ≥39°C, OR ≥38–38.9°C + 1 of: maternal tachycardia, fetal tachycardia, uterine tenderness, purulent amniotic fluid, maternal leukocytosis
  • Treatment: Ampicillin 2g IV q6h + Gentamicin 5mg/kg IV q24h; add Clindamycin 900mg IV q8h for C-section. Continue until 24–48h after delivery and afebrile
  • Neonatal implications: Neonatal sepsis workup; pediatrics involvement at delivery
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 48–72h on gentamicin + clindamycin → consider resistant organism; pelvic abscess? retained products of conception?
  • Persistent fever after adequate antibiotics → pelvic ultrasound; retained POC requires surgical evacuation
  • Group A Strep isolated → aggressive treatment; streptococcal toxic shock possible; monitor closely
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Septic shock → ICU resuscitation + IV antibiotics + source control
  • Suspected septic pelvic thrombophlebitis (fever not responding to antibiotics + negative imaging) → add heparin anticoagulation
  • Retained products of conception → GYN consultation for suction curettage
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Afebrile × 48h + improving → oral step-down (amoxicillin-clavulanate or doxycycline) not routinely required for postpartum endometritis if IV course completed
  • Most postpartum endometritis: 5–7 days IV gentamicin + clindamycin; no oral follow-up needed if responding
  • Chorioamnionitis: continue antibiotics for 24h after delivery; maternal fever resolution confirms response

Recognition

  • Urethritis/cervicitis: dysuria, discharge, often asymptomatic in women
  • PID: pelvic/lower abdominal pain, cervical motion tenderness, adnexal tenderness, fever
  • Disseminated gonococcal infection (DGI): triad of tenosynovitis + dermatitis (pustular/hemorrhagic skin lesions) + polyarthralgia; OR septic arthritis (monoarticular)
  • Pharyngitis, proctitis, conjunctivitis
  • Risk: multiple partners, MSM, prior STI, inconsistent condom use

Workup

  • NAAT: Urine (men), vaginal/endocervical swab (women), rectal/pharyngeal if extragenital exposure — most sensitive
  • Culture: Required if resistance testing needed or medico-legal context
  • Gram stain: Gram-negative intracellular diplococci in urethral discharge (70–90% sensitive in men; <50% in women)
  • Always test for co-infection: Chlamydia NAAT, syphilis serology (RPR), HIV, Hepatitis B/C if not immune
  • DGI: Blood cultures (positive in ~50%), joint fluid culture (positive in ~25%)

Empiric Therapy

SyndromeRegimenNotes
Uncomplicated urogenital/rectalCeftriaxone 500mg IM × 1 (1g if >150kg)+ Doxycycline 100mg PO BID × 7d if chlamydia not excluded
Pharyngeal gonorrheaCeftriaxone 500mg IM × 1 + test-of-cure at 1–2 weeksPharynx hardest to treat — confirm eradication
DGI (skin/tenosynovitis)Ceftriaxone 1g IV/IM q24h× 7 days or until 24–48h after improvement → step down to oral (if susceptible)
Gonococcal septic arthritisCeftriaxone 1g IV q24h× 7–14 days; joint washout if not improving
Gonococcal conjunctivitisCeftriaxone 1g IM × 1 + saline irrigationOphthalmology consultation

Do NOT use fluoroquinolones or azithromycin monotherapy — widespread resistance. Ceftriaxone is the only reliable first-line agent.

Source Control

  • DGI / septic arthritis: joint washout (arthrocentesis) for purulent effusion; serial drainage if reaccumulating
  • Ophthalmia neonatorum / adult conjunctivitis: copious saline irrigation
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • NAAT positive but no response to ceftriaxone → send culture for susceptibility testing; report to health department
  • DGI not improving at 48–72h → reconsider diagnosis (reactive arthritis, other bacterial arthritis), obtain culture from blood/joint
  • Ceftriaxone allergy → ID consultation; spectinomycin 2g IM (not available in US) — manage with desensitization or alternative regimen per IDSA
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Gonococcal meningitis or endocarditis → IV ceftriaxone 1–2g q12h × 10–14d; neurosurgery/cardiology involvement
  • Septic arthritis with joint destruction or failure to drain → orthopedic surgery
  • Ophthalmia with corneal involvement → urgent ophthalmology
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • DGI with skin/tenosynovitis, improving on IV × 24–48h, susceptible organism → step down to oral cefixime 400mg BID to complete 7d total
  • Septic arthritis responding to drainage + IV → oral step-down after 7d
  • Test-of-cure: only required for pharyngeal gonorrhea; not routinely needed for genital sites with ceftriaxone

Recognition

  • Urethritis/cervicitis: often asymptomatic (most common STI in US); dysuria, mucopurulent discharge
  • PID: pelvic pain, cervical motion tenderness (often co-infection with gonorrhea)
  • Epididymo-orchitis: unilateral testicular pain/swelling in men <35 (vs older men where GNR dominate)
  • Proctitis (MSM): rectal pain, discharge, bleeding
  • LGV (serovars L1–L3): painful inguinal lymphadenopathy (buboes), severe proctitis in MSM
  • Reactive arthritis (formerly Reiter syndrome): arthritis + urethritis + conjunctivitis

Workup

  • NAAT: Urine (men), vaginal/endocervical/vulvovaginal swab (women) — gold standard
  • Rectal/pharyngeal NAAT if extragenital exposure in MSM
  • Always co-test for gonorrhea
  • Screen: all sexually active women ≤25 annually; older women with risk factors; MSM annually (or q3–6 months if high risk)
  • LGV: specialized PCR or culture (serovar typing)

Empiric Therapy

SyndromeRegimenDuration
Urogenital/rectal chlamydiaDoxycycline 100mg PO BID (preferred)7 days
Alternative (lower efficacy)Azithromycin 1g PO × 1Single dose
PregnancyAzithromycin 1g PO × 1Single dose
LGVDoxycycline 100mg PO BID21 days
Epididymo-orchitis (<35yr)Ceftriaxone 500mg IM × 1 + Doxycycline 100mg PO BID10 days doxycycline
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Symptoms persisting after treatment → retest (NAAT); consider re-exposure, partner not treated, or doxycycline non-adherence
  • Azithromycin treatment failure → doxycycline (azithromycin now second-line per CDC 2021 — lower efficacy data)
  • Reactive arthritis after chlamydia — treat acute infection; NSAIDs for arthritis; antibiotics do not prevent reactive arthritis
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • PID with tubo-ovarian abscess (TOA) → IV therapy, GYN consult for drainage if >3–4cm or not responding
  • LGV proctitis in HIV+ → prolonged treatment, may need 6 weeks
  • Reactive arthritis with severe joint inflammation → rheumatology
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Test-of-cure not routinely needed (except pregnancy, pharyngeal, or LGV)
  • Re-test at 3 months (high reinfection rate — not treatment failure)
  • Partner treatment mandatory — expedited partner therapy (EPT) permitted in most states

Recognition

  • Primary: Painless indurated genital/oral/anal chancre (heals spontaneously in 3–6 weeks)
  • Secondary: Maculopapular rash (palms + soles classic), condyloma lata, mucous patches, flu-like, alopecia — 2–10 weeks after primary
  • Latent: Early (<1yr) or late (>1yr) — asymptomatic; diagnosed serologically
  • Tertiary: Gummas, aortitis (aortic regurgitation), neurosyphilis
  • Neurosyphilis: Any stage — meningitis, cranial neuropathy, uveitis, tabes dorsalis, general paresis; consider LP in any neurologic/ocular symptoms
  • HIV co-infection → atypical presentation, higher neurosyphilis risk, may need LP regardless of stage

Workup

  • Non-treponemal: RPR or VDRL — quantitative titer (tracks treatment response); false positives in pregnancy, autoimmune, viral illness
  • Treponemal: FTA-ABS, TPPA, TP-EIA — confirmatory; remains positive lifelong
  • Reverse algorithm: Many labs run treponemal first — if positive, confirm with RPR titer
  • LP for neurosyphilis: Any neurologic/ocular symptoms; HIV+ with late latent; treatment failure; CSF-VDRL specific but insensitive (60% sensitivity)
  • Always test: HIV, hepatitis B/C, gonorrhea, chlamydia

Empiric Therapy

StageRegimenDuration
Primary / Secondary / Early latent (<1yr)Benzathine PCN G 2.4 MU IM × 1Single dose
Late latent / Unknown durationBenzathine PCN G 2.4 MU IM weekly × 33 weeks
NeurosyphilisPenicillin G 3–4 MU IV q4h10–14 days
PCN allergy (non-pregnant)Doxycycline 100mg PO BID14d (early) or 28d (late)
PCN allergy (pregnant or neurosyphilis)Penicillin desensitizationThen standard PCN regimen

Jarisch-Herxheimer reaction: Fever/chills/hypotension 2–8h after first penicillin dose — self-limited; warn patients; especially common in secondary syphilis. Manage with antipyretics.

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • RPR titer not declining 4-fold at 6–12 months → treatment failure vs re-infection; LP to exclude neurosyphilis; re-treat
  • HIV+ patient with primary/secondary not responding → higher dose or repeat dose; LP to exclude neurosyphilis
  • Ocular symptoms (uveitis, optic neuritis) at any stage → LP + ophthalmology; treat as neurosyphilis
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Neurosyphilis → 10–14 days IV penicillin G, then consider benzathine PCN G 2.4 MU IM weekly × 3 (some guidelines)
  • Ocular syphilis → ophthalmology urgently; visual loss can be permanent
  • Congenital syphilis (neonatal) → pediatric ID consultation; full neonatal evaluation
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • RPR titer declining (4-fold decrease) at 6–12 months confirms response
  • Early syphilis: follow-up at 6 and 12 months; late: 6, 12, 24 months
  • Treponemal tests (FTA-ABS) remain positive for life — do not use for treatment monitoring

Recognition

  • Primary: painful grouped vesicles → ulcers on genitalia, perianal area; inguinal lymphadenopathy; systemic symptoms; urinary retention possible
  • Recurrent: milder, shorter; prodrome of tingling/burning; often no systemic symptoms
  • Asymptomatic shedding: transmission occurs without lesions
  • Complications: sacral radiculopathy (urinary retention, constipation), meningitis (Mollaret recurrent meningitis — HSV-2), disseminated HSV (immunocompromised)

Workup

  • Clinical diagnosis in typical presentation
  • PCR from lesion: Most sensitive — swab base of ulcer; better than culture
  • Culture: Viable if lesions active; lower sensitivity than PCR
  • Type-specific serology (IgG): HSV-1/HSV-2 — useful for asymptomatic partner counseling, not acute diagnosis
  • Consider: syphilis RPR, gonorrhea/chlamydia NAAT, HIV in genital ulcer disease

Empiric Therapy

IndicationRegimenDuration
Primary episodeValacyclovir 1g PO BID7–10 days
Recurrent episodeValacyclovir 500mg PO BID3 days
Suppressive therapyValacyclovir 500mg PO daily (1g daily if ≥10 recurrences/year)Ongoing — reassess annually
Severe / disseminatedAcyclovir 5–10mg/kg IV q8hUntil improving, then oral step-down
Acyclovir-resistant (immunocompromised)Foscarnet 40–80mg/kg IV q8–12hUntil healed
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Atypical appearance → culture/PCR; consider syphilis, chancroid, LGV, fixed drug eruption
  • Immunocompromised patient with non-healing lesions → resistance testing; consider foscarnet
  • Urinary retention → urological assessment; may need catheterization; consider sacral radiculopathy
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Encephalitis (HSV-1 > HSV-2) → IV acyclovir 10mg/kg q8h × 14–21 days; neurology
  • Disseminated HSV (immunocompromised) → IV acyclovir + ID consultation
  • Meningitis → IV or high-dose oral acyclovir; neurology
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Primary episode resolved, tolerating PO → oral suppression if frequent recurrences or partner uninfected
  • Suppression reduces recurrence frequency and asymptomatic shedding by ~50%
  • Pregnancy: suppression from 36 weeks gestation reduces neonatal transmission risk

Recognition

  • Lower abdominal/pelvic pain + cervical motion tenderness AND/OR uterine/adnexal tenderness
  • Fever, vaginal discharge, abnormal uterine bleeding (variable)
  • Polymicrobial: N. gonorrhoeae, C. trachomatis, vaginal flora (anaerobes, GNR, streptococci)
  • Complications: Tubo-ovarian abscess (TOA), Fitz-Hugh-Curtis syndrome (perihepatic adhesions → RUQ pain), infertility, ectopic pregnancy risk, chronic pelvic pain
  • Diagnose and treat empirically — delay risks complications

Workup

  • NAAT: Gonorrhea + Chlamydia (cervical/vaginal swab)
  • Pelvic ultrasound: Rule out TOA (>3–4cm = inpatient therapy + possible drainage); note: US can be normal with PID
  • CBC: Leukocytosis (supportive; not required for diagnosis)
  • Pregnancy test: Mandatory — rule out ectopic pregnancy
  • Consider laparoscopy if diagnosis uncertain or no response to treatment at 72h

Empiric Therapy

SettingRegimenDuration
Outpatient (mild-moderate)Ceftriaxone 500mg IM × 1 + Doxycycline 100mg PO BID + Metronidazole 500mg PO BID14 days oral
Inpatient (severe/TOA/unable to exclude surgical emergency)Cefoxitin 2g IV q6h + Doxycycline 100mg IV/PO q12hIV until 24–48h improvement → oral doxycycline + metronidazole × 14d total
Inpatient alternativeClindamycin 900mg IV q8h + Gentamicin 2mg/kg load → 1.5mg/kg q8hIV until improvement → oral step-down

Source Control

  • TOA: IV antibiotics first; drainage if >3–4cm, not responding at 48–72h, or ruptured → IR-guided drainage or GYN surgical drainage
  • Ruptured TOA → surgical emergency
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 72h → US to reassess for TOA, consider laparoscopy to confirm diagnosis and exclude surgical emergency
  • NAAT negative for gonorrhea/chlamydia → PID still possible (polymicrobial); continue treatment
  • Recurrent PID → consider retained IUD (remove if not improving; IUD removal is no longer routine at diagnosis)
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Ruptured TOA with peritonitis → emergent GYN surgery
  • Septic shock from PID → ICU resuscitation + IV antibiotics + source control (drainage/surgery)
  • Fitz-Hugh-Curtis perihepatitis with severe pain → confirm diagnosis; responds to PID antibiotics
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • IV therapy: afebrile × 24–48h, pelvic tenderness improving → oral step-down to complete 14d
  • Outpatient: follow-up within 72h to confirm response; hospitalize if no improvement
  • Partner treatment mandatory (current and partners within 60 days)

Recognition

  • Unilateral testicular pain + swelling + tenderness; possible epididymal swelling (posterior)
  • Men <35yr: Usually STI — C. trachomatis, N. gonorrhoeae
  • Men >35yr or who practice insertive anal sex: Enteric GNR — E. coli, Klebsiella, Pseudomonas
  • All ages: Consider urinary tract source (associated UTI, recent instrumentation, urologic abnormality)
  • Distinguish from testicular torsion: Torsion = sudden onset, younger patients, absent cremasteric reflex, abnormal lie — surgical emergency

Workup

  • Scrotal ultrasound with Doppler: Increased blood flow (epididymo-orchitis) vs absent flow (torsion) — critical distinction
  • NAAT: Urine or urethral swab for gonorrhea + chlamydia
  • Urinalysis + urine culture: Rule in/out UTI/bacteriuria
  • CBC, CMP: If systemic illness
  • STI screening: Syphilis RPR, HIV

Empiric Therapy

Likely pathogenRegimenDuration
STI (<35yr or MSM)Ceftriaxone 500mg IM × 1 + Doxycycline 100mg PO BID10 days doxycycline
Enteric GNR (>35yr or anal sex)Levofloxacin 500mg PO daily10 days
Both possibleCeftriaxone 500mg IM × 1 + Levofloxacin 500mg PO daily10 days

Source Control

  • Abscess formation → scrotal ultrasound-guided aspiration or surgical drainage
  • Torsion must be excluded before any other management
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 72h → scrotal ultrasound to assess for abscess; consider scrotal MRI; reconsider diagnosis (torsion missed? fungal in immunocompromised?)
  • Gram-negative bacilli on urine culture in young man → urologic evaluation for anatomic abnormality
  • Bilateral epididymitis → uncommon; consider TB, brucellosis, fungal in right epidemiologic context
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Testicular torsion not excluded → urology consultation immediately (surgical emergency within 6h)
  • Sepsis, abscess on ultrasound → admission, IV antibiotics, urology consultation for drainage
  • Immunocompromised with no STI risk → broader coverage, ID consultation
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Afebrile, scrotal tenderness improving → continue oral course
  • Scrotal support and NSAIDs for symptomatic relief
  • Follow-up at 3 days to confirm response; if not improving — imaging
PopulationLikely PathogensRegimenDuration
Sexually active men <35 yearsC. trachomatis, N. gonorrhoeaeCeftriaxone 500 mg IM ×1 + doxycycline 100 mg PO BID10 days (doxy)
Men who have sex with men (MSM) + anal insertive sexGC, chlamydia, enteric GNRsCeftriaxone 500 mg IM ×1 + levofloxacin 500 mg PO daily10 days (levo)
Older men (>35) or urinary tract pathogensE. coli, Enterococcus, GNRsLevofloxacin 500 mg PO daily or TMP-SMX DS BID10–14 days

Scrotal ultrasound to exclude testicular torsion (surgical emergency) and abscess. NAAT testing for GC/Chlamydia. Screen for HIV and syphilis.

Recognition

  • Trichomoniasis: Yellow-green frothy vaginal discharge, dyspareunia, pruritus, "strawberry cervix"; men often asymptomatic urethritis
  • Bacterial Vaginosis: Thin homogeneous gray-white discharge, fishy odor (amine), vaginal pH >4.5; itching variable; not an STI but associated with sexual activity
  • BV Amsel criteria (3 of 4): Homogeneous discharge, clue cells on wet prep, pH >4.5, positive whiff test (KOH)

Workup

  • Trichomonas NAAT: Most sensitive — urine or vaginal/urethral swab; wet prep (motile organisms, 60% sensitive)
  • BV: Wet prep (clue cells, absent lactobacilli); Amsel criteria; pH >4.5; whiff test positive
  • Always co-test: gonorrhea + chlamydia NAAT, HIV, syphilis RPR

Empiric Therapy

ConditionRegimenDuration
Trichomoniasis (women)Metronidazole 500mg PO BID7 days
Trichomoniasis (men)Metronidazole 2g PO × 1Single dose
Trichomoniasis (HIV+)Metronidazole 500mg PO BID7 days
BVMetronidazole 500mg PO BID7 days
BV (alternative)Clindamycin 300mg PO BID7 days
BV in pregnancyMetronidazole 500mg PO BID7 days (all trimesters safe)
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Trichomonas not responding → resistance possible; tinidazole 2g PO daily × 7 days; CDC consultation for refractory cases
  • Recurrent BV → long treatment (metronidazole gel QHS × 21d) or vaginal boric acid 600mg QHS × 21d; evaluate partner
  • Persistent symptoms after treatment → test-of-cure for Trichomonas (NAAT 2 weeks post-treatment); high reinfection rate
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Trichomonas in pregnancy → treat; test-of-cure; partner treatment mandatory
  • Pelvic actinomycosis (IUD-associated BV-like presentation with induration) → ID consultation; prolonged antibiotics + IUD removal
  • BV with preterm birth history in pregnancy → treat regardless of symptoms
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Partner treatment for Trichomonas mandatory (EPT acceptable); abstain until both treated
  • BV: partner treatment not routinely recommended
  • Avoid alcohol during metronidazole (disulfiram reaction)
🦴 MSK & Spinal
● Time-critical
🚨 Neurological Emergency: Paralysis present >24–36 hours is often irreversible even with surgical decompression. Early recognition and urgent decision-making are essential.

Anatomy — Why It Matters

  • Posterior SEA: More common (~75%); posterior to spinal cord/thecal sac; direct compression mechanism; more amenable to surgical decompression
  • Anterior SEA: Less common (~25%); anterior to cord; often from vertebral osteomyelitis with anterior epidural spread; cord ischemia via anterior spinal artery compression contributes to deficits; surgery more complex
  • Cervical/thoracic involvement → cord compression → myelopathy; Lumbar → cauda equina syndrome (more recoverable)

Clinical Stages (Heusner Classification)

StageFindingsUrgency
IBack pain, fever, spinal tenderness — no neurologic deficitUrgent workup; may trial medical therapy in select cases
IIStage I + nerve root pain (radiculopathy)Urgent MRI; surgical consultation
IIIMotor weakness, sensory deficit, bowel/bladder dysfunctionEMERGENT — surgical decompression target <12–24h
IVComplete paralysisEmergency; decompression within hours — outcomes poor if paralysis >24–36h

Diagnosis

Classic Triad (present together in only <15% of cases)
  • Fever
  • Back pain (present in ~95% — most common and reliable symptom)
  • Neurologic deficit (late finding)
Labs
  • ESR >30 mm/h (sensitivity ~90%), CRP elevated (>95%), WBC elevated in ~60–70%
  • Blood cultures ×2 — positive in ~60–70%; high yield given predominance of S. aureus bacteremia
  • Peripheral WBC may be normal — do not use to exclude diagnosis
Imaging
  • MRI entire spine with gadolinium — gold standard; sensitivity ~90%, specificity ~90% for SEA; image entire spine (30% of SEA multifocal)
  • T1: isointense or hypointense epidural collection; T1 with gadolinium: rim-enhancing abscess
  • T2: hyperintense collection; adjacent vertebral T2 signal = concurrent vertebral osteomyelitis (common)
  • CT myelogram: alternative if MRI unavailable/contraindicated

Microbiology

Organism%Source
S. aureus (MSSA + MRSA)60–70%Skin/soft tissue, IV access, IVDU, hematogenous
Gram-negative rods~15%UTI, GU procedures, IVDU
Streptococcus~10%Dental, pharyngeal, GI source
Coag-neg Staph~5%Post-procedure/post-op
Anaerobes, polymicrobial<5%Decubitus ulcers, perirectal abscess

Risk Factors

  • IVDU (highest single risk — up to 30% of SEA cases in some centers)
  • Diabetes mellitus, chronic renal failure, alcoholism
  • Immunosuppression (HIV, steroids, chemotherapy)
  • Spinal procedures: epidural catheter, lumbar puncture, spinal injections, spine surgery
  • Recent bacteremia (S. aureus SAB → SEA in ~5–10%)
  • Degenerative spine disease (microtrauma facilitates seeding)

Management — Surgical vs Medical Decision

Surgical Decompression Indications (most patients)
  • Any neurologic deficit (Stage III–IV) — emergent
  • Neurological deterioration on medical therapy
  • Stage II with MRI showing significant cord/cauda equina compression
  • Failed medical therapy (>72h without improvement, rising CRP)
  • Cervical SEA (high risk of rapid deterioration)
  • Abscess amenable to CT-guided drainage (as alternative/bridge to open surgery in select cases)
Medical Management Alone — Strict Criteria (IDSA-aligned)
  • No neurologic deficit (Stage I)
  • Thoracic or lumbar location (not cervical)
  • Small abscess without significant cord compression on MRI
  • Prohibitive surgical risk (multiple comorbidities, high anesthesia risk)
  • Patient declines surgery
  • Requires: Neurological checks every 4–8h; immediate surgical reassessment if any deterioration; daily CRP monitoring
Empiric Antibiotics
  • Vancomycin 15–20 mg/kg IV q8–12h (AUC-guided, target AUC 400–600) + Ceftriaxone 2 g IV q12h
  • If Pseudomonas risk (IVDU, structural lung disease, prior Pseudomonas): replace ceftriaxone with cefepime 2 g IV q8h
  • Start immediately after blood cultures drawn — do not delay for imaging in neurologically deteriorating patient
  • Hold antibiotics until CT-biopsy only if Stage I and stable and blood cultures negative
Duration of Therapy
ScenarioDuration
SEA without vertebral osteomyelitis, surgically drained, cultures identified4–6 weeks total
SEA + concurrent vertebral osteomyelitis (most cases)6–8 weeks (vertebral osteo dictates duration)
SEA medically managed without surgery6–8 weeks; some experts recommend 8–12 weeks
Oral step-down per OVIVA criteriaAfter 2–4 weeks IV if clinically responding and susceptible organism with high-bioavailability PO agent available

Prognosis

  • Mortality: ~5–15% overall; higher with delayed diagnosis, septic shock, advanced age
  • Neurological recovery: directly correlates with pre-operative neurological grade and duration of deficits
  • Stage I–II: most patients recover fully with prompt treatment
  • Stage III: 50–75% meaningful recovery if decompressed within 12–24h
  • Stage IV (>24–36h paralysis): rarely recover motor function even with surgery; decompression may still be performed for pain, bladder/bowel preservation

Cross-link: ⬆ Vertebral Osteomyelitis (frequently concurrent) | High-Yield Syndromes → Spinal Epidural Abscess (existing collapsible)

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Neurologic deficit worsening despite antibiotics → urgent MRI + neurosurgery regardless of initial decision to manage medically
  • Not improving at 48h on antibiotics → repeat MRI; ensure adequate drainage if collection present
  • MRSA expected but blood cultures growing MSSA → de-escalate to β-lactam (superior to vancomycin)
  • Cultures negative → consider MTb (especially if risk factors, apical or thoracic); send MTb PCR from blood/urine
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Any new or worsening neurologic deficit → emergent neurosurgery regardless of prior conservative decision
  • Bladder or bowel dysfunction (cauda equina) → surgical emergency within hours
  • Large abscess with cord compression on MRI → surgical decompression
  • Failed 24–48h medical management → move to surgical drainage
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • MSSA confirmed → switch from vancomycin to nafcillin or cefazolin (same day)
  • Clinically improved, afebrile, CRP falling, neurologically stable → transition planning at 6–8 weeks (OVIVA trial: oral equivalent for stable osteomyelitis)
  • No neurologic deficit + small phlegmon → consider IV-to-oral switch after clinical stability achieved

Classification

TypeSourcePathogensGeography
PrimaryHematogenous seeding of psoas muscle (no identifiable adjacent source)S. aureus (MRSA/MSSA) > Streptococcus, GNRDeveloping countries, IVDU; tropical regions (Mycobacterium, Salmonella)
SecondaryDirect extension from adjacent structureEnteric organisms, Bacteroides, Streptococcus, Mycobacterium TBDeveloped countries — most psoas abscesses are secondary

Secondary Sources (most common in developed world)

  • Crohn's disease / IBD (most common) — often right-sided
  • Vertebral osteomyelitis / spondylodiscitis (TB or pyogenic)
  • Diverticulitis, appendicitis, perforated viscus
  • Renal/perinephric abscess
  • Infected hip prosthesis, sacroiliac joint infection

Clinical Features

  • Classic triad: Fever + back/flank pain + hip pain/flexion contracture — present in only ~30%
  • Psoas sign: Passive hip extension causes pain (right psoas sign in appendicitis also)
  • Patients often hold hip in flexion (psoas spasm); limp; knee pain (referred)
  • Can be remarkably subtle — vague low back or groin pain for weeks

Diagnosis

  • CT abdomen/pelvis with contrast: Imaging of choice — hypodense collection within psoas ± gas; also identifies underlying cause
  • MRI: Superior for evaluating vertebral involvement (osteomyelitis/discitis) and adjacent soft tissue
  • Blood cultures: Positive in ~30–50%
  • Aspiration culture: Essential — guides antibiotic selection; MTb culture if TB suspected (up to 6 weeks to grow)

Treatment

  • CT-guided percutaneous drainage + IV antibiotics: First-line for most cases; cure rate >90% for primary abscess
  • Empiric antibiotics: Vancomycin + Ceftriaxone (cover S. aureus + enteric GNR); add Metronidazole if secondary to GI source
  • Surgical drainage: For multiloculated abscess, failed percutaneous drainage, or when source requires surgical correction (e.g., resection of Crohn's segment)
  • TB psoas abscess: RIPE therapy × 6–9 months; percutaneous drainage of large collections; do NOT rush to surgery
  • Duration of antibiotics: 4–6 weeks; longer for vertebral osteomyelitis source (8–12 weeks)
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving after drainage + antibiotics → re-image: multiloculated? inadequate drainage? second collection?
  • Crohn's disease source → GI surgery consultation; medical treatment of underlying IBD essential
  • MTb not excluded → send aspirate for MTb culture (6 weeks), AFB stain, MTb PCR
  • Poor drainage catheter output → may be too viscous; consider upsizing or surgical drainage
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Septic shock → ICU resuscitation before or concurrent with drainage
  • Vertebral osteomyelitis with neurologic compromise → neurosurgery
  • Failed percutaneous drainage (multiloculated, viscous pus) → surgical drainage
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Drain output minimal, collection resolved on imaging, afebrile → drain removal
  • Culture-directed: 4–6 weeks total antibiotics; oral step-down after clinical stability
  • MTb psoas abscess: oral RIPE therapy; surgery rarely needed
○ Condition-specific

Diagnosis — Distinguishing Septic from Crystal Arthropathy

⚠️ Critical Pearl: Septic arthritis and gout/pseudogout frequently coexist (10–20% of cases). Crystals on synovial fluid analysis do NOT rule out concurrent infection — send cultures on all inflamed joints.
ParameterNormalNon-inflammatory (OA)Inflammatory (Crystal/RA)Septic
WBC (cells/mm³)<200<2,0002,000–50,000>50,000 (often >100,000)
PMN%<25%<25%50–75%>75–90%
Glucose= serum= serumSlightly ↓↓↓ (<50% serum)
AppearanceClearClear/yellowYellow, turbidPurulent, opaque

No WBC threshold is diagnostic — risk increases with higher counts, but up to 30% of septic joints have WBC <50,000, especially with immunosuppression, gonococcal arthritis, or early presentation. Clinical gestalt + Gram stain + culture.

Microbiology by Population

PopulationLikely Organisms
Adults (most common)S. aureus (MSSA/MRSA) — 40–50%; Streptococcus spp. 20%
Sexually active adults <40 yrsN. gonorrhoeae — most common in this age group; consider DGI (migratory arthralgia + pustular rash + tenosynovitis)
Elderly / prosthetic joint adjacentCoagulase-negative Staph, gram-negative rods
IVDUS. aureus (including MRSA), Pseudomonas, Candida; unusual joints (SC, SI joints, sternoclavicular)
After intra-articular injectionCoag-negative Staph, S. aureus, skin flora
Immunocompromised / travelSalmonella, Brucella, fungi, mycobacteria

Management

Joint Drainage — Critical and Urgent
  • Drainage is required — not optional. Purulent synovial fluid destroys cartilage within hours to days via proteolytic enzymes and cytokines
  • Daily needle aspiration to dryness for accessible joints (knee, shoulder, ankle, wrist) — continue until fluid clears
  • Surgical arthroscopic/open drainage: Hip (almost always), shoulder (if inaccessible/loculated), joints not responding to needle aspiration ×3, prosthetic joint adjacent infection
  • Send fluid: cell count/differential, Gram stain + culture (aerobic/anaerobic), crystal analysis (CPP, urate)
Empiric Antibiotics
Clinical SettingRegimen
Gram-positive cocci on Gram stain OR no Gram stain result, non-gonococcal suspicionVancomycin 15–20 mg/kg IV q8–12h (target AUC 400–600)
Gram-negative rods on Gram stainCeftriaxone 2 g IV q24h or cefepime 2 g IV q8h (if Pseudomonas risk)
Possible gonococcal (sexually active, DGI presentation)Ceftriaxone 1 g IV q24h — step down after 24–48h improvement
No organisms, immunocompetentVancomycin ± ceftriaxone (pending cultures)
Definitive Therapy & Duration
OrganismIV PhasePO Step-DownTotal Duration
MSSACefazolin 2 g IV q8h × 2 weeksCephalexin 1 g QID or dicloxacillin 500 mg QID3–4 weeks total
MRSAVancomycin or daptomycin × 2 weeksTMP-SMX DS 2 tabs BID + rifampin 300 mg BID (if hardware); or linezolid 600 mg BID3–4 weeks total
StreptococcusCeftriaxone 2 g IV q24h × 2 weeksAmoxicillin 1 g TID or cephalexin 500 mg QID2–3 weeks total
N. gonorrhoeaeCeftriaxone 1 g IV q24h × 1–2 daysCefixime 400 mg PO BID7–14 days total
GNR (non-Pseudomonas)Ceftriaxone 2 g IV q24hFQ if susceptible; TMP-SMX3–4 weeks total

OVIVA Trial (NEJM 2019): Early oral switch (at 1 week) non-inferior to 6 weeks IV for bone/joint infections overall. For septic arthritis specifically: oral step-down is reasonable once clinically improving and organism identified with known sensitivities. Requires agent with excellent bioavailability (>80%): FQs, TMP-SMX, linezolid, clindamycin, metronidazole.

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 48h despite arthrocentesis + antibiotics → repeat aspiration/imaging; excluded crystal arthropathy? Brucella, Lyme, fungal in endemic/travel context?
  • WBC <50,000 in joint fluid → gout/pseudogout more likely; crystal analysis critical; both can coexist
  • Gonorrhea negative but sexually active young adult + polyarthralgia + skin lesions → DGI; treat empirically with ceftriaxone
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Osteomyelitis extension (MRI showing bony involvement) → orthopedic surgery, prolonged antibiotics
  • Failed needle drainage (hip, shoulder, reaccumulating) → arthroscopic or open surgical washout
  • Septic shock from septic arthritis → ICU resuscitation + emergent joint drainage
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • MSSA confirmed → de-escalate from vancomycin to nafcillin or cefazolin same day
  • Joint sterile on repeat aspiration, improving → IV to oral step-down at 2 weeks (OVIVA trial supports oral for selected MSK infections)
  • Total duration: 2–4 weeks for native joint septic arthritis; longer if osteomyelitis present

Classification

TypeMechanismCommon BonesTypical Population
HematogenousBacteremia seeds metaphysisVertebrae (adults), long bones (children)Adults: vertebral osteo; IVDU; hemodialysis
Contiguous (no vascular insufficiency)Spread from adjacent infected tissue, trauma, surgerySkull, mandible, sternum, pelvis, long bonesPost-op, trauma, bite wounds, overlying SSTI
Contiguous (with vascular insufficiency)Ischemia + infectionFoot (metatarsals, calcaneus)Diabetics, PAD — see Diabetic Foot section

Diagnosis

Labs
  • ESR and CRP — elevated in most; CRP more sensitive for acute changes and monitoring treatment response (normalizes faster than ESR)
  • WBC — often normal or mildly elevated; low sensitivity
  • Blood cultures ×2 — positive in ~40–50% of hematogenous osteomyelitis (highest yield before antibiotics)
  • Procalcitonin — less validated than for bloodstream infections; may be helpful in monitoring
Imaging — Sequence Matters
ModalitySensitivitySpecificityWhen to Use
Plain X-ray~60%~75%Always first — rules out fracture, tumor; lytic changes lag 10–21 days behind infection
MRI with gadolinium~90%~85%Gold standard — best for soft tissue extension, marrow involvement, epidural spread; order for all suspected osteomyelitis unless contraindicated
CT scan70–80%~90%Better cortical detail; useful when MRI contraindicated; guides biopsy
Bone scan (Tc-99m)~90%~70%Multi-focal disease; MRI unavailable; high false-positive rate with fracture/arthropathy
WBC-labeled scan (In-111 or Tc-99m HMPAO)~80%~90%Diabetic foot, spinal implants — more specific than bone scan; less affected by non-infectious bone pathology
PET-CT (FDG)~95%~90%Chronic osteomyelitis, implant assessment; not widely available; excellent for multi-focal workup
Bone Biopsy — Critical for Diagnosis and Guiding Therapy
Do not treat without microbiologic diagnosis if at all possible. Empiric therapy without cultures leads to treatment failure, prolonged therapy, and drug toxicity. Hold antibiotics until biopsy if patient is clinically stable.
  • CT-guided percutaneous bone biopsy preferred — sensitivity ~50–70% for culture
  • Open surgical biopsy if CT-guided fails or surgical debridement planned
  • Send: aerobic + anaerobic cultures, AFB culture + stain, fungal culture, histopathology (acute inflammatory cells, necrotic bone = osteomyelitis)
  • If blood cultures positive with typical organism (S. aureus, Strep) and clinical picture consistent → bone biopsy may be deferred; consult ID
  • Hold antibiotics 48–72h before biopsy if clinically stable to maximize culture yield

Empiric Therapy (Pending Cultures)

Clinical SettingRegimen
Standard (MRSA prevalence <20%, no prior MDR)Cefazolin 2 g IV q8h (add ceftriaxone for GNR coverage if suspected)
MRSA risk or unknown prevalenceVancomycin 15–20 mg/kg IV q8–12h (AUC-guided dosing preferred)
IVDU, immunocompromised, prior GNRVancomycin + cefepime (or pip-tazo for anaerobic/polymicrobial coverage)

Definitive Therapy & Oral Step-Down (OVIVA)

OrganismIV OptionOral Option (OVIVA criteria)Duration
MSSACefazolin 2 g IV q8hCephalexin 1 g QID or dicloxacillin 500 mg QID (or levofloxacin + rifampin if implant present)6 weeks total (3–4 weeks if complete surgical resection)
MRSAVancomycin (AUC 400–600) or daptomycin 6 mg/kg IV q24hTMP-SMX DS 2 tabs BID + rifampin 300–450 mg BID; or linezolid 600 mg BID (cost/toxicity limiting)6 weeks; indefinite suppression if hardware retained with high relapse risk
MSSA/MRSA + hardware (no removal)Vancomycin or cefazolin per aboveAdd rifampin for biofilm penetration (rifampin always with companion drug — never monotherapy)6 weeks → chronic oral suppression
Pseudomonas aeruginosaCefepime 2 g IV q8h or pip-tazoCiprofloxacin 750 mg PO BID (if MIC ≤0.5 mcg/mL)6 weeks
StreptococcusCeftriaxone 2 g IV q24h or Penicillin G 4M units IV q4hAmoxicillin 1 g TID4–6 weeks
Anaerobes (diabetic foot, oral flora)Pip-tazo or ampicillin-sulbactamAmoxicillin-clavulanate 875/125 mg BID; or metronidazole 500 mg TID4–6 weeks

OVIVA Trial (NEJM 2019): 1054 patients with bone/joint infections randomized to IV or early oral switch at 1 week. Treatment failure 14.6% oral vs 13.2% IV — oral non-inferior (90-day follow-up). Key requirement: oral agent with >80% bioavailability and susceptible organism. Agents that qualify: FQs, TMP-SMX, linezolid, metronidazole, clindamycin, co-amoxiclav.

Monitoring Treatment Response

  • CRP at baseline, 1 week, 3 weeks, end of therapy — expect 50% reduction by week 2
  • ESR lags — may not normalize until 4–6 weeks after cure
  • Clinical: fever resolution, pain improvement, wound/sinus tract healing
  • Repeat MRI: Not routinely recommended for monitoring — changes persist for months even after cure; repeat only if clinical worsening or new symptoms
  • Inflammatory markers that fail to trend down → treatment failure, resistant organism, undrained focus

Surgical Indications

  • Failure of antibiotic therapy
  • Chronic osteomyelitis with sequestrum (devitalized/avascular bone that serves as nidus — must be removed)
  • Spinal osteomyelitis with epidural extension, neurologic compromise, or spinal instability
  • Infected hardware that cannot be retained
  • Insufficient bone sample for microbiological diagnosis
  • Abscess formation requiring drainage

Cross-link: High-Yield Syndromes → Diabetic Foot Infections (probe-to-bone, imaging approach)

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 4–6 weeks → MRI to reassess; inadequate debridement? Resistant organism? Wrong diagnosis (Pott disease, malignancy, Charcot)?
  • Blood cultures negative + no biopsy → treat empirically but consider IR-guided bone biopsy before committing to 6-week course
  • MSSA but still on vancomycin → de-escalate immediately; β-lactam superior
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Bone necrosis / sequestrum on imaging → orthopedic surgical debridement; antibiotics alone will fail
  • Vascular insufficiency (diabetic foot) → vascular surgery consultation; revascularization before antibiotics can be effective
  • Spinal osteomyelitis with neurologic deficit → neurosurgery; emergent decompression if cord compromised
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • MSSA confirmed → switch from vancomycin to cefazolin or nafcillin same day
  • Clinically improving + CRP declining at 2 weeks → consider oral step-down (OVIVA trial: ciprofloxacin + rifampin or co-trimoxazole + rifampin equivalent to IV for stable MSK infections)
  • Total duration: 6 weeks hematogenous; 4–8 weeks contiguous; longer if hardware retained or necrotic bone present

Also called pyogenic vertebral osteomyelitis (PVO), infectious spondylitis, or spondylodiscitis. Distinct from but often co-existing with spinal epidural abscess (SEA). ESC/ESCMID 2015 Guidelines.

Clinical Presentation

  • Back/neck pain (most common, present in >90%) — localized, often worse with movement and percussion over spine
  • Fever (in only 50–60% of cases — absence does not exclude diagnosis)
  • Subacute onset typically — average 6–8 weeks from symptom onset to diagnosis (frequently delayed)
  • Neurologic symptoms: radiculopathy, weakness, bowel/bladder dysfunction → indicates epidural extension
  • Bacteremia source may be identified: UTI, IVDU, recent dental procedure, IV line, skin/soft tissue

Risk Factors

  • Diabetes mellitus, immunosuppression, malignancy
  • Intravenous drug use (IVDU)
  • Recent bacteremia (S. aureus bacteremia → vertebral seeding in ~12% of cases)
  • Hemodialysis (staphylococcal bacteremia via vascular access)
  • Recent spinal instrumentation or procedure
  • Age >60 (degenerative changes facilitate hematogenous seeding)
  • Urologic procedures (GN bacteremia seeding)
📌 High-Yield Pearl: Any patient with S. aureus bacteremia should have the spine evaluated clinically and with MRI if there is any back pain, even mild. S. aureus has the highest propensity for vertebral seeding of all bacteremic organisms.

Microbiology

OrganismFrequencyKey Association
S. aureus (MSSA + MRSA)40–50%Most common; hematogenous; IVDU; hemodialysis; skin source
Coagulase-negative Staph10–15%Post-spinal instrumentation
Streptococcus spp.10–15%Dental source; GI manipulation
Gram-negative rods (E. coli, Klebsiella, Pseudomonas)15–20%UTI source; IVDU; urologic procedures
Brucella spp.VariableEndemic areas (Middle East, Mediterranean); livestock exposure; underpasteurized dairy; often involves lumbar spine + sacroiliac joint
Mycobacterium tuberculosis (Pott's disease)Variable globallyThoracic most common; gibbus deformity; psoas abscess; TB exposure history
Fungi (Aspergillus, Candida)RareImmunocompromised; post-neurosurgery; IVDU

Diagnosis

Labs
  • ESR (>50 mm/h in >90%), CRP (elevated in >95%), WBC (often normal or mild elevation)
  • Blood cultures ×2 before antibiotics — positive in 50–70%; highest-yield single test if positive avoids biopsy
  • HIV, RPR (rule out syphilitic spondylitis in endemic areas)
  • Brucella serology (Rose-Bengal test, SAT titers) if exposure history
  • Quantiferon/T-SPOT if TB risk factors; AFB smear/culture from other sites
Imaging
  • MRI spine with gadolinium (entire spine) — gold standard; T2 hyperintensity in disc + adjacent vertebral endplates (discitis pattern); vertebral endplate erosion; epidural/paraspinal extension; sensitivity ~96%, specificity ~92%
  • CT-guided biopsy for microbiological diagnosis if blood cultures negative
  • Plain films: late finding — loss of disc height, endplate erosion, may not appear for 3–6 weeks
  • CT scan: bone destruction detail, surgical planning; less sensitive than MRI for early disease
When to Biopsy vs Treat Empirically
  • Biopsy first (hold antibiotics if clinically stable) — blood cultures negative, diagnosis uncertain, atypical organisms suspected (TB, Brucella, fungi)
  • Treat empirically without biopsy — hemodynamic instability, neurologic deterioration, blood cultures positive with known pathogen and consistent clinical picture
  • CT-guided percutaneous needle biopsy: sensitivity ~50–65% (lower in prior antibiotic use); consider repeat biopsy or surgical biopsy if first negative with high suspicion

Treatment

OrganismIV TherapyOral Step-Down (OVIVA)Duration
MSSACefazolin 2 g IV q8h × 2–4 weeksCephalexin 1 g QID or dicloxacillin 500 mg QID6 weeks total
MRSAVancomycin (AUC-guided) × 2–4 weeksTMP-SMX DS 2 tabs BID + rifampin 300 mg BID; or linezolid 600 mg BID6 weeks total
StreptococcusCeftriaxone 2 g IV q24h × 2–4 weeksAmoxicillin 1 g TID6 weeks total
GNR (E. coli, Klebsiella)Ceftriaxone 2 g IV q24hCiprofloxacin 500–750 mg BID (if susceptible)6 weeks total
PseudomonasCefepime 2 g IV q8h or pip-tazoCiprofloxacin 750 mg BID (MIC ≤0.5)6–8 weeks
BrucellaDoxycycline 100 mg PO BID + rifampin 600 mg PO daily (or doxy + streptomycin IM × 2–3 weeks)Continue PO combination6–8 weeks minimum; ID consult
TB (Pott's disease)RIPE induction × 2 monthsRI continuation12–18 months total; orthopedic/neurosurgery consult

Surgical Indications

  • Neurologic deficit or deterioration — urgent decompression; do not delay
  • Spinal instability or deformity (significant vertebral body destruction)
  • Failure of medical therapy (persistent pain, fever, bacteremia, rising CRP after 4–6 weeks)
  • Epidural abscess requiring drainage (see Spinal Epidural Abscess section)
  • Large paraspinal abscess not amenable to CT-guided drainage
  • Need for definitive microbiological diagnosis when CT-guided biopsy fails

Monitoring & Follow-up

  • CRP weekly during treatment — most reliable marker of response; should normalize by weeks 4–6
  • ESR monthly — lags; may remain mildly elevated after cure
  • Repeat MRI: At 4–6 weeks if clinical response inadequate (persistent fever, pain, rising CRP); not routinely in responding patients — imaging changes persist for months after microbiological cure
  • Spine consultation for all cases — assess for instability, need for bracing, surgical candidacy
  • Follow-up blood cultures after 48–72h of therapy if initial bacteremia — confirm clearance (especially S. aureus)

Cross-link: ⬆ Spinal Epidural Abscess (often concurrent) | 🔧 LP technique if CSF evaluation needed

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • MRI shows disc destruction but blood cultures negative → IR-guided CT biopsy of disc/bone before antibiotics if stable; yield 50–70%
  • Not responding to appropriate antibiotics → resistant organism? Brucella? TB (Pott disease — apical or thoracic location, psoas abscess)? Fungal?
  • MSSA on cultures but still on vancomycin → switch to nafcillin/cefazolin immediately
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • New or worsening neurologic deficit → emergent neurosurgery; cord compression requires decompression within hours
  • Epidural abscess extending >3 levels or causing myelopathy → surgical emergency
  • Hemodynamic instability or septic shock → ICU resuscitation + urgent source control (drain epidural collection if present)
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • MSSA confirmed → oral step-down with ciprofloxacin + rifampin at 2 weeks if clinically stable and CRP improving (OVIVA/ZEPHyR data)
  • No neurologic deficit, pain improving, CRP declining → transition to outpatient OPAT or oral at 6 weeks
  • Total duration: minimum 6 weeks; 8–12 weeks if epidural extension, hardware, or slow response
🚨 Neurological Emergency: Paralysis present >24–36 hours is often irreversible even with surgical decompression. Early recognition and urgent decision-making are essential.

Anatomy — Why It Matters

  • Posterior SEA: More common (~75%); posterior to spinal cord/thecal sac; direct compression mechanism; more amenable to surgical decompression
  • Anterior SEA: Less common (~25%); anterior to cord; often from vertebral osteomyelitis with anterior epidural spread; cord ischemia via anterior spinal artery compression contributes to deficits; surgery more complex
  • Cervical/thoracic involvement → cord compression → myelopathy; Lumbar → cauda equina syndrome (more recoverable)

Clinical Stages (Heusner Classification)

StageFindingsUrgency
IBack pain, fever, spinal tenderness — no neurologic deficitUrgent workup; may trial medical therapy in select cases
IIStage I + nerve root pain (radiculopathy)Urgent MRI; surgical consultation
IIIMotor weakness, sensory deficit, bowel/bladder dysfunctionEMERGENT — surgical decompression target <12–24h
IVComplete paralysisEmergency; decompression within hours — outcomes poor if paralysis >24–36h

Diagnosis

Classic Triad (present together in only <15% of cases)
  • Fever
  • Back pain (present in ~95% — most common and reliable symptom)
  • Neurologic deficit (late finding)
Labs
  • ESR >30 mm/h (sensitivity ~90%), CRP elevated (>95%), WBC elevated in ~60–70%
  • Blood cultures ×2 — positive in ~60–70%; high yield given predominance of S. aureus bacteremia
  • Peripheral WBC may be normal — do not use to exclude diagnosis
Imaging
  • MRI entire spine with gadolinium — gold standard; sensitivity ~90%, specificity ~90% for SEA; image entire spine (30% of SEA multifocal)
  • T1: isointense or hypointense epidural collection; T1 with gadolinium: rim-enhancing abscess
  • T2: hyperintense collection; adjacent vertebral T2 signal = concurrent vertebral osteomyelitis (common)
  • CT myelogram: alternative if MRI unavailable/contraindicated

Microbiology

Organism%Source
S. aureus (MSSA + MRSA)60–70%Skin/soft tissue, IV access, IVDU, hematogenous
Gram-negative rods~15%UTI, GU procedures, IVDU
Streptococcus~10%Dental, pharyngeal, GI source
Coag-neg Staph~5%Post-procedure/post-op
Anaerobes, polymicrobial<5%Decubitus ulcers, perirectal abscess

Risk Factors

  • IVDU (highest single risk — up to 30% of SEA cases in some centers)
  • Diabetes mellitus, chronic renal failure, alcoholism
  • Immunosuppression (HIV, steroids, chemotherapy)
  • Spinal procedures: epidural catheter, lumbar puncture, spinal injections, spine surgery
  • Recent bacteremia (S. aureus SAB → SEA in ~5–10%)
  • Degenerative spine disease (microtrauma facilitates seeding)

Management — Surgical vs Medical Decision

Surgical Decompression Indications (most patients)
  • Any neurologic deficit (Stage III–IV) — emergent
  • Neurological deterioration on medical therapy
  • Stage II with MRI showing significant cord/cauda equina compression
  • Failed medical therapy (>72h without improvement, rising CRP)
  • Cervical SEA (high risk of rapid deterioration)
  • Abscess amenable to CT-guided drainage (as alternative/bridge to open surgery in select cases)
Medical Management Alone — Strict Criteria (IDSA-aligned)
  • No neurologic deficit (Stage I)
  • Thoracic or lumbar location (not cervical)
  • Small abscess without significant cord compression on MRI
  • Prohibitive surgical risk (multiple comorbidities, high anesthesia risk)
  • Patient declines surgery
  • Requires: Neurological checks every 4–8h; immediate surgical reassessment if any deterioration; daily CRP monitoring
Empiric Antibiotics
  • Vancomycin 15–20 mg/kg IV q8–12h (AUC-guided, target AUC 400–600) + Ceftriaxone 2 g IV q12h
  • If Pseudomonas risk (IVDU, structural lung disease, prior Pseudomonas): replace ceftriaxone with cefepime 2 g IV q8h
  • Start immediately after blood cultures drawn — do not delay for imaging in neurologically deteriorating patient
  • Hold antibiotics until CT-biopsy only if Stage I and stable and blood cultures negative
Duration of Therapy
ScenarioDuration
SEA without vertebral osteomyelitis, surgically drained, cultures identified4–6 weeks total
SEA + concurrent vertebral osteomyelitis (most cases)6–8 weeks (vertebral osteo dictates duration)
SEA medically managed without surgery6–8 weeks; some experts recommend 8–12 weeks
Oral step-down per OVIVA criteriaAfter 2–4 weeks IV if clinically responding and susceptible organism with high-bioavailability PO agent available

Prognosis

  • Mortality: ~5–15% overall; higher with delayed diagnosis, septic shock, advanced age
  • Neurological recovery: directly correlates with pre-operative neurological grade and duration of deficits
  • Stage I–II: most patients recover fully with prompt treatment
  • Stage III: 50–75% meaningful recovery if decompressed within 12–24h
  • Stage IV (>24–36h paralysis): rarely recover motor function even with surgery; decompression may still be performed for pain, bladder/bowel preservation

Cross-link: ⬆ Vertebral Osteomyelitis (frequently concurrent) | High-Yield Syndromes → Spinal Epidural Abscess (existing collapsible)

Full PJI content (MSIS 2018 criteria, DAIR vs two-stage exchange, antibiotic principles, rifampin for biofilm, suppressive therapy) is covered in High-Yield Syndromes → Prosthetic Joint Infection.

Key Pearls for Hospitalists

  • Always send synovial fluid cell count/differential AND culture when tapping a prosthetic joint — thresholds for diagnosis are lower than native joint (WBC >3,000 cells/mm³)
  • S. aureus bacteremia + prosthetic joint = PJI until proven otherwise — TEE, orthopedic consultation
  • Do NOT start antibiotics before joint aspiration in a stable patient — culture is essential for treatment decisions
  • Rifampin is never given as monotherapy for biofilm-associated infections — always paired with a companion drug (FQ, TMP-SMX) to prevent resistance
  • Two-stage exchange is preferred over DAIR for chronic PJI (>4 weeks from implant) and high-virulence organisms (MRSA, resistant GNR)

Recognition

  • Superficial bursal sac infection; prepatellar and olecranon most common
  • Warm, fluctuant, tender swelling directly over bursa; limited pain with joint motion (differentiates from septic arthritis)
  • Risk: direct trauma, overlying skin abrasion, repetitive pressure, steroid injection, gout
  • Causative organism: S. aureus (~80%); MRSA in community-acquired or healthcare-associated; Streptococci; GNR rare
  • Distinguish from septic arthritis: joint ROM usually preserved in septic bursitis; aspiration location different

Workup

  • Bursal aspiration: Cell count, Gram stain, culture, glucose, crystals (rule out gout/pseudogout co-infection)
  • WBC >2,000/µL suggests infection (lower threshold than joint); Gram stain positive in ~50%
  • X-ray: Assess for gas, foreign body, bony involvement
  • MRI: If deep bursal involvement or osteomyelitis suspected
  • Blood cultures: if systemic toxicity

Empiric Therapy

SeverityRegimenDuration
Mild (no systemic symptoms)TMP-SMX DS PO BID (covers MRSA) OR Doxycycline 100mg PO BID7–14 days
ModerateTMP-SMX DS PO BID + serial aspiration10–14 days
Systemic/severe/MRSA concernVancomycin 15–20 mg/kg IV q8–12hUntil improving → oral step-down; 14 days total
MSSA confirmedDicloxacillin 500mg PO QID or Cephalexin 500mg PO QID14 days

Source Control

  • Needle aspiration (serial): Drainage of purulent material is central to treatment; repeat every 1–3 days until dry
  • Surgical bursectomy: for refractory cases, bursal wall thickening, or recurrent infection
  • Drainage tube: if fluctuant and large collection
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving after 48–72h of antibiotics + aspiration → MRSA? Inadequate drainage? Osteomyelitis extension (MRI)?
  • Culture grows unexpected organism (GNR, fungus) → immunocompromised? Direct inoculation?
  • Gout/pseudogout crystals on aspiration → may coexist with infection; treat both
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Septic arthritis cannot be excluded (joint involvement on exam or imaging) → orthopedic consultation + joint aspiration
  • Osteomyelitis on MRI → orthopedic surgery + prolonged antibiotics
  • Necrotizing bursitis (rare) → surgical debridement
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Bursal sac dry on aspiration, afebrile, erythema resolving → continue oral antibiotics to complete 10–14 days
  • MSSA confirmed → de-escalate from TMP-SMX/vancomycin to β-lactam
  • Immobilization and elevation of affected extremity during treatment

Recognition

  • Sterile arthritis occurring 1–4 weeks after genitourinary (Chlamydia, gonorrhea) or enteric infection (Salmonella, Shigella, Campylobacter, Yersinia)
  • Classic triad (Reiter): arthritis + urethritis + conjunctivitis — present together in minority of cases
  • Arthritis: asymmetric, oligoarticular, lower extremities predominant; heel pain (enthesitis — Achilles/plantar fascia); dactylitis ("sausage toe")
  • Mucocutaneous: circinate balanitis, keratoderma blennorrhagica (hyperkeratotic skin lesions on palms/soles), oral ulcers
  • HLA-B27 positive in 50–80% — more severe/chronic course

Workup

  • Clinical diagnosis — no definitive test
  • STI testing: Chlamydia/gonorrhea NAAT (urine or swab) — treat if positive
  • Stool culture: If enteric trigger suspected
  • Joint aspiration: Rule out septic arthritis — sterile in reactive arthritis (WBC typically 10,000–50,000; predominantly PMN)
  • HLA-B27: Not required for diagnosis; prognostic value
  • CBC, ESR, CRP (elevated); rheumatoid factor/ANA (negative)

Empiric Therapy

ComponentTreatment
Underlying STI (Chlamydia present)Doxycycline 100mg PO BID × 7d (longer courses do not prevent reactive arthritis but treat infection)
Arthritis — acuteNSAIDs first-line (naproxen 500mg BID or indomethacin 25–50mg TID); joint aspiration for effusion
Enthesitis / dactylitisNSAIDs; local corticosteroid injection
Refractory arthritis (>3–6 months)Sulfasalazine 2–3g/day; methotrexate; anti-TNF (if HLA-B27+, axial involvement)
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Persistent synovitis without improvement → reconsider crystal arthropathy (gout/pseudogout), undifferentiated spondyloarthropathy, viral arthritis (parvovirus, rubella), Lyme arthritis
  • Joint cultures positive for bacteria → this is septic arthritis, not reactive arthritis; treat accordingly
  • Systemic inflammatory markers markedly elevated + not improving → consider early spondyloarthropathy, psoriatic arthritis
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Septic arthritis cannot be excluded → orthopedic consultation + joint aspiration
  • Uveitis developing → ophthalmology urgently (can cause permanent vision loss if untreated)
  • Aortic regurgitation (associated with HLA-B27 spondyloarthropathies) → cardiology evaluation
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Acute phase typically resolves in 3–6 months in most patients
  • Treat documented underlying STI regardless of arthritis management
  • Recurrent episodes possible — reassess sexual health practices; PrEP consideration; HLA-B27+ = higher recurrence risk

Overview

  • Sterile inflammatory arthritis triggered by a remote infection (not direct joint infection); formerly "Reiter syndrome"
  • Triad (classic but incomplete in most): Arthritis + Urethritis/cervicitis + Conjunctivitis/uveitis — "can't see, can't pee, can't climb a tree"
  • Triggering organisms: Chlamydia trachomatis (urogenital — most common in developed world); Salmonella, Shigella, Campylobacter, Yersinia (enteric); Clostridioides difficile
  • HLA-B27: Present in ~60–80% of reactive arthritis patients; predisposes to more severe/chronic disease

Clinical Features

  • Arthritis: Asymmetric oligoarthritis — lower extremity predominant (knee, ankle, subtalar joints); "sausage digit" (dactylitis); sacroiliitis; onset 1–4 weeks after triggering infection
  • Extra-articular: Circinate balanitis (painless penile lesions), keratoderma blennorrhagica (hyperkeratotic skin lesions on palms/soles), oral ulcers, uveitis/conjunctivitis, aortic regurgitation (rare, chronic)
  • Usually self-limiting (3–6 months); ~20% develop chronic disease

Diagnosis

  • Clinical — no definitive test; exclude septic arthritis (joint aspiration if effusion present)
  • Joint fluid: Inflammatory (WBC 5,000–50,000, predominantly PMN); culture negative (sterile)
  • STI testing: Urine NAAT for Chlamydia/GC regardless of urogenital symptoms
  • Stool culture: If recent diarrheal illness
  • HLA-B27: Positive supports diagnosis; not required
  • Imaging: Enthesitis (tendon/ligament insertion inflammation) on MRI; sacroiliitis on pelvis MRI in chronic disease

Treatment

ComponentDetails
Treat triggering infectionIf Chlamydia identified: Doxycycline 100mg PO BID × 7 days (or azithromycin 1g × 1); treat partners. Enteric organisms: usually self-limiting by time of diagnosis — antibiotics do not alter arthritis course
NSAIDs (first-line for arthritis)Indomethacin 25–50mg PO TID or Naproxen — full anti-inflammatory doses × 4–6 weeks; most effective for pain and swelling
Intra-articular corticosteroidsFor monoarticular or oligoarticular involvement; systemic steroids for severe systemic disease
DMARDs (chronic/refractory)Sulfasalazine 500mg–3g/day; methotrexate for chronic disease; TNF-α inhibitors for axial involvement
🩹 Skin & Soft Tissue
● Time-critical

Key Principles

  • SURGICAL EMERGENCY: Mortality increases 7-9% for every hour of delay in debridement
  • Types: Type I (polymicrobial, diabetics/elderly), Type II (Group A Strep or S. aureus monomicrobial)
  • Clinical clues: Pain out of proportion, rapid progression, crepitus, bullae, skin necrosis, systemic toxicity

LRINEC Score (Laboratory Risk Indicator for Necrotizing Fasciitis)

VariableValuePoints
CRP≥150 mg/L4
WBC15-251
WBC>252
Hemoglobin11-13.5 g/dL1
Hemoglobin<11 g/dL2
Sodium<135 mEq/L2
Creatinine>1.6 mg/dL2
Glucose>180 mg/dL1

Interpretation: ≥6 = high suspicion (PPV 92%); ≥8 = strongly predictive. But do NOT use to rule out—low score doesn't exclude nec fasc!

Empiric Therapy

  • Broad-spectrum: Vancomycin + Piperacillin-tazobactam (or Meropenem) + Clindamycin
  • Clindamycin rationale: Inhibits toxin production (Eagle effect—severe GAS may not respond to beta-lactams alone in high inoculum)
  • Duration: Continue until debridements complete, clinically stable, cultures guide de-escalation

Surgical Principles

  • OR within 12-24 hours of presentation (ideally within 6h)
  • Finger test: If tissue planes dissect easily with blunt finger, confirms diagnosis
  • Repeat debridement every 24-48h until clean margins
  • Consider IVIG for streptococcal toxic shock (2g/kg single dose)
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not responding as expected post-debridement → return to OR within 24h; no downtime between debridements if margins not clean
  • GAS (Group A Strep) confirmed → ensure clindamycin on board (toxin inhibition); consider IVIG for streptococcal toxic shock
  • Polymicrobial with unusual organisms → check source (perineal = GI/GU source, consider Fournier's), immunosuppression
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Streptococcal toxic shock (TSS) → ICU + IVIG 1–2 g/kg (evidence supporting use in streptococcal TSS)
  • Hemodynamic instability → ICU concurrent with OR preparation; do not delay OR for stabilization
  • Extension to deep fascial planes of neck, chest, or abdomen → multidisciplinary surgical team
  • Gas gangrene (Clostridium) → hyperbaric oxygen referral if available
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Wound granulating, margins clean, afebrile, bacteremia cleared → narrow antibiotics to culture-directed
  • After source control: 2–3 weeks typical; GAS: penicillin G + clindamycin; complete 14 days minimum
  • Wound closure/reconstruction when infection fully controlled — plastic surgery involvement

Recognition

  • Preseptal (periorbital) cellulitis: Infection anterior to orbital septum; eyelid erythema/edema; normal vision, no proptosis, no pain with eye movement, full EOM
  • Orbital cellulitis: Posterior to orbital septum; proptosis, restricted/painful EOM, ophthalmoplegia, vision changes, chemosis; ophthalmic emergency
  • Source: Sinusitis (ethmoid most common — shares medial wall with orbit), periorbital trauma/insect bite, dental infection, dacryocystitis
  • Common organisms: S. aureus (including MRSA), S. pneumoniae, Streptococci, H. influenzae (children unvaccinated), anaerobes (dental/chronic sinus)
  • Mucormycosis: uncontrolled DM + sinusitis + orbital involvement = emergency

Workup

  • CT orbits + sinuses with contrast (urgent): Distinguishes preseptal from orbital; identifies subperiosteal or orbital abscess; sinus disease
  • MRI superior for soft tissue detail; CT faster and usually sufficient acutely
  • Blood cultures: Before antibiotics if orbital cellulitis or systemic symptoms
  • CBC: Leukocytosis
  • Ophthalmology consultation: visual acuity, IOP, EOM assessment; mandatory for orbital cellulitis

Empiric Therapy

TypeSettingRegimen
Preseptal — mild (outpatient)OutpatientAmoxicillin-clavulanate 875/125mg PO BID
Preseptal — moderate/MRSA concernInpatientVancomycin 15–20mg/kg IV q8–12h ± Ceftriaxone
Orbital cellulitis (no abscess)InpatientVancomycin 15–20mg/kg IV q8–12h + Ceftriaxone 2g IV q24h + Metronidazole 500mg IV q8h
Orbital abscessInpatient + SurgerySame IV regimen + ENT / ophthalmology drainage
Mucormycosis suspected (DM + pale necrotic tissue)ICU + SurgeryLiposomal AmB 5–10mg/kg IV q24h + emergent surgical debridement

Source Control

  • Subperiosteal / orbital abscess: surgical drainage (ENT or ophthalmology + ENT); often combined with endoscopic sinus surgery
  • Mucormycosis: emergent debridement + systemic antifungal — do NOT wait
  • Dental source: dental extraction/drainage as indicated
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Preseptal cellulitis not improving at 24–48h → repeat CT to reassess for orbital extension; escalate to inpatient IV
