  • Orbital cellulitis with no identifiable sinus source → consider cavernous sinus thrombosis (bilateral findings, sixth nerve palsy, severe headache)
  • Necrotic tissue or black eschar with DM + sinus involvement → Mucormycosis until proven otherwise; emergent treatment
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Vision change or ophthalmoplegia not improving at 24h on IV → OR for abscess drainage
  • Cavernous sinus thrombosis (extension of orbital cellulitis) → IV antibiotics + consider anticoagulation (controversial); neurosurgery/neurology
  • Mucormycosis → emergent surgical debridement + liposomal amphotericin; high mortality without immediate action
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Orbital cellulitis + abscess drained, improving, vision stable → continue IV antibiotics until clear clinical improvement (7–14 days IV total)
  • Preseptal cellulitis responding → oral step-down amox-clav to complete 7–10 days
  • Ophthalmology follow-up for visual acuity confirmation after orbital cellulitis

Critical Distinction: Preseptal vs. Orbital

FeaturePreseptal (Periorbital)Orbital (Postseptal)
AnatomyAnterior to orbital septumPosterior to orbital septum (within orbit)
ProptosisAbsentPresent
OphthalmoplegiaAbsentPresent (painful EOM limitation)
Vision changeNoneMay be reduced — emergency
Pain with eye movementAbsentPresent
SeverityLow — outpatient possibleHIGH — admit, IV antibiotics, ophthalmology urgent
TreatmentAmox-clav PO or AugmentinIV antibiotics ± urgent drainage

Subperiosteal Abscess / Cavernous Sinus Thrombosis

  • Subperiosteal abscess: CT finding of fluid collection between periorbita and orbital wall; requires surgical drainage + IV antibiotics
  • Cavernous sinus thrombosis: Bilateral involvement, high fever, headache, signs of meningismus; proptosis + ophthalmoplegia bilaterally; treat with IV antibiotics + anticoagulation + ophthalmology/neurosurgery

Source & Microbiology

  • Preseptal: Skin/eyelid trauma, insect bite, hordeolum — S. aureus, Streptococcus pyogenes
  • Orbital: Sinusitis (ethmoid most common) in 90% — S. aureus (MRSA), Streptococci, H. influenzae (children), anaerobes in chronic sinusitis

Management of Orbital Cellulitis

  • CT orbits with contrast: Mandatory — distinguish preseptal vs. orbital vs. subperiosteal abscess
  • Empiric IV antibiotics: Vancomycin + Ceftriaxone 2g IV q12h (covers MRSA, Strep, H. flu)
  • Urgent ophthalmology consult — vision loss possible within hours from compressive optic neuropathy
  • ENT consult: For sinusitis source; drainage if sinus obstruction
  • Surgical drainage: If subperiosteal abscess >10mm, progressive despite 24–48h IV antibiotics, or vision threatened
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 24h on IV → CT to reassess for abscess/orbital extension not visible on initial imaging
  • No sinus source identified → consider cavernous sinus thrombosis (bilateral findings, CN VI palsy, severe headache)
  • Necrotic tissue or black eschar + DM/immunocompromised → Mucormycosis emergency; escalate immediately
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Visual change or new ophthalmoplegia on IV therapy → OR for abscess drainage
  • Cavernous sinus thrombosis → IV antibiotics + anticoagulation (controversial) + neurosurgery/neurology
  • Mucormycosis → emergent debridement + liposomal amphotericin; high mortality without immediate action
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Orbital cellulitis + abscess drained, improving, vision stable → continue IV 7–14 days total
  • Preseptal responding → oral amoxicillin-clavulanate to complete 7–10 days
  • Ophthalmology follow-up for visual acuity confirmation after orbital cellulitis
○ Condition-specific

Recognition

  • Superficial dermis + lymphatic involvement; sharply demarcated raised erythema with clear borders (distinguishes from deeper cellulitis)
  • Face (butterfly, via strep pharyngitis) or lower extremities (via skin disruption)
  • Causative organism: S. pyogenes (Group A Strep) in almost all cases; occasionally Group C/G strep
  • Systemic: fever, rigors, malaise often precede local findings
  • Salmon-red color, warm, indurated, painful; lymphangitis streaking common

Workup

  • Clinical diagnosis — no routine cultures needed for uncomplicated
  • Blood cultures: If systemic toxicity, immunocompromise, or bacteremia suspected
  • CBC: Leukocytosis (often striking)
  • Mark borders with surgical marker to assess progression
  • Swab/culture not useful (organism rarely isolated from skin surface)

Empiric Therapy

SettingRegimenDuration
Mild (outpatient)Amoxicillin 500mg PO TID OR Penicillin V 500mg PO QID5–7 days
Moderate (inpatient)Penicillin G 2–4 MU IV q4–6h OR Ampicillin 2g IV q6hUntil improving → oral step-down; 10–14 days total
PCN allergyClindamycin 300–450mg PO TID (confirm susceptibility)5–10 days

Penicillin is first-line — GAS is always penicillin-susceptible. No need for antistaphylococcal coverage unless purulent features present.

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 48–72h → reconsider diagnosis: deep tissue involvement (necrotizing fasciitis), venous thrombosis, contact dermatitis, insect bite reaction
  • Rapidly expanding borders despite antibiotics → urgent surgical assessment to exclude NecFasc
  • Fever persisting beyond 72h with appropriate antibiotics → blood cultures, consider bacteremia workup
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Spreading beyond marked borders at 24h on IV therapy → reassess for NecFasc immediately; consider dermatology, surgery
  • Bullae formation → may indicate deeper infection or streptococcal TSS; ID consultation
  • Systemic toxicity out of proportion to local findings → consider GAS toxic shock, blood cultures, ID consult
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Borders stable or receding, afebrile × 24–48h → oral step-down (penicillin or amoxicillin)
  • Elevation of affected extremity critical for response — lymphatic involvement means gravity-dependent edema worsens
  • Recurrent erysipelas: prophylactic penicillin V 250mg BID or benzathine PCN G 1.2 MU IM monthly

Recognition

  • Dog bites: Crush injury; Pasteurella multocida (rapid onset), Capnocytophaga canimorsus (severe in asplenic/immunocompromised — sepsis, DIC)
  • Cat bites: Deep puncture wounds; Pasteurella multocida in >50% — infection within hours (not days)
  • Human bites: Clenched fist injuries (fight bite over MCP joint) → Eikenella corrodens, streptococci, anaerobes, MRSA; viridans streptococci
  • Wild animal: Rabies exposure assessment mandatory
  • Time to infection: Cat bites = hours; dog bites = 12–24h; human bites = variable

Workup

  • Wound culture: If frank infection; not recommended for prophylaxis wounds
  • X-ray: All clenched fist injuries — foreign body (tooth fragment), fracture, joint space air
  • Blood cultures: If systemic signs or Capnocytophaga suspected (rapid sepsis in asplenic)
  • Rabies risk assessment: bat, raccoon, skunk, fox exposure (unprovoked); consult public health
  • Tetanus: assess vaccination status; give Td/Tdap if not current

Empiric Therapy

ScenarioRegimenDuration
Prophylaxis (dog/cat/human bite, not yet infected)Amoxicillin-clavulanate 875/125mg PO BID3–5 days
Established infection (outpatient)Amoxicillin-clavulanate 875/125mg PO BID5–7 days
Established infection (inpatient)Ampicillin-sulbactam 3g IV q6h OR Pip-Tazo 4.5g IV q6hUntil improving → oral
PCN allergy (mild)Doxycycline + Metronidazole5–7 days
Cat bite — PasteurellaAmoxicillin-clavulanate (NOT cephalexin or dicloxacillin — resistant)7 days
Human bite — MRSA riskAdd TMP-SMX DS or Doxycycline to amox-clav5–7 days

Source Control

  • Clenched fist injury over MCP joint: wound exploration (OR if needed) to assess joint capsule violation, tendon involvement, joint irrigation
  • Puncture wounds: do NOT close primarily if >6–8h old or over joint; consider delayed primary closure
  • Deep infection with abscess: surgical drainage
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not responding to amox-clav → consider MRSA (especially human bites), Capnocytophaga (dog bites in immunocompromised), or unusual organisms
  • Cat bite wound appearing infected within hours → Pasteurella — clinical diagnosis; change to amox-clav if on anything else
  • Rapidly spreading cellulitis after dog bite in asplenic patient → Capnocytophaga canimorsus sepsis — medical emergency
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Capnocytophaga sepsis (asplenic + dog bite + rapid deterioration) → ICU, IV penicillin or amp-sulbactam, aggressive resuscitation
  • Joint space violation on X-ray or exam → orthopedic surgery for irrigation and debridement
  • Rabies post-exposure: wound irrigation + rabies vaccine + HRIG (hepatitis C immune globulin) per public health protocol
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Infection resolving, tolerating PO, improving erythema → oral amox-clav to complete course
  • Clenched fist injuries: orthopedic follow-up to assess joint/tendon function
  • Rabies prophylaxis completion: 4-dose series days 0, 3, 7, 14 (HRIG day 0 only)

Recognition

  • Bacterial infection of skeletal muscle; tropical pyomyositis (previously) now common in temperate climates
  • Stages: Invasive (muscle pain/swelling, early, no fluctuance) → Suppurative (abscess, fever, leukocytosis) → Late (sepsis, organ failure)
  • Large muscle groups: quadriceps, iliopsoas, gluteals most common
  • Risk: IVDU, HIV, DM, trauma, vigorous exercise preceding infection, immunocompromise
  • Causative organism: S. aureus (75–90%); MRSA increasing; Group A Strep (tropical); GNR (immunocompromised)

Workup

  • MRI with contrast (gold standard): T2 signal change with rim enhancement; identifies abscess vs cellulitis vs phlegmon; guides drainage planning
  • CT with contrast: Alternative if MRI unavailable; less sensitive for early disease
  • Ultrasound: Bedside; identifies abscess for aspiration; misses early/deep infections
  • Blood cultures × 2: Bacteremia in 5–20%
  • CBC (leukocytosis), CRP (markedly elevated), creatinine (renal failure in severe)
  • Wound/aspiration culture: guides definitive therapy

Empiric Therapy

ScenarioRegimen
Empiric (MRSA coverage required)Vancomycin 15–20mg/kg IV q8–12h
MSSA confirmedNafcillin 2g IV q4h OR Cefazolin 2g IV q8h
GNR suspected (immunocompromised, IVDU)Vancomycin + Cefepime 2g IV q8h
Oral step-down (MSSA, tolerating PO)Dicloxacillin 500mg PO QID OR Cephalexin 500mg PO QID
Oral step-down (MRSA)TMP-SMX DS PO BID OR Doxycycline 100mg PO BID

Source Control

  • Abscess drainage (mandatory): CT or US-guided percutaneous drainage for suppurative stage; repeat imaging if not improving
  • Surgical drainage for inaccessible locations, multiloculated collections, necrotizing extension
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving after drainage + antibiotics → inadequate drainage (re-image); resistant organism; necrotizing fasciitis extension (MRI for tissue planes)
  • Disproportionate pain/systemic toxicity → necrotizing fasciitis cannot coexist — urgent surgical assessment
  • Blood cultures positive → source control (abscess drainage mandatory); evaluate for endocarditis (TTE)
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Necrotizing extension identified → OR immediately
  • Septic shock → ICU + vasopressors + IV antibiotics + urgent drainage
  • Compartment syndrome (tense swelling, paresthesias) → fasciotomy
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Abscess drained, blood cultures negative × 48h, improving → IV to oral step-down when afebrile + tolerating PO
  • Total duration: 3–4 weeks typically (longer if bacteremia, deep/inaccessible abscess)
  • MSSA confirmed → switch from vancomycin to nafcillin/cefazolin same day

Recognition

  • Folliculitis: Superficial hair follicle infection; small pustules with central hair; S. aureus most common; also gram-negatives (hot tub — Pseudomonas), fungi (Malassezia), non-infectious
  • Furuncle (boil): Deep follicular abscess; tender nodule → fluctuant; S. aureus (MRSA in purulent SSTI)
  • Carbuncle: Confluent furuncles with multiple draining sinuses; usually nape of neck, back; systemic symptoms more common; higher MRSA prevalence
  • MRSA prevalence: >50% of community-onset purulent SSTI in US

Workup

  • Clinical diagnosis for most
  • Wound culture: From purulent material after I&D — guides therapy; important for recurrent or treatment-failure cases
  • Blood cultures: If systemic toxicity, immunocompromise, carbuncle with fever
  • No routine imaging unless deep extension suspected

Empiric Therapy

LesionTreatmentAntibiotics
FolliculitisWarm compresses; topical mupirocin or clindamycinOral only if extensive or systemic symptoms
Furuncle ≤2cm, no systemic featuresI&D is definitiveTMP-SMX DS PO BID × 5d after I&D (reduces recurrence and treatment failure)
Furuncle with systemic features or immunocompromisedI&D + systemic antibioticsTMP-SMX DS PO BID OR Doxycycline 100mg PO BID × 5–7d (covers MRSA)
CarbuncleSurgical drainage (often OR for multiple loculations)TMP-SMX DS PO BID × 5–7d; IV vancomycin if systemically ill

Source Control

  • Incision and drainage (I&D): Primary treatment for furuncle/carbuncle — adequate drainage is more important than antibiotics alone
  • IDSA recommends I&D + antibiotics (over I&D alone) based on NEJM 2017 data — TMP-SMX reduces treatment failure
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not responding after I&D + antibiotics → culture-directed therapy; consider MRSA with TMP-SMX resistance; inadequate drainage
  • Recurrent furunculosis → decolonization protocol: mupirocin nasal ointment BID × 5d, chlorhexidine washes × 5d; treat household contacts
  • Systemic SIRS/sepsis from carbuncle → blood cultures, IV vancomycin, surgical drainage, ID consultation
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Spreading cellulitis beyond furuncle despite I&D → IV antibiotics, reassess for deep tissue involvement
  • Fever persisting after apparently adequate I&D → examine for satellite abscesses; consider abscess ultrasound
  • Immunocompromise + bacteremia from furuncle → evaluate for metastatic infection (endocarditis, spinal epidural abscess)
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • I&D complete, afebrile, cellulitis resolving → oral TMP-SMX to complete 5–7 day course
  • Test culture results at 48–72h → adjust if susceptibility profile warrants
  • Decolonization for recurrent cases: repeat after treatment course; household contact evaluation

Definitions

EntityDefinitionKey Features
FolliculitisSuperficial infection of hair folliclePustule at follicle opening; minimal erythema; S. aureus most common; also Pseudomonas (hot tub), Malassezia (pityrosporum)
Furuncle (boil)Deep infection of hair follicle + surrounding subcutisTender, fluctuant nodule; central necrotic plug; S. aureus; MRSA increasingly common
CarbuncleCoalescence of multiple furuncles with subcutaneous extensionLarger, indurated mass with multiple draining puncta; systemic symptoms common; nape of neck, back, thigh

Treatment

EntityInterventionAntibiotics
FolliculitisWarm compresses; topical mupirocin or clindamycinUsually not needed; oral if extensive or systemic symptoms
Furuncle (small, no cellulitis)I&D — primary therapy; warm compressesTMP-SMX DS BID × 5–7d (post-I&D — reduces treatment failure per NEJM 2017 RCT)
CarbuncleI&D + wound careTMP-SMX DS BID × 5–7d or doxycycline 100mg BID × 5–7d
Systemic signs/feverI&D + IV antibioticsVancomycin IV (MRSA coverage)

Recurrent Furunculosis

  • MRSA decolonization protocol (≥2 episodes in 6 months):
  • Mupirocin 2% nasal ointment BID × 5 days (anterior nares)
  • CHG 4% body wash daily × 5–14 days (focus on skin folds, axilla, groin)
  • Household contacts: treat simultaneously; environmental decontamination (wash bedding, towels at high heat)
  • Hot tub folliculitis (Pseudomonas aeruginosa): Self-limiting in immunocompetent; no antibiotics needed; avoid hot tub until culture-negative
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not responding to TMP-SMX → check susceptibility; MRSA with TMP-SMX resistance possible; consider doxycycline
  • Recurrent furunculosis → decolonization protocol (mupirocin nasal BID × 5d + chlorhexidine washes); treat household contacts
  • Hot tub folliculitis (Pseudomonas) → self-limited; no antibiotics needed in immunocompetent
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Cellulitis spreading beyond furuncle border → IV vancomycin; reassess for NecFasc
  • Systemic toxicity + carbuncle → IV vancomycin + surgical drainage + blood cultures + endocarditis evaluation
  • Immunocompromised patient + bacteremia from furuncle → evaluate for metastatic foci (TTE, MRI spine)
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • I&D complete + afebrile → oral TMP-SMX DS BID × 5–7 days to complete
  • MSSA confirmed → switch to cephalexin or dicloxacillin
  • Decolonization for recurrent cases: repeat after treatment course; 5-day mupirocin + chlorhexidine protocol
🏥 Perioperative
📌 Key Principle: Fever in the first 48–72h after surgery is almost always non-infectious (inflammatory response to tissue injury). Reflexive antibiotic prescribing without source evaluation contributes significantly to antibiotic overuse and C. difficile risk.

The "5 W's" — Timing-Based Differential

Timing"W"EtiologyEvaluation
Day 0–2WindPhysiologic inflammatory response, atelectasis (microatelectasis — does NOT cause fever despite the teaching; likely cytokine surge from surgery). Also: transfusion reaction, malignant hyperthermia, adrenal insufficiency, medication reactionExamine chest, O₂ sat; incentive spirometry; CXR only if respiratory symptoms. Routine CXR in afebrile post-op patients has very low yield
Day 1–3Wind (PNA)Aspiration pneumonia/pneumonitis (esp. prolonged intubation, NG tube, neurologic dysfunction, obesity); early HAPCXR, O₂ sat, respiratory exam; sputum culture if CXR positive; treat with appropriate antibiotics if HAP criteria met
Day 3–5WaterUTI, CAUTI — especially with urinary catheter; also IV catheter phlebitisUA with culture (if catheterized and symptomatic); remove/replace catheter; line site inspection
Day 3–7WoundSurgical site infection (SSI) — superficial incisional (most common); stitch abscess; hematoma (sterile but may become infected)Inspect wound daily; look for erythema, warmth, fluctuance, drainage, dehiscence; open and culture if fluctuant; wound cultures from swab alone are unreliable
Day 5–7WalkingDVT/PE — venous thromboembolism; also septic thrombophlebitis from IV catheter; drug fever (day 5–10)Lower extremity POCUS or formal duplex if DVT suspected; CT-PA if PE suspected; inspect all IV sites; review medication list for culprits
Day 7+Wonder drugsDrug fever (antibiotics — esp. beta-lactams, sulfa; phenytoin; heparin); deep SSI or organ/space infection; anastomotic leak; C. difficile; abscess; PICC/catheter infection; infected hematoma; osteomyelitis of sternum/spineComprehensive review: wound imaging (CT if deep infection), C. diff testing if diarrhea, blood cultures if septic, drug list review, TEE if prosthetic valve/CIED

Unique Postoperative Situations

SituationKey Considerations
Fever immediately intraoperative or within 1–2hMalignant hyperthermia (succinylcholine/inhalational agents; treat with dantrolene); transfusion reaction; bacteremia from contaminated field; adrenal crisis
Persistent fever despite apparent source treatmentUndrainable collection, resistant organism, wrong diagnosis, drug fever (drug fever is diagnosis of exclusion — resolves 72–96h after stopping offending drug)
Fever after cardiac surgeryFirst 4 days: post-pericardiotomy syndrome (pleuritic chest pain, friction rub, diffuse ST elevation; responds to NSAIDs); mediastinitis/sternal wound infection (usually day 5–10+); CIED infection; IE on new valve
Fever after neurosurgery/spinal surgeryChemical meningitis (day 1–3, aseptic, CSF pleocytosis from blood/surgical debris — treat with NSAIDs, not antibiotics); bacterial meningitis (rare; typically >48h); SSI; CSF leak/fistula; hardware infection
Fever after joint replacementFirst 48–72h: physiologic; day 3+: periprosthetic infection — low threshold for joint aspiration; PJI in first 3 months = acute postoperative PJI (DAIR candidate)
Fever after laparotomy/bowel surgeryAnastomotic leak (typically day 5–7): fever + abdominal pain + leukocytosis + tachycardia → CT abdomen with contrast; ileus can delay presentation; surgical emergency if free perforation

Laboratory & Workup — What to Order (and What Not To)

  • Do NOT reflexively order: UA/urine culture in catheterized patient without symptoms, blood cultures for isolated low-grade fever (<38.5°C) in stable post-op day 0–2 patient, CXR for fever alone without respiratory symptoms in the first 48h
  • Always order blood cultures ×2 for: temperature >38.9°C, rigors, hemodynamic instability, suspicion of deep infection, fever after day 5
  • CRP/ESR: not useful in early post-op (elevated due to surgery); useful after week 2 for tracking trajectory
  • CBC: leukocytosis common first 48h post-op; trend more important than single value

CDC/NHSN SSI Classification

TypeDepthCriteriaTypical Presentation
Superficial Incisional SSISkin and subcutaneous tissue onlyWithin 30 days of operation; purulent drainage, organisms from culture, pain/tenderness/swelling/erythema AND wound deliberately opened by surgeon OR clinical diagnosisDay 5–10: wound erythema, warmth, purulent drainage; localized
Deep Incisional SSIFascia and muscle layersWithin 30 days (or 90 days if implant); purulent drainage from deep space; spontaneous dehiscence or reopening by surgeon due to fever/pain; abscess or infection on imagingDeeper wound pain, fever, possible fascial bulge; CT may show fluid collection
Organ/Space SSIAny anatomy opened/manipulated beyond fasciaWithin 30 days (or 90 days if implant); purulent drainage from drain in organ/space; abscess or infection on imaging or re-operationIntra-abdominal abscess, anastomotic leak, empyema, hepatic abscess, mediastinitis; often delayed presentation

NHSN Surveillance window: 30 days for procedures without implant; 90 days for procedures with implant placement (joint replacement, spinal hardware, vascular graft, cardiac devices).

SSI Management

Superficial SSI
  • Open the wound: Open incision over area of fluctuance or purulence; culture deep tissue (not skin swab); irrigate and pack wound
  • Antibiotics NOT routinely required for simple superficial SSI after wound opening (adequate source control)
  • Add antibiotics if: Systemic signs (fever, leukocytosis), erythema extending >2 cm from wound edge, immunocompromised, hardware beneath wound, or cellulitis not improving with wound opening alone
  • Empiric: Cefazolin IV or cephalexin PO (cover skin flora); add MRSA coverage (TMP-SMX or vancomycin) if MRSA risk factors or no improvement
Deep Incisional SSI
  • Requires surgical exploration — debridement, fascial disruption assessment, culture
  • Wound VAC (negative pressure wound therapy) for large wounds, improving healing and reducing risk of ongoing infection
  • Antibiotics per culture; broader coverage for deep structures (vancomycin + ceftriaxone or pip-tazo pending cultures)
  • Duration: typically 1–2 weeks after adequate source control unless hardware involved
Organ/Space SSI
  • Requires image-guided or surgical drainage — antibiotics alone rarely sufficient
  • CT abdomen/pelvis with contrast for intra-abdominal collections
  • CT-guided percutaneous drainage vs surgical drainage based on collection anatomy and patient stability
  • Anastomotic leak: general surgery consultation urgently — may require diversion, takedown, or fecal peritonitis management
  • Duration: 4–7 days after adequate source control (per STOP-IT trial data for intra-abdominal infections)

Microbiology by Operative Site

Procedure TypeCommon Organisms
Clean (cardiac, orthopedic, vascular)S. aureus (MSSA/MRSA), CoNS, Streptococcus
Clean-contaminated (GI, GU, gynecologic, respiratory)S. aureus + gram-negative rods (E. coli, Klebsiella) ± anaerobes
Contaminated/Dirty (perforated viscus, established abscess, GI contents spillage)Polymicrobial: Enterococcus, Bacteroides fragilis, E. coli, Pseudomonas, Candida in severely ill
Neurosurgery/spineS. aureus, CoNS; late infections: Propionibacterium acnes (indolent)

General Principles — All Implant Infections

  • Biofilm is the fundamental challenge: bacteria encased in extracellular polymeric matrix on implant surface are protected from host immune defenses and antibiotics; organisms in biofilm exhibit phenotypic tolerance (not resistance) to antibiotics at 100–1000× higher concentrations
  • Rifampin is the most biofilm-active antibiotic for gram-positive organisms (particularly Staphylococcus); always use with companion drug to prevent resistance emergence; never monotherapy
  • Source control decision (retain vs remove hardware) is the most important treatment decision and should involve the implanting surgeon and ID
  • Coagulase-negative Staphylococcus (CoNS) appears low-virulence but causes the majority of indolent implant infections — do not dismiss positive cultures

Spinal Hardware Infection

CategoryTimingStrategy
Early postoperative (<30 days)Usually S. aureus, gram-negative rodsDébridement + hardware retention usually attempted if hardware providing structural stability; vancomycin + cefepime empirically; convert to targeted therapy; rifampin added for Staph once wound healing; 3–6 months total antibiotics; suppressive therapy may follow
Late-acute hematogenous (>30 days, <3 weeks symptoms)S. aureus most common; hematogenous seeding from distant sourceDébridement + retention if hardware stable and well-incorporated; similar antibiotic approach to early; ID consultation required
Chronic / indolent (>3 weeks symptoms)CoNS, Propionibacterium acnes, MRSAHardware removal often necessary for cure; 6+ months antibiotic therapy; chronic suppression if hardware retained; multidisciplinary decision
  • MRI is the preferred imaging for assessing soft tissue involvement; metal artifact reduction sequences (MARS) improve visualization around implants
  • Nuclear medicine (WBC-labeled scan or PET-CT) can be useful when MRI severely limited by artifact
  • Propionibacterium acnes (Cutibacterium acnes): Common cause of indolent spinal hardware infection — slow-growing, requires prolonged anaerobic culture incubation (≥14 days); penicillin-susceptible; may present months to years post-op

Vascular Graft Infection

  • Incidence: 1–6% of vascular grafts; mortality 25–75% for aortic graft infection
  • Early (<4 months): S. aureus, gram-negative rods; usually presents as SSI or perigraft fluid
  • Late (>4 months): CoNS, S. epidermidis, MRSA, Pseudomonas; indolent presentation; graft-enteric fistula (presents as GI bleed — herald bleed then massive hemorrhage)
  • Diagnosis: CT angiography (perigraft fluid/gas), tagged WBC scan, PET-CT; blood cultures ×2
  • Graft-enteric fistula: Upper GI bleed + history of aortic graft → EGD to evaluate duodenum (most common site); CT if EGD unrevealing; vascular surgery emergency
  • Treatment: Complete graft excision + in-situ or extra-anatomic reconstruction (if infected field) + antibiotics; for stable patients with limited infection, some success with aggressive débridement + graft preservation + lifelong suppressive antibiotics (ID + vascular surgery decision)
  • Empiric antibiotics: Vancomycin + piperacillin-tazobactam (or carbapenem); culture-directed step-down; duration typically 4–6 weeks minimum post-operatively

Mesh Infection (Abdominal Wall / Hernia Repair)

  • Incidence: 0.5–10% depending on mesh type (biologic < synthetic), wound class, patient factors
  • Early (<30 days): S. aureus, gram-negative rods, polymicrobial; usually presents as SSI around mesh
  • Late (>30 days): CoNS, MRSA; indolent fistula, chronic drainage, mesh extrusion
  • Diagnosis: CT abdomen/pelvis with contrast (perimesh fluid, gas, sinus tract)
  • Treatment: Lightweight synthetic mesh and biologic mesh — débridement + retention sometimes possible with adequate drainage + antibiotics; heavyweight synthetic mesh — usually requires removal; sinus tract to skin → mesh excision typically required
  • Empiric: Vancomycin + pip-tazo for initial presentation; narrow based on cultures; duration 4–6 weeks if mesh retained

Breast Implant Infection

  • Early (<6 weeks): S. aureus, gram-negative rods; treat with antibiotics; implant removal often required if not improving in 48–72h
  • Late: CoNS, atypical mycobacteria (M. abscessus, M. fortuitum — rapidly growing); indolent periprosthetic infection; chronic drainage
  • Atypical mycobacteria: Require specific susceptibility testing; treat with macrolide (clarithromycin) + amikacin ± imipenem based on species; ID consultation required; implant removal usually necessary
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Single positive blood culture with CoNS → contaminant vs true infection; repeat cultures; clinical correlation essential before committing to hardware removal
  • Not responding to suppressive antibiotics alone → hardware removal almost always required for cure; antibiotics are adjunctive
  • Wound dehiscence + hardware exposure → surgical debridement urgently; exposed hardware = infected hardware
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Unstable hardware + systemic infection → surgical consultation for explantation
  • Vascular graft infection → vascular surgery emergency; excision + extra-anatomic bypass or in-situ reconstruction
  • Septic emboli from infected hardware → endovascular specialist + ID; may require urgent explantation
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • After hardware removal + adequate debridement: S. aureus → 6 weeks IV; CoNS → 2–4 weeks
  • Retained hardware (cannot remove) → chronic suppression with oral antibiotics indefinitely; rifampin combination for biofilm-active therapy
  • MSSA confirmed → β-lactam superior to vancomycin; de-escalate same day

Cardiac Surgery

InfectionTimingKey Points
Sternal wound infection / MediastinitisTypically day 5–14 post-CABG/sternotomyRisk factors: bilateral IMA harvest, obesity, diabetes, reoperation, prolonged CPB; presents with sternal instability, wound dehiscence, fever, leukocytosis, purulent drainage; CT chest: peristernal fluid/gas; S. aureus (MRSA) and CoNS most common; treatment: surgical debridement (sternal rewiring or VAC) + vancomycin ± pip-tazo × 4–6 weeks; high mortality (10–30%)
Prosthetic valve endocarditis (PVE)Early: <60 days; Late: >60 days post-opEarly PVE: CoNS, S. aureus, GNR, Candida (from intraoperative/perioperative sources); late PVE: similar to NVE organisms; TEE required; treat as IE with surgery decision per Duke criteria; see High-Yield Syndromes → Infective Endocarditis
CIED infection (pacemaker/ICD pocket or lead)Days to months post-implantSee High-Yield Syndromes → CIED Infections

Spinal Surgery

InfectionTimingKey Points
Superficial/deep SSIDay 3–10S. aureus (MRSA in ~30%); risk: longer operative time, obesity, diabetes, posterior approach; wound opening + irrigation + débridement; hardware usually retained if early; vancomycin empirically
Spinal hardware infectionEarly (<30d) or lateSee Hardware section above
Aseptic/chemical meningitisDay 1–3Blood products in CSF → inflammatory pleocytosis (often PMN-dominant); fever, headache, neck stiffness; CSF: elevated WBC (hundreds to thousands), elevated protein, normal glucose, negative culture; treat with NSAIDs; antibiotics NOT indicated; clinical distinction from bacterial meningitis requires culture result
Discitis after discectomyDays to weeks post-opPersistent/recurrent back pain, elevated CRP; MRI: disc space T2 signal; culture-guided therapy; if blood cultures and CT-guided biopsy negative and clinically stable, some cases treated empirically × 6 weeks based on MRI pattern

Orthopedic Surgery (Arthroplasty)

TimingClassificationStrategy
<3 months from implantAcute postoperative PJIDAIR if implant stable and well-fixed; vancomycin + rifampin (once wound healing); target 6 weeks IV/PO then oral suppression
>3 months from implant, symptoms <3 weeksAcute hematogenous PJIDAIR considered; identify and treat source of bacteremia
>3 months from implant, symptoms >3 weeksChronic PJITwo-stage exchange preferred; 6+ weeks antibiotics between stages

Full PJI content: High-Yield Syndromes → Prosthetic Joint Infection

Colorectal Surgery — Anastomotic Leak

  • Incidence: 3–10% for colorectal anastomoses; higher for low rectal anastomoses, emergency surgery, malnutrition, steroid use
  • Presentation: usually day 5–7; fever, abdominal pain, leukocytosis, tachycardia, increasing drain output with feculent character
  • Diagnosis: CT abdomen/pelvis with contrast — peranastomotic fluid, gas, extravasation; water-soluble contrast enema if CT equivocal
  • Management: Broad-spectrum antibiotics (pip-tazo or meropenem) + source control; contained leak → CT-guided drainage ± diverting ostomy; free perforation → surgical emergency, wash-out, diversion; anastomotic takedown if necessary
  • Long-term: increased risk of anastomotic stricture and pelvic sepsis

Full surgical antimicrobial prophylaxis tables (by procedure, PCN allergy alternatives, MRSA risk, weight-based dosing, re-dosing intervals) are in Prophylaxis Tables → Surgical Antimicrobial Prophylaxis.

Common Inpatient Prophylaxis Questions

QuestionAnswer
How long should surgical prophylaxis be continued?Single dose or ≤24h for virtually all procedures. No benefit from extending based on drains, catheters, or lines. ASHP/IDSA/SIS/SHEA 2013: strong evidence against prolonged prophylaxis.
Should prophylaxis be continued if the patient has a drain?No. Drains do not extend prophylaxis indication.
Should prophylaxis be continued in the ICU post-cardiac surgery?≤24h only (cefazolin). Some institutions extend to 48h for open-heart surgery — controversial and not guideline-supported; do not extend further.
Patient was given cefazolin but now has positive MRSA screen?Do not add vancomycin retrospectively to prophylaxis. If MRSA screen was positive preoperatively, patient should have received vancomycin + cefazolin prophylaxis.
Penicillin allergy — which alternative for orthopedic/cardiac surgery?Vancomycin 15 mg/kg IV (infuse over ≥60 min to avoid red man syndrome). Cross-reactivity between cephalosporins and penicillin is very low (<2%); consider cefazolin with allergy evaluation if only non-IgE-mediated allergy reported.
Re-dosing during prolonged surgery?Cefazolin: re-dose every 4h (or with blood loss >1.5L); ampicillin-sulbactam: every 2h; vancomycin: not typically re-dosed due to long half-life.
✈️ Travel & Vector-borne

Recognition

  • Cyclic fevers (tertian/quartan), rigors, diaphoresis; headache, myalgia, nausea
  • Travel history mandatory: Exposure within 3 months; endemic area (sub-Saharan Africa, South Asia, SE Asia, Latin America, Caribbean)
  • Severe malaria (P. falciparum): Altered consciousness, seizures, ARDS, renal failure, severe anemia (Hgb <7), hypoglycemia, hyperparasitemia (>5%), shock, abnormal bleeding
  • Relapsing malaria: P. vivax / P. ovale have liver hypnozoites → relapses months after primary infection
  • Blood smear malaria is a STAT test — should result within 1 hour

Workup

  • Thick + thin blood smear × 3 (q12–24h if initial negative) — identify species + quantify parasitemia
  • Malaria RDT (rapid antigen): Sensitive for P. falciparum; less reliable for non-falciparum or low parasitemia
  • Malaria PCR: Species identification when smear unclear; mixed infections
  • CBC (anemia, thrombocytopenia), glucose (hypoglycemia in severe), LFTs, creatinine, LDH
  • Parasitemia >5% or clinical signs of severity → IV artesunate + ICU

Empiric Therapy

ScenarioRegimenNotes
P. falciparum — uncomplicatedArtemether-lumefantrine (Coartem) × 3 daysTake with fatty food; most reliable option
P. falciparum — severeIV Artesunate (available via CDC/hospital pharmacy)Superior to quinine; load 2.4mg/kg IV × 3 doses then daily until oral
P. vivax / ovale (chloroquine-sensitive)Chloroquine 1g PO → 500mg at 6h, 24h, 48h + Primaquine × 14dCheck G6PD before primaquine (hemolysis risk)
P. vivax — chloroquine-resistant (SE Asia)Artemether-lumefantrine + PrimaquinePrimaquine eradicates liver hypnozoites
P. malariaeChloroquine (no primaquine needed — no hypnozoites)—
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Parasitemia not falling at 48h → suspect drug resistance; switch regimen; ID consultation; contact CDC Malaria Hotline (770-488-7788)
  • Not improving despite appropriate treatment → severe malaria threshold (check parasitemia, glucose, mental status)
  • Species misidentification → re-examine smear; P. knowlesi can be mistaken for P. malariae but causes severe disease
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Severe malaria → ICU; IV artesunate; monitor glucose q4h; exchange transfusion if >10% parasitemia with complications (controversial)
  • ARDS from P. falciparum → lung-protective ventilation; avoid overzealous fluids
  • Cerebral malaria → avoid corticosteroids (harmful); anticonvulsants for seizures; artesunate IV
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Parasitemia clearing, tolerating oral, fever resolved → transition to oral artemisinin combination
  • P. vivax/ovale: confirm G6PD normal before starting primaquine 30mg daily × 14d
  • Follow-up smear at 7 and 28 days to confirm clearance; any fever recurrence → repeat smear

Diagnosis

  • Blood smear (thick + thin) × 3 sets at 12–24h intervals if initial negative — gold standard for species ID and parasitemia quantification
  • Rapid diagnostic test (RDT/malaria antigen test): HRP2 antigen (P. falciparum) + pan-plasmodium antigen; sensitivity ~95% for P. falciparum; less reliable for non-falciparum; use alongside smear
  • PCR: Highest sensitivity, species confirmation, resistance genotyping; reference lab

Species & Treatment

SpeciesSeverityRegimen
P. falciparum (or unknown species) — UncomplicatedNot severeArtemether-lumefantrine (Coartem) 4 tabs PO BID × 3 days (preferred) or Atovaquone-proguanil (Malarone) 4 adult tabs daily × 3 days
P. falciparum — SevereHigh parasitemia, organ dysfunction, cerebralIV artesunate 2.4 mg/kg IV at 0, 12, 24h then q24h × total 3 days (contact CDC at 770-488-7788 for access); follow with oral completion therapy
P. vivax / P. ovale (chloroquine-sensitive)UncomplicatedChloroquine 600 mg base PO then 300 mg at 6h, 24h, 48h + primaquine 30 mg base PO daily × 14 days (test G6PD first)
P. vivax / P. ovale (chloroquine-resistant — Papua New Guinea, Indonesia)—Artemether-lumefantrine + primaquine; OR quinine + doxycycline + primaquine
P. malariae—Chloroquine (no primaquine needed — no hypnozoites)
P. knowlesi (Southeast Asia)Can cause severe malariaTreat as P. falciparum

Severe Malaria — ICU Criteria

  • Parasitemia >5%, cerebral malaria (coma), ARDS, renal failure, severe anemia (Hgb <7), hypoglycemia, circulatory collapse, jaundice + other dysfunction
  • Avoid quinidine (cardiac toxicity) — IV artesunate is now standard of care in US (available via CDC)
  • Exchange transfusion: No longer routinely recommended; may consider in rare cases of >15% parasitemia with organ failure
  • Clinical: Gradual-onset fever, relative bradycardia (Faget's sign), splenomegaly, rose spots (2–4% — pink macules on trunk), diarrhea or constipation, elevated LFTs; WBC often LOW (leukopenia)
  • Diagnosis: Blood cultures (highest yield week 1, ~60–80%); stool/bone marrow culture; Widal test (poor sensitivity/specificity); PCR emerging
  • Treatment:
Severity / RegionRegimenDuration
Uncomplicated, non-resistantCiprofloxacin 500 mg PO BID or azithromycin 1 g PO day 1 then 500 mg daily (preferred in South/Southeast Asia — FQ resistance common)5–7 days
Severe / hospitalizedCeftriaxone 2 g IV q24h10–14 days (7–10 days if good response)
Multidrug-resistant (resistant to amp, chloramphenicol, TMP-SMX)Ceftriaxone or fluoroquinolone (if susceptible)10–14 days
XDR typhoid (Pakistan, H58 clade)Azithromycin (if susceptible) or carbapenemsConsult ID
  • Dexamethasone for severe typhoid with shock or impaired consciousness: 3 mg/kg IV then 1 mg/kg q6h × 8 doses
  • Complications: Intestinal perforation/hemorrhage (~3%), reactive arthritis, neuropsychiatric typhoid
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Blood cultures negative but clinical syndrome consistent → bone marrow culture most sensitive (90%); stool culture positive later in illness
  • Not improving on fluoroquinolone → high resistance from South Asia (XDR typhoid); switch to ceftriaxone or azithromycin
  • Rose spots and relative bradycardia + travel history → typhoid regardless of initial negative cultures; incubation 6–30 days
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Intestinal perforation (sudden worsening pain + peritoneal signs) → surgical emergency; bowel perforation occurs in 3–5%
  • GI hemorrhage (melena, hematochezia) → surgery + GI consultation; mucosal sloughing in Peyer patches
  • Hepatitis, encephalopathy, myocarditis, hemolytic anemia → systemic typhoid; ICU + dexamethasone for severe encephalopathy
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Fever typically resolves by day 5–7 on appropriate antibiotics
  • Total duration: 7–14 days for uncomplicated; 14 days for complicated (perforation, organ involvement)
  • Test-of-cure stool culture 4 weeks after treatment to confirm clearance (public health requirement in food handlers)

Recognition

  • Abrupt high fever + severe retro-orbital headache + myalgia/arthralgia + ± maculopapular rash (appears day 3–5 with defervescence)
  • Travel to endemic area (Caribbean, Puerto Rico, USVI, tropical/subtropical — also South Florida)
  • "Breakbone fever" — extreme bone/joint pain is characteristic
  • Warning signs for severe dengue: Abdominal pain, persistent vomiting, mucosal bleeding, fluid accumulation (ascites, pleural), rapid liver enlargement, rapid clinical deterioration, thrombocytopenia <20,000
  • Secondary infection with different serotype → highest risk for severe dengue (antibody-dependent enhancement)

Workup

  • NS1 antigen (days 1–5): Highly sensitive in early illness
  • Dengue PCR (days 1–5): Most specific; send in first week
  • Dengue IgM/IgG serology (day 5+): Confirms after viremic phase
  • CBC: Leukopenia + thrombocytopenia (hallmark); rising hematocrit signals plasma leakage
  • LFTs: Elevated (hepatitis); coagulation studies
  • Urine for proteinuria

Empiric Therapy

No specific antivirals — supportive care. Careful fluid management is the cornerstone of treatment.

SeverityManagement
UncomplicatedOral hydration; acetaminophen for pain/fever; monitoring
Dengue with warning signsIV isotonic crystalloid; close monitoring of hematocrit, urine output, vital signs
Severe dengue (shock, bleeding, organ impairment)ICU; IV fluid resuscitation; blood products for significant bleeding; platelet transfusion only for active bleeding or procedure (<20,000)

Avoid NSAIDs and aspirin — increase bleeding risk. Avoid steroids. Do NOT transfuse prophylactic platelets.

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Normal WBC or leukocytosis → less typical; consider chikungunya, Zika, leptospirosis, malaria
  • Thrombocytopenia but no dengue serology/antigen → send malaria smear; dengue and malaria can co-exist
  • Fever persisting >7 days → consider dengue hemorrhagic fever or secondary bacterial infection
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Plasma leakage (hemoconcentration, ascites, pleural effusion) → early aggressive IV fluids + close monitoring; avoid over-resuscitation
  • Dengue shock syndrome → ICU resuscitation; careful fluid titration (risk of pulmonary edema)
  • Major hemorrhage → blood products; GI bleeding → GI consultation
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Defervescence day 3–7 is critical period — plasma reabsorption can cause fluid overload; reduce IV fluids
  • Platelet count nadir typically day 5–7; spontaneous recovery expected
  • Discharge safe when: afebrile × 24h, improving, platelets rising, no warning signs, adequate oral intake

Recognition

  • Ixodes tick (deer tick) — Northeast, Midwest US; tick attached ≥36h required for transmission
  • Early localized: Erythema migrans ≥5cm expanding rash ± flu-like; treat clinically without waiting for serology
  • Early disseminated: Multiple EM lesions, Bell palsy, AV block (palpitations, near-syncope), radiculopathy, aseptic meningitis
  • Late: Lyme arthritis (large joint monoarthritis, knee most common)

Workup

  • 2-tier serology: ELISA → if positive/equivocal → Western blot; false-negative if <2 weeks
  • Neuroborreliosis: LP (pleocytosis, CSF Lyme index)
  • Carditis: ECG — PR prolongation, AV block
  • Arthritis: Synovial fluid cell count + PCR for Borrelia
  • Do NOT order urine Lyme antigen or PCR blood — unreliable

Empiric Therapy

SyndromeRegimenDuration
EM / early localizedDoxycycline 100mg PO BID10–14d
Facial palsy aloneDoxycycline 100mg PO BID14–21d
Neuroborreliosis / meningitisCeftriaxone 2g IV q24h14–28d
Carditis (1°/2° AVB)Doxycycline 100mg PO BID14–21d
Carditis (3° AVB / admitted)Ceftriaxone 2g IV → oral step-down14–21d total
Lyme arthritisDoxycycline 100mg PO BID28d
Pregnancy / childrenAmoxicillin 500mg PO TID14–21d
Post-exposure prophylaxisDoxycycline 200mg PO × 1Within 72h, tick ≥36h attached
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not responding → confirm 2-tier serology; consider alternative dx (reactive arthritis, viral)
  • Post-treatment Lyme disease syndrome ≠ active infection — do not re-treat with prolonged antibiotics
  • Arthritis failing oral → IV ceftriaxone × 28d; still failing → antibiotic-refractory Lyme (immune-mediated) → rheumatology
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • 3rd degree AV block → admit, telemetry, IV therapy, temporary pacing capability
  • CNS involvement (encephalitis, myelitis) → neurology + IV ceftriaxone
  • Severe systemic illness with multiorgan involvement
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • EM resolving, afebrile, tolerating PO → complete oral course; no test-of-cure needed
  • Carditis with AV block resolving → transition oral to complete 14–21 days
  • Serology does NOT guide de-escalation — antibodies persist years after cure

Recognition

  • Anaplasmosis: Ixodes tick (same as Lyme) — Northeast/Midwest; morulae in neutrophils
  • Ehrlichiosis: Lone star tick (Amblyomma) — Southeast; morulae in monocytes; rash in ~30%
  • Classic presentation: Abrupt fever, severe headache, myalgia, malaise 5–14d post-bite
  • Classic lab triad: Leukopenia + thrombocytopenia + elevated LFTs
  • Co-infection: Ixodes tick also carries Lyme and Babesia — consider all three

Workup

  • CBC: Leukopenia (key — distinguishes from RMSF) + thrombocytopenia
  • CMP: Elevated AST/ALT (2–5× ULN)
  • Peripheral smear: Morulae in 20–80% (neutrophils = Anaplasma; monocytes = Ehrlichia)
  • PCR (whole blood): Most sensitive — send before antibiotics
  • Serology (IFA): 4× rise in convalescent titer — not acutely useful
  • Co-infection workup: Lyme 2-tier serology, Babesia thick/thin smear + PCR

Empiric Therapy

RegimenDoseDuration
Doxycycline (first-line)100mg IV/PO q12h10–14d (3d afebrile minimum)
Pregnancy / intoleranceRifampin 300mg PO q12h10d

Treat empirically — expect clinical improvement at 24–48h. Failure to improve challenges the diagnosis.

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving at 48–72h → reconsider RMSF, viral illness, bacteremia, drug reaction
  • Leukocytosis instead of leukopenia → less typical; broaden differential
  • No Ixodes tick exposure → consider other rickettsial diseases
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Encephalopathy, seizures → LP, neurology consult, IV doxycycline
  • Respiratory failure, severe thrombocytopenia with bleeding → ICU admission
  • Septic shock → aggressive resuscitation + continue doxycycline
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Afebrile × 3 days, leukopenia resolving → complete orally
  • Minimum 10 days; 14 days if severe or CNS involved
  • Lyme co-infection confirmed → doxycycline covers both; adjust duration per Lyme requirements
  • Empiric: Doxycycline 100 mg PO/IV twice daily ×10–14 d; treat promptly on suspicion regardless of age.
  • Presentation: fever, cytopenias, elevated LFTs; can be severe in immunocompromised.

Recognition

  • Vector: Dermacentor variabilis (dog tick) — Southeast/Mid-Atlantic; April–September peak
  • Classic triad: Fever + severe headache + rash — triad present in only ~60% at presentation
  • Rash: Day 2–5; wrists/ankles → trunk (centripetal spread); petechial/purpuric; involves palms/soles
  • Spotless RMSF: 10–15% — no rash; highest mortality due to delayed treatment
  • DO NOT WAIT for lab confirmation — mortality doubles after day 5 without treatment

Workup

  • IFA serology: Often negative in first week — do not delay treatment for results
  • Skin biopsy PCR: Most sensitive during acute rash
  • CBC: Thrombocytopenia; WBC normal or elevated (leukopenia NOT typical — differentiates from Anaplasmosis)
  • CMP: Hyponatremia, elevated LFTs, elevated creatinine

Empiric Therapy

RegimenDoseDuration
Doxycycline (ALL ages)100mg IV/PO q12hMin 3d afebrile; 7–14d total

No reliable alternative. Doxycycline is safe in children — dental risk outweighed by severity.

🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving 48–72h → Ehrlichiosis, meningococcemia, viral hemorrhagic fever
  • Normal or elevated WBC → RMSF still possible; leukopenia more typical of Anaplasmosis
  • 30% of RMSF patients don't recall tick bite — don't exclude based on history
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • DIC, ARDS, renal failure, neurologic deterioration → ICU
  • Purpura fulminans → aggressive ICU support, surgical assessment for necrosis
  • Fever persisting >5d on therapy → ID consultation, reconsider diagnosis
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Afebrile × 3 days, clinically improving → complete orally
  • Minimum 7 days; 10–14 days for severe disease
  • Clinical response guides duration — no confirmatory serology needed to stop

Recognition

  • Q fever (Coxiella burnetii): Inhalation of infected aerosols from livestock (cattle, sheep, goats); farmers, veterinarians, abbatoir workers
  • Acute Q fever: atypical pneumonia, granulomatous hepatitis, or febrile illness (fever + severe headache)
  • Chronic Q fever: endocarditis (months–years later) — always in setting of valvular disease or immunocompromise
  • Scrub typhus (Orientia tsutsugamushi): Trombiculid mite bite; Asia-Pacific; eschar at bite site (pathognomonic); fever + headache + rash

Workup

  • Q fever serology (IFA): Phase I IgG ≥1:800 = chronic Q fever; Phase II IgG ≥1:200 = acute Q fever; 2-week convalescent titer for confirmation
  • PCR: Best in acute phase (before antibiotics)
  • Every patient with acute Q fever needs TTE/TEE — endocarditis may be the presentation or develop silently
  • Scrub typhus: Serology (IFA), PCR from eschar scraping or blood; look for eschar (often hidden in skin folds)

Empiric Therapy

ConditionRegimenDuration
Acute Q feverDoxycycline 100mg PO BID14–21 days
Chronic Q fever (endocarditis)Doxycycline 100mg PO BID + Hydroxychloroquine 200mg PO TID≥18 months; monitor with serology
Q fever in pregnancyTMP-SMX throughout pregnancy (doxycycline contraindicated)Until 32 weeks gestation
Scrub typhusDoxycycline 100mg PO BID7 days
Scrub typhus — pregnancy / doxy intolerantAzithromycin 500mg PO daily3–7 days
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Acute Q fever not responding → confirm diagnosis (serology); consider chronic phase (endocarditis)
  • Elevated Phase I IgG + cardiac symptoms → chronic Q fever endocarditis (long treatment course)
  • Scrub typhus not responding → azithromycin (resistance documented in some Asian strains)
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Chronic Q fever endocarditis → cardiology + ID; valve surgery if hemodynamically significant
  • Severe acute Q fever with ARDS or hepatitis → ICU support; doxycycline IV
  • Serology rising at 6 months after acute Q fever → risk of chronification; cardiac echo, ID follow-up
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Acute Q fever: doxycycline × 2–3 weeks; follow Phase I/II IgG at 3, 6, 12 months
  • Chronic endocarditis: hydroxychloroquine alkalinizes phagolysosome → enhances doxycycline activity; treat minimum 18 months
  • End of chronic treatment: Phase I IgG <1:800 and Phase II <1:200 × 2 serial titers

Recognition

  • Inhalation or cutaneous contact with contaminated soil/water in endemic areas: SE Asia, Northern Australia, India, Caribbean (increasingly recognized)
  • Presentations: pneumonia (most common — mimics TB; cavitary, upper lobe), bacteremia, localized skin/soft tissue, genitourinary, neurological
  • Diabetes is the #1 risk factor — 50% of cases
  • Latent melioidosis: dormant for years → reactivation with immunosuppression (can mimic TB reactivation)
  • High mortality if not treated

Workup

  • Blood cultures: Positive in ~40–60% of bacteremic cases; alert lab to hold for 7–10 days (slow grower)
  • Culture from relevant site: Sputum, urine, wound, pus — Burkholderia selective agar
  • Serology (IHA): variable sensitivity/specificity depending on endemic exposure; not reliable for acute diagnosis
  • CT chest: Cavitary lesions, consolidation, nodules — non-specific but prompts culture
  • Inform lab: special handling required

Empiric Therapy

PhaseRegimenDuration
Intensive (induction)Ceftazidime 2g IV q6–8h OR Meropenem 1g IV q8h10–14 days (longer if severe, neurological, or bacteremic)
Eradication (oral step-down)TMP-SMX DS 2 tabs PO BID + Doxycycline 100mg PO BID3–6 months (3 months uncomplicated; 6 months complicated/bone/CNS)

Do NOT use ciprofloxacin — intrinsically resistant. Do NOT use amoxicillin-clavulanate as primary therapy. Eradication phase mandatory — relapse rate 10–26% without it.

Source Control

  • Drainage of any loculated abscess
  • Source control critical for localized infections
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not improving on ceftazidime → switch to meropenem; confirm organism identification; resistance possible
  • Relapse after eradication therapy → re-treat; check TMP-SMX susceptibility; consider CNS/bone source
  • Mimics TB → culture all specimens; do not start empiric TB treatment without culture confirmation
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Septic shock → meropenem preferred over ceftazidime; ICU; aggressive resuscitation
  • Neurological melioidosis (brain abscess, meningitis) → meropenem × 4–6 weeks intensive; TMP-SMX × 6–12 months eradication
  • Respiratory failure → ARDS management; continue antibiotics
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Intensive phase: switch to oral TMP-SMX + doxycycline when clinically stable, tolerating PO, improving
  • Eradication phase is mandatory — premature discontinuation leads to relapse
  • Long-term follow-up: relapse can occur years later; educate patients on symptoms; re-expose to soil/water precautions

Recognition

  • Mild febrile illness: low-grade fever, maculopapular rash (pruritic), arthralgia (small joints), non-purulent conjunctivitis
  • 80% of infections asymptomatic; symptomatic illness usually mild and self-limited (3–7 days)
  • Serious complications: Guillain-Barré syndrome (1 in 4,000 symptomatic infections); Congenital Zika syndrome (microcephaly, brain malformations, hearing loss — greatest risk 1st trimester)
  • Transmission: Aedes mosquito (also sexual transmission — longer persistence in semen); blood transfusion
  • Endemic: Caribbean, Puerto Rico, USVI, Latin America, Pacific Islands; occasional Florida clusters

Workup

  • PCR (urine preferred > serum): Most sensitive in first 7 days of symptoms; urine PCR positive longer than serum
  • Serology (IgM): After 7 days; cross-reactive with dengue/flaviviruses — confirm with PRNT at CDC
  • Pregnant women with any exposure: test immediately regardless of symptoms; serial fetal ultrasounds
  • GBS evaluation: nerve conduction studies, LP (albumino-cytologic dissociation)
  • Report to public health; CDC guidance for pregnancy management updated regularly

Empiric Therapy

No specific antivirals. No vaccine (investigational).

IndicationManagement
Symptomatic ZikaAcetaminophen; hydration; rest. Avoid NSAIDs/aspirin until dengue excluded (bleeding risk)
GBSIVIG 2g/kg OR plasmapheresis; neurology; ICU if respiratory compromise
PregnancyMaternal and fetal monitoring; fetal MRI if microcephaly suspected; maternal-fetal medicine consultation
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Rash + fever + travel → dengue must be excluded (overlapping presentation, management differs)
  • Weakness developing after Zika → GBS until proven otherwise; emergency neurology referral
  • Prolonged fever (>7 days) → consider secondary bacterial infection or co-infection with dengue/chikungunya
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • GBS with respiratory failure → ICU, mechanical ventilation, IVIG/PLEX
  • Pregnant patient with Zika exposure → maternal-fetal medicine; fetal ultrasound every 4 weeks; fetal MRI if brain abnormality suspected
  • Congenital Zika with microcephaly → pediatric neurology, ophthalmology, audiology at birth
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Symptomatic illness: self-limited; no specific treatment; supportive care
  • Sexual transmission prevention: male partner should use condoms × 3 months after illness (or exposure without symptoms × 2 months); female partner × 2 months
  • Pregnancy counseling: condom use with any partner with Zika exposure; delay conception (CDC guidance varies by evidence)

Recognition

  • Abrupt high fever + severe bilateral symmetric polyarthralgia (distal joints — wrists, ankles, fingers) — arthralgias are the dominant feature (vs dengue where myalgia/bone pain dominates)
  • Maculopapular rash in 40–75% (typically after day 2–5, can be pruritic)
  • Aedes mosquito; Caribbean (Puerto Rico, USVI), Latin America; travel
  • Chronic arthritis in 30–40% — months to years; most common in elderly and those with prior joint disease
  • Rare severe: encephalitis, myocarditis, hepatitis, bullous skin lesions — neonates and elderly at highest risk

Workup

  • PCR (serum): Days 1–6 — most sensitive in acute viremic phase
  • Serology (IgM/IgG): After day 5; IgM persists for months
  • CBC: Leukopenia + lymphopenia + thrombocytopenia (less pronounced than dengue)
  • CRP/ESR: Markedly elevated; persists in chronic arthritis
  • Exclude dengue co-circulation: send dengue NS1 + PCR simultaneously

Empiric Therapy

No specific antivirals. No vaccine available in US.

PhaseManagement
Acute (first 3 months)Rest + hydration; Acetaminophen (first-line for fever/pain). Add NSAIDs after dengue excluded.
Subacute arthritis (3 weeks–3 months)NSAIDs; low-dose oral corticosteroids if NSAIDs insufficient; chloroquine
Chronic arthritis (>3 months)NSAIDs; hydroxychloroquine 200–400mg daily; methotrexate for refractory
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Persisting fever >7 days → exclude dengue co-infection (simultaneous circulation common), secondary bacterial infection
  • Monoarticular involvement → septic arthritis? Lyme? Crystal arthropathy? Chikungunya is typically polyarticular
  • Neurologic symptoms (confusion, seizure) → rare chikungunya encephalitis; CSF PCR
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Encephalitis → ICU; supportive; IVIG (limited evidence); neurology
  • Neonatal chikungunya (peripartum transmission) → NICU admission; high neonatal morbidity
  • Severe thrombocytopenia with active bleeding → hematology; blood products as needed
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Acute fever: self-limited 7–10 days
  • Chronic arthritis: educate patient — may persist months to years; rheumatology follow-up for persistent cases
  • Avoid NSAIDs in dengue co-exposure; acetaminophen safe to use throughout

Overview

  • Alphavirus transmitted by Aedes mosquitoes (A. aegypti, A. albopictus); the latter allows spread to temperate climates
  • Name: Makonde language — "that which bends up" (describing stooped posture from joint pain)
  • Geography: Sub-Saharan Africa, Indian Ocean islands, South/Southeast Asia, Caribbean, Americas; outbreaks increasingly worldwide including Europe (Italy, France)

Clinical Features

  • Acute phase (1–2 weeks): Abrupt-onset high fever (39–40°C), severe polyarthralgia (bilateral, symmetric, distal predominant — wrists, ankles, fingers), maculopapular rash (40–75%), headache, myalgia
  • Arthralgia is the hallmark — often debilitating; can affect multiple joints simultaneously; periarticular edema
  • Chronic arthritis: 30–40% have joint pain >3 months; some develop rheumatoid-like arthritis lasting years — worse in older patients, those with pre-existing arthritis
  • Distinguishing from dengue: Chikungunya — arthralgia dominant, rash common, hemorrhage rare; Dengue — myalgia/bone pain dominant, thrombocytopenia, hemorrhagic complications

Diagnosis

  • RT-PCR: During febrile phase (first 5–7 days) — most sensitive
  • Serology (IgM/IgG): IgM appears at day 5–7; persists months; confirms diagnosis in subacute/chronic presentation
  • Labs: Lymphopenia (distinguishes from dengue which has thrombocytopenia); elevated CRP/ESR; mild LFT elevation

Treatment

  • No specific antiviral: Supportive care
  • Acute phase: Acetaminophen for fever; avoid NSAIDs/aspirin until dengue excluded; rest, fluids
  • Chronic arthritis: NSAIDs (after dengue excluded); chloroquine or hydroxychloroquine for prolonged arthralgia; methotrexate for severe chronic arthritis; short-course corticosteroids for severe flares
  • No vaccine available (as of 2025 — mVSV-based vaccine under development)
5

Special populations

Immunocompromised · HIV · OIs · Fungal · Viral

Neutropenic Fever

Definitions

  • Fever: Single temp ≥38.3°C OR ≥38.0°C sustained for ≥1 hour
  • Neutropenia: ANC <500/µL OR <1000/µL with expected decline to <500 within 48h
  • Profound neutropenia: ANC <100/µL
  • Prolonged neutropenia: Expected >7 days

MASCC Risk Index (Multinational Association for Supportive Care in Cancer)

CharacteristicPoints
Burden of illness: Mild or no symptoms5
Burden of illness: Moderate symptoms3
No hypotension (SBP ≥90)5
No COPD4
Solid tumor or no prior fungal infection4
Outpatient at fever onset3
No dehydration3
Age <60 years2

Score ≥21: Low risk—may consider outpatient oral therapy (quinolone + amoxicillin-clavulanate)

Score <21: High risk—requires inpatient IV therapy

Empiric Therapy

RiskRegimen
Standard (high-risk)Cefepime 2g IV q8h OR Piperacillin-tazobactam 4.5g IV q6h OR Meropenem 1g IV q8h
+ MRSA risk factorsAdd Vancomycin (hemodynamic instability, skin/catheter infection, pneumonia, known MRSA colonization)
+ Pseudomonas riskUse anti-pseudomonal beta-lactam (cefepime, pip-tazo, meropenem)
Low risk (outpatient)Ciprofloxacin 750mg PO q12h + Amoxicillin-clavulanate 875mg PO q12h

Add Antifungal If:

  • Persistent fever >4-7 days despite broad-spectrum antibiotics
  • Prolonged neutropenia (>10 days expected)
  • Hematologic malignancy or HSCT recipient
  • Options: Caspofungin, Micafungin, Liposomal Amphotericin B, Voriconazole (if Aspergillus suspected)

Duration

  • Continue until ANC ≥500 AND afebrile for 48h (if source unidentified)
  • If documented infection, complete appropriate course for that infection
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Fever persisting >72–96h despite broad-spectrum anti-pseudomonal antibiotics → add antifungal (fluconazole or echinocandin); CT chest to look for invasive fungal infection
  • New pulmonary infiltrates developing on antibiotics → send BAL; consider invasive aspergillosis (galactomannan, CT halo sign); start voriconazole empirically if high suspicion
  • Chest CT shows halo sign → Aspergillus until proven otherwise; start voriconazole, ID consult
  • Blood cultures growing unexpected organism → review GI source, skin breakdown, mucositis; ensure port/PICC not source
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Hemodynamic instability → sepsis protocol; consider ICU
  • Pneumonia + respiratory failure → ICU, consider BAL before escalating antibiotics further
  • Invasive fungal infection suspected → ID + pulmonology; bone marrow transplant team if applicable
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • ANC recovering to >500/µL + afebrile × 48h + clinically well → can consider stopping antibiotics
  • Low-risk patient (MASCC ≥21) with negative cultures → consider oral step-down (ciprofloxacin + amoxicillin-clavulanate)
  • Source identified → narrow to targeted coverage once susceptibilities available

Transplant & Biologic Therapy Infections

Infection risk in SOT and patients on immunomodulatory therapy.

Infection risk in SOT follows a predictable timeline driven by net state of immunosuppression, surgical complications, and donor-derived infections.

Timeline Framework

PeriodTimingDominant Infections
Early (<1 month)Post-transplant Surgical/nosocomial: Wound infections, SSI, anastomotic leaks; CLABSI, VAP, CAUTI (ICU complications)
Donor-derived: Unexpected donor infections transmitted with organ (e.g., unrecognized rabies, HSV, donor bacteremia, WNV)
Recipient-derived: Pre-existing colonization (MRSA, VRE, MDR GNR, Candida)
Classic OIs (PCP, CMV) are RARE this early — still have residual immunity
Middle (1–6 months)Peak immunosuppression Opportunistic infections dominate:
• CMV (most important — see below)
• PCP (Pneumocystis jirovecii) — if not on prophylaxis
• Aspergillus, Candida (invasive)
• Nocardia, Listeria
• Toxoplasma (especially heart transplant: D+/R−)
• Cryptococcus
• BK polyomavirus nephropathy (kidney transplant)
• Hepatitis B/C reactivation
Late (>6 months)Maintenance immunosuppression Community-acquired infections (similar to general population) + ongoing OI risk:
• CMV, EBV-related PTLD (post-transplant lymphoproliferative disorder)
• Late Cryptococcus, Nocardia
• Skin cancers (HPV-related), BCC, SCC
• Patients on high maintenance immunosuppression remain at OI risk

CMV in SOT

Risk CategorySerostatusRiskManagement
HighestDonor+ / Recipient− (D+/R−)60–80% primary CMV disease without prophylaxisValganciclovir prophylaxis × 6 months (lung/heart); 3–6 months others
HighD−/R+ (reactivation)20–40%Valganciclovir prophylaxis × 3 months or preemptive monitoring
LowD−/R−<5% (de novo)No routine prophylaxis; test at risk exposure

Key Prophylaxis Protocols (typical)

  • PCP: TMP-SMX SS daily × 6–12 months (lifelong in lung transplant); dapsone or atovaquone if intolerant
  • Fungal: Fluconazole (liver, kidney) or voriconazole/micafungin (lung) × 1–3 months post-transplant
  • Toxoplasma: TMP-SMX (also covers PCP); pyrimethamine + dapsone if intolerant
  • Nocardia: TMP-SMX prophylaxis also provides coverage
  • BK virus: Monitor urine BK PCR; if viremia develops → reduce immunosuppression; no antivirals proven

TNF-α Inhibitors (etanercept, infliximab, adalimumab, certolizumab, golimumab)

  • TB: Most important concern — reactivation latent TB; screen with TST or IGRA before starting; treat LTBI (INH × 9 months or rifampin × 4 months) before initiating if positive; can start TNF inhibitor 1–2 months into LTBI treatment
  • Histoplasma, Coccidioides, Blastomyces: Reactivation of dimorphic fungi — screen for endemic exposures; avoid in active endemic fungal infection
  • Listeria, Salmonella — increased susceptibility
  • Hepatitis B reactivation: Screen all patients; treat with antivirals before starting (see Hep B card)
  • Do NOT start during active serious infection

Rituximab (anti-CD20 — B-cell depletion)

Highest risk for HBV reactivation of any biologic — screen ALL patients; antiviral prophylaxis mandatory if HBsAg+ or anti-HBc+. Continue prophylaxis ≥12 months after last rituximab dose.

  • PCP: Significant risk — TMP-SMX prophylaxis recommended during and for 6–12 months after rituximab
  • JC virus / PML: Progressive multifocal leukoencephalopathy — rare but devastating; check JC antibody status before initiating in high-risk patients
  • Hypogammaglobulinemia: IVIG replacement if IgG <400 with recurrent infections
  • COVID-19: Poor antibody response to vaccines; consider prophylactic monoclonal antibodies in immunocompromised

JAK Inhibitors (tofacitinib, baricitinib, upadacitinib)

  • Herpes zoster: Significantly increased risk — ensure VZV vaccination before starting (live vaccine, so must be before immunosuppression; recombinant zoster vaccine (Shingrix) preferred, can give on therapy)
  • TB, fungal: Similar risk profile to TNF inhibitors — screen appropriately
  • VTE risk: Particularly with higher doses of tofacitinib — not strictly ID but important safety consideration

High-dose Corticosteroids (≥20mg/day prednisone equivalent × >4 weeks)

  • PCP: TMP-SMX prophylaxis when ≥20mg/day prednisone × >4 weeks (or equivalent)
  • Strongyloides hyperinfection: Screen with serology in patients from endemic areas before starting; treat if positive (ivermectin 200mcg/kg × 2 doses)
  • Candida, Aspergillus: Increased risk at high doses and prolonged courses
  • HBV reactivation: Screen and prophylax as above

General Principles

  • Hold biologics during active serious infection — restart after adequate treatment and clinical resolution
  • Vaccination before initiating immunosuppression: Pneumococcal, influenza, hepatitis A/B, VZV (live vaccines must be given ≥4 weeks before starting; inactivated vaccines can be given anytime)
  • Live vaccines are contraindicated on most biologics (MMR, varicella, zoster live-attenuated, intranasal flu, yellow fever, oral typhoid)

🛡️ Immunocompromised Host

Solid Organ Transplant — Infection Timeline

HIGH YIELD
Net State of Immunosuppression Concept

The risk of infection in transplant recipients is determined by the net state of immunosuppression: the cumulative effect of all immunosuppressive agents × dose × duration, plus host factors (viral coinfections — CMV, EBV, HCV — that independently impair immunity).

Timeline of Infections Post-Transplant
Time PeriodLikely InfectionsKey Pathogens
0–1 Month
(Post-operative period)		 Surgical site infections, donor-derived infections, HAIs S. aureus, gram-negative rods, Candida (wound/line/UTI), C. difficile. Surgical complications (bile leak, anastomotic leak). If unusual pathogens in first month — consider donor-derived transmission.
1–6 Months
(Peak immunosuppression)		 Opportunistic infections predominate CMV (highest risk — D+/R- serology), PJP, Listeria, Nocardia, endemic fungi (Histo, Cocci, Crypto), Toxoplasma (heart transplant with D+/R-), BK virus (kidney), Aspergillus (lung transplant). Active CMV prophylaxis/preemptive monitoring in this window.
>6 Months
(Maintenance immunosuppression)		 Community-acquired + late opportunistic Community infections (pneumonia, UTI) dominate in patients doing well. Late opportunistic infections in patients with chronic rejection, GVHD, augmented immunosuppression, or prior CMV disease. EBV-PTLD (B-cell lymphoma). Skin cancers (accelerated by immunosuppression).
Organ-Specific Infection Risks
Transplant TypeUnique InfectionsNotes
LungAspergillus (highest risk of all SOT), Pseudomonas, CMV pneumonitis, PJP, NTMLung exposed to environment directly; longer antifungal prophylaxis; most infectious deaths of all SOT types
LiverIntra-abdominal infections, biliary infections, HCV recurrence (now cured with DAAs), HBV reactivationFirst month dominated by surgical complications; CMV and fungal risk 1–6 months
KidneyUTI (most common), BK nephropathy, CMVBK viremia monitored q3 months in first 2 years; reduce MMF if BK viremia >10,000 copies
HeartCMV, Toxoplasma (D+/R- = high risk), AspergillusToxoplasma heart serology checked pre-transplant; D+/R- → TMP-SMX prophylaxis × 6 months
Pancreas/IsletIntra-abdominal infections, CMV, candidiasisHigh exocrine leak rate; surgical complications predominate early
📘 Standard Prophylaxis Post-Transplant
  • PJP: TMP-SMX 1 SS or DS tab daily × 6–12 months (all SOT; longer in lung, heart)
  • CMV: Valganciclovir 900 mg PO daily × 3–6 months (D+/R-); preemptive monitoring (R+) with weekly PCR → treat if threshold reached
  • Candida (liver/pancreas): Fluconazole 400 mg daily × 4 weeks in high-risk (re-operation, renal failure, prolonged OR)
  • HSV/VZV: Acyclovir or valacyclovir in seropositive patients — typically 1 year post-transplant
  • Toxoplasma (heart D+/R-): TMP-SMX × 6 months (also covers PJP)

Hematopoietic Stem Cell Transplant (HSCT) — Infection Timeline

HIGH YIELD
Three Phases of Infection Risk
PhaseTimeImmune DefectsKey Pathogens
Phase 1
Pre-engraftment		 0–30 days Profound neutropenia (ANC <100), mucositis, central lines Gram-positive (CoNS, VGS, S. aureus — line/mucositis), Gram-negative (ESBL, Pseudomonas), Candida (gut flora breach from mucositis), Herpes simplex (reactivation), C. difficile
Phase 2
Post-engraftment		 30–100 days Impaired cell-mediated immunity; GVHD; steroid use CMV (peak risk for D+/R-; preemptive monitoring essential), Aspergillus (especially with GVHD), PJP, Toxoplasma, HHV-6 encephalitis, adenovirus, RSV
Phase 3
Late		 >100 days Chronic GVHD → prolonged immune dysfunction; functional asplenia in some Encapsulated bacteria (S. pneumoniae, H. influenzae, N. meningitidis) — treat like asplenic patient; late CMV, EBV-PTLD, VZV reactivation (years post-HSCT), community respiratory viruses
Allogeneic vs. Autologous Risk
  • Allogeneic HSCT: Higher risk — GVHD, prolonged T-cell deficiency, steroid use. All phases significant. CMV prophylaxis/preemptive therapy required.
  • Autologous HSCT: Lower risk — no GVHD; neutropenic phase only (∼2 weeks); CMV reactivation less common. Bacterial/fungal infections pre-engraftment.

Biologic & Disease-Modifying Agents — Infection Risks

HIGH YIELD
Agent ClassExamplesKey Infection RisksPre-Tx Screening
TNF-α Inhibitors Infliximab, etanercept, adalimumab, certolizumab TB reactivation (most important — 5× increased risk); Histoplasmosis, Coccidioidomycosis, Blastomycosis; Listeria; Legionella; opportunistic infections including PJP (less common than HIV doses) TB: TST or IGRA — treat LTBI before starting (isoniazid × 9 months, or rifampin × 4 months)
HBV: HBsAg + anti-HBc → antiviral prophylaxis before starting
HCV, HIV screening; varicella/zoster vaccination before if seronegative
Anti-IL-6 Tocilizumab, sarilumab Bacterial infections; mask fever and CRP (CRP suppressed by IL-6 blockade — classic board pearl: CRP unreliable as infection marker); TB risk lower than TNF-i TB screening; HBV screening
Anti-CD20 (B-cell depleting) Rituximab, obinutuzumab, ofatumumab HBV reactivation (highest risk of all biologics — 20-40% if HBsAg+, 1-10% if anti-HBc+ only); PJP; hypogammaglobulinemia → recurrent bacterial sinopulmonary infections; late T-cell defects (months to years after) HBV: HBsAg AND anti-HBc — prophylactic antivirals for all HBsAg+; prophylaxis or monitoring for anti-HBc+ only
HIV, HCV, TB
JAK Inhibitors Tofacitinib, baricitinib, ruxolitinib, upadacitinib Herpes zoster (2-4× increased risk — PJP prophylaxis recommended; Shingrix vaccination before starting if possible); TB reactivation; bacterial infections; cryptococcosis; VZV reactivation prominent TB screening; HBV screening; consider PJP prophylaxis with TMP-SMX; Shingrix before starting (wait 2 weeks after vaccination)
Checkpoint Inhibitors (anti-PD1, anti-CTLA4) Nivolumab, pembrolizumab, ipilimumab Not primarily immunosuppressive — immune-related adverse events (irAEs) actually cause inflammatory syndromes. High-dose steroids for irAEs → superimposed PJP risk. Paradoxically may unmask TB/endemic fungi by reversing tolerance. JC virus → PML possible. TB screening if high-risk; consider PJP prophylaxis if requiring high-dose/prolonged steroids for irAEs
Corticosteroids Prednisone ≥20 mg/day × ≥4 weeks PJP (most important); Strongyloides hyperinfection; TB reactivation; candidiasis; Aspergillus; bacterial infections TB screening; Strongyloides serology if endemic exposure; consider PJP prophylaxis with TMP-SMX
Complement inhibitors Eculizumab, ravulizumab Encapsulated bacteria — Neisseria meningitidis (10,000× increased risk of meningococcal disease); also N. gonorrhoeae, H. influenzae, S. pneumoniae Mandatory vaccinations BEFORE starting: Meningococcal (MenACWY + MenB), Hib, pneumococcal (PCV15/20 + PPSV23). Ongoing penicillin prophylaxis recommended in many guidelines.
⚡ HIGHEST YIELD BIOLOGIC PEARLS FOR BOARDS
  • TNF inhibitors → screen and treat LTBI before starting (TST/IGRA mandatory)
  • Rituximab → HBV reactivation — screen HBsAg AND anti-HBc; prophylactic antivirals for all HBsAg+
  • Tocilizumab suppresses CRP — CRP cannot be used as infection biomarker in patients on IL-6 blockade
  • Eculizumab → meningococcal vaccines mandatory before starting; highest infectious risk is N. meningitidis
  • JAK inhibitors → Shingrix before starting; herpes zoster prophylaxis consideration

Primary Immunodeficiency — Infection Patterns

Defect TypeConditionCharacteristic InfectionsClues
Antibody (B-cell)XLA (Bruton's), CVID, IgA deficiencyEncapsulated bacteria (S. pneumoniae, H. influenzae, N. meningitidis), Giardia (IgA deficiency), Campylobacter bacteremia, recurrent sinopulmonary infections, Cryptosporidium (CVID)Recurrent bacterial infections starting after 6 months (maternal IgG wanes); low immunoglobulins
T-cell / Combined (T+B)DiGeorge, SCID, Wiskott-AldrichOpportunistic infections (PJP, Candida, CMV, EBV, Cryptosporidium), disseminated BCG after vaccination, live vaccine complicationsEarly infancy; opportunistic infections; contraindication to live vaccines
PhagocyteCGD, leukocyte adhesion deficiency (LAD), Chediak-HigashiCGD: catalase-positive organisms (S. aureus, Aspergillus, Nocardia, Burkholderia cepacia, Serratia, gram-negative bacteria). LAD: severe/recalcitrant bacterial infections without pus formation.CGD: recurrent deep-seated abscesses/granulomas; nitroblue tetrazolium (NBT) test diagnostic
