Asplenia & Functional Hyposplenism

HIGH YIELD
Causes of Hyposplenism
  • Surgical splenectomy: Trauma, ITP, hereditary spherocytosis, portal hypertension
  • Functional asplenia: Sickle cell disease (autoinfarction by age 5), celiac disease, bone marrow infiltration, heavy alcohol use, SLE, hypothyroidism
OPSI — Overwhelming Post-Splenectomy Infection
  • Presentation: Rapidly fulminant sepsis — may start as mild flu-like illness then deteriorate within hours to multi-organ failure, DIC, death. Mortality 50–70% without rapid treatment.
  • Classic organisms: Encapsulated bacteria — S. pneumoniae (50-90%), H. influenzae type b, N. meningitidis. Less common: E. coli, S. aureus, Capnocytophaga canimorsus (dog bite), Babesia (tick-borne).
  • Capnocytophaga canimorsus: Classic board case — dog bite + asplenic patient → rapidly fatal sepsis. Treat with amoxicillin-clavulanate or penicillin.
Vaccinations — MANDATORY for Asplenic Patients
VaccineTiming (Elective Splenectomy)Schedule
Pneumococcal (PCV20 or PCV15 → PPSV23)>2 weeks before surgery (ideally 4–6 weeks)PCV20 × 1; OR PCV15 → PPSV23 ≥8 weeks later. PPSV23 booster at 5 years if high-risk.
Meningococcal (MenACWY)>2 weeks before surgeryMenACWY × 1 (Menveo or Menactra); booster q5 years as long as asplenic
Meningococcal B (MenB)>2 weeks before surgery2-dose or 3-dose series depending on product (Bexsero or Trumenba); booster q3–5 years
Hib (H. influenzae type b)>2 weeks before surgery1 dose if not previously vaccinated

Emergency splenectomy: Vaccinate ≥2 weeks post-operatively (earlier vaccination less immunogenic due to surgical stress).

Antibiotic prophylaxis: Amoxicillin 250–500 mg PO daily (or penicillin V). Most guidelines recommend for children lifelong; adults at minimum for 2 years post-splenectomy or lifelong if high risk. Amoxicillin-clavulanate if Capnocytophaga coverage desired.

⚡ Clinical Emergency: Asplenic Patient with Fever

Treat as OPSI until proven otherwise:
1. Blood cultures × 2 immediately
2. Empiric antibiotics within 30 minutes: Ceftriaxone 2 g IV (covers pneumococcus, meningococcus, H. influenzae)
3. Add vancomycin if penicillin-resistant pneumococcus suspected
4. Do NOT wait for cultures — mortality accelerates by the hour

HIV — Inpatient Management

DHHS Guidelines 2024 · WHO 2023 · IDSA HIV guidelines

Routine HIV Screening (CDC 2006)

  • All patients ages 13–64 in healthcare settings — at least once as part of routine care
  • Annual screening for high-risk individuals (MSM, PWID, multiple partners, STI history)
  • Testing: 4th-generation HIV-1/2 Ag/Ab combination immunoassay (detects p24 antigen + antibodies) — window period ~18 days
  • Reactive → HIV-1/2 differentiation assay → if indeterminate → HIV-1 RNA viral load

Newly Diagnosed HIV — Baseline Labs

TestClinical Use
CD4+ T-cell countImmune status; OI prophylaxis thresholds (<200: PCP; <100: toxo; <50: MAC)
HIV viral load (RNA PCR)Baseline; target undetectable (<20–50 copies/mL) on ART
HIV resistance genotypeBefore ART initiation; guides regimen selection
HLA-B*5701Screening before abacavir use (hypersensitivity)
CBC, CMP, LFTs, lipid panel, urinalysisBaseline organ function; drug toxicity monitoring
Hepatitis A/B/C serologyCoinfection screening; vaccination status (HBV, HAV)
Syphilis RPR, GC/Chlamydia NAATSTI co-screening
TB testing (IGRA preferred in HIV)Latent TB screening
CMV IgG, Toxoplasma IgGSerostatus; guides prophylaxis decisions
G6PDIf primaquine/dapsone considered
Pap smear (women)Cervical cancer screening (higher frequency in HIV)

When to Start ART

  • DHHS 2024: ART should be started in all people living with HIV regardless of CD4 count — including during hospitalization for OI or acute illness
  • Exception — delay ART if: Cryptococcal meningitis (start after 4–6 weeks of induction antifungal — early ART increases IRIS mortality, COAT trial); TB meningitis (delay 8 weeks, A5221 trial); CMV retinitis in some settings
  • Toxoplasmosis, PCP, MAC, most bacterial OIs: Start ART within 2 weeks of OI treatment (ACTG A5164 trial — earlier ART → reduced AIDS/death)

Preferred Regimens (ART-naïve) — DHHS 2024

Regimen (single-tablet preferred)Notes
Biktarvy (bictegravir/tenofovir AF/emtricitabine)Most prescribed; high barrier to resistance; renal dose adjustment needed if eGFR <30
Dovato (dolutegravir/lamivudine)2-drug regimen; not if VL >500k or HBV coinfection
Triumeq (dolutegravir/abacavir/lamivudine)Requires HLA-B*5701 negative; CVD risk consideration with abacavir
Descovy + dolutegravir or cabotegravirTAF preferred over TDF if renal/bone concerns

INSTI-based regimens preferred (dolutegravir, bictegravir, cabotegravir) — high efficacy, high barrier to resistance, good tolerability.

  • Paradoxical worsening of existing OI or unmasking of new infection after ART initiation due to immune recovery
  • Typically occurs weeks to months after ART start; risk highest with very low baseline CD4 and rapid virologic suppression
  • Common IRIS presentations: Cryptococcal meningitis (↑ ICP), TB (lymphadenopathy enlargement, fever, worsening infiltrates), MAC, CMV, HBV/HCV flare, PML worsening
  • Cryptococcal IRIS: LP for ICP management (therapeutic LPs); prednisone not routinely helpful (unlike TB-IRIS)
  • TB-IRIS management: Continue ART + TB therapy; prednisone 1.5 mg/kg/day × 2 weeks then taper (NEJM 2010 CARINEMO trial)
  • General IRIS management: Continue ART; NSAIDs for mild; prednisone for severe; only stop ART if life-threatening CNS involvement
ART DrugInteracting DrugInteraction / Management
Dolutegravir / bictegravir / cabotegravirPolyvalent cations (Ca²⁺, Mg²⁺, Al³⁺, Fe)Chelation → ↓ INSTI absorption; separate by 2h before or 6h after
Boosted PI (ritonavir, cobicistat)Statins (simvastatin, lovastatin)Contraindicated — severe myopathy/rhabdomyolysis; use rosuvastatin or atorvastatin (low dose)
Boosted PI / cobicistatRifampinContraindicated — rifampin is potent CYP3A4 inducer → subtherapeutic PI levels; use rifabutin instead
Efavirenz (NNRTI)RifampinIncrease efavirenz dose to 800 mg if weight >60 kg
DolutegravirRifampinDouble dolutegravir dose to 50 mg BID (vs standard 50 mg daily)
Ritonavir/cobicistatAzole antifungals (fluconazole, voriconazole)Complex bidirectional interactions — consult clinical pharmacist
TDF (tenofovir DF)NSAIDs, aminoglycosidesAdditive nephrotoxicity; prefer TAF (tenofovir alafenamide) or alternative

Always use an interaction checker (HIV-DrugInteractions.org, Liverpool) before adding/changing medications in HIV patients on ART.

Recognition

  • Subacute onset: dry cough, progressive dyspnea, fever; may be insidious (weeks)
  • Hypoxemia disproportionate to clinical appearance; exertional desaturation classic
  • Risk: HIV with CD4 <200 (or <14%), solid organ/stem cell transplant, hematologic malignancy, corticosteroids ≥20mg/day × ≥1 month, biologic therapy
  • CXR: bilateral interstitial infiltrates (ground-glass); up to 25% normal early — get CT
  • LDH elevated (non-specific but sensitive — very high LDH = poor prognosis)

Workup

  • CT chest: Ground-glass opacities, bilateral symmetric
  • BAL: Gold standard — GMS or DFA stain; PCP PCR (very sensitive but may detect colonization)
  • Induced sputum: 50–80% sensitivity (lower than BAL); appropriate first step
  • β-D-glucan: Elevated (>80 pg/mL) — high sensitivity (~94%); low specificity; negative β-DG makes PCP unlikely
  • ABG: PaO2 and A-a gradient critical for treatment decisions (steroids indicated if PaO2 <70 or A-a >35)
  • CD4 count, HIV viral load (if HIV)

Empiric Therapy

RegimenDoseDuration
TMP-SMX IV (first-line)15–20 mg/kg/day (TMP component) IV divided q6–8h21 days
TMP-SMX PO (mild disease, tolerating PO)TMP-SMX DS 2 tabs PO TID21 days
Adjunctive prednisone (PaO2 <70 or A-a >35)40mg PO BID × 5d → 40mg daily × 5d → 20mg daily × 11d21 days total (start within 72h of antibiotics)
TMP-SMX intolerant (sulfa allergy)Primaquine 30mg PO daily + Clindamycin 900mg IV TID (or 450mg PO QID)21 days
Atovaquone (mild only)750mg PO BID with fatty meal21 days
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not responding at 5–7 days on TMP-SMX → BAL if not done (confirm diagnosis); consider resistance (rare); consider second organism (CMV co-infection, bacterial superinfection)
  • G6PD deficiency before primaquine — check G6PD; hemolytic anemia risk
  • Worsening after steroids → IRIS? new infection? inadequate steroid taper?
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Respiratory failure requiring supplemental O2 >4L or intubation → ICU; HFNC trial before intubation
  • Bilateral infiltrates with rapid deterioration → ICU, bronchoscopy for BAL if not already done
  • Pneumothorax (spontaneous) — known complication of PCP; chest tube if tension
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Clinical improvement typically 4–8 days; do not stop early
  • Transition to TMP-SMX PO DS 1 tab TID when tolerating PO and O2 stable
  • After acute treatment: TMP-SMX DS 1 tab daily for secondary prophylaxis until CD4 >200 × 3 months on ART

Opportunistic Infections in Immunocompromised Hosts

HIV/AIDS, transplant recipients, and immunosuppressed patients. CD4 count guides prophylaxis and differential. Cross-reference: 📚 IMCRG: HIV/AIDS

Recognition

  • Immunocompetent: CMV mononucleosis (fever, malaise, atypical lymphocytosis, mild LFT elevation); heterophile-negative
  • Transplant/immunocompromised: CMV disease by organ — colitis (diarrhea, bleeding), pneumonitis (dyspnea, hypoxia), hepatitis, retinitis, esophagitis, encephalitis
  • CMV retinitis: visual changes (floaters, scotomata, decreased acuity) — HIV CD4 <50
  • Risk: D+/R- serostatus highest risk in transplant; monitor viral load weekly post-transplant

Workup

  • CMV PCR (quantitative): Blood — guide to preemptive treatment and response; most useful in transplant
  • Tissue biopsy (gold standard for end-organ disease): Owl-eye inclusion bodies on histology; CMV IHC
  • Ophthalmology: Dilated fundus exam for retinitis — hemorrhagic "pizza pie" retinopathy
  • BAL: CMV culture/PCR for pneumonitis (but CMV in BAL may be co-pathogen, not cause — clinical correlation)
  • CMV IgG/IgM serology: useful for pretransplant risk stratification, not acute diagnosis in immunocompromised

Empiric Therapy

IndicationRegimenDuration
CMV disease (non-retinitis)Ganciclovir 5mg/kg IV q12h (induction)2–3 weeks induction → maintenance
CMV retinitis (HIV)Ganciclovir 5mg/kg IV q12h OR intravitreal ganciclovir implant + oral valganciclovirInduction 14–21d → lifelong suppression until CD4 >100 × 6 months
Maintenance / secondary prophylaxisValganciclovir 900mg PO dailyOngoing per transplant protocol or until CD4 recovery
Ganciclovir-resistant CMVFoscarnet 60mg/kg IV q8h OR 90mg/kg IV q12h3–4 weeks; ID consultation
Preemptive (transplant — rising viral load)Valganciclovir 900mg PO BID (induction) → 900mg daily (maintenance)Until PCR undetectable × 2
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • CMV viral load rising on valganciclovir → check UL97 resistance (ganciclovir resistance); switch to foscarnet if confirmed
  • GI symptoms not improving → EGD/colonoscopy with biopsy — mucosal CMV disease requires tissue diagnosis
  • CMV in BAL alone without clinical pneumonitis → may not require treatment; clinical correlation essential
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Pneumonitis with respiratory failure → ICU; consider IVIG adjunct; ganciclovir IV
  • Retinitis with macular or optic disc involvement → immediate ophthalmology; vision-threatening emergency
  • CMV encephalitis → IV ganciclovir + foscarnet combination; neurology
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • PCR undetectable on 2 consecutive measurements → transition to maintenance dose (valganciclovir 900mg daily)
  • Transplant protocol: continue prophylaxis for 3–6 months post-transplant per institution protocol
  • HIV/CMV retinitis: maintain suppression until CD4 >100 × 6 months on ART; fundus re-exam before stopping

Recognition

  • Disseminated MAC (HIV): CD4 <50; fever, night sweats, weight loss, diarrhea; hepatosplenomegaly, lymphadenopathy; markedly elevated ALP
  • Pulmonary MAC (non-HIV): Elderly women, nodular-bronchiectatic form (Lady Windermere); or fibrocavitary form (COPD, structural lung disease)
  • MAC lymphadenitis: Children, immunocompetent
  • Diagnosis requires >1 positive sputum culture OR 1 positive BAL/bronchoscopy OR positive blood culture (disseminated)

Workup

  • Blood cultures (mycobacterial): Positive in >90% of disseminated HIV-associated MAC (BACTEC or lysis centrifugation)
  • Bone marrow biopsy: High yield when blood cultures pending; AFB on smear/culture
  • CT chest: Pulmonary MAC pattern (bronchiectasis, nodules, tree-in-bud; cavities in fibrocavitary)
  • Sputum AFB × 3: Pulmonary MAC diagnosis
  • Susceptibility testing: macrolide (azithromycin/clarithromycin) susceptibility critical

Empiric Therapy

IndicationRegimenDuration
Disseminated MAC (HIV)Azithromycin 500mg PO daily + Ethambutol 15mg/kg PO daily ± Rifabutin 300mg daily (severe)≥12 months; until CD4 >100 × 6 months on ART
Pulmonary MAC (nodular-bronchiectatic)Azithromycin 500mg 3×/week + Rifampin 600mg 3×/week + Ethambutol 25mg/kg 3×/week (intermittent)Minimum 12 months after sputum culture conversion
Pulmonary MAC (fibrocavitary)Azithromycin 250mg daily + Rifampin 600mg daily + Ethambutol 15mg/kg daily12 months after culture conversion
Prophylaxis (HIV, CD4 <50 — now less common with early ART)Azithromycin 1200mg PO weeklyUntil CD4 >100 × 3 months
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Macrolide resistance → treatment failure; if prior macrolide exposure, always test susceptibility; use amikacin-containing regimen
  • Not responding clinically despite susceptible organism → drug levels adequate? adherence? immune reconstitution inflammatory syndrome (IRIS)?
  • IRIS after ART initiation + MAC → inflammatory lymphadenopathy, fever — continue ART + MAC treatment; consider steroids if severe
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Pulmonary MAC with massive hemoptysis → bronchial artery embolization; thoracic surgery for localized disease
  • IRIS with respiratory compromise → systemic corticosteroids + continue treatment
  • Disseminated MAC with septic shock (rare) → ICU; IV amikacin + oral macrolide + ethambutol
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Pulmonary MAC: culture conversion at 6 months is key milestone — if not converting, reassess regimen
  • Disseminated MAC (HIV): ART initiation timing — ideally 2 weeks after MAC treatment started (reduces IRIS)
  • Primary prophylaxis can be discontinued when CD4 >100 × 3 months on ART — no longer universally recommended with early ART

Recognition

  • Subacute onset (days–weeks): headache, fever, nausea; meningismus often absent or mild; altered mental status
  • Elevated ICP: Headache + visual changes + papilledema — most life-threatening complication; kills more than the fungus
  • HIV (CD4 <100), transplant, hematologic malignancy, high-dose corticosteroids
  • Pulmonary cryptococcosis (non-CNS): may be asymptomatic or cough/dyspnea — lower risk for dissemination in immunocompetent

Workup

  • LP (mandatory): Opening pressure (often >250 mmH2O); CSF CrAg (highly sensitive/specific); India ink (60–80% sensitivity); CSF culture; cell count (usually lymphocytic, modest elevation)
  • Serum CrAg: >95% sensitivity for cryptococcal meningitis in HIV; also useful for monitoring
  • CD4 count, HIV viral load
  • CT head before LP (rule out mass lesion, but do not delay LP if no focal findings or papilledema)
  • Fungal blood cultures (positive in ~70%)

Empiric Therapy

PhaseRegimenDuration
InductionLiposomal AmB 3–4 mg/kg IV q24h + Flucytosine 25mg/kg PO QID2 weeks
ConsolidationFluconazole 400mg PO daily8 weeks
Maintenance (HIV)Fluconazole 200mg PO dailyUntil CD4 >100 × 6 months on ART
Non-HIV / transplant inductionSame; consolidation/maintenance per transplant guidelines × 12 monthsLonger if immunocompromised

Source Control

  • ICP management is critical: Repeat LP daily (target OP <200 mmH2O) until ICP controlled; remove 20–30 mL CSF per LP
  • Lumbar drain or VP shunt if LP cannot control ICP
  • Acetazolamide and corticosteroids NOT recommended for elevated ICP in Cryptococcus
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • Not clinically improving at 2 weeks → repeat LP (ongoing elevated ICP?); CSF culture still positive → consider AmB + 5-FC combination extension; check flucytosine levels (target 40–60 mcg/mL)
  • Fluconazole-resistant Cryptococcus → MIC testing; voriconazole or posaconazole salvage with ID consultation
  • IRIS post-ART initiation with cryptococcal meningitis → hold or defer ART; corticosteroids for severe IRIS (controversial)
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • ICP >350 mmH2O with herniation signs → emergent large volume LP; neurosurgery for lumbar drain or shunt
  • Coma or altered consciousness → ICU; aggressive ICP management; consider empiric treatment of elevated ICP
  • Respiratory failure (disseminated) → ICU; ARDS management
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Induction complete, clinically improved, CSF culture negative at 2 weeks → transition to fluconazole consolidation
  • ART initiation in HIV: defer 5 weeks after starting antifungal treatment (reduces IRIS mortality — COAT trial)
  • Maintenance fluconazole can be stopped when CD4 >100 × 6 months on ART; serum CrAg negative

Recognition

  • HIV with CD4 <100 + positive Toxoplasma IgG → ring-enhancing lesions on MRI = Toxoplasma until proven otherwise
  • Headache, fever, focal neurologic deficits (hemiparesis, aphasia, visual field defect), altered mental status, seizures
  • Multiple bilateral lesions typical (basal ganglia, corticomedullary junction)
  • Response to empiric treatment confirms diagnosis (10–14 day clinical/radiographic response)

Workup

  • MRI brain with gadolinium: Ring-enhancing lesions with surrounding edema; may have "eccentric target sign" (pathognomonic)
  • Toxoplasma IgG (serum): Positive in ~95% of active CNS toxoplasmosis — negative serology argues strongly against (consider other diagnosis)
  • LP (if safe): CSF PCR for Toxoplasma; often sent with CMV, EBV, HSV, Cryptococcus CrAg
  • CD4 count; HIV viral load
  • Response to empiric treatment at 10–14 days confirms diagnosis — brain biopsy if no response

Empiric Therapy

RegimenDoseDuration
Pyrimethamine (induction)200mg PO × 1 load → 50–75mg PO daily6 weeks induction
+ Sulfadiazine1,000–1,500mg PO QID6 weeks
+ Leucovorin (prevents bone marrow toxicity)10–25mg PO daily (increase to 50mg if cytopenias)Throughout and 1 week after pyrimethamine
Sulfa allergy alternativePyrimethamine + Clindamycin 600mg IV/PO TID6 weeks
Secondary prophylaxisPyrimethamine 25mg + Sulfadiazine 500mg QID + Leucovorin 25mg — all dailyUntil CD4 >200 × 6 months on ART
🔄 Reconsider the diagnosis or antibiotic if…
  • No clinical improvement at 10–14 days → brain biopsy to rule out CNS lymphoma (PCNSL), fungal, TB, other
  • IgG negative + ring-enhancing lesion → strongly consider CNS lymphoma (PCNSL); send SPECT or PET, CSF for EBV PCR (PCNSL)
  • Worsening on therapy → IRIS? steroid-responsive PCNSL? new OI?
🚨 Escalate / surgical consultation if…
  • Herniation risk (large lesions, mass effect) → corticosteroids (dexamethasone); neurosurgery for decompression if needed
  • Seizures → anticonvulsants; Toxoplasma causes cortical irritation; maintain anticonvulsants through treatment
  • Septic shock (rare in CNS toxo alone) → assess for other concurrent OI
✓ Step down / de-escalate when…
  • Clinical improvement by day 10–14 + radiographic improvement at 6 weeks → transition to secondary prophylaxis
  • ART initiation: initiate within 2–3 weeks of OI treatment if CD4 very low (optimize adherence first)
  • Secondary prophylaxis discontinued when CD4 >200 × 6 months and clinically stable

Risk Groups

  • HIV: CD4 <100 cells/μL with positive Toxoplasma IgG (reactivation)
  • Transplant recipients: Especially heart transplant (D+/R-)

Clinical Features

  • Headache, fever, altered mental status
  • Focal neurologic deficits (hemiparesis, ataxia, cranial nerve palsies)
  • Seizures

Diagnosis

  • MRI Brain: Multiple ring-enhancing lesions, typically in basal ganglia. Surrounding edema.
  • Toxoplasma IgG: Usually positive (reactivation). Negative IgG makes diagnosis unlikely.
  • Response to empiric therapy: Clinical and radiographic improvement within 2 weeks supports diagnosis
  • Brain biopsy: If no response to treatment or atypical features

Differential: Toxoplasma vs. CNS Lymphoma

FeatureToxoplasmosisPrimary CNS Lymphoma
LesionsMultiple, basal gangliaOften single, periventricular
Toxo IgGUsually positiveMay be negative
CD4 countUsually <100Usually <50
CSF EBV PCRNegativeOften positive
Response to empiric TxYes (2 weeks)No

Treatment

RegimenDoseDuration
Pyrimethamine200mg PO × 1 load, then 50-75mg PO daily≥6 weeks
Sulfadiazine1000-1500mg PO q6h≥6 weeks
Leucovorin10-25mg PO dailyContinue with pyrimethamine (prevents bone marrow suppression)

Alternative (Sulfa Allergy)

  • Pyrimethamine + Clindamycin: Clindamycin 600mg IV/PO q6h + Pyrimethamine + Leucovorin
  • TMP-SMX: 5 mg/kg (TMP) IV/PO BID (alternative if available)

Maintenance Therapy

  • After ≥6 weeks acute therapy, continue at half doses as maintenance
  • Discontinue when CD4 >200 for ≥6 months on ART

Prophylaxis

  • Indication: CD4 <100 AND positive Toxoplasma IgG
  • Regimen: TMP-SMX 1 DS daily (also covers PCP)

Histoplasmosis (Disseminated)

  • Risk: CD4 <150; endemic areas (Ohio/Mississippi River valleys)
  • Features: Fever, weight loss, hepatosplenomegaly, pancytopenia, skin lesions
  • Diagnosis: Urine/serum Histoplasma antigen (very sensitive), blood culture (fungal isolator)
  • Treatment: Liposomal AmB 3 mg/kg/day IV × 1-2 weeks (induction), then itraconazole 200mg PO TID × 3 days, then BID × ≥12 months

Coccidioidomycosis (Disseminated)

  • Risk: CD4 <250; endemic areas (Southwest US, Central America)
  • Features: Pneumonia, meningitis, skin lesions, bone involvement
  • Diagnosis: Coccidioides IgM/IgG, urine/serum antigen, culture
  • Treatment: Fluconazole 400-800mg daily (non-meningeal) or AmB (severe). Meningitis: Fluconazole 800-1200mg daily lifelong.

Progressive Multifocal Leukoencephalopathy (PML)

  • Cause: JC virus reactivation
  • Risk: CD4 <100; also natalizumab, rituximab
  • Features: Focal neurologic deficits, cognitive decline, no fever/headache
  • Diagnosis: MRI (white matter lesions, no enhancement, no mass effect), CSF JC virus PCR
  • Treatment: No specific antiviral. Immune reconstitution (ART) is only treatment. May develop IRIS.

Microsporidiosis

  • Risk: CD4 <100
  • Features: Chronic watery diarrhea, wasting
  • Diagnosis: Modified trichrome stain of stool, PCR
  • Treatment: Albendazole 400mg PO BID + ART

🍄 Fungal Infections

Candidiasis

HIGH YIELD
Species & Resistance Patterns
SpeciesFrequencyFluconazoleEchinocandinNotes
C. albicansMost commonUsually susceptibleSusceptibleStandard first-line options apply
C. glabrata2nd most commonOften resistant (dose-dependent)SusceptibleEchinocandin preferred; check susceptibility
C. parapsilosisCommon (NICU, lines)SusceptibleIntrinsically less susceptibleFluconazole acceptable; biofilm on devices
C. tropicalisHematologic malignancyMay be resistantSusceptibleEchinocandin preferred in high-risk
C. kruseiRareIntrinsically resistantSusceptibleNever use fluconazole
C. aurisEmerging MDRUsually resistantUsually susceptible (check)Contact precautions; notify infection control
Candidemia Management
  • Always clinically significant — never a contaminant, even single bottle
  • First-line: Echinocandin (caspofungin 70 mg IV load → 50 mg/day; micafungin 100 mg/day; anidulafungin 200 mg load → 100 mg/day)
  • Step-down to fluconazole: Acceptable after clinical improvement, known susceptible species, negative repeat cultures — fluconazole 800 mg load → 400 mg/day
  • Remove central line when feasible — significant reduction in mortality
  • Duration: 14 days from first negative blood culture (uncomplicated)
📘 IDSA 2016 Candidemia Workup (All Patients)
  • Dilated fundoscopic exam (endophthalmitis — present in 5-10%)
  • Repeat blood cultures q48h until negative (document clearance)
  • Echocardiogram if prolonged candidemia (>5 days) or prosthetic valve
  • Remove central lines when feasible
  • ID consultation for complex cases
⚡ BOARD PEARLS: Candida
  • Candida peritonitis (post-op, perforated viscus): fluconazole or echinocandin × 4 weeks
  • Esophageal candidiasis: Fluconazole 200 mg PO × 14–21 days (no need for IV)
  • Oropharyngeal (thrush): Clotrimazole troches or nystatin swish × 7–14 days; fluconazole if severe
  • Vulvovaginal: Fluconazole 150 mg PO × 1 dose; topical azole alternatives
  • Prophylaxis in ICU: Controversial; consider fluconazole if high risk (abdominal surgery, TPN, prolonged broad-spectrum abx)

Invasive Aspergillosis

HIGH YIELD
Risk Groups (Who Gets Invasive Aspergillosis)
  • Prolonged neutropenia (ANC <100 for >10 days) — hematologic malignancy, HSCT
  • Allogeneic HSCT — especially with GVHD and steroid therapy
  • Solid organ transplant — lung transplant highest risk; liver/kidney lower risk
  • High-dose corticosteroids (≥20 mg prednisone × >3 weeks)
  • Inherited immunodeficiency — chronic granulomatous disease (CGD), STAT3 defects
  • COVID-19-associated pulmonary aspergillosis (CAPA) — ICU patients on steroids
  • Influenza-associated aspergillosis (IAPA) — mechanically ventilated
Clinical Syndromes
  • Invasive pulmonary aspergillosis (IPA): Most common — fever, cough, pleuritic chest pain, hemoptysis; may be insidious in profound neutropenia
  • Tracheobronchitis: In lung transplant recipients — ulcerative/pseudomembranous; diagnosis by bronchoscopy
  • Sinusitis (invasive): Diabetics, neutropenic; extend to orbit/CNS; aggressive surgical debridement required
  • CNS aspergillosis: Hematogenous dissemination — ring-enhancing lesions, high mortality
  • Aspergilloma (chronic): Pre-existing lung cavities (TB, sarcoid); hemoptysis; local treatment only unless invasive
  • Allergic bronchopulmonary aspergillosis (ABPA): Asthma/CF patients; IgE elevation, central bronchiectasis; NOT invasive — treat with steroids ± itraconazole
Diagnosis
TestSensitivitySpecificityNotes
CT Chest — Halo Sign~80% (early)ModerateNodule surrounded by ground-glass halo = angioinvasion. Early finding — disappears in days. Air-crescent sign = late (recovery).
Serum Galactomannan (GM)~70-80% (heme malignancy)~85%Sensitivity lower in solid organ transplant and non-neutropenic hosts. False positives: pip-tazo (less now), mold colonization, certain foods.
BAL Galactomannan~85%~90%Higher sensitivity than serum for IPA; preferred when available
Beta-D-Glucan (BDG)~75%~80%Non-specific (positive in PJP, Candida, Fusarium too); useful for ruling out fungal infection when negative
PCR (Aspergillus)~85%~75%Increasingly available; 2 positive results improve specificity
Culture (BAL/tissue)~30-50%~99%Low sensitivity but highly specific; required for species identification and susceptibility testing
Tissue biopsy~50%~99%Gold standard for proven IPA; septate branching hyphae (45° angle) on histopathology

Key histopathology: Septate hyphae with 45° acute angle branching — distinguishes from Mucor (non-septate, 90° angle). Culture required for species identification (A. fumigatus most common, A. terreus — amphotericin resistant).

Treatment
ScenarioFirst-LineDoseDuration
Invasive Pulmonary (IPA)Voriconazole6 mg/kg IV q12h × 2 doses (load), then 4 mg/kg IV q12h; step to PO 200–300 mg BIDMinimum 6–12 weeks; continue until immunosuppression resolved and imaging improved
Isavuconazole (alternative)Isavuconazonium372 mg IV/PO q8h × 6 doses (load), then 372 mg q24hSame as above; fewer drug interactions, better tolerated than voriconazole
Voriconazole intolerant or resistantLiposomal AmB3–5 mg/kg IV dailyUntil clinical response, then step down to azole
Salvage (refractory)Micafungin or CaspofunginStandard dosingAdd-on to azole or monotherapy; ID/heme consult
📘 Voriconazole TDM (Therapeutic Drug Monitoring)
  • Target trough: 1–5.5 mcg/mL (efficacy <1; neurotoxicity >5.5)
  • Check level: After 5 days (steady state); adjust dose and recheck
  • Highly variable PK: CYP2C19 polymorphisms — poor metabolizers (Asian populations) may accumulate; ultra-rapid metabolizers may sub-therapeutic
  • Major drug interactions: Rifampin (contraindicated — reduces voriconazole levels 93%), rifabutin, phenytoin, carbamazepine
  • Side effects: Visual disturbances (most common — hallucinations, photopsia), hepatotoxicity, photosensitivity, periostitis with prolonged use, QTc prolongation
Prophylaxis
  • HSCT (allogeneic, engraftment/GVHD): Posaconazole 300 mg PO daily OR voriconazole — during neutropenia and GVHD treatment
  • Hematologic malignancy (induction chemo): Posaconazole 200 mg PO TID (with food) reduces invasive fungal infection mortality vs. fluconazole (NEJM 2007)
  • Lung transplant: Voriconazole or inhaled amphotericin B; protocol varies by center

Mucormycosis (Mucorales)

HIGH YIELD
Classic Risk Factor Triad
  • Diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA) — most common; low pH and high glucose favor Mucor growth. Rhinocerebral pattern predominates.
  • Iron overload / deferoxamine therapy — deferoxamine acts as siderophore, providing iron to Mucor; classic board association
  • Hematologic malignancy / prolonged neutropenia — pulmonary pattern predominates; overlaps with Aspergillus in this population
  • Other: Solid organ transplant, high-dose steroids, trauma/burns (cutaneous), injection drug use, COVID-19 (especially with steroid use)
Clinical Syndromes
  • Rhinoorbital-cerebral (ROCM): Most common — starts with sinusitis, spreads to orbit (proptosis, ophthalmoplegia), then intracranially. Black eschar on palate/nasal mucosa = pathognomonic. Rapid and fatal without treatment.
  • Pulmonary: Hematologic malignancy/HSCT — fever, hemoptysis, cavitation. "Reverse halo sign" on CT (ground glass surrounded by consolidation) — relatively specific.
  • Cutaneous: Post-trauma, burns, injection sites. Local necrosis with black discoloration.
  • Disseminated: Multiple organ involvement; extremely high mortality (>80%).
  • GI: Rare; neonates, malnourished patients.
Diagnosis
  • Histopathology: Broad, non-septate (pauci-septate) hyphae with 90° right-angle branching — contrasts with septate Aspergillus at 45°. Ribbon-like and irregular morphology.
  • Culture: Required for genus/species ID; grows on standard media as cottony colonies. Notify lab to hold for mold growth.
  • Galactomannan / Beta-D-glucan: Typically NEGATIVE — distinguishes from Aspergillus. GM-negative invasive mold = think Mucor.
  • CT (sinus/chest): Multi-sinus involvement, bony erosion (ROCM); reverse halo sign (pulmonary).
Treatment — Requires Triple Approach
  1. Antifungal: Liposomal amphotericin B 5–10 mg/kg IV daily (higher doses than standard candidiasis/aspergillosis). Continue until clinical improvement, then step down to posaconazole 300 mg PO BID for consolidation/maintenance.
  2. Surgical debridement: Urgent and aggressive — remove all necrotic tissue. Serial debridements often required. May require orbital exenteration for ROCM with orbital involvement.
  3. Reverse underlying risk factors: Correct DKA aggressively (pH normalization). Stop/reduce steroids if possible. Control hyperglycemia. Discontinue deferoxamine.

Voriconazole and fluconazole are NOT active against Mucorales — critical distinction from Aspergillus. Posaconazole and isavuconazole have activity (salvage/step-down).

⚡ CRITICAL BOARD PEARL

Galactomannan NEGATIVE + non-septate 90° branching hyphae + DKA or iron overload = Mucormycosis, NOT Aspergillus. Starting voriconazole (active against Aspergillus) in this scenario is a classic clinical error — it has no activity against Mucor.

Histoplasmosis

Epidemiology & Exposure
  • Endemic: Ohio and Mississippi River valleys; Central America, parts of South America
  • Exposure: Bird/bat droppings, soil disturbance (construction, spelunking, chicken coops, demolition)
  • Dimorphic fungus: Mold in environment → yeast at 37°C (body temperature)
Clinical Syndromes
SyndromePopulationFeatures
Acute pulmonaryImmunocompetent, large exposureSelf-limited flu-like illness; hilar lymphadenopathy on CXR; resolves spontaneously in most
Subacute pulmonaryMild immunosuppressionProlonged weeks of fever, fatigue, cough; mediastinal lymphadenopathy
Disseminated (PDH)HIV (CD4 <150), transplant, TNF inhibitorsFever, weight loss, hepatosplenomegaly, pancytopenia, elevated LDH and alkaline phosphatase; may mimic sepsis
CNS histoplasmosisSeverely immunocompromisedChronic meningitis, focal CNS lesions; high mortality
Mediastinal fibrosisRare complication (non-infectious)Fibrous tissue occludes mediastinal structures; SVC syndrome, pulmonary hypertension
Diagnosis
  • Urine Histoplasma antigen: Most sensitive (90%+) for disseminated/severe disease; also positive in serum. Less sensitive for mild pulmonary.
  • Serology (complement fixation, immunodiffusion): H and M bands on immunodiffusion diagnostic; CF ≥1:32 significant. Takes weeks to develop — not useful acutely.
  • Culture (blood, BAL, bone marrow): Gold standard; slow growth (2–6 weeks) on fungal media
  • Histopathology: Small oval intracellular yeast within macrophages — GMS or PAS stain. "Owl's eye" appearance.
  • BAL: For pulmonary disease — culture + antigen + stain
Treatment
SyndromeTreatmentDuration
Mild acute pulmonaryNo treatment (self-limited) OR itraconazole 200 mg TID × 3 days → BID if symptoms >4 weeks3 months if treating
Moderate-severe pulmonaryLiposomal AmB 3 mg/kg/day × 1–2 weeks → itraconazole 200 mg TID × 3 days → BID12 weeks total
Disseminated (PDH) — mild/moderateItraconazole 200 mg TID × 3 days → BID12 months
Disseminated (PDH) — severe/HIVLiposomal AmB 3 mg/kg/day IV × 1–2 weeks → itraconazole 200 mg TID × 3 days → BID12 months minimum; lifelong if CD4 persistently <150
CNS histoplasmosisLiposomal AmB 5 mg/kg/day × 4–6 weeks → itraconazole12+ months; indefinite if immunosuppressed

Itraconazole TDM: Check serum level after 2 weeks — target trough ≥1 mcg/mL (random level ≥1 mcg/mL). Poor oral absorption if gastric pH elevated — avoid PPIs; take with acidic beverage if needed.

Coccidioidomycosis

Epidemiology
  • Endemic: Sonoran Desert — Arizona, California (Central Valley), New Mexico, West Texas; expanding northward with climate change
  • Exposure: Soil disturbance, construction, archaeology, military training in Southwest; risk after dust storms
  • High-risk populations: African American and Filipino descent (dissemination risk ×50× higher), HIV/AIDS (CD4 <250), pregnancy (3rd trimester), transplant, TNF inhibitors, diabetes
Clinical Syndromes
  • Primary pulmonary (60% asymptomatic): "Valley Fever" — fever, cough, pleuritic chest pain, arthralgias ("desert rheumatism"), erythema nodosum/multiforme. Most resolve spontaneously.
  • Chronic pulmonary: Cavities, nodules, fibrosis — may persist for years
  • Disseminated: Skin (verrucous lesions, subcutaneous abscesses), bone/joints (spine, knees), meningitis. Meningitis = most feared complication — high mortality without lifelong therapy.
Diagnosis
  • Serology (EIA, complement fixation): IgM early (first 3 weeks); IgG later (complement fixation CF titer ≥1:16 suggests dissemination or severe disease). CF titer tracks response to therapy.
  • Urine/serum antigen (MiraVista): Useful in HIV/immunocompromised — may be seronegative
  • Culture: Highly infectious in lab — notify lab. Spherules in tissue (distinct from hyphae in environment).
  • Histopathology: Spherules filled with endospores — pathognomonic
  • CSF: Lymphocytic pleocytosis, low glucose; CF titer often positive. LP essential if CNS suspected.
Treatment
SyndromeTreatmentDuration
Mild self-limited pulmonary (immunocompetent)Observation; treat if symptoms >6 weeks or risk factors for disseminationN/A (watchful waiting)
Non-meningeal, non-severeFluconazole 400–800 mg PO daily OR itraconazole 200 mg PO BID3–6+ months; until resolution
Severe/disseminated (non-meningeal)Amphotericin B (lipid formulation) 3–5 mg/kg/day → step down to azoleUntil improved, then oral azole × years
MeningitisFluconazole 800–1200 mg PO/IV daily (AmB intrathecal rarely used now)LIFELONG — stopping therapy leads to relapse; indefinite fluconazole required

Blastomycosis

Key Features
  • Endemic: Overlaps with Histoplasma — Ohio/Mississippi valleys, Great Lakes region, southeastern US; also parts of Canada
  • Exposure: Moist soil, decomposing wood, near waterways; outdoor workers, hunters
  • Dimorphic: Mold in environment, broad-based budding yeast at body temperature (distinguishing feature)
Clinical Presentation
  • Pulmonary: Can mimic bacterial pneumonia, lung cancer, or TB — lobar consolidation, mass lesions, cavitation. Often misdiagnosed initially.
  • Skin: Most common extrapulmonary site — verrucous (warty) or ulcerative skin lesions; violaceous, crusted borders
  • Bone: Osteolytic lesions; spine, ribs, long bones
  • GU: Prostatitis, epididymo-orchitis — uncommon but classic board finding
  • CNS: Rare; meningitis or abscess in severely immunocompromised
Diagnosis & Treatment
  • Histopathology/KOH: Broad-based budding yeast (8–15 µm) with thick refractile cell wall — highly specific
  • Culture: Definitive; dimorphic growth pattern
  • Antigen (urine/serum): MiraVista Blastomyces antigen — cross-reacts with Histoplasma
  • Mild-moderate: Itraconazole 200 mg TID × 3 days → BID × 6–12 months
  • Severe/CNS: Liposomal AmB → itraconazole; CNS requires 12+ months

🦠 Viral Diseases

Herpesviruses — Overview

HIGH YIELD
HSV-1 and HSV-2
SyndromeFirst-Line TreatmentDose & Duration
Orolabial herpes (primary)Valacyclovir1 g PO BID × 7–10 days
Orolabial herpes (recurrent)Valacyclovir2 g PO BID × 1 day (episodic) OR 500 mg daily (suppression)
Genital herpes (primary)Acyclovir or ValacyclovirAcyclovir 400 mg PO TID × 7–10 days; Valacyclovir 1 g BID × 7–10 days
Genital herpes (recurrent)Valacyclovir500 mg BID × 3 days (episodic) OR 500 mg daily (suppression)
Suppressive therapy (≥6 recurrences/year)Valacyclovir500–1000 mg daily; reassess annually
HSV encephalitisAcyclovir IV10 mg/kg IV q8h × 14–21 days; adjust for renal function
Neonatal HSVAcyclovir IV20 mg/kg IV q8h × 14 days (SEM) or 21 days (CNS/disseminated)
Severe/disseminated (immunocompromised)Acyclovir IV10 mg/kg IV q8h × 14–21 days → oral suppression
Acyclovir-resistant HSVFoscarnet40 mg/kg IV q8h × 21 days (renal dosing); monitor lytes

Acyclovir resistance: Thymidine kinase mutation — suspect if no response after 7–10 days IV acyclovir in immunocompromised. Foscarnet is treatment of choice. Cidofovir second-line.

Varicella-Zoster Virus (VZV)
SyndromeTreatmentDurationNotes
Primary varicella (chickenpox) — adultsAcyclovir 800 mg PO 5×/day OR Valacyclovir 1 g TID7 days; start within 72h of rash onsetHospitalize if pneumonitis, encephalitis, or immunocompromised
Herpes zoster (shingles) — immunocompetentValacyclovir 1 g PO TID7 days; start within 72hReduces severity and duration of PHN; famciclovir 500 mg TID × 7 days alternative
Herpes zoster — immunocompromisedAcyclovir 10 mg/kg IV q8hUntil no new lesions + 48h, then PO to complete 10–14 daysDissemination risk; IV mandatory until controlled
HZO (ophthalmic zoster)Valacyclovir 1 g TID7–10 daysUrgent ophthalmology; tip-of-nose sign (Hutchinson's) predicts ocular involvement
Ramsay Hunt syndromeValacyclovir + prednisone7 days eachFacial nerve palsy + auricular vesicles + hearing loss triad
VZV meningitis/encephalitisAcyclovir 10 mg/kg IV q8h14–21 daysCSF PCR diagnostic; also consider VZV vasculopathy (stroke syndrome)

Post-herpetic neuralgia (PHN) prevention: Shingrix (recombinant zoster vaccine) 2 doses IM × 2–6 months apart — age ≥50, immunocompromised ≥18 years (ask about vaccination status). 97% efficacy vs. zoster; markedly reduces PHN.

Post-exposure prophylaxis (PEP): VZIG (VariZIG) within 10 days for immunocompromised or pregnant non-immune patients exposed to varicella. Vaccine within 5 days for healthy susceptible adults.

Epstein-Barr Virus (EBV)
  • Infectious mononucleosis: Classic triad — fever, pharyngitis with exudate, lymphadenopathy. Splenomegaly in 50%. Monospot/EBV-VCA IgM. Supportive care — no specific antiviral. Avoid amoxicillin (maculopapular rash in ~80% with EBV pharyngitis).
  • Complications: Airway obstruction (steroids if severe), splenic rupture (avoid contact sports 3–4 weeks), hemophagocytic lymphohistiocytosis (HLH), hemolytic anemia, thrombocytopenia
  • EBV-associated malignancies: Burkitt lymphoma (African children), nasopharyngeal carcinoma, Hodgkin lymphoma, CNS lymphoma in HIV (EBV PCR in CSF), post-transplant lymphoproliferative disorder (PTLD)
  • PTLD: B-cell proliferation in transplant recipients — reduce immunosuppression first-line; rituximab for CD20+ PTLD; rare cases require chemotherapy
Human Herpesviruses 6, 7, 8 (HHV-6, 7, 8)
  • HHV-6: Roseola infantum (exanthem subitum) in young children — high fever then rash. In HSCT: encephalitis, marrow suppression — ganciclovir or foscarnet.
  • HHV-7: Second cause of roseola; rarely clinically significant in adults.
  • HHV-8 (KSHV): Kaposi sarcoma (HIV, immunocompromised), primary effusion lymphoma, Castleman disease. Treat underlying HIV/immunosuppression; chemotherapy for advanced KS.

Viral Hepatitis (HAV, HBV, HCV, HDV, HEV)

Full coverage in the GI & Hepatic section above — see Viral Hepatitis section for all five hepatitis viruses with treatment protocols.

HBV Reactivation with Immunosuppression — HIGH YIELD

A critical but often-missed clinical scenario:

  • Risk agents: Rituximab (highest risk — >20% reactivation if HBsAg+), anthracyclines, corticosteroids (>10 mg/day × >4 weeks), TNF inhibitors, JAK inhibitors, checkpoint inhibitors
  • Screening (BEFORE immunosuppression): Check HBsAg AND anti-HBc (core antibody). Anti-HBc+ alone (resolved infection) carries reactivation risk with rituximab and high-intensity chemotherapy.
  • Prophylaxis: HBsAg+ patients: Entecavir 0.5 mg/day OR tenofovir 300 mg/day starting 1 week before immunosuppression; continue 6–12 months after
  • Anti-HBc+ only (resolved): Prophylaxis with rituximab or high-risk chemo; monitoring (HBV DNA q1–3 months) with lower-risk regimens
  • Failure to screen = preventable fulminant hepatitis

Influenza

HIGH YIELD
Antivirals — Indications and Treatment
DrugClassDoseNotes
Oseltamivir (Tamiflu)Neuraminidase inhibitor75 mg PO BID × 5 days (treatment); 75 mg PO daily (prophylaxis)First-line; renal adjustment required (CrCl <30: 30 mg BID); most effective within 48h
Zanamivir (Relenza)Neuraminidase inhibitor (inhaled)10 mg inhaled BID × 5 daysAlternative if oseltamivir-resistant; contraindicated in asthma/COPD (bronchospasm)
Peramivir (Rapivab)Neuraminidase inhibitor (IV)600 mg IV × 1 dose (uncomplicated); daily for severeIV option for unable to take PO/inhaled; only FDA-approved IV influenza antiviral
Baloxavir (Xofluza)Endonuclease inhibitor (cap-dependent)40–80 mg PO × 1 dose (weight-based)Single-dose convenience; not for immunocompromised (resistance risk); not in pregnancy
Who Must Be Treated (Regardless of 48h Window)
  • Hospitalized patients with confirmed or suspected influenza
  • Severe/progressive illness (pneumonia, respiratory failure, sepsis)
  • High-risk outpatients: age ≥65, age <2, pregnancy, chronic cardiopulmonary disease, immunocompromised, BMI >40, nursing home residents, American Indian/Alaska Native
  • Consider for any symptomatic patient presenting within 48h (reduces duration even in healthy adults)

Treat even if rapid influenza test is negative — sensitivity only 50–70%; negative test does NOT rule out influenza.

Influenza Complications
  • Primary influenza pneumonia: Viral pneumonia — bilateral infiltrates, no bacteria on Gram stain; no antibiotic benefit
  • Secondary bacterial pneumonia: S. aureus (MSSA/MRSA), S. pneumoniae, H. influenzae — occurs days after initial improvement; add appropriate antibiotics
    • ARDS: H1N1 and avian influenza (H5N1, H7N9) — high ARDS risk; early prone positioning, lung-protective ventilation
  • Myocarditis/pericarditis: Rare but important; elevated troponin, ECG changes
  • Influenza-associated pulmonary aspergillosis (IAPA): ICU, steroids, mechanically ventilated — check galactomannan, BAL
  • Encephalopathy/encephalitis: Children more than adults; supportive care
  • Reye syndrome: Children, aspirin use — hepatic failure, encephalopathy; NEVER give aspirin to children with viral illness

Respiratory Viruses (RSV, Parainfluenza, Adenovirus, Rhinovirus)

RSV (Respiratory Syncytial Virus)
  • High-risk groups: Infants (<6 months), elderly (>65), heart/lung disease, immunocompromised (HSCT — high mortality)
  • Clinical: Bronchiolitis (infants); adults usually upper respiratory; HSCT — progression from upper to lower respiratory tract infection (LRI) is key concern
  • Diagnosis: Nasal swab PCR (preferred, high sensitivity); rapid antigen (lower sensitivity in adults)
  • Treatment:
    • Adults/general: Supportive care primarily
    • Nirsevimab (Beyfortus): Monoclonal antibody — single dose prophylaxis for all infants (<8 months) going into RSV season (2023 ACIP recommendation)
    • Palivizumab: Older monoclonal antibody — monthly injections; reserved for high-risk infants (CHD, prematurity, CLD)
    • RSV vaccines: Abrysvo (Pfizer), mResvia (Moderna) — adults ≥60; Abrysvo also approved for maternal immunization (pregnant 32–36 weeks)
    • HSCT/immunocompromised: Inhaled or IV ribavirin + IVIG or palivizumab (limited evidence; often used for documented RSV LRI)
Human Metapneumovirus (hMPV)
  • Similar to RSV — bronchiolitis in infants, pneumonia in elderly/immunocompromised
  • Diagnosed by PCR respiratory panel; no specific antiviral treatment
Adenovirus
  • Clinical: Pharyngitis/conjunctivitis (military barracks, daycares), pneumonia, GI infection, hemorrhagic cystitis (HSCT)
  • Immunocompromised: Disseminated adenovirus (HSCT) — high mortality; quantitative PCR for monitoring
  • Treatment: Cidofovir IV (with probenecid hydration) for serious adenovirus in immunocompromised; brincidofovir (oral prodrug) — FDA-approved for adenovirus in HSCT (2021)

Flaviviruses & Arboviruses (Dengue, Zika, West Nile, Chikungunya)

Dengue Fever
  • Vector: Aedes aegypti mosquito; tropical/subtropical regions worldwide; dengue 1–4 serotypes
  • Classic: "Breakbone fever" — high fever, severe myalgias/arthralgias, retro-orbital headache, maculopapular rash, leukopenia, thrombocytopenia
  • Severe dengue: Plasma leakage (ascites, pleural effusion), severe bleeding, organ impairment — occurs during defervescence (days 4–6). Warning signs: severe abdominal pain, persistent vomiting, rapid breathing, mucosal bleeding, lethargy, liver enlargement, increasing hematocrit.
  • Diagnosis: NS1 antigen (first 5 days), dengue PCR (first week), IgM/IgG serology (after day 5)
  • Treatment: Supportive — IV fluids for plasma leakage, avoid NSAIDs/aspirin (bleeding risk), acetaminophen for fever. No specific antiviral.
  • Pearl: Secondary dengue infection (different serotype) → antibody-dependent enhancement → higher risk of severe dengue
Zika Virus
  • Vector: Aedes mosquito; also sexual transmission (persists in semen weeks to months)
  • Clinical (adults): Usually mild — fever, rash, conjunctivitis, arthralgias; most asymptomatic
  • Complications: Guillain-Barré syndrome (rare); congenital Zika — microcephaly, brain malformations (critical in pregnancy)
  • Pregnancy: Screen all symptomatic pregnant women with travel exposure; consult maternal-fetal medicine; serial fetal ultrasounds
  • Diagnosis: Serum/urine Zika RT-PCR (first 1–2 weeks); IgM serology (cross-reacts with dengue/flaviviruses)
  • Treatment: Supportive only; no antiviral
Chikungunya
  • Vector: Aedes mosquito; endemic Africa, Asia, Americas; Caribbean outbreaks; local US transmission reported (Florida)
  • Clinical: Fever + severe symmetric polyarthralgia/arthritis (hands, wrists, ankles) — "that which bends up." Rash (maculopapular). Arthralgias can persist months to years (chronic chikungunya).
  • Diagnosis: PCR (acute), IgM/IgG serology
  • Treatment: NSAIDs/acetaminophen for joint pain; no specific antiviral. Chloroquine studied but not established.
West Nile Virus — see Viral Encephalopathies section

WNV, EEE, St. Louis encephalitis, Japanese encephalitis, rabies — covered in Viral Encephalopathies section above.

Human Papillomavirus (HPV)

Key Concepts
  • Low-risk types (6, 11): Condylomata acuminata (genital warts), recurrent respiratory papillomatosis
  • High-risk types (16, 18, 31, 33, 45): Cervical, oropharyngeal, anal, vulvar, vaginal, penile cancers. HPV 16/18 → 70% of cervical cancers.
  • Screening: Cervical Pap smear + HPV co-testing per ASCCP guidelines; anal Pap (men who have sex with men, HIV+)
Vaccination (Gardasil 9)
  • Routine: Ages 11–12 (2-dose series if starting <15 years; 3-dose if starting 15–26 years)
  • Catch-up: Ages 13–26 regardless of prior sexual activity
  • Shared decision: Ages 27–45 (less benefit — most already exposed; discuss with patient)
  • Immunocompromised: 3-dose series recommended regardless of age
  • HIV: Vaccinate up to age 26 routinely; consider up to 45
Treatment of HPV-Associated Conditions
  • Genital warts: Patient-applied: imiquimod 5% cream, podophyllotoxin 0.5% solution. Provider-applied: trichloroacetic acid (TCA), cryotherapy, surgical excision. No treatment superior for all cases.
  • Cervical dysplasia: Per ASCCP guidelines — colposcopy, LEEP, cryotherapy based on grade
  • Anal HSIL: High-resolution anoscopy + ablation in high-risk individuals (HIV, MSM)

Polyomaviruses (BK Virus, JC Virus)

BK Virus
  • BK nephropathy (BKVAN): Kidney transplant recipients — viral reactivation causes tubulointerstitial nephritis; rising creatinine, decoy cells on urine cytology
  • Hemorrhagic cystitis: HSCT recipients; gross hematuria
  • Diagnosis: Urine BK PCR (screening); plasma BK PCR (>10,000 copies/mL = significant viremia); kidney biopsy for definitive BKVAN diagnosis
  • Treatment: Reduce immunosuppression (primary intervention — no proven antiviral); cidofovir or leflunomide used in refractory cases (limited evidence)
JC Virus — Progressive Multifocal Leukoencephalopathy (PML)
  • Risk: HIV (CD4 <100), natalizumab (MS treatment — 1:100 risk with anti-JCV antibody+), rituximab, other biologics
  • Clinical: Subacute focal neurologic deficits, cognitive decline — NO fever, NO headache (distinguishes from bacterial/fungal causes)
  • Imaging: MRI — non-enhancing white matter lesions, no mass effect (contrast enhancement suggests IRIS)
  • Diagnosis: CSF JC virus PCR; brain biopsy if PCR negative with high suspicion
  • Treatment: No effective antiviral. Restore immune function: ART for HIV, stop natalizumab (plasmapheresis to remove drug), reduce immunosuppression. Immune reconstitution (IRIS-PML) can cause paradoxical worsening — steroids for severe IRIS.

Parvovirus B19

  • Erythema infectiosum (5th disease): Children — "slapped cheek" rash, lacy rash on trunk/extremities; self-limited
  • Aplastic crisis: Patients with underlying hemolysis (sickle cell, hereditary spherocytosis, thalassemia) — transient red cell aplasia, severe anemia, reticulocytopenia
  • Chronic pure red cell aplasia: Immunocompromised (HIV, HSCT) — persistent anemia, reticulocytopenia
  • Hydrops fetalis: Maternal infection during pregnancy → fetal anemia → heart failure → hydrops; intrauterine transfusion may be lifesaving
  • Arthritis: Adults (especially women) — symmetric small joint arthritis, may mimic rheumatoid arthritis
  • Diagnosis: Parvovirus IgM/IgG; PCR for immunocompromised (serology may be falsely negative)
  • Treatment: Supportive for most; IVIG for chronic pure red cell aplasia in immunocompromised (reduces viremia, allows erythropoiesis to resume)

Viral Hemorrhagic Fevers (VHF)

DiseaseVirus/FamilyVector/ExposureTreatmentIsolation
EbolaFilovirusContact with infected blood/body fluids; fruit bats (reservoir)Supportive; Atoltivimab/maftivimab/odesivimab (Inmazeb) — FDA approved for Ebola Zaire; Ansuvimab (Ebanga)Strict contact/droplet; PPE; CDC notification
MarburgFilovirusCave exposure (bats); contact transmissionSupportive; no approved specific treatmentStrict contact isolation; public health notification
LassaArenavirusWest Africa; rodent contact/excreta; person-to-person possibleRibavirin IV (early treatment improves survival)Standard + contact; airborne for aerosol-generating procedures
Hantavirus (HCPS)BunyavirusRodent droppings (US — deer mouse); not person-to-personSupportive; ICU + ECMO for cardiopulmonary shockStandard precautions; NO person-to-person
Yellow FeverFlavivirusAedes mosquito; Central Africa, S. AmericaSupportive onlyStandard; vector control

Approach to suspected VHF: Travel history to endemic region + hemorrhagic fever → immediately isolate, notify infection control and public health, defer invasive procedures. Do not send to general lab without prior notification.

6

Stewardship & disposition

Duration · IV→PO · Renal dosing · OPAT · Prophylaxis

Duration of Therapy Summary

Evidence-based duration recommendations. Shorter courses often equivalent; longer courses increase resistance risk.

InfectionDurationNotes
Community-Acquired Pneumonia5 days (minimum)Stop when afebrile ≥48h and clinically stable. Longer not better.
Hospital-Acquired / VAP7 daysPer 2016 IDSA guidelines. Longer for Pseudomonas or slow response.
Aspiration Pneumonia5-7 daysSame as CAP if no abscess/empyema.
Lung Abscess4-6 weeks (minimum)Until imaging shows resolution or significant improvement.
Empyema2-4 weeksWith drainage. Duration based on clinical response.
COPD Exacerbation (if antibiotics indicated)5 daysAzithromycin × 3d or doxycycline × 5d typically used.

Complicated UTI / Pyelonephritis7-14 daysDepends on clinical response, source control
Catheter-Associated UTI7 daysRemove/replace catheter. 5 days may be adequate for rapid response.
UTI in Men7-14 daysAll UTIs in men are considered complicated.
Prostatitis (acute bacterial)2-4 weeksFQ preferred for prostatic penetration.

IV to PO Conversion Guide

Transition to oral therapy when patient is hemodynamically stable, afebrile, tolerating PO, and GI tract functional.

DrugOral BioavailabilityIV Dose → PO DoseKey Notes
Fluoroquinolones~95-100%IV = PO (1:1)Avoid with divalent cations (Ca, Mg, Fe, antacids). Levofloxacin 750mg IV = 750mg PO
Linezolid~100%IV = PO (1:1)600mg IV = 600mg PO. Can switch immediately.
Metronidazole~100%IV = PO (1:1)500mg IV = 500mg PO. Excellent absorption.
TMP-SMX~95%IV = PO (1:1)DS (160/800mg) = standard oral dose
Fluconazole~90%IV = PO (1:1)Can switch day 1 if tolerating PO
Doxycycline~95%IV = PO (1:1)100mg IV = 100mg PO. Take with food to reduce GI upset.
Clindamycin~90%IV = PO (1:1)600mg IV q8h = 450mg PO q6h (adjust for frequency)
Voriconazole~96%IV = PO (1:1)Loading then maintenance. Take 1h before or 1h after meals.

Clinical Pearl: For drugs with excellent bioavailability, there is NO benefit to continuing IV when PO is tolerated. Switch early to reduce line days, cost, and infection risk.

DrugOral BioavailabilityConversionKey Notes
Amoxicillin-Clavulanate~75-90%Use PO for step-down875/125mg PO BID is standard
Cephalexin~90%Use for PO step-down from cefazolin500mg PO QID or 1g PO TID
Ciprofloxacin~70-80%400mg IV q12h = 500mg PO q12hAbsorption reduced by dairy, antacids
DrugOral BioavailabilityNotes
Vancomycin (IV)~0% systemicOral vancomycin is for C. diff only (stays in GI tract). No PO equivalent for systemic infections.
Aminoglycosides~0% systemicNo oral option. Must complete IV course.
Amphotericin B~0% systemicNo oral option for systemic infections.
Beta-lactams (most)Variable, often <50%Piperacillin-tazobactam, cefepime, meropenem have no PO equivalents. Step down to different oral agent.
Daptomycin~0%IV only. No oral option.

Step-Down Strategy for IV-Only Drugs: Transition to a different antibiotic class with PO option. Example: Pip-tazo → amoxicillin-clavulanate; Cefepime → levofloxacin; Vancomycin → linezolid (if appropriate).

📖 Drug Reference Guide: 💊 Vancomycin, 💊 Cefepime, 💊 Meropenem, 💊 Ciprofloxacin

Outpatient Parenteral Antimicrobial Therapy (OPAT)

Criteria for safe discharge on IV antibiotics, monitoring, and common regimens. Cross-reference: Duration of Therapy, Renal Dosing

Appropriate Candidates

  • Infection requiring IV antibiotics that cannot be treated with oral alternatives
  • Clinically stable (afebrile, hemodynamically stable, no acute decompensation)
  • Reliable IV access (PICC line, midline, or port)
  • Able to manage home infusion or has caregiver support
  • Access to OPAT services (infusion center, home health, or self-administration)
  • Reliable follow-up and monitoring capability

Relative Contraindications

  • Active IV drug use (high risk of line misuse) — consider supervised infusion or oral alternatives
  • Unstable housing or lack of refrigeration (for some drugs)
  • Cognitive impairment without reliable caregiver
  • History of severe allergic reaction or line complications
  • Ongoing diagnostic uncertainty

Before Discharge Checklist

  • □ Source control achieved (if applicable)
  • □ PICC/Midline placed and functioning
  • □ Culture data available and therapy appropriate
  • □ Duration of therapy defined
  • □ OPAT referral placed (infusion services, home health)
  • □ Labs scheduled (see monitoring below)
  • □ Follow-up appointments (ID clinic, PCP)
  • □ Patient/caregiver education completed
  • □ Drug supply secured (prior authorization if needed)

Once-Daily IV Antibiotics (Preferred for OPAT)

DrugTypical DoseCommon IndicationsKey Monitoring
Ceftriaxone1-2g IV q24hBone/joint, endocarditis (Strep), UTI, skinCBC, LFTs; well-tolerated
Ertapenem1g IV q24hIntra-abdominal, diabetic foot (polymicrobial), ESBLCBC; seizure risk (rare)
Daptomycin6-10 mg/kg IV q24hMRSA bacteremia, endocarditis, bone/jointCK weekly (myopathy), BMP
Dalbavancin1500mg IV × 1 or 1000mg then 500mg week 2ABSSSI, bone/joint (selected cases)Minimal; single/two-dose regimen
Oritavancin1200mg IV × 1ABSSSISingle dose; avoid with warfarin (interferes with INR)

Antibiotics Requiring More Frequent Dosing

DrugTypical DoseOPAT Considerations
Vancomycin15-20 mg/kg IV q8-12hRequires trough monitoring; consider AUC-based dosing. Nephrotoxic.
Cefazolin2g IV q8h (or 2g q12h with probenecid)MSSA bone/joint, endocarditis. Can give via elastomeric pump.
Cefepime2g IV q8h (or extended infusion)Pseudomonas coverage. Neurotoxicity in renal impairment.
Piperacillin-Tazobactam3.375-4.5g IV q6-8hBroad-spectrum; requires frequent dosing or extended infusion.
Ampicillin2g IV q4hEnterococcal infections, Listeria. Difficult for OPAT due to frequent dosing.

Oral Alternatives to Consider (Avoid OPAT if Possible)

Per OVIVA trial and other data, oral antibiotics with excellent bioavailability may be equivalent to IV for many infections including osteomyelitis:

  • Fluoroquinolones: Levofloxacin, ciprofloxacin (excellent bioavailability)
  • Linezolid: 100% bioavailability; MRSA coverage
  • TMP-SMX: Good for MRSA, some GNRs
  • Doxycycline: MRSA SSTIs, some respiratory
  • Metronidazole: Anaerobic coverage

Bottom Line: Before committing to OPAT, always ask: "Is there an oral option with equivalent efficacy?"

General Monitoring Schedule

TimeframeLabs
Baseline (before discharge)CBC, BMP, LFTs
WeeklyCBC, BMP (Cr), LFTs
As indicatedDrug levels, CK, additional tests per drug

Drug-Specific Monitoring

DrugSpecific MonitoringFrequency
VancomycinTrough (or AUC), BMP (nephrotoxicity)Trough: 48-72h after start, then weekly. BMP weekly.
AminoglycosidesRandom level (Hartford), BMP, audiometry if prolongedLevel per nomogram; BMP weekly
DaptomycinCK (myopathy)Weekly; hold if CK >10× ULN or symptoms
LinezolidCBC (myelosuppression), especially plateletsWeekly; risk increases after 2 weeks
Beta-lactamsCBC (neutropenia, eosinophilia), LFTs, BMPWeekly; neutropenia more common with prolonged use
TMP-SMX (IV)CBC, BMP (hyperkalemia), LFTsWeekly
Amphotericin BBMP (K, Mg, Cr), CBCDaily initially, then 2-3× weekly

When to Hold and Call

  • Fever or new symptoms: Treatment failure, new infection, drug reaction
  • Rash: Drug allergy (may need to switch)
  • Line issues: Redness, swelling, pain at site; difficulty infusing
  • Lab abnormalities: Significant Cr rise, cytopenias, LFT elevations >3× ULN

Osteomyelitis

OrganismIV OptionOral OptionDuration
MSSACefazolin 2g IV q8hCephalexin 1g PO QID or Dicloxacillin 500mg PO QID6 weeks
MRSAVancomycin or DaptomycinLinezolid 600mg PO BID or TMP-SMX DS 2 tabs PO BID + Rifampin6 weeks
PseudomonasCefepime 2g IV q8h or CeftazidimeCiprofloxacin 750mg PO BID (if susceptible)6 weeks

Endocarditis (Native Valve)

OrganismRegimenDuration
Viridans Strep (penicillin-susceptible)Ceftriaxone 2g IV q24h4 weeks (or 2 weeks with gentamicin synergy)
MSSACefazolin 2g IV q8h6 weeks
MRSAVancomycin or Daptomycin 8-10 mg/kg IV q24h6 weeks
Enterococcus (ampicillin-susceptible)Ampicillin 2g IV q4h + Ceftriaxone 2g IV q12h6 weeks

Note: Endocarditis OPAT requires close ID follow-up and careful patient selection.

Septic Arthritis (Native Joint)

  • MSSA: Cefazolin 2g IV q8h → 2-4 weeks total (with adequate drainage)
  • MRSA: Vancomycin (trough 15-20) or Daptomycin → 2-4 weeks total
  • GNR: Based on susceptibilities; 2-4 weeks total

Intra-Abdominal Infections

  • Ertapenem 1g IV q24h: Excellent once-daily option for polymicrobial infections after source control
  • Duration: 4-7 days after source control (per STOP-IT trial)

Types of IV Access for OPAT

TypeDurationProsCons
PICCWeeks to monthsDurable, easy to place, suitable for most infusionsDVT risk (~3-5%), infection risk, may not be placed if small veins
Midline1-4 weeksLower DVT risk than PICC, no chest X-ray neededCannot infuse vesicants, shorter duration
PortMonths to yearsVery durable, lower infection rate when not accessedRequires surgical placement, more expensive

Line Complications

  • Occlusion: Unable to infuse or withdraw. Try TPA (alteplase) instillation.
  • Infection: Exit site infection vs. CLABSI. Exit site: local care, may salvage line. CLABSI: usually requires line removal.
  • Thrombosis: Arm swelling, pain. Confirm with ultrasound. Anticoagulate; line removal often not necessary if functional.
  • Dislodgement: Confirm position; may need replacement.

Patient Education

  • Keep dressing clean and dry
  • Flush line per protocol (usually heparin or saline)
  • Signs to report: fever, redness/swelling at site, arm swelling, difficulty infusing
  • Never use line for non-prescribed purposes

Antimicrobial Prophylaxis Tables

Surgical prophylaxis, neutropenia/transplant prophylaxis, and asplenia management.

General Principles

  • Timing: Give within 60 minutes before incision (120 min for vancomycin and fluoroquinolones)
  • Re-dosing: Re-dose intraoperatively for prolonged procedures (>2 half-lives) or significant blood loss
  • Duration: Single dose or ≤24 hours postoperatively for most procedures
  • No benefit from continuing antibiotics based on drains, catheters, or "until lines removed"

Surgical Prophylaxis by Procedure

ProcedureFirst-LinePCN AllergyMRSA Risk*
Cardiac (sternotomy, CABG, valve)Cefazolin 2g IVVancomycin 15 mg/kgAdd Vancomycin
Orthopedic (arthroplasty, spine fusion, ORIF)Cefazolin 2g IVVancomycin 15 mg/kgAdd Vancomycin
Vascular (arterial, lower extremity amputation)Cefazolin 2g IVVancomycin 15 mg/kgAdd Vancomycin
ColorectalCefazolin 2g + Metronidazole 500mg IV (or Ertapenem 1g)Clindamycin 900mg + Gentamicin; or Metronidazole + FluoroquinoloneAdd Vancomycin
AppendectomyCefazolin 2g + Metronidazole 500mg (or Cefoxitin 2g)Metronidazole + Fluoroquinolone—
Cholecystectomy (high-risk only)Cefazolin 2g IVClindamycin + Gentamicin—
HysterectomyCefazolin 2g IVClindamycin 900mg + Gentamicin—
Cesarean sectionCefazolin 2g IV (before incision)Clindamycin 900mg + Gentamicin—
CraniotomyCefazolin 2g IVVancomycin 15 mg/kgAdd Vancomycin
Head and neck (clean-contaminated)Cefazolin 2g + Metronidazole 500mg (or Ampicillin-Sulbactam 3g)Clindamycin 900mg—
Urologic (clean-contaminated)Cefazolin 2g IV or FluoroquinoloneFluoroquinolone or Aminoglycoside—

*MRSA risk factors: known MRSA colonization, recent MRSA infection, institution with high MRSA prevalence. Add vancomycin to (not replace) cefazolin.

Weight-Based Dosing

  • Cefazolin: 2g if <120 kg; 3g if ≥120 kg
  • Vancomycin: 15 mg/kg (round to nearest 250mg); max 2g single dose

Cross-link: 📚 IMCRG: Perioperative Management

Definitions

  • Neutropenia: ANC <500 cells/μL, or <1000 and expected to fall to <500
  • Prolonged neutropenia: >7 days
  • Profound neutropenia: ANC <100 cells/μL

Antibacterial Prophylaxis

Risk GroupProphylaxisNotes
High-risk (expected prolonged/profound neutropenia: AML induction, HSCT conditioning)Levofloxacin 500mg PO dailyStart with chemotherapy; reduces GNR bacteremia. Continue until ANC recovery or fever develops.
Standard-risk (most solid tumor chemo)Generally not recommendedRisk does not outweigh benefit of fluoroquinolone exposure.

Antifungal Prophylaxis

SettingRegimenTarget Organisms
AML induction, MDSPosaconazole 300mg PO daily (or 200mg TID suspension)Candida + Aspergillus (mold-active)
Allogeneic HSCT (pre-engraftment)Fluconazole 400mg PO daily OR Posaconazole/VoriconazoleCandida; mold-active agents if high Aspergillus risk
Allogeneic HSCT (with GVHD)Posaconazole 300mg PO dailyCandida + Aspergillus (high mold risk)
Autologous HSCTFluconazole 400mg PO dailyCandida (lower mold risk)

Antiviral Prophylaxis

IndicationRegimenNotes
HSV seropositive + high-intensity chemo or HSCTAcyclovir 400mg PO BID or Valacyclovir 500mg PO dailyPrevents HSV reactivation (mucositis, esophagitis)
CMV high-risk (D+/R- transplant)Valganciclovir 900mg PO daily OR preemptive monitoringSee CMV section above

PCP Prophylaxis in Hematologic Malignancy

  • Indications: ALL, alemtuzumab, fludarabine, high-dose steroids (≥20 mg × ≥4 weeks), allogeneic HSCT
  • Regimen: TMP-SMX 1 DS daily or 1 DS 3× weekly

High-Risk Organisms

  • Encapsulated bacteria: Streptococcus pneumoniae (most common), Haemophilus influenzae, Neisseria meningitidis
  • Other: Capnocytophaga canimorsus (dog bites), Babesia (endemic areas), Plasmodium (malaria)

Overwhelming Post-Splenectomy Infection (OPSI): Fulminant sepsis with high mortality (50-70%). Risk is lifelong but highest in first 2 years post-splenectomy.

Vaccinations (Critical)

VaccineTiming (Elective Splenectomy)Timing (Emergent/Unvaccinated)
Pneumococcal (PCV20 or PCV15 → PPSV23)≥2 weeks pre-op≥2 weeks post-op. PCV15 then PPSV23 ≥8 weeks later.
Meningococcal (MenACWY + MenB)≥2 weeks pre-op≥2 weeks post-op
Haemophilus influenzae type b (Hib)≥2 weeks pre-op≥2 weeks post-op
InfluenzaAnnuallyAnnually

Note: If splenectomy is urgent, vaccinate ≥2 weeks post-op (immune response is suboptimal immediately after surgery).

Antibiotic Prophylaxis

SettingRecommendation
Children <5 yearsPenicillin VK 125-250mg PO BID until age 5 (at minimum)
Adults post-splenectomyConsider prophylaxis for 1-2 years (especially if immunocompromised). Amoxicillin 250-500mg PO daily or Penicillin VK 250mg PO BID.
Lifelong prophylaxisConsider if: immunocompromised, history of OPSI, poor antibody response to vaccination

Patient Education — Wallet Card

  • Carry a wallet card indicating asplenic status
  • Fever = Emergency: Seek immediate medical attention for any fever. Do not wait.
  • Standby antibiotics: Prescribe amoxicillin-clavulanate 875/125mg to take at first sign of illness if unable to reach medical care quickly
  • Animal bites: High risk for Capnocytophaga; seek care immediately and start antibiotics
  • Travel: Malaria prophylaxis essential in endemic areas; Babesia risk in Northeast US

Management of Febrile Asplenic Patient

  • Empiric antibiotics IMMEDIATELY — do not wait for cultures
  • Regimen: Ceftriaxone 2g IV + Vancomycin (if MRSA risk or critically ill)
  • Blood cultures, but do not delay antibiotics
  • Consider ICU admission for close monitoring

Who Needs Prophylaxis?

Prophylaxis is recommended only for patients at highest risk of adverse outcomes from endocarditis:

  • Prosthetic cardiac valve or prosthetic material used for valve repair
  • Previous infective endocarditis
  • Certain congenital heart diseases:
    • Unrepaired cyanotic CHD
    • Repaired CHD with residual defects at or near prosthetic material
    • Repaired CHD with prosthetic material <6 months post-procedure
  • Cardiac transplant recipients with valvulopathy

Note: MVP, bicuspid aortic valve, and most other valve disorders do NOT require prophylaxis per current AHA guidelines.

Which Procedures?

  • Dental: Procedures involving gingival tissue, periapical region of teeth, or perforation of oral mucosa
  • NOT indicated: Routine GI or GU procedures (including EGD, colonoscopy)

Regimens

SituationRegimenDose (30-60 min before procedure)
StandardAmoxicillin2g PO
Unable to take POAmpicillin OR Cefazolin/Ceftriaxone2g IV/IM OR 1g IV/IM
Penicillin allergy (non-severe)Cephalexin2g PO
Penicillin allergy (severe/anaphylaxis)Azithromycin OR Clindamycin500mg PO OR 600mg PO
