🏥 Infection Prevention & Control

Isolation Precautions

HIGH YIELD
Precaution TypePPE RequiredRoomIndications
Standard Precautions Gloves (blood/body fluids), gown (splash risk), mask (splash risk), eye protection (splash risk), hand hygiene always Any room ALL patients, ALL settings — baseline; assumes any patient may be infectious
Contact Precautions Gloves + gown for ALL contact with patient or patient environment Private room preferred; cohort if not available MRSA, VRE, ESBL, CRE, C. diff, Acinetobacter, C. auris, scabies, impetigo, RSV (infants), norovirus
Droplet Precautions Surgical mask within 3 feet of patient; gown and gloves per standard Private room preferred; door may remain open Influenza, COVID-19 (≥ droplet), Bordetella, Mycoplasma, N. meningitidis, mumps, rubella, Group A Strep pharyngitis
Airborne Precautions N95 respirator (fit-tested) for all who enter room; PAPR for high-risk aerosol-generating procedures Negative-pressure room (AIIR — >12 air changes/hour); door CLOSED at all times MAVT: Measles, Airborne (Aspergillus in immunocompromised), Varicella/Zoster (disseminated), Tuberculosis. Also: SARS-CoV-2 during AGPs, smallpox, Monkeypox (contact + airborne for AGPs)
⚡ High-Yield Isolation Mnemonics
  • MAVT (Airborne): Measles, Aspergillus (immunocompromised), Varicella/Zoster, TB
  • Contact precautions "MDROs + Skin": MRSA, VRE, CRE, ESBL, C. diff, C. auris + skin/wound infections, RSV, norovirus
  • C. difficile: Contact precautions + SOAP AND WATER handwashing (alcohol does not kill C. diff spores)
  • Duration of precautions: MRSA/VRE: until 3 negative swabs (institutional policy varies); C. diff: until 48h after last liquid stool; TB: until 3 negative AFB smears on 3 separate days

Hand Hygiene

WHO "5 Moments" for Hand Hygiene
  1. Before touching a patient
  2. Before clean/aseptic procedures
  3. After body fluid exposure risk
  4. After touching a patient
  5. After touching patient surroundings
Alcohol-Based Hand Rub (ABHR) vs. Soap & Water
ABHR (Preferred)Soap & Water (Required)
EfficacySuperior for most pathogens; kills bacteria, enveloped viruses, most fungiRequired to remove/kill certain pathogens
When requiredMost clinical situationsC. difficile spores (ABHR insufficient); norovirus (controversial — ABHR has some efficacy; soap preferred); visibly soiled hands
Duration15–30 seconds until dry20 seconds minimum; dry with paper towel

Compliance: Hand hygiene compliance is the single most evidence-based intervention for preventing HAIs. Regular auditing and feedback improve compliance rates.

HAI Prevention Bundles (CLABSI, CAUTI, VAP, SSI)

HIGH YIELD
CLABSI Prevention Bundle
  • Hand hygiene before line placement and dressing changes
  • Maximal sterile barrier precautions (cap, mask, gown, sterile gloves, full drape) during insertion
  • Chlorhexidine skin antisepsis (2% chlorhexidine preferred; allow to dry)
  • Avoid femoral site (highest infection risk); subclavian lowest infection risk; IJ intermediate
  • Daily review of line necessity — remove as soon as no longer needed
  • Chlorhexidine-impregnated dressings for high-risk patients
  • Antibiotic or antiseptic-impregnated catheters if institutional CLABSI rate remains high despite bundle
CAUTI Prevention Bundle
  • Insert only when indicated (specific criteria — not for incontinence, not for nursing convenience)
  • Aseptic insertion technique; smallest appropriate catheter
  • Maintain closed drainage system — never disconnect unless necessary
  • Keep bag below bladder level — never let bag touch floor
  • Daily review of catheter necessity — remove as soon as possible
  • Catheter securement to prevent movement/trauma
  • Routine catheter change NOT recommended unless malfunctioning
  • Antimicrobial-coated catheters: silver-alloy catheters show modest CAUTI reduction; routine use debated
VAP Prevention Bundle
  • HOB elevation 30–45° (reduces microaspiration)
  • Daily sedation interruption ("sedation vacation") + spontaneous breathing trial assessment
  • Oral care with chlorhexidine 0.12% BID (reduces oral colonization)
  • Subglottic secretion drainage (specialized ETT with subglottic port) — reduces early-onset VAP
  • Daily assessment of readiness for extubation — minimize ventilator days
  • VTE prophylaxis + stress ulcer prophylaxis (part of original "ventilator bundle")
  • Avoid routine ventilator circuit changes (more changes = higher risk)
SSI Prevention Bundle
  • Appropriate antibiotic prophylaxis: Within 60 minutes before incision (within 120 min for vancomycin/fluoroquinolones); re-dose if procedure >4 hours or blood loss >1.5 L; discontinue within 24 hours post-op
  • Normothermia: Maintain intraoperative temperature >36°C (warming blankets, warmed fluids)
  • Glycemic control: Perioperative glucose <180–200 mg/dL (avoid hypoglycemia)
  • Minimize blood transfusion when possible
  • Sterile surgical technique: Appropriate draping, skin antisepsis (chlorhexidine-alcohol preferred)
  • MRSA nasal decolonization (mupirocin 2% nasal ointment × 5 days pre-op) for cardiac/orthopedic surgery
  • Showering with chlorhexidine night before and morning of surgery

Healthcare Worker Exposures (Needlestick, Blood/Body Fluid)

HIGH YIELD
Immediate Management of Needlestick/Splash
  1. Wound/exposure site: Wash with soap and water (needlestick); flush mucous membranes with water or saline. Do NOT squeeze wound or use bleach.
  2. Report immediately to occupational health / employee health
  3. Source patient testing: Rapid HIV, HBsAg, anti-HCV (with consent if possible — public health laws vary by state)
  4. Risk assessment — start PEP while awaiting results if indicated
HIV Post-Exposure Prophylaxis (PEP)
Details
When to startWithin 72 hours (ideally <4 hours); efficacy decreases with delay; after 72 hours — not recommended
Preferred regimenTenofovir DF/emtricitabine (TDF/FTC) 300/200 mg PO daily + Dolutegravir 50 mg PO daily × 28 days
AlternativeTDF/FTC + Raltegravir 400 mg BID × 28 days
Duration28 days — must complete full course
Follow-up HIV testingBaseline, 6 weeks, 12 weeks; some recommend 6 months if source has acute HIV
Transmission riskPercutaneous (needlestick): ~0.3% per exposure from known HIV+ source; mucous membrane: ~0.09%; intact skin: negligible
Hepatitis B (HBV) Post-Exposure Prophylaxis
HCW StatusSource HBsAg+Source Unknown
Vaccinated, anti-HBs ≥10 mIU/mLNo treatmentNo treatment
Vaccinated, anti-HBs <10 mIU/mL (non-responder)HBIG × 2 doses (1 month apart); consider re-vaccination seriesConsider HBIG × 1 + revaccination
UnvaccinatedHBIG 0.06 mL/kg IM × 1 + Begin HBV vaccine series within 24 hoursBegin HBV vaccine series
Vaccination in progressHBIG × 1 + complete vaccination seriesComplete vaccination series

HBIG timing: Most effective within 24 hours; up to 7 days for sexual exposure.

Hepatitis C (HCV) Post-Exposure
  • No post-exposure prophylaxis available (no vaccine, no effective PEP)
  • Transmission risk percutaneous: ~1.8% per exposure (higher than HIV, lower than HBV)
  • Monitoring protocol: HCV antibody + HCV RNA at baseline; HCV RNA at 3–6 weeks; HCV antibody + RNA at 4–6 months
  • Early treatment if infection occurs: DAA therapy (sofosbuvir/velpatasvir × 12 weeks) — >95% cure rate. Some experts treat early acute HCV (<24 weeks from infection) with 8–12 week shortened course.
HCW Vaccination Requirements
VaccineRequirementNotes
InfluenzaAnnual — mandatory at most US hospitalsReduces patient mortality; HCW with contraindication should wear mask
Hepatitis BRequired for all HCWs with blood/body fluid exposure riskDocument anti-HBs post-vaccination titer; booster if non-responder
VaricellaRequired; 2-dose series if no history of disease or prior vaccinationDocument serology if uncertain immune status
MMRRequired; 2 doses if born after 1957Seronegative HCW exposed to measles → prophylaxis within 72h (MMR vaccine) or IVIG within 6 days
COVID-19Most healthcare systems require or strongly recommendUpdated formulations annually similar to influenza
Tdap1 dose; Td booster q10 yearsCocooning strategy — protects neonates in NICU/obstetrics

Outbreak Investigation — Applied Epidemiology

HIGH YIELD
Steps in Outbreak Investigation (Systematic Framework)
  1. Verify the diagnosis — Confirm cases are real; review laboratory and clinical criteria. Rule out laboratory errors or reporting artifacts.
  2. Confirm the existence of an outbreak — Compare current incidence to baseline (expected rate). Epidemic curve plots cases over time.
  3. Define a case — Case definition includes person (demographics), place (unit, hospital), time (onset dates), clinical criteria. Differentiate confirmed, probable, possible cases.
  4. Find all cases — Epidemic curve — Plot onset dates to identify common source vs. propagated outbreak pattern.
  5. Describe and orient the data (Descriptive Epidemiology) — Person (who), Place (where), Time (when). Calculate attack rates by subgroups.
  6. Develop hypotheses — Identify potential source/vehicle/reservoir based on descriptive data.
  7. Analytical study — Case-control or cohort study to test hypotheses. Identify risk factors.
  8. Implement and evaluate control measures — Simultaneously with investigation. Specific to pathogen (contact precautions, environmental cleaning, cohorting).
  9. Communicate findings — Infection control, administration, public health as appropriate.
Epidemic Curve Interpretation
PatternInterpretationExample
Point sourceSharp peak within 1 incubation period; all exposed simultaneouslyContaminated food at a single meal event; single-dose chemical exposure
Continuous common sourceSustained elevated cases over multiple incubation periods; ongoing exposureContaminated water supply; infected food worker over weeks
Propagated (person-to-person)Successive peaks each separated by one incubation period; successive generationsInfluenza, norovirus in a closed institution; measles outbreak
MixedInitial point source then person-to-person spreadNorovirus: initial contaminated buffet → then spread among roommates
Key Biostatistics Concepts for ID
MeasureDefinitionFormula
Attack rateProportion of exposed who develop disease during an outbreakCases / Exposed persons × 100
Relative Risk (RR)Rate of disease in exposed vs. unexposed (cohort study)Risk in exposed / Risk in unexposed
Odds Ratio (OR)Odds of exposure in cases vs. controls (case-control study); approximates RR for rare disease(a×d) / (b×c) from 2×2 table
Attributable RiskExcess risk due to specific exposureRisk(exposed) − Risk(unexposed)
Number Needed to Treat (NNT)Number of patients who must receive treatment to prevent 1 adverse outcome1 / Absolute Risk Reduction
SensitivityTrue positive rate — ability to detect disease when presentTP / (TP + FN)
SpecificityTrue negative rate — ability to rule out disease when absentTN / (TN + FP)
PPVProbability disease present given positive test — affected by prevalenceTP / (TP + FP)
NPVProbability disease absent given negative test — affected by prevalenceTN / (TN + FN)

High-yield board pearl: In a low-prevalence setting, even a highly specific test will have many false positives (low PPV). In a high-prevalence setting, even a highly sensitive test will have many false negatives (low NPV relative to pretest probability).

Healthcare Quality Improvement Metrics
  • Standardized Infection Ratio (SIR): Observed HAIs / Predicted HAIs. SIR <1.0 = better than predicted; used for national benchmarking (NHSN).
  • Device-day denominators: Central line-days, ventilator-days, catheter-days — adjusts for patient exposure time when calculating HAI rates
  • Process vs. outcome measures: Process = bundle compliance (e.g., % of patients with daily line necessity review); outcome = CLABSI rate per 1000 central line-days
  • PDSA cycles (Plan-Do-Study-Act): Standard QI methodology for iterative improvement in HAI prevention programs

Disinfection, Sterilization & Environmental Control

Spaulding Classification
CategoryDevice TypeRequired LevelExamples
CriticalEnters sterile tissue or vasculatureSterilization (kills all microorganisms including spores)Surgical instruments, cardiac catheters, implants, needles, endoscopes that enter sterile cavities
Semi-criticalContacts mucous membranes or non-intact skinHigh-level disinfection (kills all except high levels of bacterial spores)Flexible endoscopes (GI/respiratory), bronchoscopes, laryngoscope blades, vaginal probes
Non-criticalContacts intact skin onlyLow-to-intermediate disinfectionBlood pressure cuffs, stethoscopes, bed rails, floors, patient furniture
Sterilization Methods
  • Steam autoclave (moist heat): Gold standard for heat-stable items. 121°C × 15 minutes at 15 psi (gravity cycle) or 132–135°C × 3–4 min (vacuum cycle)
  • Ethylene oxide (ETO): For heat-sensitive items (electronics, plastics). Long aeration time required (toxic gas).
  • Hydrogen peroxide gas plasma: Low temperature; rapid; for heat-sensitive items; no toxic residues
  • Glutaraldehyde (2.4%): High-level disinfection for endoscopes — 20-minute contact time; cidex. Sterilization with 10-hour soak.
Environmental Cleaning for Specific Pathogens
PathogenRecommended DisinfectantNotes
C. difficileEPA-registered sporicidal (hypochlorite/bleach 1000–5000 ppm, or hydrogen peroxide vapor)Standard quaternary ammonium compounds are NOT effective against C. diff spores; bleach required in C. diff rooms
NorovirusHypochlorite (bleach 1000–5000 ppm); hydrogen peroxideQuaternary ammonium inadequate; wash hands with soap + water (not just ABHR)
MRSA/VRE/CREQuaternary ammonium or accelerated hydrogen peroxide (standard hospital disinfectants)Enhanced cleaning protocols; terminal cleaning with UV disinfection shown to reduce acquisition
C. aurisEPA-registered products specifically tested against C. auris (many common disinfectants less effective)Persists on surfaces weeks; contact precautions + enhanced environmental cleaning essential

Infections in Long-Term Care Facilities (LTCF)

Key Considerations
  • Most common infections in LTCF: UTI (most common diagnosis, but often overtreated), pneumonia (highest mortality), skin/soft tissue (pressure ulcers, cellulitis), GI (C. difficile, norovirus outbreaks)
  • Asymptomatic bacteriuria: Very common in LTCF residents (20–50% of women, 15–40% of men with catheters); do NOT treat — treat only if symptomatic UTI criteria met (dysuria, new/worsening urinary sx, fever, altered mental status with no other cause)
  • Antibiotic stewardship: McGeer criteria for initiating antibiotics in LTCF — evidence-based minimum criteria for each infection type to reduce inappropriate antibiotic use
  • MDRO management: Isolation precautions adapted for LTCF — balance infection control with patient quality of life and dignity; cohorting residents; dedicated staff if possible during outbreaks
  • Vaccination in LTCF: Annual influenza (residents and staff), pneumococcal (PCV20 × 1 if not previously vaccinated), COVID-19 boosters, zoster (Shingrix × 2)

Calculators

CrCl (Cockcroft-Gault)

SIRS / qSOFA Quick

Antibiotic Safety in Pregnancy

FDA pregnancy categories replaced by PLLR (2015); below reflects current evidence-based safety classification.

Drug / ClassSafetyKey Concerns
Penicillins (amoxicillin, ampicillin, pip-tazo)SAFEFirst-line in pregnancy for most indications; no known teratogenicity
Cephalosporins (all generations)SAFENo known teratogenicity; preferred over many alternatives in pregnancy
CarbapenemsLIKELY SAFELimited human data; animal studies reassuring; use when benefits outweigh risks
AzithromycinSAFEPreferred macrolide in pregnancy; used for CAP, chlamydia
Erythromycin (base, not estolate)LIKELY SAFEEstolate form associated with cholestatic hepatitis — avoid; base/stearate acceptable
ClarithromycinAVOIDAnimal teratogenicity (cardiovascular defects); limited human data — use azithromycin instead
ClindamycinSAFENo known teratogenicity; use for anaerobic coverage, GBS prophylaxis (PCN-allergic)
MetronidazoleSAFE (all trimesters)Previously avoided in 1st trimester; meta-analyses show no increased teratogenicity; single-dose regimens preferred for BV in 1st trimester
VancomycinLIKELY SAFEUse when indicated for MRSA; crosses placenta — monitor fetal wellbeing; ototoxicity theoretical concern at high levels
DaptomycinUSE WITH CAUTIONLimited human data; animal studies reassuring; use when alternatives inadequate
LinezolidUSE WITH CAUTIONLimited human data; animal toxicity; use for MRSA when vancomycin not feasible
NitrofurantoinAVOID AT TERM (≥38 weeks)Safe in 1st/2nd trimester for cystitis; risk of neonatal hemolytic anemia at term; avoid in G6PD deficiency
Fluoroquinolones (ciprofloxacin, levofloxacin)AVOID (generally)Cartilage toxicity in animal studies; arthropathy in pediatric data; teratogenicity not clearly established in humans; avoid unless no alternatives (e.g., anthrax, MDR TB, MDR UTI)
Doxycycline / TetracyclinesCONTRAINDICATED (2nd/3rd trimester)Permanent tooth discoloration and enamel hypoplasia in fetus; bone growth inhibition; safe in 1st trimester only if no alternatives (e.g., RMSF — doxycycline is still preferred for RMSF even in pregnancy due to severity)
TMP-SMXAVOID (1st trimester + at term)Folate antagonist — neural tube defect risk in 1st trimester; kernicterus risk at term; safe in 2nd trimester with folic acid supplementation
AminoglycosidesAVOID (if alternatives exist)8th nerve toxicity (fetal ototoxicity — sensorineural hearing loss, vestibular damage); renal toxicity; use only if life-threatening infection with no alternatives
ChloramphenicolAVOID AT TERM"Gray baby syndrome" — cardiovascular collapse in neonate; avoid near delivery

ID-Specific Drug Interactions

High-yield interactions most relevant to infectious disease practice.

Azoles are potent CYP3A4 inhibitors — they dramatically increase levels of many co-administered drugs. This is the single most clinically important drug interaction class in ID practice.

Drug affectedAzoleEffectManagement
TacrolimusAll azoles (esp. voriconazole, itraconazole)Tacrolimus levels increase 3–10× — severe nephrotoxicity, neurotoxicityReduce tacrolimus dose by 50–75%; monitor levels daily
CyclosporineAll azolesCyclosporine levels increase 2–5×Reduce cyclosporine dose; monitor levels
Sirolimus / EverolimusAll azolesSirolimus levels increase enormously — potentially toxicAvoid combination if possible; if needed, hold sirolimus and monitor
WarfarinFluconazole (most potent), all azolesINR increase — bleeding risk; fluconazole 400mg can double INRReduce warfarin dose 25–50% when starting fluconazole; monitor INR closely
DOACs (apixaban, rivaroxaban)All azolesDOAC levels increase → bleeding riskAvoid strong CYP3A4/P-gp inhibitors (itraconazole, ketoconazole, voriconazole) with DOACs; fluconazole moderate risk — use with caution
Statins (simvastatin, lovastatin, atorvastatin)Itraconazole, voriconazoleStatin AUC increases dramatically — myopathy/rhabdomyolysisHold simvastatin/lovastatin; use pravastatin or rosuvastatin (less CYP3A4-dependent)
QT-prolonging drugs (amiodarone, quinidine, antipsychotics)All azoles (also prolong QT)Additive QT prolongation + increased drug levels → torsades de pointesECG monitoring; avoid combinations when possible; use with extreme caution
Phenytoin, carbamazepineAll azolesBidirectional: Azoles inhibit metabolism of anticonvulsants (↑ levels); anticonvulsants induce CYP and reduce azole levelsMonitor anticonvulsant levels; increase voriconazole dose; consider alternative anticonvulsant
Benzodiazepines (midazolam, triazolam)All azolesProlonged sedation — levels increase significantlyUse lorazepam (renal elimination, no CYP metabolism) instead
Voriconazole: visual disturbance, hepatotoxicityUnique to voriconazolePhotopsia/visual disturbance (30%), elevated LFTs (15%), photosensitivity, periostitis (long-term)Baseline + monthly LFTs; voriconazole trough levels (target 1–5.5 mcg/mL); avoid prolonged sun exposure

Rifampin is one of the most potent CYP enzyme inducers known. It dramatically DECREASES levels of nearly every drug metabolized by CYP3A4, 2C9, 2C19, and P-gp. Drug interactions are pervasive and often severe.

Drug classEffectManagement
Immunosuppressants (tacrolimus, cyclosporine, sirolimus)Levels decrease 80–90% — transplant rejectionAvoid rifampin in transplant patients if at all possible; if must use, increase doses 3–5× and monitor levels daily; rifabutin is a weaker inducer — preferred alternative for mycobacterial infections in transplant
Azole antifungals (voriconazole, itraconazole)Voriconazole levels drop >90% — treatment failureAvoid rifampin + voriconazole (CONTRAINDICATED); rifabutin if needed (monitor levels)
WarfarinINR decreases dramatically — thrombosis riskIncrease warfarin dose significantly (often 2–3×); monitor INR every 1–3 days when starting/stopping rifampin
Oral contraceptivesEstrogen/progestin levels decrease — contraceptive failureUse barrier contraception throughout rifampin course and for 1 month after
DOACsDOAC levels decrease — loss of anticoagulationAvoid combination; use warfarin instead (adjustable dose)
Antiretrovirals (PIs, integrase inhibitors)ART levels decrease — virologic failureRifabutin preferred over rifampin in HIV+ patients on ART; consult HIV specialist
MethadoneLevels decrease — withdrawalMonitor carefully; increase methadone dose; PWID on MAT — critical counseling
DoxycyclineLevels decrease — may compromise efficacy for Lyme/brucellosis/Q feverAvoid if possible; alternative antibiotic regimens for combination therapy

Rifabutin (weaker inducer): Preferred alternative to rifampin when drug interactions are prohibitive — particularly in transplant recipients and HIV+ patients. Still causes interactions, but magnitude is smaller. Used for MAC prophylaxis/treatment and in HIV/TB co-treatment.

Risk Classification

RiskAntibioticsNotes
High riskMoxifloxacin, azithromycin (IV > PO), clarithromycin, erythromycin IV, fluconazole (at high doses), voriconazoleAvoid in QTc >500ms; use with extreme caution with other QT-prolonging drugs
Moderate riskCiprofloxacin, levofloxacin, fluconazole (standard doses), amphotericin B (electrolyte effects)Monitor ECG if baseline QTc borderline or other risk factors
Lower riskBeta-lactams, clindamycin, doxycycline, TMP-SMX, metronidazole, aminoglycosidesGenerally safe regarding QT

Risk Factors for QT Prolongation / Torsades

  • Baseline QTc >450ms (women), >440ms (men)
  • Hypokalemia, hypomagnesemia, hypocalcemia — correct before QT-prolonging antibiotics
  • Bradycardia, heart failure, left ventricular hypertrophy
  • Female sex (longer baseline QTc)
  • Concomitant QT-prolonging drugs (antipsychotics, antiarrhythmics, antiemetics)

Alternatives When QT Prolongation is a Concern

  • CAP: Use beta-lactam + azithromycin PO (lower risk) vs. moxifloxacin alone; or beta-lactam monotherapy for non-severe CAP
  • Fungal: Liposomal amphotericin B instead of voriconazole; echinocandin instead of azole
  • MAC: Azithromycin PO at standard doses has lower risk than IV; monitor ECG at baseline
InteractionDetails
Antacids, calcium, iron, multivitaminsChelation — reduce fluoroquinolone absorption by 40–90%; take fluoroquinolone 2h before or 6h after; IV fluoroquinolone avoids this
WarfarinCiprofloxacin inhibits CYP1A2 — warfarin levels increase; monitor INR when starting/stopping
MethotrexateRenal tubular secretion competition — MTX levels increase; monitor CBC and renal function
NSAIDs + quinolonesIncreased CNS seizure threshold lowering — avoid in seizure-prone patients
Tendon ruptureBlack box warning — Achilles tendon most common; risk increases with steroids, age >60, renal failure, organ transplant; discontinue immediately if tendon pain
Peripheral neuropathyCan be permanent; FDA black box warning; discontinue if symptoms develop
Aortic aneurysm/dissectionFDA warning (2018) — epidemiologic association; avoid in patients with known aortic disease if alternatives exist
CNS effectsConfusion, seizures, hallucinations — especially in elderly; avoid in patients with CNS disease or seizure history

Serologic Testing Interpretation

Quick reference for interpreting commonly ordered infectious disease serologies.

HBsAgAnti-HBsAnti-HBc IgMAnti-HBc IgG (total)HBV DNAInterpretation
+−+++Acute HBV infection
+−−+VariesChronic HBV (active replication if DNA detectable)
−+−+−Past infection — immune (natural)
−+−−−Vaccine-induced immunity
−−−+−Remote past infection (anti-HBs waned) — check DNA to exclude occult HBV
−−−−−Susceptible — never infected, not vaccinated
+−−+Low/undetectableChronic HBV, inactive carrier (HBeAg-negative, low viral load)
+−−+HighChronic HBV, HBeAg-negative (active) hepatitis — treatment likely needed

HBeAg / Anti-HBe: HBeAg positive = higher replication; anti-HBe positive = lower replication (but HBeAg-negative hepatitis can still have high DNA)

TestWindow PeriodUse
4th generation Ag/Ab combo (HIV-1/2 Ag/Ab)~18 daysStandard initial screening — detects both antigen (p24) and antibody; recommended by CDC
HIV-1/2 differentiation immunoassay—Confirmatory step if combo assay reactive — distinguishes HIV-1 from HIV-2
HIV-1 RNA (NAAT)~10 daysEarliest detection; used when acute HIV suspected despite negative Ag/Ab; detects acute HIV in "window period"
Western blot (historical)—No longer recommended for confirmation — replaced by Ag/Ab combo + differentiation assay algorithm

Acute HIV — When to Suspect

  • Mononucleosis-like syndrome: fever, lymphadenopathy, pharyngitis, rash, myalgias, headache, 2–4 weeks after potential exposure
  • Combo Ag/Ab may be negative in very early acute HIV — send HIV RNA if high suspicion (RNA positive before antibody)
  • Starting ART during acute HIV reduces viral reservoir and may affect long-term outcomes
TestInterpretation
CMV IgG positivePast infection (latent CMV); relevant as DONOR and RECIPIENT serostatus for transplant (D+/R− = highest risk pairing)
CMV IgM positiveRecent/acute infection OR reactivation (IgM can reappear with reactivation); not specific for primary infection
CMV PCR (quantitative — "viral load")Active replication — guides treatment decisions and monitoring in transplant/immunocompromised; threshold for treatment varies by institution and transplant type
CMV pp65 antigenemiaHistorical alternative to PCR; detects viral antigen in WBCs; less sensitive, more labor-intensive — largely replaced by PCR

CMV Disease vs. CMV Infection

  • CMV infection: Detectable CMV DNA in blood — may be asymptomatic (especially early)
  • CMV disease: CMV infection + symptoms (fever, cytopenias) OR end-organ damage (colitis, pneumonitis, retinitis, hepatitis)
  • Treatment threshold: Most centers treat when CMV PCR above institutional threshold OR with any symptoms
  • Treatment: Valganciclovir PO (preferred) or ganciclovir IV for severe disease; foscarnet if ganciclovir-resistant; monitor CMV PCR weekly until undetectable
TestAcute MonoPast InfectionNever Infected
Monospot (heterophile antibody)Positive (80–90% in adults by 2nd week)NegativeNegative
EBV VCA IgMPositiveNegativeNegative
EBV VCA IgGPositive (rising titer)Positive (stable)Negative
EBV EA (early antigen)Positive (70%)Usually negativeNegative
EBV EBNA IgGNegative (appears 2–4 months after infection)PositiveNegative

Pearl: Monospot negative in first week (50%) and in children under 4 years (negative in 60–80%); EBV-specific serology confirms in these cases. EBNA negativity confirms recent/acute infection (vs. past).

Resistance Mechanisms Reference

Understanding mechanisms guides antibiotic selection when susceptibilities are not available or when clinical failure occurs.

MechanismEnzymeOrganismsResistant toTreatment options
ESBL (Extended-spectrum β-lactamase)CTX-M (most common), TEM, SHVE. coli, Klebsiella, ProteusAll penicillins, cephalosporins (1st–4th gen), aztreonam; NOT carbapenemsCarbapenems (ertapenem, meropenem, imipenem); ceftolozane-tazobactam, ceftazidime-avibactam for selected ESBL
AmpC β-lactamaseInducible AmpC (chromosomal)SPICE organisms: Serratia, Pseudomonas, Indole+ Proteus, Citrobacter, Enterobacter (+ Morganella, Aeromonas)Induction with 3rd-gen cephalosporins; may be stable on sensitivity but derepresses with use — "inducible AmpC"Cefepime (4th gen, stable to AmpC); carbapenems; piperacillin-tazobactam (variable); avoid 3rd-gen cephalosporins even if "sensitive"
KPC (Klebsiella pneumoniae carbapenemase)KPC-2, KPC-3K. pneumoniae (also E. coli, Enterobacter)All β-lactams including carbapenems; variable aztreonam resistanceCeftazidime-avibactam (CAZ-AVI); meropenem-vaborbactam; imipenem-cilastatin-relebactam; combination therapy for severe infections
MBL (Metallo-β-lactamase) — NDM, VIM, IMPNDM-1, VIM-1/2, IMPKlebsiella, E. coli, Pseudomonas, AcinetobacterAll β-lactams including carbapenems AND CAZ-AVI (NDM)Aztreonam-avibactam (NDM-producing organisms — aztreonam not hydrolyzed by MBL); cefiderocol; colistin + polymyxin (last resort); combination therapy
OXA-48OXA-48, OXA-48-likeK. pneumoniae, E. coli (Mediterranean, Middle East)Carbapenems (often intermediate/resistant); variable cephalosporinsCAZ-AVI; combination therapy; aztreonam (if no co-produced ESBL)
CRAB (Carbapenem-resistant Acinetobacter)OXA-23, OXA-24, OXA-58A. baumanniiAll β-lactams; often pan-resistantSulbactam-based therapy (ampicillin-sulbactam high dose); cefiderocol; colistin (nephrotoxic); sulbactam-durlobactam (newer)

Clinical Pearl — SPICE Organisms

When treating SPICE organisms (Serratia, Pseudomonas, Indole-positive Proteus, Citrobacter, Enterobacter) with 3rd-generation cephalosporins — even when the isolate tests "susceptible" — resistance can emerge during therapy through AmpC derepression. Use 4th-gen cephalosporins (cefepime) or carbapenems for serious infections. "Cephalosporin-susceptible Enterobacter — derepression nightmare" is a known clinical scenario.

ResistanceMechanismTreatment
MRSAmecA gene → PBP2a (altered penicillin-binding protein with low β-lactam affinity)Vancomycin (AUC-guided); daptomycin (non-pulmonary); linezolid (bacteriostatic); ceftaroline (anti-MRSA cephalosporin)
Vancomycin-intermediate S. aureus (VISA)Thickened cell wall sequesters vancomycinDaptomycin; linezolid; ceftaroline; consult ID
VRSAvanA gene (acquired from VRE)Linezolid; TMP-SMX; chloramphenicol; ID consult mandatory
VRE (vanA)vanA operon — modified peptidoglycan precursor D-Ala-D-LacLinezolid; daptomycin; tigecycline; no β-lactam or glycopeptide activity
VRE (vanB)vanB — modified precursor; inducible resistanceSame as vanA; check susceptibilities (vanB can occasionally appear susceptible to teicoplanin)
Penicillin-resistant S. pneumoniaeAltered PBPs (mutational — no β-lactamase)High-dose penicillin if MIC ≤2; ceftriaxone (high-dose 2g q12h for CNS); meropenem for vancomycin + ceftriaxone-resistant strains; respiratory fluoroquinolones
ESBL E. coli/KlebsiellaPlasmid-mediated β-lactamase (CTX-M)Carbapenems (see above)
  • MCR-1 (and variants): Plasmid-mediated phosphoethanolamine transferase — modifies LPS, reducing colistin binding; most concerning aspect is mobile/transferable resistance
  • Organisms: E. coli, K. pneumoniae, Salmonella; emerging worldwide; still relatively rare in US
  • Clinical significance: Colistin is last-resort agent for pan-resistant GNR; MCR-mediated resistance against it is alarming
    • Treatment options when MCR present:Extremely limited — cefiderocol (siderophore cephalosporin); rifampin combinations; fosfomycin (if UTI); aztreonam-avibactam (if MBL co-produced)
  • Infection control: Contact precautions; report to infection prevention; public health notification for novel MCR strains
